# Institutio Oratoria Hebraica: A Classical Rhetoric of the Rabbinic Tradition

*In the Manner of the Ancient Rhetoricians, with Examples Drawn Exclusively from the Literature of the Sages*

---

### Editor's Preface

The treatise that follows is, of course, a fiction -- but a fiction grounded in scholarly reality. No ancient rhetorician ever composed a handbook of rhetoric using examples drawn from the rabbinic academies of Roman Palestine. Yet every element of such a handbook exists independently: the rhetorical theory is that of Aristotle, Quintilian, Cicero, and the authors of the progymnasmata handbooks; the illustrative examples are drawn, without exception, from the Mishnah, Tosefta, tannaitic midrashim, Avot de-Rabbi Natan, and the baraitot preserved in the Talmuds.

What we offer here is a thought experiment in comparative rhetoric: what would a classical *Institutio Oratoria* look like if its author had studied not in the schools of Athens and Rome but in the *batei midrash* of Yavneh, Usha, and Sepphoris? The conceit owes its plausibility to the substantial body of scholarship -- from Saul Lieberman's *Hellenism in Jewish Palestine* (1950) and David Daube's pioneering essay on rabbinic methods and Hellenistic rhetoric (1949) through Henry Fischel's studies of the chreia in rabbinic literature (1968, 1973), Marion Moeser's comparative analysis of the anecdote (2002), and Richard Hidary's comprehensive *Rabbis and Classical Rhetoric* (2018) -- demonstrating deep structural parallels between Greco-Roman rhetorical education and rabbinic pedagogical practice.

The voice of this reconstruction is that of the classical rhetorical tradition: formal, systematic, instructional, proceeding from definition to classification to illustration in the orderly manner of Theon, Hermogenes, Aphthonius, and Quintilian. Where the companion volume, *The Progymnasmata of the Bet Midrash*, speaks in the voice of a senior rabbi addressing his students -- direct, authoritative, anchored in Scripture and the chain of tradition -- this volume speaks in the voice of the rhetorician-theorist, analyzing the same material from the outside.

The reader will find the same rabbinic texts discussed in both volumes, but refracted through fundamentally different lenses. The rabbi says, "We have received a tradition"; the rhetorician says, "We observe in the schools of the Judaean sages a practice that corresponds precisely to what Quintilian describes." The rabbi cites Scripture as the ultimate authority; the rhetorician cites Aristotle and Theon as the framework within which the Judaean evidence is organized and interpreted. The rabbi speaks from within the tradition; the rhetorician speaks about the tradition from the vantage point of comparative analysis. Both perspectives illuminate; neither alone is sufficient. Together, they reveal a rhetorical culture of extraordinary sophistication -- one that deserves to be studied alongside, and in some respects above, the better-known rhetorical traditions of Greece and Rome.

A note on terminology: throughout, we employ the standard Greek and Latin nomenclature of the rhetorical tradition (*chreia*, *progymnasmata*, *inventio*, *dispositio*, *elocutio*) alongside the Hebrew and Aramaic terms used by the sages themselves. Where possible, we have supplied Hebrew calques -- *hamtza'ah* for *inventio*, *seder* for *dispositio*, *ne'imah* for *pronuntiatio* -- though the reader should understand that these are our constructions, not established rabbinic technical vocabulary. The footnotes provide references both to the primary rhetorical treatises and to the rabbinic sources, as well as to the modern scholarly literature that has made this comparative enterprise possible. For the complete catalog of chreia parallels undergirding Part II, Section C of this handbook, the reader should consult the companion volume, *Tannaitic Parallels to Theon's Chreia Taxonomy: A Comprehensive Catalog*.[^1]

---

## PART I: FOUNDATIONS

> *"Speech is the boundary between man and the beasts; rhetoric is the boundary between the orator and the crowd."*

### I.A. What Is Rhetoric?

We begin, as all treatises on the art must begin, with the question of definition. What is rhetoric? The authorities of our tradition have offered several formulations, each illuminating a different facet of the art.

Aristotle, in the first book of his *Rhetoric*, defines it as "the faculty of observing in any given case the available means of persuasion" (1.2.1355b).[^2] This definition emphasizes the *analytic* dimension of rhetoric: the orator's task is to survey the available materials -- the evidence, the audience's dispositions, the logical and emotional resources at hand -- and to select from them the most effective combination. Rhetoric, on this view, is less a productive art than a perceptive one: it teaches the orator not how to create arguments out of nothing but how to see the arguments that already exist in a given situation.

Quintilian, in the second book of his *Institutio Oratoria*, after surveying and rejecting numerous definitions, settles upon "the science of speaking well" (*bene dicendi scientia*, 2.15.34), where "well" encompasses not only effectiveness but moral probity.[^3] This definition adds an ethical dimension absent from Aristotle's: the orator who speaks effectively but immorally is not, on Quintilian's view, a true orator. Rhetoric is not morally neutral; it is, or should be, the art of the good person speaking skillfully.

The author of the *Rhetorica ad Herennium* describes it more practically as the theory that provides rules and precepts for speaking (*ratio dicendi*, 1.2.2).[^4] This is a more modest and more pedagogical definition: rhetoric is a *techne*, an art with teachable principles and learnable rules.

Cicero, in his youthful *De Inventione*, calls it "a part of the science of civil government" (*civilis scientiae pars*, 1.5.6) -- an art indispensable to the well-ordered commonwealth.[^5] This definition situates rhetoric within a political context: it is not a self-sufficient discipline but a component of the larger project of governing a community through persuasion rather than force.

These definitions, though formulated in the schools of Athens and Rome, find their fullest embodiment in a tradition that those schools never examined: the rhetorical culture of the Judaean sages.

For in the academies of Judaea, one discovers a system of rhetorical training as complete, as rigorous, and as internally consistent as anything produced by the Greek or Roman schools -- and in certain respects, more remarkable.

The Judaean sages cultivated all five divisions of rhetoric: invention, arrangement, style, memory, and delivery. They practiced a full sequence of preliminary exercises corresponding to the Greek progymnasmata: fable, narrative, chreia, refutation, confirmation, commonplace, encomium, comparison, impersonation, description, thesis, and the proposal of a law. They developed formal rules for judicial, deliberative, and epideictic discourse. And they did all of this within a pedagogical framework -- the *bet midrash*, or "house of inquiry" -- that bears striking structural resemblance to the rhetorical schools of the Mediterranean world.[^6]

What makes the Judaean achievement particularly remarkable is the degree to which it was accomplished *without explicit theorization*. The Greek and Roman rhetoricians produced voluminous theoretical treatises -- Aristotle's *Rhetoric*, Quintilian's twelve-book *Institutio Oratoria*, the handbooks of Theon, Hermogenes, Aphthonius. The Judaean sages, by contrast, produced no treatise on rhetoric. Their rhetorical practice was embedded in their legal, homiletical, and pedagogical activity rather than abstracted from it and analyzed separately. They mastered the art of rhetoric without producing a theory of rhetoric -- a feat comparable to mastering a language without producing a grammar. The theory, as it were, was implicit in the practice; our task is to make it explicit.

Each of these classical definitions finds resonance in the Judaean context. Aristotle's emphasis on *observing* the available means of persuasion corresponds to the Judaean sage's task of *finding* (*limtzo*) the arguments latent in a scriptural text. Quintilian's insistence on *speaking well* -- with both effectiveness and moral integrity -- corresponds to the Judaean ideal of the *talmid hakham* (wise student) who combines intellectual brilliance with ethical probity. The *Rhetorica ad Herennium*'s conception of rhetoric as a teachable art with formal rules corresponds to the Judaean system of hermeneutical *middot* (rules of interpretation). And Cicero's vision of rhetoric as essential to civil governance corresponds to the Judaean understanding of the sage as both scholar and public servant -- one whose rhetorical skill serves the community's need for justice, instruction, and moral guidance.

Consider the evidence. Of a certain Rabbi Meir, it is reported:

> <span dir="rtl">אמר רבי אחא בר חנינא: גלוי וידוע לפני מי שאמר והיה העולם שאין בדורו של רבי מאיר כמותו, ומפני מה לא קבעו הלכה כמותו? שלא יכלו חבריו לעמוד על סוף דעתו, שהוא אומר על טמא טהור ומראה לו פנים, על טהור טמא ומראה לו פנים</span>
>
> *Rabbi Aha bar Hanina said: It is revealed and known before the One who spoke and the world came into being that there was none in Rabbi Meir's generation equal to him. And why did they not fix the law according to his view? Because his colleagues could not fathom the full depth of his reasoning, for he would declare the impure to be pure and show justification for it, and the pure to be impure and show justification for it.* (Babylonian Talmud, Eruvin 13b)[^7]

Here is a man who could marshal one hundred and fifty arguments on one side of a question and one hundred and fifty on the other -- each with its own face of plausibility. No student of Gorgias or Protagoras could have claimed more. And yet the Judaean tradition did not regard this capacity with the suspicion that Plato directed against the sophists. Rather, it recognized -- as Aristotle did in his more measured assessment -- that the ability to argue on both sides of a question is the foundation of true judgment. The sage who cannot see the case from every angle cannot render reliable decisions. Rabbi Meir's virtuosity was not sophistry; it was the Judaean analogue of what Aristotle meant by the "available means of persuasion."

At the same time, the Judaean tradition maintained a tension -- productive rather than paralyzing -- between rhetorical skill and truth. The sage who argues brilliantly for a false position has demonstrated *ability* but not *wisdom*. The tradition reports that Rabbi Meir's very brilliance became a problem: "His colleagues could not fathom the full depth of his reasoning" -- they could not always tell which of his arguments he himself endorsed and which he offered as demonstrations. The result was that "they did not fix the law according to his view." This is a remarkable institutional response: the community recognized the danger of unlimited rhetorical virtuosity and built a structural check against it. The sage of supreme argumentative power is honored but not followed -- his *inventio* is admired, but the community reserves its assent for those whose positions can be reliably identified.

This dialectic between rhetorical power and communal trust runs throughout the Judaean tradition and distinguishes it from both the sophistic tradition (which celebrates rhetorical power for its own sake) and the Platonic tradition (which distrusts rhetoric altogether). The Judaean position is closer to Aristotle's: rhetoric is a genuine and necessary art, but it must be exercised within institutional constraints that prevent its abuse.

Consider also the oft-cited principle:

> <span dir="rtl">לא בשמים היא</span>
>
> *It is not in heaven.* (Deuteronomy 30:12, cited in Babylonian Talmud, Bava Metzia 59b)

In the famous narrative of the Oven of Akhnai, Rabbi Eliezer marshals every form of persuasion -- logical argument, miraculous demonstration, even a heavenly voice declaring him correct -- and yet the majority rejects his position. Rabbi Yehoshua's response, quoting Scripture against the heavenly voice, establishes a principle of extraordinary rhetorical significance: truth in matters of law is determined not by supernatural authority but by the human community of interpreters, arguing according to established rules, reaching conclusions by majority vote. This is a theory of rhetoric as constitutive of truth -- not merely persuasion about truth but the very mechanism by which truth is established in the human sphere.

The system of rhetorical transmission in the Judaean academies operated through a remarkable institution that has no precise parallel in the schools of Greece and Rome: the *meturgeman*, or official interpreter-orator. The master would speak his teaching in a low voice or in compressed form, and the *meturgeman* would then deliver it to the assembled audience in full, amplified, and rhetorically polished form.[^8] This division of labor between the originator of content and the deliverer of discourse is a sophistication that the Western tradition never systematized. It recognizes implicitly what Aristotle states explicitly: that the arts of *inventio* (discovery of arguments) and *pronuntiatio* (delivery) are distinct competencies, and that mastery of one does not guarantee mastery of the other.

Moreover, the Judaean academies maintained formal rules of debate that would have impressed any Roman advocate. Speakers were required to address the substance of their opponents' arguments rather than attacking their persons. Interruption was prohibited: a sage who enters "into the words of his fellow" (*nikhnas le-tokh divrei havero*) is classified among those who lack the qualities of a scholar.[^9] Precedent and authority were carefully weighed. The order of speaking was regulated -- in some contexts the junior scholars spoke first, to ensure that their views were not overwhelmed by the authority of the senior masters.[^10] These procedural norms constitute a fully articulated set of debate rules comparable to those governing Roman forensic practice.

There is a further dimension to the Judaean rhetorical system that the student should appreciate. In the Greek and Roman traditions, rhetoric is understood as an art whose primary arena is public life -- the courtroom, the assembly, the ceremonial occasion. The orator speaks to an audience of citizens, and his purpose is civic: to persuade, to deliberate, to celebrate. In the Judaean tradition, the primary arena of rhetoric is the *bet midrash* -- the school -- and the orator's audience is composed not of citizens in their civic capacity but of scholars in their intellectual capacity. This means that the Judaean rhetorical tradition is, in a sense, more purely intellectual than the classical tradition: it is rhetoric in the service of *learning* rather than rhetoric in the service of *governance*. The purpose of persuasion in the *bet midrash* is not to win a vote or secure a verdict but to establish the correct interpretation of a text, the proper application of a law, or the true meaning of a tradition.

This does not make Judaean rhetoric less rigorous than classical rhetoric; if anything, it makes it more rigorous. The audience of scholars is a far more demanding audience than the audience of citizens. Citizens can be moved by emotional appeals, by the speaker's personal authority, by the urgency of the political moment. Scholars demand logic, evidence, textual precision, and the capacity to withstand sustained questioning. The Judaean orator who cannot answer the objections of his fellow scholars has failed more completely than the Roman orator who loses a case -- for the Roman orator may blame the jury's bias, while the Judaean scholar has no one to blame but his own reasoning.

Moreover, the Judaean tradition develops a concept that has no precise equivalent in classical rhetoric: the idea that rhetorical engagement with sacred texts is itself an act of worship. When the sage expounds Torah, he is not merely exercising his intellectual faculties; he is participating in a divine activity. The Torah was given through speech ("And God *spoke* all these words"), and its ongoing interpretation through speech is understood as a continuation of the revelatory process. This theological dimension gives Judaean rhetoric a seriousness and urgency that classical rhetoric, for all its civic importance, does not possess. The stakes of getting it right are not merely practical but metaphysical.

The student of rhetoric will find, as we proceed through this treatise, that the Judaean tradition offers examples of extraordinary richness for every division and subdivision of the classical system. In some areas -- particularly in the chreia, the parable, and the arts of memory -- the Judaean material is more abundant and more varied than anything surviving from the Greek and Roman schools. We shall endeavor to present this material with the same systematic precision that Quintilian brought to his treatment of the Roman rhetorical tradition, allowing the reader to observe for himself how fully the Judaean sages, working within their own idiom and for their own purposes, mastered the universal art of rhetoric.

---

### I.B. The Three Genres of Discourse

Aristotle, in the third chapter of the first book of his *Rhetoric*, establishes a threefold division of oratory that has governed all subsequent rhetorical theory:

> "The species of rhetoric are three in number, for such is the number of the classes of listeners to speeches. For a speech is composed of three factors -- the speaker, the subject, and the person addressed -- and it is to the last of these, the hearer, that the end or purpose of the speech is directed." (Rhetoric 1.3.1358a-b)[^11]

These three genres are: the **judicial** (*genus iudiciale*, *dikanikon*), concerned with accusation and defense regarding past actions; the **deliberative** (*genus deliberativum*, *symbouleutikon*), concerned with persuasion and dissuasion regarding future policy; and the **epideictic** (*genus demonstrativum*, *epideiktikon*), concerned with praise and blame regarding present character or achievement.

The same threefold division that Aristotle established is observed, with equal precision and often with greater procedural elaboration, in the judicial, deliberative, and ceremonial discourse of the Judaean sages. What is remarkable about the Judaean material is not merely the presence of these three genres -- one would expect any sophisticated culture to produce judicial, deliberative, and ceremonial speech -- but the degree to which the Judaean tradition has *theorized* the distinctions among them, formalized the rules governing each, and preserved detailed examples of all three in its literary record. The Mishnah, in particular, may be read as a compendium of rules for the proper conduct of all three genres of discourse: its judicial sections (Sanhedrin, Makkot, Shevuot) prescribe the rules of forensic rhetoric; its legislative sections (virtually every tractate) record the results of deliberative rhetoric; and its liturgical sections (Berakhot, Yoma, Sukkah, Megillah) preserve the forms and formulas of epideictic and ceremonial speech.

#### The Judicial Genre (Genus Iudiciale)

The Judaean courts -- the *batei din* -- operated with a system of forensic procedure that in many respects exceeded in formal rigor anything described in Roman rhetorical treatises. The Mishnah devotes entire tractates to judicial process, and the rules governing advocacy, examination of witnesses, and deliberation constitute a comprehensive code of forensic rhetoric.

The fundamental text is Mishnah Sanhedrin 4:1:

> <span dir="rtl">אחד דיני ממונות ואחד דיני נפשות בדרישה ובחקירה, שנאמר: משפט אחד יהיה לכם. מה בין דיני ממונות לדיני נפשות? דיני ממונות בשלושה, דיני נפשות בעשרים ושלושה. דיני ממונות פותחין בין לזכות בין לחובה, דיני נפשות פותחין לזכות ואין פותחין לחובה</span>
>
> *Both civil and capital cases require thorough examination and inquiry, as it is said: "One law shall you have" (Leviticus 24:22). How do civil cases differ from capital cases? Civil cases are adjudicated by three judges, capital cases by twenty-three. In civil cases, deliberation may open either for acquittal or for conviction; in capital cases, deliberation opens for acquittal but not for conviction.*[^12]

The rhetorician will immediately observe several features of this system that merit attention. First, the requirement that both types of cases undergo "thorough examination and inquiry" (*derishah va-haqirah*) establishes a formal standard of forensic investigation comparable to the Roman *quaestio*. Second, the distinction between civil and capital procedure -- particularly the rule that capital deliberation must open with arguments for acquittal -- constitutes a structural bias toward the defense that has no precise parallel in Roman law but reflects a sophisticated understanding of how the order of arguments (*dispositio*) shapes outcomes. Third, the stipulation that in capital cases the junior judges speak first ensures that the persuasive authority of senior members does not preemptively silence dissent -- a procedural insight that Roman rhetoricians discussed in theory but rarely implemented in practice.[^13]

Further rules specify that in capital cases, a judge who has argued for conviction may subsequently change his position to argue for acquittal, but one who has argued for acquittal may not change to argue for conviction. Conviction requires a majority of at least two, while acquittal requires only a simple majority. These procedural asymmetries reflect a rhetorical theory in which the structure of discourse itself is weighted toward a preferred outcome -- a remarkable anticipation of what modern theorists call "structural persuasion."

The Mishnah also preserves a formal admonition delivered to witnesses in capital cases (Sanhedrin 4:5) that is itself a masterpiece of epideictic rhetoric deployed within a judicial context:

> <span dir="rtl">לפיכך נברא אדם יחידי, ללמדך שכל המאבד נפש אחת מעלה עליו הכתוב כאילו איבד עולם מלא, וכל המקיים נפש אחת מעלה עליו הכתוב כאילו קיים עולם מלא</span>
>
> *Therefore Adam was created alone, to teach you that whoever destroys a single soul, Scripture charges it as though he had destroyed an entire world; and whoever preserves a single soul, Scripture credits it as though he had preserved an entire world.*[^14]

This passage, recited before witnesses in every capital trial, functions as what classical rhetoric calls a *prooimion* -- a preliminary address designed to establish the gravity of the occasion and dispose the hearers toward care, attention, and moral seriousness. Its rhetorical power lies in the movement from the particular (this individual life) to the universal (an entire world) -- a figure of amplification (*auxesis*) that any student of Quintilian would recognize.

The passage continues with a further rhetorical argument:

> <span dir="rtl">ולהגיד גדולתו של הקדוש ברוך הוא: שאדם טובע כמה מטבעות בחותם אחד, כולן דומין זה לזה; ומלך מלכי המלכים הקדוש ברוך הוא טבע כל אדם בחותמו של אדם הראשון, ואין אחד מהם דומה לחברו</span>
>
> *And to declare the greatness of the Holy One, blessed be He: a human stamps many coins with one die, and they are all alike; but the King of Kings, the Holy One, stamped every person with the die of the first human, and not one of them is like his fellow.*

This is an argument from analogy (*argumentum a simili*) of remarkable sophistication. The analogy between divine creation and coin-stamping illustrates the uniqueness of each human life through comparison with a familiar manufacturing process. The rhetorical effect is to make the abstract theological claim (every person is unique) tangible and intuitively compelling. The entire admonition -- theological argument, analogical illustration, moral application -- constitutes a miniature forensic speech embedded within the procedural rules of the court: a speech *about* speech, reminding the witnesses that their words in court carry cosmic consequences.

#### The Deliberative Genre (Genus Deliberativum)

The deliberative genre -- discourse concerned with persuading or dissuading an assembly regarding future action -- finds its Judaean expression primarily in the debates of the academies over matters of law, public policy, and communal governance.

A paradigmatic example is the deliberative crisis recorded at Babylonian Talmud, Berakhot 27b-28a, concerning the deposition of Rabban Gamaliel as head of the academy at Yavneh. The narrative describes a sequence of deliberative speeches in which the question before the assembly is fundamentally one of future policy: should the academy be governed by a strict gatekeeper who restricts admission, or should its doors be opened to all qualified students?

> <span dir="rtl">ההוא יומא דאותבוה לרבי אלעזר בן עזריה, אתוסיפו כמה ספסלי. אמר רבי יוחנן: פליגי רב ושמואל, חד אמר: אתוסיפו ארבע מאה ספסלי, וחד אמר: שבע מאה ספסלי</span>
>
> *On the day that Rabbi Elazar ben Azariah was installed [as head], many benches were added. One authority says four hundred benches were added, and another says seven hundred.*[^15]

The debate that led to this transformation was a classic instance of deliberative oratory: competing visions of institutional governance were argued before the assembled scholars, and the outcome reshaped the future direction of the academy. The narrative preserves the arguments on both sides: Rabban Gamaliel's position that unrestricted access would dilute the quality of scholarship, and the opposing view that Torah must be accessible to all who seek it. The resolution -- the opening of the doors -- was itself a performative deliberative act, and the tradition records that on that very day numerous legal questions that had previously been unresolved were settled, suggesting that the expansion of deliberative participation produced superior collective judgment.[^16]

Another instance of deliberative rhetoric is found in the debates at Yavneh regarding the reconstruction of religious practice after the destruction of the Temple in 70 CE. The sages faced a series of urgent policy questions: which Temple rituals should be preserved in modified form, which should be suspended, and how should the authority previously invested in the Temple priesthood be redistributed? These deliberations are recorded throughout the Mishnah, particularly in tractates Rosh Hashanah, Ta'anit, and Megillah, and they display all the features of classical deliberative discourse: appeals to precedent, arguments from expediency and from honor, and the weighing of competing goods.

A further example deserves attention. The dispute between Rabban Gamaliel and Rabbi Yehoshua over the calendar (Mishnah Rosh Hashanah 2:8-9) is a deliberative crisis with constitutional implications. Rabban Gamaliel, as head of the Sanhedrin, has the authority to fix the calendar; Rabbi Yehoshua, on the basis of his own astronomical calculations, reaches a different conclusion about the day of the New Moon and hence the date of Yom Kippur. Rabban Gamaliel issues a decree:

> <span dir="rtl">שלח לו רבן גמליאל: גוזרני עליך שתבוא אצלי במקלך ובמעותיך ביום שחל יום הכיפורים להיות בחשבונך</span>
>
> *Rabban Gamaliel sent word to him: I decree that you come to me with your staff and your money on the day that is Yom Kippur according to your reckoning.*

The deliberative question at stake -- whether institutional authority or individual judgment should prevail in matters of communal practice -- is resolved through a sequence of consultations. Rabbi Akiva consoles Rabbi Yehoshua with a scriptural argument: whatever the court declares, even if it errs, is binding by divine mandate. Rabbi Dosa ben Harkinas offers a pragmatic argument: to challenge the authority of the present court is to challenge the authority of every court since Moses. These are classic deliberative *topoi*: the argument from institutional stability and the argument from precedent. Rabbi Yehoshua ultimately complies, carrying his staff and money on what he believes to be Yom Kippur -- a dramatic act of submission to communal authority that resolves the deliberative crisis through performance rather than further debate.

The entire narrative is a case study in deliberative rhetoric: how a community resolves a conflict between competing sources of authority when the stakes are not merely theoretical but practical -- when the question is not "What is true?" but "What shall we do?"

#### The Epideictic Genre (Genus Demonstrativum)

The epideictic genre -- discourse concerned with praise and blame, typically delivered on ceremonial occasions -- is richly represented in Judaean tradition, though scholars of classical rhetoric have not always recognized its forms.

The primary Judaean epideictic form is the *hesped*, the funeral eulogy. The Mishnah and Talmud devote considerable attention to the art of eulogizing, and the Babylonian Talmud at Mo'ed Qatan 25a-b preserves a remarkable anthology of funeral orations delivered for the great sages -- orations that display sophisticated command of the rhetorical figures of amplification, comparison, and antithesis:

> <span dir="rtl">כי הוה ניחא נפשיה דרבי אבהו, אמרי עליה דריש לקיש: אילו נגנב ספר תורה, לא הייתי מצטער כמו שאני מצטער</span>
>
> *When Rabbi Abbahu died, they said of Resh Laqish: Were a Torah scroll stolen, I would not grieve as I grieve now.*

The rhetorical strategy here is comparison (*synkrisis*): the deceased sage is set alongside the most sacred object in Judaean culture, the Torah scroll, and declared to exceed it in value. This is precisely the technique of amplification through comparison that Quintilian recommends for epideictic oratory (Inst. Or. 3.7.21).[^17]

The tractate Mo'ed Qatan further records specific rules for the composition and delivery of funeral eulogies. The orator should begin with praise of the deceased's specific virtues rather than general platitudes; he should address the dead directly, using the second person ("Woe for your departure!"); he should evoke the grief of the community through figures of lamentation; and he should conclude with a consoling reflection on the meaning of the deceased's life. These prescriptions constitute a miniature handbook of epideictic composition, embedded within the legal discussion of mourning practices.

The Babylonian Talmud also records that different eulogies were composed for sages of different rank and distinction:

> <span dir="rtl">כי הוה ניחא נפשיה דרב ספרא, לא קרעו רבנן עליה. אמרי: לא גמרינן מיניה. אמר להו אביי: מי תנן "חכם שמת" ולא "חכם שלמד"? "חכם שמת — הכל קרוביו"</span>
>
> *When Rav Safra died, the sages did not rend their garments for him. They said: We did not learn from him. Abaye said to them: Does the Mishnah say "a scholar who taught"? It says "a scholar who died -- all are his relatives."*

This exchange reveals a debate about the scope of epideictic obligation: does the community owe public mourning (an epideictic act) to every scholar, or only to those from whom it has directly learned? Abaye's response extends the obligation to all scholars, regardless of personal connection -- a universalizing move that broadens the community of epideictic obligation beyond the circle of personal acquaintance.

The Judaean tradition also preserves epideictic discourse in the form of encomia -- formal praise of virtues and of exemplary figures. The collection known as Pirkei Avot ("Chapters of the Fathers") may be read as an extended epideictic composition: a chain of concise portrayals of the character and wisdom of the great sages, each one functioning as a miniature encomium. Similarly, the passage in Mishnah Sotah 9:15 that catalogs the qualities that ceased with the deaths of various sages -- "When Rabbi Meir died, the composers of parables ceased; when Ben Azzai died, the diligent students ceased; when Ben Zoma died, the expositors ceased" -- is a serial epideictic performance, a chain of *laudationes funebres* compressed into a single elegiac catalog.[^18]

The Judaean tradition also produced a distinctive epideictic form that has no exact classical parallel: the chain of praise in which the death of each sage is marked by the cessation of a particular virtue:

> <span dir="rtl">משמת רבי מאיר, בטלו מושלי משלים. משמת בן עזאי, בטלו השקדנים. משמת בן זומא, בטלו הדרשנים. משמת רבי יהושע, פסקה טובה מן העולם. משמת רבן שמעון בן גמליאל, בא גובאי ורבו צרות. משמת רבי אלעזר בן עזריה, פסק העושר מן החכמים. משמת רבי עקיבא, בטל כבוד התורה</span>
>
> *When Rabbi Meir died, the composers of parables ceased. When Ben Azzai died, the diligent students ceased. When Ben Zoma died, the expositors ceased. When Rabbi Yehoshua died, goodness departed from the world. When Rabban Shimon ben Gamaliel died, locusts came and troubles multiplied. When Rabbi Elazar ben Azariah died, wealth departed from the sages. When Rabbi Akiva died, the glory of the Torah ceased.* (Mishnah Sotah 9:15)

This passage is a serial epideictic performance of remarkable compression. Each sage is eulogized in a single clause, and each clause identifies the sage with a specific quality or virtue. The rhetorical effect is cumulative: as the list progresses, the reader experiences the progressive impoverishment of the intellectual world -- each death diminishing the totality of available human excellence. This is amplification through enumeration (*congeries*), a technique classified by the *Rhetorica ad Herennium* (4.40.52) as one of the figures of thought.

Festival addresses and wedding orations also belong to the epideictic genre. The Talmud records specific formulas for praising a bride at her wedding -- "Beautiful and gracious" or "As she is" -- and the debate about whether one should praise her qualities or describe them accurately (Babylonian Talmud, Ketubot 16b-17a) is itself a fascinating deliberative discussion about the ethics of epideictic speech: is the purpose of praise to honor the truth or to bring joy to the celebrants? Beit Hillel's position -- that every bride should be praised as "beautiful and gracious" -- reflects a theory of epideictic discourse as a social performance whose purpose is communal harmony rather than factual precision.

One observes in the Judaean tradition, as in the Greek and Roman traditions, that epideictic discourse is not merely ornamental but serves essential communal functions: the articulation of shared values, the definition of exemplary character, and the reinforcement of collective identity through the celebration of cultural heroes.

---

### I.C. The Five Canons of Rhetoric

Classical rhetorical theory, from the *Rhetorica ad Herennium* (1.3.3) through Cicero's *De Inventione* (1.9.9) and Quintilian's *Institutio Oratoria* (3.3.1), identifies five divisions or "canons" of the art: invention (*inventio*), arrangement (*dispositio*), style (*elocutio*), memory (*memoria*), and delivery (*pronuntiatio* or *actio*).[^19]

These five canons provide a comprehensive framework for analyzing any rhetorical tradition. Together, they encompass the entire process of speech production: the discovery of what to say (invention), the ordering of what has been discovered (arrangement), the selection of words in which to express it (style), the retention of the composition in memory (memory), and the physical act of speaking it aloud (delivery). A rhetorical tradition that lacks any one of these canons is, by classical standards, incomplete. The claim we advance here is that the Judaean tradition possesses all five -- and that in at least two of them (invention and memory), the Judaean achievement is unmatched in the ancient world.

Each of these canons finds a fully developed counterpart in the rhetorical practice of the Judaean sages.

#### The First Canon: Invention (Inventio / Hamtza'ah)

Invention -- the discovery of arguments and proofs suitable to a given case -- is universally acknowledged as the most fundamental division of rhetoric. Aristotle devoted most of his *Rhetoric* to it. Cicero calls it "the most important of all the divisions" (De Inv. 1.9.7). Quintilian treats it as the foundation upon which all other rhetorical competencies rest.

In the schools of the Judaean sages, invention was cultivated with a systematic rigor that has no parallel in the classical tradition. The instrument of this cultivation was a set of formal hermeneutical rules (*middot*) -- logical principles for the generation of arguments from authoritative texts. The most widely received set of these rules is attributed to a certain Rabbi Ishmael, who is said to have articulated thirteen principles by which arguments are derived from Scripture:[^20]

> <span dir="rtl">רבי ישמעאל אומר: בשלוש עשרה מידות התורה נדרשת</span>
>
> *Rabbi Ishmael says: The Torah is expounded by thirteen rules.*

These thirteen rules constitute, in effect, a systematic toolkit for *inventio* -- a formal method for discovering arguments where none are immediately apparent. Let us enumerate them in terms familiar to the student of rhetoric:

1. **Inference from minor to major** (*qal va-homer*): If a proposition holds in a lesser case, it holds *a fortiori* in a greater case. This corresponds precisely to what Aristotle calls the argument from the more and the less (*Rhetoric* 2.23.4) and what Roman rhetoricians term *argumentum a minore ad maius*.[^21]

2. **Analogy from equal cases** (*gezerah shavah*): When the same term appears in two different legal contexts, the rules governing one case may be transferred to the other. This is a form of argument by verbal analogy, comparable to the rhetorical *simile* or *paradigma* at the level of logical inference rather than stylistic ornament.

3. **Generalization from one text** (*binyan av mi-katuv ehad*): A principle established in one passage may be applied as a precedent to govern all similar cases. This corresponds to the rhetorical technique of arguing from the general rule (*argumentum a genere*).

4. **Generalization from two texts** (*binyan av mi-shnei ketuvim*): When the same principle can be derived from two independent sources, its authority is strengthened. This corresponds to the rhetorical technique of confirmation through multiple witnesses or testimonies.

5-6-7. **General and particular** (*kelal u-ferat*, *ferat u-khelal*, *kelal u-ferat u-khelal*): These three rules govern the logical relationship between general principles and particular applications -- a set of distinctions that Aristotle discusses under the heading of induction and deduction but that the Judaean sages developed with far greater formal precision.

8-11. **Rules of contextual inference**: Four rules governing how the meaning of a passage is determined by its context, its parallel formulations, and its relationship to other passages. These correspond to what Quintilian discusses as *interpretatio* -- the art of construing texts in their proper context (Inst. Or. 7.6).[^22]

12. **Inference from context** (*davar ha-lamed me-inyano*): The meaning of an ambiguous term is determined by the context in which it appears.

13. **Resolution of contradictions** (*shnei ketuvim ha-makhishim*): When two texts appear to contradict each other, a third text is sought to resolve the contradiction. This rule governs the harmonization of apparently conflicting authorities -- a task that occupies the center of both Judaean legal discourse and Roman jurisprudential rhetoric.

No rhetorical tradition known to us has produced a more rigorous or more comprehensive system of argument-generation than these thirteen rules. Where the Greek and Roman traditions offer general principles of *inventio* -- the topics (*topoi*), the commonplaces (*loci communes*), the modes of reasoning -- the Judaean tradition offers a specific, numbered, formally articulated set of logical operations that can be applied mechanically to generate arguments from any given text. The student trained in these rules possesses an inexhaustible source of *inventio*: given any passage of Scripture, he can generate arguments by analogy, by inference from major to minor or minor to major, by generalization, by contextualization, and by harmonization of contradictions. It is as though Aristotle's *Topics* had been compressed into a portable toolkit and placed in the hands of every student on the first day of his rhetorical education.

Let us illustrate the generative power of these rules with a concrete example. The first rule, *qal va-homer* (inference from minor to major), is exemplified throughout the tannaitic literature. A characteristic instance appears in the Mekhilta de-Rabbi Ishmael (Beshalach 1), where the sage argues: "If the bones of Joseph, who was but one individual, were attended to by Moses himself, how much more so should the whole community of Israel attend to the mitzvot that concern them all." The logical structure is: if principle P holds for the lesser case (one individual), then *a fortiori* it holds for the greater case (the entire community). This is precisely the form of reasoning that Aristotle describes in *Rhetoric* 2.23.4, and it generates arguments with machine-like reliability: given any established ruling, the sage can ask, "If this holds in the lesser case, does it not hold all the more in the greater?" -- and thereby produce a new argument that did not previously exist.

The second rule, *gezerah shavah* (verbal analogy), demonstrates how the invention system operates at the level of language. When the word *shemirah* ("guarding") appears in two different legal contexts -- say, in the laws of the Sabbath and in the laws of property -- the rules governing one context may be transferred to the other by verbal analogy. This is not mere wordplay; it is a formal principle of argument-generation that creates systematic connections across the entire body of law. The result is a network of legal reasoning in which no passage stands alone: every text is potentially connected to every other text through shared vocabulary, and each connection generates new arguments.

Moreover, the Judaean tradition recognizes -- as the best Roman rhetoricians also recognized -- that invention is not a solitary activity but a dialogical one. The technique of *machloqet* (formal disputation between scholars) functions as a collaborative engine of *inventio*: by forcing each participant to anticipate and answer the objections of his opponent, the disputation process generates arguments that neither party could have discovered alone. This is what Cicero describes in the *De Oratore* (2.30.130) as the value of *disputatio in utramque partem* -- arguing on both sides of a question -- and it is precisely what the Judaean sages practiced in their daily academic sessions.

The loss of a skilled disputant was felt as a catastrophic impoverishment of the academy's inventive capacity. When Resh Laqish (Rabbi Shimon ben Lakish) died, Rabbi Yohanan grieved inconsolably. They brought him Rabbi Elazar ben Pedat as a replacement study partner, and for every ruling Rabbi Yohanan stated, Rabbi Elazar cited a supporting tradition. Rabbi Yohanan wept:

> <span dir="rtl">כשהייתי אומר דבר, היה ריש לקיש מקשה לי עשרים וארבע קושיות, ואני משיב לו עשרים וארבע תשובות, וממילא רווחא שמעתתא. ואת אמרת: תניא דמסייע לך? אטו לא ידענא דשפיר קאמינא?</span>
>
> *When I would state a ruling, Resh Laqish would raise twenty-four objections, and I would give twenty-four answers, and thereby the teaching was clarified. But you say: "A tradition supports you!" Do I not already know that what I say is correct?* (Babylonian Talmud, Bava Metzia 84a)

This is a remarkable theoretical statement about the nature of *inventio*: the purpose of dialectical challenge is not merely to test whether a proposition is true but to generate the arguments -- the "twenty-four answers" -- that demonstrate *why* it is true. Without the provocation of objection, those arguments would never come into existence. The Judaean system of collaborative invention through disputation is, in effect, an institutional mechanism for maximizing the yield of *inventio*.

#### The Second Canon: Arrangement (Dispositio / Seder)

Arrangement -- the ordering of arguments and materials into an effective sequence -- receives extensive treatment in all classical rhetorical handbooks. The standard division of a speech into *prooemium* (introduction), *narratio* (statement of facts), *confirmatio* (proof), *refutatio* (refutation), and *peroratio* (conclusion) is set forth by the *Rhetorica ad Herennium* (1.4.4), Cicero (*De Inv.* 1.19.14), and Quintilian (*Inst. Or.* 3.9.1).[^23]

The Judaean sages developed their own system of arrangement for public discourse, most clearly visible in the structure of the formal *derashah* (public exposition). The classical Judaean *derashah* follows a recognizable sequence:

1. **Petihta** (opening): The speaker begins not with his main text but with a seemingly unrelated verse, often from a distant part of Scripture. Through a chain of interpretive links, he leads the audience from this opening verse to the passage that is his true subject. This technique -- beginning at a distance and gradually converging on the point -- corresponds to what classical rhetoric calls the *insinuatio*: an indirect approach to the subject designed to arouse curiosity and engage the audience's attention before the main argument is presented (Rhet. ad Her. 1.9.6).[^24]

2. **Guf ha-derashah** (body of the exposition): The central argument, typically structured around the interpretation of a specific scriptural passage, with supporting arguments drawn from the hermeneutical rules discussed above.

3. **Hatimah** (closing): The discourse concludes with a return to the opening theme, creating a ring composition that provides rhetorical closure. This corresponds to what Quintilian calls the *peroratio* with *recapitulatio* -- the final section that gathers up the threads of the argument and brings them to a satisfying conclusion (Inst. Or. 6.1.1). The *hatimah* typically ends with a formulaic expression of hope, consolation, or eschatological promise -- a conventional closing that signals to the audience that the discourse has reached its appointed end and invites an emotional response (comfort, hope, resolve) to complement the intellectual response elicited by the body of the argument.

The ring structure of the *petihta-guf-hatimah* sequence deserves particular emphasis. The return to the opening verse at the close creates what classical rhetoric calls an *inclusio* -- a framing device that encloses the argument within a symmetrical boundary. The effect is twofold: structurally, it provides the audience with a satisfying sense of completion (the discourse ends where it began); intellectually, it transforms the opening verse, which was initially opaque, into a passage now illuminated by everything that has intervened. The listener re-encounters the opening words with new understanding, and the gap between initial puzzlement and final comprehension is itself a source of rhetorical pleasure. The *petihta* form is, in this sense, a narrative of intellectual discovery: the audience experiences, in the course of a single discourse, the movement from confusion to clarity that the Greek rhetoricians call *anagnorisis* (recognition).

This tripartite structure -- opening, body, and closing -- is, of course, universal to rhetorical practice. What is distinctive about the Judaean form is the indirectness of the *petihta*, which demands of the audience a sustained act of interpretive anticipation. The listener must hold in mind the opening verse, follow the chain of argument through its intermediate links, and experience the intellectual satisfaction of seeing the distant starting point arrive at its destination. This is a more demanding rhetorical structure than the straightforward *prooemium-narratio-confirmatio* of Roman forensic oratory, and it presupposes an audience trained in the habits of close textual attention.

The Judaean tradition also developed distinctive principles of arrangement for legal discourse. In the Mishnah, laws are arranged not chronologically (as in a historical code) nor alphabetically (as in an encyclopedia) but topically and associatively -- by subject matter, by verbal connection, and sometimes by the logic of opposition. The tractate Berakhot, for example, moves from the Shema (the foundational declaration of divine unity) to the Amidah (the standing prayer), then to blessings over food, and finally to blessings over various experiences -- a sequence that mirrors the rhythms of the day and the expanding circles of religious obligation (from the individual's first words upon waking to his engagement with the wider world). This is *dispositio* at the level of an entire corpus rather than an individual speech, and its organizational logic -- though different from the rhetorical handbooks' prescriptions for speech arrangement -- reflects a sophisticated theory of how material should be sequenced for maximum pedagogical effectiveness.

A further principle of Judaean arrangement deserves notice: the practice of juxtaposing contrasting cases to sharpen the distinctions between them. The Mishnah frequently presents a rule and its exception, a case and its counter-case, in immediate sequence:

> <span dir="rtl">דיני ממונות פותחין בין לזכות בין לחובה, דיני נפשות פותחין לזכות ואין פותחין לחובה</span>
>
> *In civil cases, one opens either for acquittal or for conviction; in capital cases, one opens for acquittal but not for conviction.* (Sanhedrin 4:1)

The rhetorical effect of this juxtaposition is clarity through contrast -- what the Romans call *distinctio*. By placing the two rules side by side, each illuminates the other: the stringency of capital procedure becomes visible only against the background of the more lenient civil procedure. This technique of contrastive arrangement pervades the Mishnah and constitutes one of its most effective rhetorical strategies.

#### The Third Canon: Style (Elocutio / Lashon)

Style -- the selection and arrangement of words -- is treated at enormous length by Quintilian (books 8-9 of the *Institutio Oratoria*), who discusses the virtues of clarity (*perspicuitas*), ornament (*ornatus*), and propriety (*aptum*). The three levels of style -- plain, middle, and grand -- are a commonplace of classical theory from Theophrastus through the *Rhetorica ad Herennium* (4.11-16) and Cicero's *Orator* (20.69-28.99).[^25]

The Judaean sages articulated their own theory of style, and it differs markedly from the classical preference for *copia* (abundance). The dominant stylistic ideal of the Judaean tradition is *brevity*:

> <span dir="rtl">לעולם ישנה אדם לתלמידו דרך קצרה</span>
>
> *One should always teach his student in concise language.* (Babylonian Talmud, Pesahim 3b)[^26]

This principle -- "teach always in the short way" -- elevates brevity from a mere virtue among others to the governing principle of rabbinic style. The reasons are both pedagogical and aesthetic. Pedagogically, concise formulations are easier to memorize, and in a culture of oral transmission, memorability is the supreme stylistic virtue. Aesthetically, the Judaean sages valued compression as a form of intellectual elegance -- a distillation of complex thought into its most essential expression.

The results of this stylistic preference are visible throughout the Mishnah, which achieves a lapidary concision that rivals the finest examples of Attic prose. Consider Hillel's maxim:

> <span dir="rtl">אם אין אני לי, מי לי? וכשאני לעצמי, מה אני? ואם לא עכשיו, אימתי?</span>
>
> *If I am not for myself, who is for me? And when I am only for myself, what am I? And if not now, when?* (Avot 1:14)

In thirteen Hebrew words, three questions encompass the entirety of ethical philosophy: the claims of self-regard, the claims of altruism, and the urgency of action. No comparable compression exists in Greek or Latin gnomic literature. The stylistic effect depends on the absolute elimination of everything that is not essential -- no qualifications, no elaborations, no transitional phrases. This is the Judaean equivalent of what the Atticists called *brachylogia* (brevity of expression), but practiced with a rigor that goes beyond anything the Atticists themselves achieved.

At the same time, the Judaean tradition recognized that brevity must not be purchased at the cost of clarity. The Mishnah's very concision sometimes produces ambiguity, and the vast commentary tradition that arose around the Mishnah may be understood, in part, as a response to the rhetorical problem of excessive compression. The ideal Judaean style, then, is not mere brevity but *meaningful* brevity: the maximum of content in the minimum of expression, without obscurity.

The Judaean stylistic ideal also placed high value on **parallelism** -- the balanced arrangement of clauses in symmetrical or antithetical pairs. This figure, which the Roman rhetoricians call *isocolon* (equal members) and *antithesis* (opposition), pervades the Mishnah's prose:

> <span dir="rtl">כל מחלוקת שהיא לשם שמים, סופה להתקיים. ושאינה לשם שמים, אין סופה להתקיים</span>
>
> *Every dispute that is for the sake of Heaven will in the end endure. And one that is not for the sake of Heaven will not in the end endure.* (Avot 5:17)

The antithetical structure -- if X, then Y; if not-X, then not-Y -- achieves both logical clarity and mnemonic force. The listener retains the teaching precisely because its symmetrical form imprints itself on the memory. Throughout the Mishnah, one encounters this technique of paired clauses, balanced oppositions, and rhythmic repetition -- a prose style that serves simultaneously the demands of beauty and the demands of oral transmission.

Another distinctively Judaean figure is the **numerical list** -- "Three things...," "Four types...," "Seven qualities..." -- which the Greek rhetoricians classify under *distributio* (Rhet. ad Her. 4.47.40) but which the Judaean sages developed into a governing compositional principle. The entire fifth chapter of Pirkei Avot is organized around numerical groupings: ten things created at twilight, ten trials of Abraham, four types of students, four types of charity-givers. Each list is a compressed treatise -- a complete taxonomy delivered in a form that is simultaneously analytical and memorable.

The Judaean tradition also cultivated certain distinctive figures of style. Chief among these is the *mashal* (parable), which functions simultaneously as a figure of speech (an extended simile or metaphor) and as a mode of argument. The formulaic introduction "To what may this be compared?" (*le-mah ha-davar domeh*) signals the deployment of this figure, and its frequency in rabbinic literature -- a single sage, Rabbi Meir, is credited with knowing three hundred fox fables alone[^27] -- indicates the centrality of figurative language to the Judaean rhetorical aesthetic.

#### The Fourth Canon: Memory (Memoria / Zikaron)

Memory -- the firm retention in the mind of the matter, words, and arrangement of a speech -- is treated by the *Rhetorica ad Herennium* (3.28-40) as one of the five essential parts of the orator's art. Quintilian devotes a substantial section of his eleventh book to mnemonic technique (Inst. Or. 11.2), discussing the method of *loci* (places) and *imagines* (images) and recommending systematic exercises for the strengthening of memory.[^28]

In no other rhetorical tradition has the art of memory been so systematically cultivated as in the academies of the Judaean sages. The centrality of memory to Judaean rhetorical education cannot be overstated. The entire corpus of the Oral Torah -- the Mishnah, the tannaitic midrashim, the baraitot -- was composed, transmitted, and preserved through memory alone for centuries before it was committed to writing. This means that the Judaean rhetorical tradition faced a challenge of memorization that dwarfed anything confronting the Greek or Roman orator: not the retention of individual speeches for particular occasions, but the retention of an entire legal and homiletical literature encompassing thousands of distinct traditions.

The Judaean solution was the institution of the *tanna* -- literally, "one who repeats" -- a specialist whose function was to serve as a living library. The *tanna* memorized vast portions of the oral tradition and could recite them on demand, functioning as a human reference work. The Babylonian Talmud describes the teaching method with vivid specificity:

> <span dir="rtl">כיצד סדר משנה? משה למד מפי הגבורה. נכנס אהרן ושנה לו משה פירקו. נסתלק אהרן וישב לשמאל משה. נכנסו בניו ושנה להם משה פירקם\... נמצאו הכל שונים ארבעה</span>
>
> *What was the order of instruction? Moses learned from the mouth of the Almighty. Aaron entered and Moses taught him his chapter. Aaron withdrew and sat at Moses' left. His sons entered and Moses taught them their chapter\... It was found that all had reviewed [the teaching] four times.* (Babylonian Talmud, Eruvin 54b)[^29]

This passage describes a system of repetitive oral instruction designed to ensure retention through multiple hearings. Each student hears the teaching four times: once from Moses directly, and three times from previous students who repeat the teaching as new learners enter. The system is, in effect, a mnemonic engine -- a pedagogical structure designed to maximize retention through structured repetition.

The rhetorician will observe that the Judaean memory system differs from the classical method of *loci* and *imagines* in a fundamental respect. The classical system is spatial: it associates items to be remembered with locations in an imagined architectural space. The Judaean system is sequential and formulaic: it relies on fixed verbal formulas, catchword associations, numerical mnemonics (lists of three, five, seven, ten), and the rhythmic patterns of the Mishnah's prose to anchor vast bodies of material in memory. Both systems are effective; but the Judaean system was tested at a scale -- the memorization of entire corpora rather than individual compositions -- that the classical system never confronted.

The mnemonic architecture of the Mishnah itself rewards careful analysis. Consider the numerical lists in Pirkei Avot chapter 5: "Ten utterances by which the world was created," "Ten generations from Adam to Noah," "Ten trials of Abraham," "Four types of students," "Four types of givers to charity." Each number serves as a mnemonic anchor -- the student remembers that there are *ten* items and can therefore audit his memory for completeness. The formulaic structure -- "X says: Y" -- serves a similar function: the name of the sage tags the teaching, and the invariable syntax creates a predictable frame within which the specific content can be securely lodged.

The Judaean sages also recognized the difference between mechanical memorization and understanding -- and they valued both, but in proper sequence:

> <span dir="rtl">סדר המשנה: שנה ואחר כך הבן</span>
>
> *The method of study: first memorize, then understand.*

This pedagogical principle -- memorization precedes comprehension -- reverses the order that a modern educator might expect. But from the standpoint of the *ars memoriae*, it is sound: the student who first commits the text to memory possesses a stable substrate upon which understanding can be built over time. Understanding without memorization is evanescent; memorization without understanding is at least durable. The ideal, of course, is both -- and the *talmid hakham* (wise student) is one who has achieved the fusion of perfect retention and deep comprehension.

#### The Fifth Canon: Delivery (Pronuntiatio / Ne'imah)

Delivery -- the regulation of voice, countenance, and gesture in the act of speaking -- is treated by all classical authorities as essential to rhetorical effectiveness. Demosthenes, when asked what are the three most important qualities in an orator, is said to have replied: "Delivery, delivery, delivery." Quintilian devotes all of Book 11, Chapter 3 of his *Institutio Oratoria* to the subject, discussing vocal quality, pace, volume, facial expression, and hand gestures with meticulous attention.[^30]

The Judaean sages developed what the Western rhetorical tradition never achieved: a complete written notation system for delivery. The system of biblical cantillation (*ta'amei ha-miqra*) -- the elaborate set of melodic and syntactic marks that accompany the Hebrew scriptural text -- constitutes a fully articulated score for the oral performance of sacred texts, specifying not only the musical contour of each phrase but also its syntactic segmentation, its emphatic stresses, and its rhetorical pacing.[^31]

The cantillation system distinguishes between disjunctive accents (which create pauses and separations) and conjunctive accents (which bind words together), thereby providing the reader with precise instructions for phrasing and emphasis. A disjunctive accent at a particular point in a verse tells the reader: pause here, let this phrase settle in the listener's mind before proceeding. A conjunctive accent says: bind these words together, let them flow without interruption. The result is a system of delivery notation as precise as a modern musical score -- and far more precise than anything available to Greek or Roman orators, who had to rely on the natural instincts of the speaker, the guidance of a teacher, or at best the vague notations of rhetorical handbooks.

The rhetorical implications of the cantillation system are far-reaching. When a reader performs the biblical text with its prescribed cantillation, he is not merely reading words aloud; he is delivering a speech according to a score composed by a tradition of vocal experts who understood -- centuries before the development of modern punctuation -- that meaning is as much a function of phrasing, emphasis, and pause as it is of vocabulary and syntax. The cantillation system encodes a complete *interpretatio* of the text in its delivery markings. Two readers who follow the same cantillation will phrase, emphasize, and pause at the same points; the result is a standardized performance that ensures the community hears the text in the same way. This is standardized *pronuntiatio* at a level of precision that Quintilian could only dream of.

Consider, by way of contrast, the resources available to the Greek or Roman orator. Quintilian advises the speaker to vary his pace, to pause at appropriate moments, to raise or lower his voice for effect (Inst. Or. 11.3.35-65). But these are general principles, not specific instructions. The orator must rely on his own judgment -- or on the guidance of a living teacher -- to determine where to pause, where to emphasize, where to accelerate. The Judaean cantillation system replaces individual judgment with a shared tradition: the delivery of the text is not left to the performer's discretion but is inscribed in the text itself, transmitted across generations with the same fidelity as the words.

Beyond cantillation, the *meturgeman* system discussed above (I.A) represents a further sophistication in the art of delivery. The master supplies the content; the *meturgeman* supplies the delivery -- the amplified voice, the dramatic gesture, the rhetorical pacing that makes the teaching accessible to a large audience. This division of labor between composer and performer anticipates what in the modern world we might call the distinction between the writer and the actor, or the composer and the musician. The Judaean sages recognized that the gifts of original thought and the gifts of public performance do not always reside in the same person, and they created an institutional structure to accommodate this insight.

The Talmud records specific guidelines for vocal delivery. A sage who teaches should do so with *ne'imah* (melodic pleasantness) -- the teaching should be aesthetically engaging, not merely informationally adequate. The principle that "one should always recite Scripture with melody and Mishnah with song" reflects an understanding that the aural quality of delivery affects not only the audience's engagement but also the speaker's own retention. Material learned with a distinctive melodic pattern is more easily recalled than material learned in flat prose -- a principle that modern cognitive science has confirmed but that the Judaean sages understood empirically two millennia ago.

---

### I.D. The Rhetorical School

Quintilian devotes the opening books of his *Institutio Oratoria* to a description of the ideal school of rhetoric: its physical setting, its educational philosophy, its curriculum, and its methods of instruction (Inst. Or. 1.1-2.21). He discusses the qualifications of the ideal teacher, the proper relationship between master and student, the sequencing of exercises from simple to complex, and the social and moral purposes of rhetorical education.[^32] If we compare Quintilian's description with what we know of the Judaean *bet midrash*, the structural parallels are remarkable.

#### The Setting

The Judaean *bet midrash* -- literally, the "house of inquiry" -- was a dedicated physical space for rhetorical-legal education, comparable to the Roman *schola* or the Greek *didaskaleion*. Students gathered around a master, seated in rows or in a semicircle, facing the teacher. The master occupied an elevated seat or a position at the front. Instruction took place in sessions of defined length, with periods of individual study and review alternating with periods of group discussion and formal debate.

The most famous description of the *bet midrash* in session appears in the account of the deposition of Rabban Gamaliel (Berakhot 27b-28a), which records that when the doors of the academy were opened to all comers, four hundred (or seven hundred) additional benches were added to accommodate the influx of students. This detail -- the physical expansion of the school space -- tells us that the *bet midrash* was a real place with real furniture, subject to the constraints of physical capacity. It was not a metaphor; it was a school, and it operated as schools do.

The Talmud provides further details about the physical arrangement of the school. Students sat in rows before the master, and the seating order reflected academic rank:

> <span dir="rtl">שלוש שורות של תלמידי חכמים יושבין לפניהם</span>
>
> *Three rows of scholars sit before them [the judges].* (Mishnah Sanhedrin 4:4)

Each student knew his place, and promotion from one row to the next was a mark of academic achievement. The most advanced students sat in the front row; the newest sat in the back. This hierarchical seating arrangement served a dual rhetorical function: it provided an audience whose responses to a teaching -- approval, objection, confusion -- were visible to the speaker, and it created a social incentive for intellectual performance, since distinguished contributions could earn promotion to a more honored seat.

The daily routine of the *bet midrash* was structured around alternating periods of instruction (the master teaching), recitation (the students repeating), and disputation (the students challenging and questioning). This tripartite rhythm -- input, memorization, contestation -- corresponds to the three phases of rhetorical education that Quintilian describes: *praelectio* (the teacher's exposition), *imitatio* (the student's imitation), and *exercitatio* (the student's independent practice) (Inst. Or. 2.5-2.7).

#### The Master-Disciple Relationship

Quintilian describes the ideal relationship between the rhetor and his student as one of paternal authority tempered by affection: the teacher should be "a father to his pupils" (*parentis loco*, Inst. Or. 2.2.4), demanding but not harsh, encouraging but not indulgent.[^33]

The Judaean tradition develops this same ideal with even greater intensity. The relationship between *rav* (master) and *talmid* (student) is modeled explicitly on the parent-child bond, and in some formulations, exceeds it:

> <span dir="rtl">יהושע בן פרחיה אומר: עשה לך רב, וקנה לך חבר</span>
>
> *Yehoshua ben Perachyah says: Make for yourself a teacher, and acquire for yourself a companion.* (Avot 1:6)

The imperative "make for yourself a teacher" (*aseh lekha rav*) implies that the student bears responsibility for choosing and attaching himself to a master -- that the pedagogical relationship is not merely administrative but volitional and personal. And the addition "acquire for yourself a companion" indicates that learning is understood as both a vertical relationship (master to student) and a horizontal one (student to fellow student). Quintilian makes a similar point when he argues that the company of fellow students provides benefits that private tutoring cannot supply (Inst. Or. 1.2.18-22): emulation, competition, and the experience of public performance.

#### The Chain of Tradition

The Judaean tradition's most distinctive contribution to the theory of rhetorical education is the concept of the *shalshelet ha-qabbalah* -- the "chain of tradition" -- set forth in the opening passage of Pirkei Avot:

> <span dir="rtl">משה קיבל תורה מסיני ומסרה ליהושע, ויהושע לזקנים, וזקנים לנביאים, ונביאים מסרוה לאנשי כנסת הגדולה</span>
>
> *Moses received the Torah from Sinai and transmitted it to Joshua, and Joshua to the Elders, and the Elders to the Prophets, and the Prophets transmitted it to the Men of the Great Assembly.* (Avot 1:1)[^34]

This passage establishes an unbroken chain of pedagogical transmission stretching from the foundational lawgiver to the contemporary academy. Its rhetorical function is to legitimize the authority of the present-day teacher by anchoring it in a continuous line of authorized transmission. The Greek and Roman traditions likewise valued pedagogical lineage -- one studied with a student of a student of Isocrates, or traced one's rhetorical ancestry to Gorgias or to Aristotle -- but the Judaean tradition formalized this lineage into a governing institutional principle. Every teacher is positioned within the chain; every student, upon mastering the tradition, adds a new link.

The rhetorical sophistication of the Avot chain passage goes beyond mere genealogy. The passage does not simply list names; it assigns to each link a characteristic teaching -- a signature chreia that defines the intellectual contribution of each generation. Simeon the Righteous contributes the three pillars (Torah, worship, lovingkindness); Antigonos of Sokho contributes the principle of disinterested service; the *zugot* (pairs) contribute complementary pairs of ethical precepts. The chain is therefore not merely a chain of *persons* but a chain of *teachings* -- a curriculum whose sequence mirrors the historical sequence of its creators. To learn Avot is to travel through the history of the tradition, encountering each master's signature contribution in the order in which it entered the intellectual patrimony.

The pedagogical implication is profound: the student who has memorized the chain of tradition possesses not only a list of authoritative names but a structured overview of the entire ethical curriculum of the Judaean school. He knows who taught what, who received from whom, and how each teaching relates to those that preceded and followed it. The chain of tradition is thus both a legitimizing genealogy and a pedagogical map -- a device that simultaneously authorizes and organizes the content of the tradition.

The chain of tradition also implies a theory of rhetorical authority: the right to teach is not self-conferred but received. One does not simply declare oneself a teacher of Torah; one must have been authorized by a teacher who was himself authorized, in an unbroken sequence stretching back to the original source. This is comparable to what the Roman rhetorical tradition called *auctoritas* -- the weight of established reputation and institutional standing -- but formalized into a principle of transmission. The Judaean sage speaks not in his own name alone but in the name of his teacher, and his teacher's teacher, and ultimately in the name of the original revelatory act. His authority is therefore simultaneously personal (earned through his own learning and character) and institutional (conferred by his position in the chain). This dual source of authority -- personal merit and institutional investiture -- is precisely analogous to the dual basis of the Roman orator's *auctoritas*: personal reputation (*dignitas*) and institutional standing (*potestas*).

#### The Curriculum: From Simple to Complex

Quintilian describes a curriculum that moves from simple exercises to complex: the student begins with basic tasks (retelling fables, narrating events, praising famous figures) and progresses through increasingly demanding exercises until he is capable of delivering complete speeches on contested questions (Inst. Or. 1.9-2.4).[^35] This graduated sequence is the *progymnasmata* -- the "preliminary exercises" -- and the handbooks of Theon, Hermogenes, Aphthonius, and Nicolaus provide detailed descriptions of each exercise in ascending order of difficulty.

The Judaean curriculum follows a strikingly similar progression, though it is described in the idiom of Torah study rather than rhetorical education. The student begins with Scripture (*miqra*), memorizing the basic texts. He then proceeds to Mishnah, learning the concise legal formulations and their associated arguments. From Mishnah he advances to Talmud -- the analysis of Mishnaic teachings, the resolution of contradictions, the derivation of new principles from established rules. Each stage demands greater intellectual sophistication than the last, and the sequence is explicitly understood as pedagogical: one does not begin with Talmud any more than one begins with full deliberative speeches.

The Mishnah also records a pedagogical ideal that echoes Quintilian's educational philosophy:

> <span dir="rtl">הם אמרו שלושה דברים: \.\.\. והעמידו תלמידים הרבה</span>
>
> *They [the Men of the Great Assembly] said three things: \.\.\. and raise up many disciples.* (Avot 1:1)[^36]

"Raise up many disciples" (*ha'amidu talmidim harbeh*) -- the educational mission is stated as a categorical imperative. The school exists not merely to preserve knowledge but to multiply it through the production of new teachers. This is the same vision that Quintilian expresses when he describes the purpose of rhetorical education as the formation of the *vir bonus dicendi peritus* -- the good man skilled in speaking -- who will in turn form others.

The Babylonian Talmud at Eruvin 54b describes the specific pedagogical methods employed:

> <span dir="rtl">תנא: כיצד סדר משנה? משה למד מפי הגבורה. נכנס אהרן ושנה לו משה פירקו\.\.\.</span>
>
> *It was taught: What was the order of instruction? Moses learned from the mouth of the Almighty. Aaron entered and Moses taught him his chapter\.\.\.*

The passage goes on to describe a system in which each teaching is repeated four times, with each repetition reinforcing the memorization of the previous hearers while introducing new students to the material. This method of graduated, repetitive instruction -- in which the more advanced students serve as teachers to the less advanced -- corresponds to what Quintilian calls the system of *mutuae exercitationes* (mutual exercises), in which students learn from and teach one another (Inst. Or. 2.2.11-15).

#### The Qualities of the Student

Just as Quintilian specifies the qualities of the ideal student -- natural talent (*ingenium*), eagerness to learn (*studium*), and the capacity for hard work (*exercitatio*) (Inst. Or. 1.3.1) -- the Judaean tradition catalogs the qualities that distinguish the worthy student from the unworthy:

> <span dir="rtl">ארבע מידות בתלמידים: מהר לשמוע ומהר לאבד — יצא שכרו בהפסדו. קשה לשמוע וקשה לאבד — יצא הפסדו בשכרו. מהר לשמוע וקשה לאבד — חכם. קשה לשמוע ומהר לאבד — זה חלק רע</span>
>
> *Four types of students: Quick to learn and quick to forget -- the gain is offset by the loss. Slow to learn and slow to forget -- the loss is offset by the gain. Quick to learn and slow to forget -- wise. Slow to learn and quick to forget -- a bad lot.* (Avot 5:12)

This taxonomy is a logical permutation matrix -- two variables (speed of acquisition, speed of loss) crossed to produce four outcomes. The rhetorician will recognize this as a systematic application of the *topos* of division (*divisio*), but its purpose is not merely classificatory; it is diagnostic. The teacher who can identify which type of student sits before him can calibrate his pedagogical approach accordingly -- repeating more for the slow learner, reinforcing more for the quick forgetter.

The ideal student is further characterized in Avot 5:7 through a catalog of seven qualities:

> <span dir="rtl">שבעה דברים בגולם ושבעה בחכם. חכם אינו מדבר בפני מי שהוא גדול ממנו בחכמה, ואינו נכנס לתוך דברי חברו, ואינו נבהל להשיב</span>
>
> *Seven qualities characterize a wise person: he does not speak in the presence of one greater than he in wisdom, he does not interrupt his fellow's words, and he is not hasty to respond\.\.\.*

These are, in effect, rhetorical virtues: the wise student knows when to speak and when to be silent, respects the protocols of academic discourse, and restrains himself from premature response. They correspond to the qualities that Quintilian recommends in the ideal student (Inst. Or. 2.5.7-13) -- modesty, attentiveness, and the willingness to submit to correction.

The rhetorician who examines the Judaean *bet midrash* will find, in sum, a complete educational institution: a physical school, a graduated curriculum, a formalized master-student relationship, a theory of pedagogical authority, a systematic set of teaching methods, and a detailed typology of student capacities. It is, in every essential respect, a school of rhetoric -- differing from the schools of Athens and Rome not in its fundamental structure but in its subject matter (Torah rather than civic oratory), its cultural idiom (Hebrew and Aramaic rather than Greek and Latin), and its ultimate purpose (the service of God rather than the service of the state).

We turn now to the preliminary exercises themselves.

---

## PART II: THE PRELIMINARY EXERCISES (PROGYMNASMATA)

The preliminary exercises -- *progymnasmata* in Greek, *praeexercitamenta* in Latin -- are the graded sequence of compositional tasks through which the student of rhetoric develops his skills before attempting the composition of complete speeches. The standard sequence, as set forth by Theon of Alexandria (first century CE), Hermogenes of Tarsus (second century CE), Aphthonius of Antioch (fourth century CE), and Nicolaus of Myra (fifth century CE), comprises between twelve and fourteen exercises, beginning with the simplest forms -- fable, narrative, chreia -- and progressing through increasingly complex tasks -- refutation, confirmation, commonplace, encomium, comparison, impersonation, description, thesis, and the proposal of a law.[^37]

The logic of the sequence deserves a word of explanation. The fable comes first because it requires the least technical skill: the student needs only to compose or retell a brief fictional narrative with a clear moral point.
