The narrative comes second because it adds the requirement of factual accuracy and structural organization -- the six elements of person, action, time, place, manner, and cause. The chreia comes third because it adds the requirement of *attribution* -- the student must not merely narrate but must assign the saying or action to a specific character with appropriate fitness. Each successive exercise builds on the skills developed in the preceding ones, so that by the time the student reaches the more advanced exercises (thesis, impersonation, the proposal of a law), he has already mastered a full repertoire of compositional techniques.

The Judaean tradition, though it does not explicitly name its pedagogical sequence as *progymnasmata*, follows a recognizably similar progression. The student begins with the simple forms -- learning to retell meshalim (fables), to narrate ma'asiyot (precedent narratives), and to recite ma'amarim (attributed sayings). He advances to more complex tasks: expanding a teaching through scriptural proof-texts and logical arguments, refuting a position through counter-arguments, composing formal eulogies and encomia, and ultimately engaging in free-form dialectical debate (*pilpul*) on contested legal and theological questions. The correspondence between this progression and the progymnasmata sequence is not exact -- the Judaean tradition emphasizes textual interpretation more heavily and political oratory less -- but the underlying pedagogical logic is the same: one begins with simple exercises and advances through increasingly demanding tasks toward independent rhetorical competence.

We shall demonstrate that the literature of the Judaean sages contains abundant and sophisticated examples of every one of these exercises. The claim we advance is not merely that isolated parallels can be found -- any sufficiently large corpus will contain occasional resemblances to any given form -- but that the Judaean tradition practiced these exercises *systematically*, in a *graduated sequence*, within an *institutional setting* (the bet midrash) devoted to the cultivation of rhetorical and interpretive competence. The parallels are not incidental but structural.

A methodological note is in order. We do not claim that the Judaean sages consciously modeled their pedagogical practices on the Greek *progymnasmata*. The question of direct historical influence -- whether the rabbis of Roman Palestine were directly acquainted with the rhetorical handbooks of Theon or Hermogenes -- remains debated in modern scholarship. Lieberman (1950) and Daube (1949) argued for significant direct contact; more cautious scholars have emphasized the possibility of independent development under similar social conditions. For our purposes, the question of historical causation is secondary. Whether the parallels result from direct borrowing, from common Near Eastern roots, or from the convergent evolution of rhetorical practices in societies that value public discourse, the structural correspondences are real and illuminating. We use the Greek categories as a lens, not as a genealogy.

In this section, we treat the first three exercises: fable, narrative, and chreia.

---

### II.A. Fable (Mythos / Mashal)

#### Definition and Classification

The fable is universally placed first in the progymnasmata sequence, as the simplest and most accessible of the preliminary exercises. Its simplicity, however, is deceptive. Quintilian, while noting that the fable "is most suited to the instruction of the ignorant" (Inst. Or. 5.11.19), acknowledges that the composition of effective fables requires genuine inventive skill: the student must select an appropriate analogy, construct a brief but coherent narrative, and ensure that the moral application is both clear and apt. The fable is the gateway to more complex exercises, but it is already, in miniature, a complete rhetorical act.

Theon defines it as follows:

> "A fable is a fictitious narrative imaging truth." (Theon, *Progymnasmata* 1)[^38]

Aphthonius offers a complementary formulation:

> "A fable is a saying which, under the guise of a fiction, shadows forth a truth." (Aphthonius, *Progymnasmata* 1)[^39]

Both definitions emphasize the dual character of the fable: it is fictitious in its surface narrative but truthful in its underlying meaning. The fiction is not an end in itself but a vehicle -- a means of conveying a truth that might be less accessible, less memorable, or less persuasive if stated directly.

Classical rhetoricians classify fables into several types. Theon distinguishes:

- **Animal fables** (Aesopic): in which animals speak and act as characters, typically illustrating human qualities
- **Rational fables**: in which human characters are used
- **Mixed fables**: in which both human and non-human characters interact

The Judaean tradition possesses examples of all three types but has developed the fable form with a sophistication and abundance that far exceeds its Aesopic counterpart. The Judaean term for the fable is *mashal* (plural *meshalim*), a word that encompasses parable, proverb, allegory, and similitude -- a broader semantic field than the Greek *mythos* or *ainos*. This semantic breadth is significant: the Judaean sage who composes a *mashal* is not confined to the narrow genre of the animal fable but has at his disposal the full range of figurative narrative -- from brief simile to extended allegory, from animal fable to royal parable to cosmic myth. The *mashal* is, in this sense, not a single form but a family of forms united by the common principle of indirect signification: saying one thing to mean another.

Where the Greek fable tradition is dominated by animal fables attributed to the semi-legendary Aesop, the Judaean tradition cultivates a wider range of fable types, with the "king parable" (*mashal le-melekh*) and the "householder parable" (*mashal le-ba'al ha-bayit*) being at least as prominent as the animal fable. The prevalence of the king parable in particular reflects the theological orientation of Judaean rhetoric: since the ultimate subject of Judaean discourse is the relationship between God and humanity, the most useful fable type is one that models that relationship through the analogy of sovereignty -- the king and his subjects, the father and his son, the master and his servants.

#### Types of Mashal in the Judaean Tradition

**1. Fox Fables (Aesopic Type)**

The Judaean sages were familiar with the animal fable tradition and cultivated it actively. The most prolific composer of fox fables in the Judaean tradition was Rabbi Meir, of whom it is recorded:

> <span dir="rtl">משמת רבי מאיר, בטלו מושלי משלים</span>
>
> *When Rabbi Meir died, the composers of parables ceased.* (Mishnah Sotah 9:15)

And more specifically:

> <span dir="rtl">שלוש מאות משלות שועלים היו לו לרבי מאיר</span>
>
> *Rabbi Meir had three hundred fox fables.* (Babylonian Talmud, Sanhedrin 38b)[^40]

Three hundred fox fables attributed to a single sage -- a corpus that would dwarf the surviving Aesopic collection. Though most of these fables have not survived, their former existence testifies to the vitality of the animal fable tradition within the Judaean rhetorical schools. The few that are preserved display the characteristic features of the Aesopic genre: brevity, animal characters embodying human types, and a pointed moral application.

One surviving example, preserved in the Babylonian Talmud (Berakhot 61b), illustrates the form. The sage Rabbi Akiva, defying a Roman decree prohibiting Torah study, is challenged by a certain Pappus ben Judah, who asks why he risks his life. Rabbi Akiva responds with a fox fable:

> <span dir="rtl">משל לשועל שהיה מהלך על גב הנהר, וראה דגים שהיו מתקבצים ממקום למקום. אמר להם: מפני מה אתם בורחים? אמרו לו: מפני רשתות שמביאין עלינו בני אדם. אמר להם: רצונכם שתעלו ליבשה, ונדור אני ואתם כשם שדרו אבותי עם אבותיכם? אמרו לו: אתה הוא שאומרים עליך פיקח שבחיות? לא פיקח אתה אלא טיפש אתה. ומה במקום חיותנו אנו מתפחדים, במקום מיתתנו על אחת כמה וכמה</span>
>
> *A parable: A fox was walking along the riverbank and saw fish darting from place to place. He said to them: From what are you fleeing? They said: From the nets that people cast upon us. He said: Would you like to come up onto dry land, and we shall dwell together as my ancestors dwelt with yours? They said: Are you the one they call the cleverest of animals? You are not clever but foolish! If in the place of our life we are afraid, how much more so in the place of our death!*[^41]

The fable displays perfect Aesopic form: animal characters (fox, fish), a dialogue that reveals the moral point, and an implicit application to the situation at hand (Torah study is the "water" in which Jews live; abandoning it for supposed safety is like fish leaving the water). The application is then made explicit: "So it is with us: if we sit and study Torah, in which it is written, 'For it is your life and the length of your days,' we are thus; if we go and neglect it, how much more so [will we perish]."

Note the structural feature that distinguishes the Judaean fable from its Aesopic counterpart: the explicit application, called the *nimshal* ("that to which the mashal is compared"). In the Aesopic tradition, the moral is typically appended as a brief *epimythion* -- "The fable teaches that...." In the Judaean tradition, the *nimshal* is a fully developed interpretive complement to the *mashal*, often as elaborate as the fable itself. The relationship between *mashal* and *nimshal* is analogous to the relationship between figure and meaning, between the rhetorical surface and the argumentative substance. This makes the Judaean fable a more rigorous tool of argumentation than the Aesopic fable, which typically contents itself with a general moral observation.

**2. King Parables (Rational Type)**

The most characteristic and abundant form of Judaean fable is the "king parable" -- a fictitious narrative in which a king (representing God) interacts with servants, sons, tenants, or subjects (representing human beings or Israel). This type corresponds to Theon's "rational fable" (using human characters) but has been developed by the Judaean sages into a distinct genre with its own conventions, formulas, and rhetorical functions.

A characteristic example, from the Mekhilta de-Rabbi Ishmael (Bahodesh 5):

> <span dir="rtl">משל למלך שנכנס למדינה. אמרו לו עבדיו: גזור עלינו גזירות. אמר להם: לאו, כשתקבלו מלכותי אגזור עליכם גזירות, שאם מלכותי אינכם מקבלים, גזירותי היאך אתם מקיימים?</span>
>
> *A parable: A king entered a province. His servants said to him: Issue decrees upon us. He said to them: No -- when you accept my sovereignty, I will issue decrees upon you. For if you do not accept my sovereignty, how will you fulfill my decrees?*[^42]

The *nimshal* explains that God first declared "I am the Lord your God" (establishing sovereignty) before issuing the commandments (the decrees). The parable thus functions as an argument about the logical priority of theological commitment over legal obligation -- a sophisticated philosophical point delivered through the accessible medium of a brief fictional narrative.

A second king parable, from Sifrei Deuteronomy 26, illustrates the form's argumentative versatility:

> <span dir="rtl">משל למלך שהיה עובר ממקום למקום, ובנו קטן רוכב על כתפו. היה הבן רואה חפץ ואומר: אבא, קנה לי חפץ זה. והוא קונה לו. פעם ראשונה, ופעם שנייה, ופעם שלישית. לימים מצאו אדם אחד. אמר לו הבן: ראית את אבא? אמר לו אביו: אתה רוכב על כתפי, וכל מה שאתה מבקש אני נותן לך, ואתה שואל לאיש ראית את אבא?!</span>
>
> *A parable: A king was passing from place to place, and his young son was riding on his shoulders. The son saw an object and said: Father, buy me that object. And he bought it for him -- once, twice, three times. Later they encountered a stranger. The son said to him: Have you seen my father? His father said to him: You are riding on my shoulders, and everything you ask I give you, and you ask someone, "Have you seen my father"?!*

The *nimshal*: Israel is carried by God (the Exodus), receives everything it requests (manna, quail, water from the rock), and yet asks at Massah, "Is the Lord among us or not?" (Exodus 17:7). The parable makes the absurdity of the question visible by translating the cosmic relationship (Israel and God) into a domestic one (child and father on whose shoulders he rides). The argumentative force derives from the shock of the child's question -- how can you ask whether your father is present when you are literally sitting on his shoulders? -- which mirrors the shock of Israel's question about God's presence after decades of continuous divine provision.

The king parable is ubiquitous in the tannaitic midrashim (Mekhilta, Sifra, Sifrei) and in the later compilations (Genesis Rabbah, Leviticus Rabbah, etc.). David Stern has cataloged the rhetorical conventions of this form, noting that the king typically stands for God, the prince for Israel, the servants for various nations or social classes, and the royal household for the cosmos.[^43] The consistency of this symbolic vocabulary means that the Judaean audience, upon hearing the formula "A parable: to a king who..." (*mashal le-melekh she-...*), immediately entered an interpretive framework, knowing what each character signified and attending to the particular twist or variation that would yield the teaching of the moment.

**3. Householder Parables and Two-Path Parables**

Beyond the king parable, the Judaean tradition cultivated additional rational fable types. The "householder parable" (*mashal le-ba'al ha-bayit*) uses the figure of a property owner managing his estate to illustrate principles of divine justice, reward, and moral responsibility. The "two-path parable" (*mashal li-shnei derakhim*) presents a traveler at a fork in the road, one path leading to safety and the other to danger, to illustrate moral choice. The two-path parable has antecedents in biblical literature (Deuteronomy 30:15-19: "I have set before you life and death, the blessing and the curse") and parallels in Greek moral philosophy (the "Choice of Heracles" in Xenophon's *Memorabilia* 2.1.21-34).

An example from Sifrei Numbers 42:

> <span dir="rtl">משלו משל, למה הדבר דומה? לאחד שהיה יושב בפרשת דרכים ולפניו שני שבילין, אחד תחילתו מישור וסופו קוצים, ואחד תחילתו קוצים וסופו מישור</span>
>
> *They told a parable: To what may this be compared? To one sitting at a crossroads with two paths before him, one whose beginning is smooth and whose end is thorns, and one whose beginning is thorns and whose end is smooth.*[^44]

The structural clarity of this type -- two options, binary outcome, moral application -- makes it an ideal pedagogical tool, and its frequency in rabbinic literature suggests that it was a standard exercise in the schools.

**4. The Mashal and Nimshal: A Structural Analysis**

Before proceeding to scriptural precedents, we must examine the formal structure that distinguishes the Judaean fable from its Greek counterpart. In the Aesopic tradition, the fable is typically followed by a brief *epimythion* -- a summary moral, often introduced by the formula "The fable teaches that..." This epimythion is appended to the fable but remains external to it; its relationship to the narrative is one of extraction (the moral is "drawn from" the story).

The Judaean tradition operates with a fundamentally different architecture. The *mashal* (fable/parable) is paired with a *nimshal* (application/referent) -- literally, "that which is compared." The *nimshal* is not a summary moral appended from outside but a parallel interpretive structure that maps the elements of the fable onto the elements of the reality it illuminates. Consider the king parable cited above (Mekhilta, Bahodesh 5): the king is God, the province is the world, the servants are Israel, the decrees are the commandments, and the acceptance of sovereignty is the declaration "I am the Lord your God." Each element of the *mashal* has a specific corresponding element in the *nimshal*, and the relationship between the two structures constitutes the argument.

This architecture makes the Judaean fable a more rigorous tool of argumentation than the Aesopic fable. The *nimshal* forces the composer to think in terms of systematic correspondence rather than vague moral analogy. It is not enough to tell a story and extract a lesson; one must construct a story whose internal relationships precisely mirror the relationships one wishes to illuminate in the real world. The *mashal-nimshal* structure thus functions as what modern rhetoricians would call an "analogical argument" -- an argument that derives its force from the structural isomorphism between two domains. It is argumentation by architecture.

The Judaean sages themselves recognized the cognitive power of the *mashal* form:

> <span dir="rtl">אל יהי משל קל בעיניך, שעל ידי משל אדם עומד על דברי תורה. משל לאדם שאבד מרגלית בביתו; לא על ידי פתילה שוה איסר הוא מוצאה?</span>
>
> *Let not the mashal be trivial in your eyes, for through the mashal a person comes to understand words of Torah. A parable: A man who lost a pearl in his house -- does he not find it by means of a wick worth merely a penny?* (Shir ha-Shirim Rabbah 1:1.8)

This is a mashal about meshalim -- a meta-parable that uses the fable form to justify the fable form. The pearl is Torah; the cheap wick is the mashal; the dark house is the human mind that cannot perceive the truth by direct inspection but requires the indirect illumination of figurative language. The passage constitutes a Judaean theory of metaphor: the function of figurative language is not ornamentation but epistemic access.

**5. Scriptural Precedent: Jotham's Fable**

The student should observe that the fable tradition in Judaean literature has deep scriptural roots. The oldest surviving fable in the Hebrew corpus is Jotham's fable of the trees (Judges 9:7-15), in which the trees seek a king:

> <span dir="rtl">הלוך הלכו העצים למשוח עליהם מלך, ויאמרו לזית: מלוכה עלינו. ויאמר להם הזית: החדלתי את דשני אשר בי יכבדו אלהים ואנשים, והלכתי לנוע על העצים?</span>
>
> *The trees went forth to anoint a king over themselves. They said to the olive tree: Reign over us. The olive tree said to them: Shall I leave my fatness, by which gods and men are honored, and go to wave over the trees?* (Judges 9:8-9)

This fable -- in which the productive trees (olive, fig, vine) decline kingship and only the worthless thornbush accepts it -- is a political allegory of precisely the type that Greek rhetoricians classified under the Aesopic genre. Its rhetorical structure deserves careful analysis. The pattern is one of ascending refusal: the olive tree (the most valuable) declines first, then the fig (second most valuable), then the vine (third). Each productive tree refuses on the same grounds: it would have to abandon its productive function to take on the unproductive role of ruler. The thornbush, which has no productive function to abandon, is the only one willing to accept. The moral -- that the best qualified are least willing to rule, and those who seek power are precisely those who should not have it -- is delivered not through explicit statement but through the cumulative effect of the narrative structure itself.

This fable's presence in Scripture gave the fable form canonical legitimacy within the Judaean tradition, ensuring that the composition and interpretation of parables would be regarded not as a foreign import from Greek rhetoric but as an indigenous Judaean practice with roots in the prophetic literature. Other scriptural parables -- Nathan's parable of the poor man's lamb (2 Samuel 12:1-4), Isaiah's Song of the Vineyard (Isaiah 5:1-7), Ezekiel's parable of the eagles and the vine (Ezekiel 17:1-10) -- further established the *mashal* as a prophetic genre, a medium through which divine truth is communicated in figurative form. The Judaean sage who composed meshalim was therefore not imitating Aesop but continuing a prophetic tradition older than Aesop himself.

#### The Relationship between Mashal and Nimshal: Hermeneutical Implications

The *mashal-nimshal* structure has implications that extend beyond rhetoric into hermeneutics -- the theory of interpretation. The relationship between mashal and nimshal is not merely illustrative but *argumentative*: the parable does not merely decorate a pre-existing truth but *generates* understanding that would not otherwise be available. When the sage tells the parable of the fox and the fish (Berakhot 61b), the parable does not merely illustrate the danger of abandoning Torah study; it enables the listener to *see* the danger in a new way, through the analogy of fish leaving water. The mashal makes visible what the abstract proposition leaves invisible -- the experiential quality of the danger, the absurdity of the proposed remedy, the self-destructiveness of the seemingly prudent course.

This cognitive function of the mashal -- its capacity to generate understanding rather than merely illustrate it -- is recognized by the Judaean tradition in its exalted estimation of the form. When the tradition records that "when Rabbi Meir died, the composers of parables ceased," it is not mourning the loss of a literary entertainer but the loss of a cognitive tool: the capacity to make abstract truths apprehensible through concrete analogy. The *mashal* is an instrument of knowledge, not merely of persuasion.

#### The Exercise

The student of the Judaean rhetorical school would be assigned to compose a *mashal* to illuminate a given teaching -- for example: "Compose a parable to explain why a person must study Torah even when it seems unprofitable." The exercise tests the student's capacity for inventive analogy: he must find a concrete narrative situation whose structure mirrors the abstract principle he wishes to teach. This is precisely the exercise that Theon prescribes when he directs the student to compose fables "suited to a given situation" (*Progymnasmata* 1), and it remains the first step in the development of rhetorical *inventio*.

A further exercise that Theon describes -- retelling a fable in different words, expanding it, or condensing it -- finds its Judaean counterpart in the multiple versions of the same parable that appear across different rabbinic collections. The king parable about sovereignty preceding legislation, for example, appears in slightly different forms in the Mekhilta, the Sifrei, and later midrashic compilations. Each version preserves the core analogy while varying the details, the emphasis, and the elaboration -- precisely the kind of variation that Theon's exercises in recitation and inflexion are designed to produce. The modern scholar who encounters these variants often assumes that they represent textual corruption or scribal error; the rhetorician recognizes them as the products of a living pedagogical tradition in which the same core exercise was performed repeatedly, with each performance producing a legitimate variant.

Theon also prescribes the exercise of composing a fable to support or refute a given proposition. This exercise is attested in the Judaean tradition by the deployment of meshalim in argumentative contexts -- a sage introduces a parable not as a freestanding narrative but as evidence for a legal or theological position. When Rabbi Akiva tells the parable of the fox and the fish to justify his continued Torah study under Roman persecution (Berakhot 61b), he is performing this exercise at the highest level: composing a fable on the spot to support a proposition ("Torah study must continue even under threat of death") in a real rhetorical situation (confrontation with a questioner). The parable is not rehearsed; it is invented in the moment of need. This is the rhetorical ideal: the student who has internalized the fable exercise so thoroughly that he can produce apt parables spontaneously, as the situation demands.

---

### II.B. Narrative (Diegema / Ma'aseh)

#### Definition and Classification

Narrative -- the exposition of events -- is the second exercise in the standard progymnasmata sequence and one of the most fundamental skills in the orator's repertoire. Quintilian goes so far as to say that the ability to narrate clearly and persuasively is "the first and most necessary part of the case" (Inst. Or. 4.2.1), since judges who cannot follow the narrative cannot evaluate the arguments that depend upon it. In forensic oratory, the *narratio* is the backbone of the speech; in epideictic oratory, it provides the biographical foundation for praise or blame; in deliberative oratory, it establishes the historical context within which future policy must be formulated.

Theon defines it thus:

> "A narrative is an exposition of an action that has happened or as if it had happened." (Theon, *Progymnasmata* 2)[^45]

The definition encompasses both factual and fictional narrative, but in its progymnasmata application, the exercise focuses on the concise narration of a single event or sequence of events. Theon further specifies the six elements that every well-formed narrative must include:

1. **Person** (*prosopon*): who performed the action
2. **Action** (*pragma*): what was done
3. **Time** (*chronos*): when it occurred
4. **Place** (*topos*): where it occurred
5. **Manner** (*tropos*): how it was done
6. **Cause** (*aitia*): why it was done[^46]

The virtues of narrative, as enumerated by Quintilian (Inst. Or. 4.2.31-61), are three: **clarity** (*saphēneia* / *perspicuitas*), so that the audience understands what happened; **brevity** (*syntomia* / *brevitas*), so that no unnecessary details encumber the account; and **plausibility** (*pithanotēs* / *verisimilitudo*), so that the audience believes the narrative to be credible.[^47]

In the Judaean tradition, the *ma'aseh* (plural *ma'asiyot*) -- the precedent narrative -- is one of the foundational literary forms. The formula *ma'aseh be-...* ("a story of..." or "it happened that...") introduces a narrative of something a sage said or did, typically in circumstances that give the narrative legal or moral significance.

The *ma'aseh* functions in Judaean legal discourse much as the *exemplum* functions in Roman forensic oratory: it establishes a precedent through the narration of a concrete case.[^48] But the Judaean *ma'aseh* differs from the Roman *exemplum* in an important respect: the Roman orator introduces *exempla* to *illustrate* a legal principle that has already been established on independent grounds; the Judaean sage introduces a *ma'aseh* to *establish* the principle itself. The narrative does not merely support the argument; it *constitutes* the argument. "Rabbi X did Y" is, in many cases, the entirety of the reasoning: the authoritative sage's action is itself the law.

The rhetorician will immediately observe that the Judaean *ma'aseh* routinely displays all six elements specified by Theon, often with exemplary concision.

#### Examples

**1. Hillel and the Converts (Shabbat 31a) -- A Narrative Demonstrating Character**

> <span dir="rtl">שוב מעשה בנכרי אחד שבא לפני שמאי. אמר לו: גיירני על מנת שתלמדני כל התורה כולה כשאני עומד על רגל אחת. דחפו באמת הבניין שבידו. בא לפני הלל. גייריה. אמר לו: דעלך סני לחברך לא תעביד — זו היא כל התורה כולה, ואידך פירושה הוא, זיל גמור</span>
>
> *Again, a certain gentile came before Shammai. He said to him: Convert me on the condition that you teach me the entire Torah while I stand on one foot. He pushed him away with the builder's cubit in his hand. He came before Hillel. He converted him. He said to him: That which is hateful to you, do not do to your neighbor -- this is the entire Torah, and the rest is commentary. Go and learn.*[^49]

Let us analyze this narrative according to Theon's six elements:

- **Person**: Three characters -- a gentile, Shammai, Hillel
- **Action**: The gentile poses an extreme request; Shammai rejects him physically; Hillel converts him and answers
- **Time**: Implied as contemporaneous with the events of Shammai and Hillel (first century BCE)
- **Place**: Before the respective sages (implied as their homes or academies)
- **Manner**: Shammai responds with physical force (the builder's cubit); Hillel responds with verbal wisdom
- **Cause**: The gentile seeks conversion; the narrative's purpose is to illustrate the contrasting characters of the two sages

The narrative also displays the three Quintilianic virtues with precision. It is **clear**: the sequence of events is instantly comprehensible. It is **brief**: the entire story is told in fewer than forty words of the original Aramaic. And it is **plausible**: the characters act consistently with their known temperaments (Shammai's rigor, Hillel's patience), lending the narrative the credibility of biographical realism.

Note also the narrative's rhetorical structure: it is a *synkrisis* -- a comparison narrative in which two characters respond to the same challenge, and the contrast between their responses constitutes the moral of the story. This is precisely the comparative narrative form that Plutarch employs in his *Parallel Lives* and that the progymnasmata handbooks prescribe as an advanced exercise (comparison, *synkrisis*). Here it appears embedded within a simple narrative exercise, demonstrating the layered complexity of even the most basic Judaean rhetorical forms.

The Shabbat 31a passage actually contains not one but three narrative units -- three different converts approaching Shammai and then Hillel, each with a different impossible demand. The first wants to be taught only the Written Torah; the second wants to be converted on the condition of becoming High Priest; the third wants the entire Torah on one foot. In each case, Shammai rejects the petitioner and Hillel accepts him. After all three have been converted, they meet:

> <span dir="rtl">לימים נזדווגו שלושתם למקום אחד. אמרו: קפדנותו של שמאי ביקשה לטורדנו מן העולם, ענוותנותו של הלל קרבנו תחת כנפי השכינה</span>
>
> *After some time, the three of them met in one place. They said: The rigor of Shammai sought to drive us from the world; the humility of Hillel brought us under the wings of the divine Presence.*

This concluding frame transforms the three individual narratives into a unified *synkrisis* with a summary judgment delivered by the characters themselves. The narrative structure is that of a deliberative trial: two approaches to the same problem (admitting converts) are tested against three different cases, and the verdict is rendered not by an external narrator but by the subjects of the experiment. This is narrative as forensic argument -- the convert's testimony functioning as evidence in the case of Hillel's pedagogical superiority.

**2. Rabban Gamaliel on a Ship -- A Narrative with All Six Elements**

> <span dir="rtl">מעשה ברבן גמליאל ורבי אלעזר בן עזריה ורבי יהושע ורבי עקיבא שהיו באים בספינה</span>
>
> *A story of Rabban Gamaliel, Rabbi Elazar ben Azariah, Rabbi Yehoshua, and Rabbi Akiva who were traveling on a ship.* (Cf. Babylonian Talmud, Makkot 24a-b)

This narrative opening is a model of the Judaean *ma'aseh* formula:

- **Person**: Four named sages
- **Action**: Traveling (with the implied events that follow)
- **Time**: During their journey (historical period implied by the named characters)
- **Place**: On a ship
- **Manner**: Together, as a group of colleagues
- **Cause**: The journey provides the occasion for a significant conversation or event

The passage that follows records that as the sages approached the site of the destroyed Temple in Jerusalem, they saw a fox emerging from the place where the Holy of Holies had been. Three of them wept; Rabbi Akiva laughed. They said to him: "Why do you laugh?" He said to them: "Why do you weep?" They said: "The place of which it is written, 'The stranger who draws near shall be put to death' -- and now foxes walk upon it. Should we not weep?" He said to them:

> <span dir="rtl">מפני כך אני משחק. דכתיב: ואעידה לי עדים נאמנים את אוריה הכהן ואת זכריה בן יברכיהו. מה עניין אוריה אצל זכריה? אלא, תלה הכתוב נבואתו של זכריה בנבואתו של אוריה. באוריה כתיב: לכן בגללכם ציון שדה תחרש. בזכריה כתיב: עוד ישבו זקנים וזקנות ברחובות ירושלים. עד שלא נתקיימה נבואתו של אוריה, הייתי מתירא שלא תתקיים נבואתו של זכריה. עכשיו שנתקיימה נבואתו של אוריה, בידוע שנבואתו של זכריה מתקיימת</span>
>
> *That is precisely why I laugh. It is written: "I took to Me faithful witnesses, Uriah the priest and Zechariah son of Jeberechiah." What has Uriah to do with Zechariah? But Scripture linked the prophecy of Zechariah to the prophecy of Uriah. In Uriah it is written: "Therefore because of you Zion shall be plowed as a field." In Zechariah it is written: "Old men and old women shall yet sit in the streets of Jerusalem." Until the prophecy of Uriah was fulfilled, I feared that the prophecy of Zechariah might not be fulfilled. Now that the prophecy of Uriah has been fulfilled, it is certain that the prophecy of Zechariah will be fulfilled.*

His colleagues responded: "Akiva, you have comforted us. Akiva, you have comforted us." (*nihamt'anu, nihamt'anu*).

The narrative moves from factual description (persons, place, circumstances) to dramatic confrontation (the fox at the Temple site) to climactic dialogue (the scriptural argument) to emotional resolution (the comfort). The rhetorical structure is that of a complete speech: the *prooimion* (the setting), the *narratio* (the fox), the *confirmatio* (Rabbi Akiva's scriptural proof-text argument, linking Uriah's prophecy of destruction to Zechariah's prophecy of restoration through a *gezerah shavah*), and the *peroratio* (the companions' emotional response). It is a perfect narrative arc that any rhetorician would recognize as *dispositio* of the highest order. And the doubled exclamation -- "you have comforted us, you have comforted us" -- is a figure of repetition (*epizeuxis*) that signals the depth of the emotional release: the comfort is so profound that a single utterance cannot contain it.

**3. A Legal Precedent Narrative (Tosefta Sukkah 2:3)**

> <span dir="rtl">מעשה בהילני המלכה בלוד שהיתה סוכתה גבוהה מעשרים אמה, והיו זקנים נכנסין ויוצאין ולא אמרו לה דבר</span>
>
> *A story of Queen Helena in Lydda whose sukkah was higher than twenty cubits, and the elders went in and out and said nothing to her.*

This *ma'aseh* functions as a legal precedent: the fact that the elders -- the legal authorities -- observed Queen Helena's sukkah without objection is taken as evidence that an excessively tall sukkah is permissible. The narrative is stripped to its barest essentials -- person (Queen Helena), place (Lydda), action (constructed a tall sukkah), manner of response (the elders' silence), and legal implication (silent approval). This is narrative as forensic evidence -- the Judaean equivalent of citing a previous ruling in a Roman court.

**4. Rabbi Akiva's Beginnings (Avot de-Rabbi Natan A, ch. 6) -- Biographical Narrative**

> <span dir="rtl">מעשה ברבי עקיבא שהיה רועה צאן של בן כלבא שבוע. ראתה בתו שהוא צנוע ומעולה. אמרה לו: אם אקדש לך, תלך לבית המדרש? אמר לה: הן. נתקדשה לו בצנעה ושלחתו לבית המדרש. הלך וישב שתים עשרה שנה לפני רבי אליעזר ורבי יהושע</span>
>
> *A story of Rabbi Akiva, who was a shepherd of the flocks of Ben Kalba Savua. His daughter saw that he was modest and excellent. She said to him: If I betroth myself to you, will you go to the study house? He said to her: Yes. She betrothed herself to him in secret and sent him to the study house. He went and sat for twelve years before Rabbi Eliezer and Rabbi Yehoshua.*[^50]

This biographical narrative displays the features of what Hermogenes calls the *diegema characterikon* -- a narrative that reveals character. All six elements are present: the person (Akiva), the action (transformation from shepherd to scholar), the time (twelve years of study), the place (the study house), the manner (through the agency of his wife's initiative), and the cause (her recognition of his latent qualities). The narrative also functions as an *exemplum* of the possibility of radical personal transformation -- a theme that the Judaean rhetorical tradition values highly and that serves deliberative purposes: it argues that one should never despair of education, regardless of one's starting point.

#### The Virtues of Judaean Narrative

A systematic comparison of Judaean narrative with the three classical virtues is instructive.

**Clarity** (*saphēneia*): The *ma'aseh* formula itself -- "A story of Rabbi X who..." -- is a device of clarity. It immediately identifies the protagonist and signals the genre. The listener knows at once that what follows is a narrative of precedent, not a theoretical argument or a scriptural interpretation. The formulaic opening functions as a genre marker comparable to the Latin "once upon a time" (*olim*) in exemplary narrative or the Greek "it is said that" (*legetai hoti*) in historical anecdote. The Judaean sages rarely employ complex narrative structures -- flashbacks, nested stories, unreliable narration. The sequence is chronological, the characters are identified, the actions are described in plain language. This deliberate simplicity is not naivete but rhetorical discipline: the *ma'aseh* exists to establish a precedent or illustrate a principle, and anything that complicates the narrative surface risks obscuring the didactic point.

**Brevity** (*syntomia*): The Judaean *ma'aseh* is among the most compressed narrative forms in world literature. The precedent narrative of Queen Helena cited above tells its story in fewer than twenty Hebrew words. Even the more elaborate character narratives -- Hillel and the converts, Akiva's transformation -- achieve in a paragraph what biographical writing in other traditions requires pages to accomplish. This economy reflects the same stylistic preference for compression that governs Judaean prose generally: in a culture of oral transmission, brevity is not merely an aesthetic preference but a survival imperative. The narrative that cannot be memorized will not be transmitted; the narrative that is too long to memorize will be shortened in transmission until it finds its sustainable form. The brevity of the *ma'aseh* is therefore not the result of conscious authorial choice alone but also of the selective pressure of oral transmission over generations.

**Plausibility** (*pithanotēs*): The Judaean tradition achieves plausibility primarily through the attribution of narratives to named, historically specific characters. The sage in a *ma'aseh* is not a generic "wise man" but a specific individual -- Rabbi Akiva, Hillel, Rabban Gamaliel -- whose known character and historical circumstances lend the narrative credibility. When the tradition reports that Shammai drove a convert away with a builder's cubit, we believe the story because Shammai is consistently characterized as stern and uncompromising; the detail of the builder's cubit (Shammai's profession was construction) adds the specificity of lived experience. This technique of plausibility through characterological consistency is precisely what Quintilian recommends when he advises the narrator to make characters "act and speak in conformity with their natures" (Inst. Or. 4.2.52).

#### The Exercise

The Judaean narrative exercise would require the student to narrate a sage's ruling or action with all six elements present, displaying the three virtues of clarity, brevity, and plausibility. The student would practice composing narratives in multiple registers: the legal precedent narrative (terse, evidentiary), the character narrative (detailed, morally instructive), and the historical narrative (contextual, commemorative). As in the progymnasmata sequence, the narrative exercise prepares the student for more complex tasks by training him in the fundamental skill of organizing events into a coherent, purposeful account.

The distinctive contribution of the Judaean tradition to narrative rhetoric lies in the concept of the *ma'aseh* as legal evidence -- a narrative whose purpose is not merely to entertain or instruct but to establish binding precedent. When a sage reports "a story of Rabbi X who did Y," the narrative functions as testimony: the past action of an authoritative figure constitutes grounds for future rulings. This fusion of narrative and jurisprudence -- the story as legal argument -- represents a sophistication of the narrative exercise that the Greek progymnasmata handbooks do not anticipate but that the Roman tradition of *exempla* in forensic oratory (Cicero, *De Inv.* 1.30.49) approaches from a different direction.

The relationship between narrative and legal argumentation in the Judaean tradition raises a further theoretical question that the rhetorician cannot ignore. In the Greek progymnasmata, the narrative exercise trains the student in a skill (clear, brief, plausible narration) that he will later deploy as one element within a larger speech. The narrative is a *part* of the speech -- specifically, the *narratio* section that follows the *prooemium* and precedes the *confirmatio*. In the Judaean tradition, by contrast, the narrative can function as a *complete* speech act: the narration of a precedent, without further argument, may suffice to settle a legal question. The *ma'aseh* is not a *part* of an argument; it *is* the argument. "A story of Rabbi X who did Y" is, in many cases, the entirety of the legal reasoning: the precedent speaks for itself.

This gives the Judaean narrative a rhetorical weight that the Greek narrative exercise does not possess. When the Mishnah narrates that "Rabban Gamaliel stood and served" at a banquet, it is not telling a story for the sake of storytelling; it is establishing a legal and ethical precedent that will govern the behavior of future generations. The narrative, in this tradition, is a jurisprudential act -- a form of legislation by example rather than by statute. The student who masters the Judaean narrative exercise has therefore mastered not merely a compositional skill but a mode of legal reasoning.

---

### II.C. Chreia (Chreia / Ma'amar Hakham)

#### Definition

Of all the preliminary exercises, the chreia occupies a position of singular importance in both the Greco-Roman and Judaean traditions. Theon defines it thus:

> "A chreia is a concise statement or action which is attributed with aptness to some specified character or to something analogous to a character." (Theon, *Progymnasmata* 3)[^51]

Hermogenes offers a complementary definition:

> "A chreia is a concise recollection aptly attributed to some character." (Hermogenes, *Progymnasmata* 3)

And Aphthonius:

> "A chreia is a concise reminiscence aptly attributed to some specified character." (Aphthonius, *Progymnasmata* 3)

Three features are essential to the chreia: (1) **conciseness** -- it is brief, not an extended narrative; (2) **attribution** -- it is ascribed to a specific, named person; and (3) **aptness** -- the statement or action is fitting to the character to whom it is attributed, revealing something essential about that person's wisdom, character, or philosophical stance.

These three features distinguish the chreia from related forms. A concise statement that is *not* attributed to a specific person is a *maxim* (*gnome*), not a chreia. An attributed statement that is *not* concise is a *reminiscence* (*apomnemoneumata*), not a chreia. And a concise, attributed statement that is *not* apt -- that is, that bears no particular fitness to its attributed speaker -- fails the fundamental test of the form. The chreia is, in this sense, a highly constrained literary genre: it must satisfy three conditions simultaneously, and the failure of any one condition disqualifies it.

The Judaean tradition recognizes both of the related forms that Theon distinguishes from the chreia. The *gnome* (maxim) corresponds to the unattributed general teachings found in Mishnah Pe'ah 1:1 ("These are things that have no prescribed measure") and in the enumerative sections of Avot chapter 5 ("Ten things were created at twilight"). The *apomnemoneumata* (reminiscence) corresponds to the extended *ma'aseh* narratives in Avot de-Rabbi Natan and the baraitot -- narratives that are attributed to specific characters but that exceed the chreia's requirement of conciseness. The chreia proper occupies the middle ground between these two forms: more specific than the gnome (because attributed), more compressed than the reminiscence (because concise).

Of all the preliminary exercises, the chreia finds its richest exemplification in the literature of the Judaean sages. The collection known as Pirkei Avot -- "Chapters of the Fathers" -- is, in effect, the largest surviving anthology of chreiai from any ancient tradition: a systematic compilation of concise, attributed, apt statements by named sages, arranged in chains of pedagogical transmission. Nothing comparable survives from the Greek or Roman tradition. Diogenes Laertius's *Lives of the Eminent Philosophers* contains many chreiai, but they are scattered through biographical narratives rather than compiled into a concentrated anthology. The gnomological collections (*gnomologia*) of the Byzantine period gathered attributed sayings, but these are later compilations drawing on earlier sources. Pirkei Avot is a primary composition -- a work created within the living tradition of the sages themselves, as a tool for rhetorical and moral education.[^52]

We shall now classify the types of chreiai found in the Judaean literature, following the taxonomy established by Theon, and demonstrate that the Judaean corpus contains examples of every type, subtype, and form of expression that the classical handbooks describe.

#### Types of Chreiai

Theon's primary division is between **sayings-chreiai** (those which make their point in words) and **action-chreiai** (those which make their point through a deed). A third category, **mixed chreiai**, combines word and deed. Within each category, further subdivisions are recognized.

We shall proceed through each type systematically, providing the classical definition, Theon's Greek or Roman example, and multiple Judaean parallels. The reader will observe that the Judaean parallels are not merely occasional resemblances but systematic correspondences: every type and subtype that Theon identifies is represented in the Judaean corpus, often with a richness and variety that exceeds the Greek material.

A note on the organization that follows: Theon's taxonomy provides the framework; the Judaean sources provide the content. We have organized the examples under Theon's categories not because the Judaean sages thought in those categories but because the categories illuminate the Judaean material in ways that the tradition's own organizing principles -- the chain of transmission, the topical arrangement of the Mishnah -- do not. The Greek lens reveals patterns in the Judaean data that would otherwise remain invisible.

##### A. Sayings-Chreiai

> "Sayings-chreiai are those which make their point in words without action." (Theon)

The sayings-chreia is the most common type in all ancient traditions, for the simple reason that wise sayings are easier to preserve and transmit than descriptions of wise actions. A saying can be quoted verbatim; an action must be narrated, and narration introduces the possibilities of distortion, abbreviation, and embellishment. The Judaean tradition, with its extraordinary emphasis on precise verbal transmission, naturally excels in this category.

Theon subdivides sayings-chreiai into two species: **statements** (unprompted utterances) and **responses** (utterances prompted by a question, inquiry, remark, or circumstance). The distinction is important because it affects the rhetorical character of the chreia: the unprompted statement reveals the sage's spontaneous values and concerns (what he chooses to say when no one asks), while the response reveals his dialectical skill and adaptability (how he handles the unexpected challenge of another's question or remark).

###### A.1. Statement Species -- Unprompted Statement

> *Definition:* Statements made without being prompted by a question or circumstance. The speaker simply declares something.
>
> *Theon's example:* Isocrates used to say that gifted students are children of the gods.

This is the dominant chreia type in Judaean literature. The standard Avot formula -- "Rabbi X says" (*Rabbi ploni omer*) or "He used to say" (*hu hayah omer*) -- introduces an unprompted declaration attributed to a named sage. The formula "he used to say" (*hu hayah omer*) is particularly close to the Greek chreia opening *ephe* ("he used to say").

Representative examples:

> <span dir="rtl">שמעון הצדיק היה משירי כנסת הגדולה. הוא היה אומר: על שלושה דברים העולם עומד — על התורה ועל העבודה ועל גמילות חסדים</span>
>
> *Simeon the Righteous was among the last of the Great Assembly. He used to say: On three things the world stands -- on Torah, on worship, and on deeds of lovingkindness.* (Avot 1:2)

> <span dir="rtl">הלל אומר: הוי מתלמידיו של אהרן, אוהב שלום ורודף שלום, אוהב את הבריות ומקרבן לתורה</span>
>
> *Hillel says: Be of the disciples of Aaron -- loving peace and pursuing peace, loving people and drawing them near to Torah.* (Avot 1:12)

> <span dir="rtl">הוא היה אומר: אם אין אני לי, מי לי? וכשאני לעצמי, מה אני? ואם לא עכשיו, אימתי?</span>
>
> *He used to say: If I am not for myself, who is for me? And when I am only for myself, what am I? And if not now, when?* (Avot 1:14)

> <span dir="rtl">שמאי אומר: עשה תורתך קבע, אמור מעט ועשה הרבה, והוי מקבל את כל האדם בסבר פנים יפות</span>
>
> *Shammai says: Make your Torah study fixed, say little and do much, and receive every person with a cheerful countenance.* (Avot 1:15)

> <span dir="rtl">רבי טרפון אומר: היום קצר, והמלאכה מרובה, והפועלים עצלים, והשכר הרבה, ובעל הבית דוחק. הוא היה אומר: לא עליך המלאכה לגמור, ולא אתה בן חורין ליבטל ממנה</span>
>
> *Rabbi Tarfon says: The day is short, the work is great, the workers are lazy, the reward is great, and the Master of the house is pressing. He used to say: It is not your duty to complete the work, but neither are you free to desist from it.* (Avot 2:15-16)

> <span dir="rtl">רבי עקיבא אומר: הכל צפוי, והרשות נתונה, ובטוב העולם נידון, והכל לפי רוב המעשה</span>
>
> *Rabbi Akiva says: Everything is foreseen, yet free will is given. The world is judged with goodness, and everything is according to the preponderance of deeds.* (Avot 3:17)

> <span dir="rtl">בן בג בג אומר: הפוך בה והפוך בה דכולא בה</span>
>
> *Ben Bag Bag says: Turn it over and turn it over, for everything is in it.* (Avot 5:22)

> <span dir="rtl">בן הא הא אומר: לפום צערא אגרא</span>
>
> *Ben He He says: According to the effort is the reward.* (Avot 5:23)

Several additional examples merit attention for their rhetorical qualities:

> <span dir="rtl">אנטיגנוס איש סוכו קיבל משמעון הצדיק. הוא היה אומר: אל תהיו כעבדים המשמשין את הרב על מנת לקבל פרס, אלא הוו כעבדים המשמשין את הרב שלא על מנת לקבל פרס</span>
>
> *Antigonos of Sokho received from Simeon the Righteous. He used to say: Be not like servants who serve the master in order to receive a reward, but be like servants who serve the master not in order to receive a reward.* (Avot 1:3)

The antithetical structure -- "not like X but like Y" -- is a figure of speech (*antithesis*) deployed within a chreia. The compressed parallel clauses, differing by a single word (*al menat* / *she-lo al menat*), create a rhetorical effect of absolute clarity: the distinction between instrumental and intrinsic service is made unforgettable through its formal symmetry.

> <span dir="rtl">רבן יוחנן בן זכאי קיבל מהלל ומשמאי. הוא היה אומר: אם למדת תורה הרבה אל תחזיק טובה לעצמך, כי לכך נוצרת</span>
>
> *Rabban Yohanan ben Zakkai received from Hillel and Shammai. He used to say: If you have learned much Torah, do not claim credit for yourself, for that is why you were created.* (Avot 2:8)

The chreia combines maxim with explanatory rationale: the imperative ("do not claim credit") is immediately followed by its justification ("for that is why you were created"). The effect is to foreclose the natural human tendency toward self-congratulation by reframing achievement as obligation fulfilled rather than merit earned.

> <span dir="rtl">רבי אליעזר אומר: הוי שקוד ללמוד תורה, ודע מה שתשיב לאפיקורוס</span>
>
> *Rabbi Eliezer says: Be diligent in the study of Torah, and know what to answer a heretic.* (Avot 2:14)

This chreia links two imperatives -- study and apologetics -- in a way that implies a rhetorical theory: the purpose of learning is not only personal enrichment but public defense of the tradition. The word *apiqoros* (from Epicurus) is itself evidence of Greco-Roman cultural contact, and the injunction to "know what to answer" presupposes the existence of philosophical challenges that require rhetorical responses.

> <span dir="rtl">עקביא בן מהללאל אומר: הסתכל בשלושה דברים ואין אתה בא לידי עבירה. דע מאין באת, ולאן אתה הולך, ולפני מי אתה עתיד ליתן דין וחשבון</span>
>
> *Akavya ben Mahalalel says: Consider three things and you will not come to sin. Know from where you came, where you are going, and before whom you will give account.* (Avot 3:1)

The triadic structure -- origin, destination, accountability -- achieves a compression of existential philosophy into three clauses that echoes the philosophical triad of Stoic ethics (nature, purpose, judgment). Whether the parallel is deliberate or convergent, the rhetorical effect is the same: three questions that encompass the entirety of the human condition.

These examples could be multiplied indefinitely. The first four chapters of Pirkei Avot alone contain more than sixty unprompted-statement chreiai -- a density of attributed sayings unmatched in any surviving Greek or Latin collection. The formulaic consistency of the attributions ("Rabbi X says" / "He used to say"), the conciseness of the statements, and the aptness of each saying to its attributed speaker make Pirkei Avot a chreia anthology of unparalleled scope and systematic organization.

###### A.2. Statement Species -- Circumstantial Statement

> *Definition:* Statements arising from a specific circumstance. The speaker reacts to something observed or experienced.
>
> *Theon's example:* Diogenes, on seeing a rich uneducated youth, said: "This fellow is silver-plated filth."

Circumstantial chreiai typically begin with "on seeing" (*idon*) or "when he saw" (*kasheRA'AH*). In the Judaean literature, the circumstantial formula often appears as "he saw... and said" or within a narrative frame introduced by *ma'aseh be-...*

> <span dir="rtl">אף הוא ראה גולגולת אחת שצפה על פני המים. אמר לה: על דאטפת אטפוך, וסוף מטיפייך יטופון</span>
>
> *He [Hillel] also saw a skull floating on the water. He said to it: Because you drowned others, they drowned you; and in the end, those who drowned you will themselves be drowned.* (Avot 2:7)[^53]

This is a classic circumstantial chreia: a sage sees something and makes a pointed remark. The formula -- seeing, followed by a gnomic utterance -- is identical to the pattern found in scores of Cynic chreiai attributed to Diogenes. Henry Fischel was the first to note this structural parallel systematically.[^54]

The rhetorician should note several features of Hillel's chreia that mark it as a work of exceptional craft. First, the *circumstance* -- a skull floating on water -- is vivid and memorable; it imprints itself on the imagination. Second, the *remark* is structured as a chain of cause and consequence expressed in Aramaic with a rhythmic pattern based on the repetition of the root *t-f* (float/drown): *al de-ateft atfukh, ve-sof metifayikh yitufun*. The alliterative wordplay is untranslatable but produces in the original a percussive, almost incantatory effect. Third, the *moral* operates on three levels: the immediate (this skull was a murderer who was himself murdered), the intermediate (retributive justice is real), and the cosmic (the cycle of violence is self-perpetuating -- "those who drowned you will themselves be drowned"). The three-level structure is compressed into a single sentence, achieving the Judaean ideal of maximal meaning in minimal expression.

> <span dir="rtl">כשבא רבי שמעון בן יוחי מן המערה, ראה אדם אחד חורש וזורע. אמר: מניחין חיי עולם ועוסקין בחיי שעה?</span>
>
> *When Rabbi Shimon bar Yohai came out from the cave, he saw a man plowing and sowing. He said: They abandon eternal life and busy themselves with temporal life?* (Sifrei Deuteronomy 305)

> <span dir="rtl">פעם אחת היה רבן יוחנן בן זכאי יוצא מירושלים, והיה רבי יהושע הולך אחריו, וראה בית המקדש חרב. אמר רבי יהושע: אוי לנו על זה שהוא חרב\.\.\. אמר לו: בני, אל יירע לך, יש לנו כפרה אחת שהיא כמותה, ואיזו זו — גמילות חסדים</span>
>
> *Once Rabban Yohanan ben Zakkai was going out of Jerusalem, and Rabbi Yehoshua was walking behind him, and he saw the Temple in ruins. Rabbi Yehoshua said: Woe to us\.\.\. He said to him: My son, do not be distressed. We have another atonement equal to it -- deeds of lovingkindness.* (Avot de-Rabbi Natan A, ch. 6)

###### B.1. Response Species -- Response to a Simple Question

> *Definition:* A response to a question requiring only agreement or disagreement, but often with a brief elaboration.
>
> *Theon's example:* Pittacus, asked if anyone escapes the gods' notice in sinning, said: "No, not even in contemplating it."

> <span dir="rtl">שוב מעשה בנכרי אחד שבא לפני שמאי. אמר לו: כמה תורות יש לכם? אמר לו: שתיים, תורה שבכתב ותורה שבעל פה</span>
>
> *Again, a gentile came before Shammai. He said to him: How many Torahs do you have? He said: Two -- the Written Torah and the Oral Torah.* (Babylonian Talmud, Shabbat 31a)

The question admits of a simple answer ("Two"), and Shammai provides it. But the chreia does not end there: the questioner declares that he accepts only one of the two, and the consequence of this partial acceptance triggers a chain of events (Shammai's rejection, Hillel's acceptance, the clever alphabet demonstration) that transforms a simple question into a complex pedagogical narrative. The simple question, in the Judaean tradition, is often the seed from which a more elaborate chreia grows.

A further example of the simple-question chreia from halakhic literature:

> <span dir="rtl">שאלו את רבי אליעזר: מי שעלתה לו בהרת כגריס ולא נראתה, מהו? אמר להם: לא יסגיר</span>
>
> *They asked Rabbi Eliezer: If a leprous mark the size of a bean arose on someone but was not seen, what is the law? He said to them: He should not be quarantined.* (Mishnah Negaim 12:5)

The halakhic question requires a simple ruling (quarantine or not), and the sage provides a direct answer. The chreia's virtue lies in its conciseness: the question is specific, the answer is definitive, and no elaboration is needed. This economy of response is itself a rhetorical achievement -- the sage who can answer complex legal questions in a single clause demonstrates mastery of his material.

###### B.2. Response Species -- Response to an Inquiry

> *Definition:* A response to a question that requires a longer answer than simple agreement or disagreement.
>
> *Theon's example:* Theano, asked how long after intercourse with a man does a woman become pure, said: "With one's own husband, immediately -- with another's, never."

This subtype is richly represented in the Judaean corpus. The most celebrated example is the fourfold inquiry attributed to Ben Zoma:

> <span dir="rtl">בן זומא אומר: איזהו חכם? הלומד מכל אדם, שנאמר: מכל מלמדי השכלתי. איזהו גיבור? הכובש את יצרו, שנאמר: טוב ארך אפים מגיבור. איזהו עשיר? השמח בחלקו, שנאמר: יגיע כפיך כי תאכל, אשריך וטוב לך. איזהו מכובד? המכבד את הבריות, שנאמר: כי מכבדי אכבד</span>
>
> *Ben Zoma says: Who is wise? One who learns from every person, as it is said: "From all my teachers I have gained understanding." Who is mighty? One who conquers his inclination, as it is said: "Better is the patient one than the mighty." Who is rich? One who is happy with his portion, as it is said: "When you eat of the labor of your hands, happy are you." Who is honored? One who honors other people, as it is said: "For those who honor Me I will honor."* (Avot 4:1)[^55]

Each of the four inquiries follows the same rhetorical pattern: a conventional term is proposed (wise, mighty, rich, honored), then redefined in counter-intuitive fashion, and the redefinition is confirmed by a scriptural proof-text. The chreia thus combines inquiry-response form with maxim, example, and redefinition -- a layering of rhetorical forms within a single unit that demonstrates extraordinary compositional sophistication.

Another paradigmatic inquiry chreia:

> <span dir="rtl">אמר להם: צאו וראו איזוהי דרך ישרה שידבק בה האדם. רבי אליעזר אומר: עין טובה. רבי יהושע אומר: חבר טוב. רבי יוסי אומר: שכן טוב. רבי שמעון אומר: הרואה את הנולד. רבי אלעזר אומר: לב טוב. אמר להם: רואה אני את דברי אלעזר בן ערך מדבריכם, שבכלל דבריו דבריכם</span>
>
> *He [Rabban Yohanan ben Zakkai] said to them: Go out and see what is the right path to which a person should cling. Rabbi Eliezer says: A good eye. Rabbi Yehoshua says: A good companion. Rabbi Yose says: A good neighbor. Rabbi Shimon says: One who foresees consequences. Rabbi Elazar says: A good heart. He said to them: I prefer the words of Elazar ben Arakh, for his words include all of yours.* (Avot 2:9)[^56]

This passage is remarkable from a rhetorical standpoint. The master poses an inquiry; five students offer competing responses; the master renders a judgment that simultaneously acknowledges the merits of all five answers while identifying one as superior. The chreia thus contains within it a miniature deliberative exercise: five arguments are weighed, and the best is selected with a reasoned justification (the argument of inclusion: Rabbi Elazar's answer subsumes the others).

A further inquiry chreia of great importance:

> <span dir="rtl">שאלו את רבי יהושע: מפני מה נתנה תורה במדבר? אמר להם: מה מדבר הפקר לכל, אף תורה הפקר לכל</span>
>
> *They asked Rabbi Yehoshua: Why was the Torah given in the wilderness? He said to them: Just as the wilderness is ownerless for all, so too the Torah is ownerless for all.* (Mekhilta de-Rabbi Ishmael, Bahodesh 8)

The inquiry concerns a theological question; the response operates through analogy. The wilderness and the Torah are linked by the property of universal accessibility -- both are *hefqer* (ownerless, available to all without restriction). The analogy is witty in that it exploits a circumstantial detail of the biblical narrative (the location of the revelation) and transforms it into a philosophical principle (the democratic nature of Torah). This is a form of argument from the *topos* of circumstance that any rhetorician would admire.

The most celebrated inquiry chreia in the entire Judaean corpus:

> <span dir="rtl">שוב מעשה בנכרי אחד שבא לפני הלל. אמר לו: גיירני על מנת שתלמדני כל התורה כולה כשאני עומד על רגל אחת. אמר לו: דעלך סני לחברך לא תעביד — זו היא כל התורה כולה, ואידך פירושה הוא, זיל גמור</span>
>
> *A gentile came before Hillel. He said: Convert me on the condition that you teach me the entire Torah while I stand on one foot. He said to him: That which is hateful to you, do not do to your neighbor -- this is the entire Torah, and the rest is commentary. Go and learn.* (Babylonian Talmud, Shabbat 31a)[^57]

The inquiry is extreme -- compress all of Torah into one sentence, deliverable in the time a man can balance on one foot. The response is masterly: a single ethical principle is offered as the essence, with all else designated as "commentary." The final imperative -- "Go and learn" -- adds a rhetorical dimension of incompleteness: the answer is sufficient as a summary but insufficient as an education. The chreia thus opens rather than closes the dialogue of learning.

###### B.3. Response Species -- Explanatory Response

> *Definition:* A response which, apart from answering the question, also provides an explanation, advice, or rationale.

> <span dir="rtl">שאלו תלמידיו את רבן יוחנן בן זכאי: מפני מה עבד עברי נרצע באוזן מכל אבריו? אמר להם: אוזן ששמעה על הר סיני "לא תגנוב" והלך וגנב — תירצע</span>
>
> *His students asked Rabban Yohanan ben Zakkai: Why is the ear of the Hebrew slave pierced rather than any other limb? He said to them: The ear that heard at Mount Sinai "You shall not steal" and went and stole -- let it be pierced.* (Avot de-Rabbi Natan B, ch. 31)[^58]

The response does not merely answer (it is the ear) but explains why (the ear is the organ that heard the commandment that was violated). The explanatory chreia is the dominant form in the halakhic midrashim (Mekhilta, Sifra, Sifrei), where the question "Why?" or "From where do we derive?" (*menayin*) generates responses that combine the answer with its scriptural or logical justification.

A particularly striking example of the explanatory response:

> <span dir="rtl">דרש רבי שמלאי: שש מאות ושלוש עשרה מצוות נאמרו לו למשה\.\.\. בא דוד והעמידן על אחת עשרה\.\.\. בא ישעיהו והעמידן על שש\.\.\. בא מיכה והעמידן על שלוש\.\.\. בא חבקוק והעמידן על אחת: וצדיק באמונתו יחיה</span>
>
> *Rabbi Simlai expounded: 613 commandments were told to Moses\.\.\. David came and reduced them to eleven\.\.\. Isaiah came and reduced them to six\.\.\. Micah came and reduced them to three\.\.\. Habakkuk came and reduced them to one: "The righteous shall live by his faith."* (Babylonian Talmud, Makkot 23b-24a)

This is an extended explanatory chreia tracing the progressive reduction of principles to their essence. The explanation does not merely answer the question but narrates a history of explanatory condensation -- each successive prophet distilling the previous formulation into fewer and more essential principles. The chreia is itself an exercise in condensation performed on the history of condensation: a vertiginous self-referential structure that demonstrates the explanatory form at its most virtuosic.

###### B.4. Response Species -- Response to a Remark

> *Definition:* Chreiai based on a remark rather than a question -- the sage responds to something said, not asked.

> <span dir="rtl">אמרו לו: עקביא, חזור בך בארבעה דברים שהיית אומר ונעשך אב בית דין לישראל. אמר להם: מוטב לי להיקרא שוטה כל ימי ולא להיעשות שעה אחת רשע לפני המקום</span>
>
> *They said to him: Akavya, retract the four things you used to say and we will make you head of the court of Israel. He said to them: Better for me to be called a fool all my days than to become wicked even for one hour before God.* (Mishnah Eduyot 5:6-7)[^59]

The colleagues' offer is not a question but a proposition -- a remarkable offer of power in exchange for intellectual compromise. Akavya's reply constitutes the chreia: a definitive statement of intellectual integrity that transforms a political negotiation into a moral declaration.

> <span dir="rtl">כשחלה רבי אליעזר, נכנסו תלמידיו לבקרו. אמרו לו: רבנו, למדנו אורחות חיים. אמר להם: הזהרו בכבוד חבריכם, ומנעו בניכם מן ההגיון, והושיבום בין ברכי תלמידי חכמים</span>
>
> *When Rabbi Eliezer fell ill, his students entered to visit him. They said to him: Our master, teach us the paths of life. He said to them: Be careful of your colleagues' honor, keep your children from superficial reasoning, and seat them among the knees of Torah scholars.* (Babylonian Talmud, Berakhot 28b)

The students' remark -- "teach us" -- is not a question but a petition. The deathbed setting lends the sage's response the weight of a testamentary utterance, and the content -- three precepts of intellectual life -- functions as a compressed curriculum delivered at the boundary between life and death.

##### C. Action-Chreiai

> "Action-chreiai are those which reveal some thought without speech." (Theon)

The action-chreia occupies a distinctive position in the chreia taxonomy. Where the sayings-chreia operates through language, the action-chreia operates through deed. The sage's action is itself the statement; words are either absent entirely or subordinate to the physical gesture. This form is relatively rare in the gnomological collections of the Greek tradition (which are primarily anthologies of sayings) but is well represented in the Judaean tradition, where the *ma'aseh* (precedent narrative) frequently records what a sage *did* rather than what he *said*.

The rhetorician should note that the action-chreia places a special demand on the narrator: since the meaning of an action is not self-evident in the way that the meaning of a statement is (or appears to be), the narrator must describe the action with sufficient precision and context to make its point clear. An action without context is merely a deed; an action narrated with appropriate context and attribution becomes a chreia -- a concise communication of meaning through performance.

###### C.1. Active Action-Chreiai

> *Definition:* "The active are those which show some aggressive act."
>
> *Theon's example:* Diogenes, on seeing a gourmand boy, struck the paedagogus with his staff.

> <span dir="rtl">גער בו ודחפו באמת הבניין שבידו</span>
>
> *He [Shammai] rebuked him and drove him out with the builder's cubit in his hand.* (Babylonian Talmud, Shabbat 31a)[^60]

Shammai's aggressive physical response -- driving away a prospective convert with a measuring stick -- is an action-chreia par excellence. The action itself constitutes the statement: proper reverence for Torah cannot be reduced to a one-sentence formula. No words are needed; the builder's cubit speaks. The rhetorical theorist will note that the builder's cubit (*ammat ha-binyan*) is not merely a convenient weapon but a symbol of Shammai's profession and, by extension, of his approach to Torah: he is a builder, a man who works with precise measurements, and his response to the convert's flippancy is the response of one who knows that Torah, like a building, cannot be constructed hastily or casually. The tool conveys the teaching.

A further active action-chreia of great power:

> <span dir="rtl">מעשה ברבן גמליאל ובזקנים שהיו מסובין בבית המשתה ביריחו\.\.\. עמד רבן גמליאל ושימש</span>
>
> *A story of Rabban Gamaliel and the elders who were reclining at a banquet in Jericho\.\.\. Rabban Gamaliel stood and served [the food].* (Mishnah Sukkah 2:7)

The action of a sage of the highest rank personally serving food to his colleagues is an aggressive act of humility -- aggressive because it reverses the expected social hierarchy and thereby makes a point about the nature of leadership. The teaching ("leadership is service") is made entirely through the action; no verbal formulation could be as effective.

> <span dir="rtl">מעשה בהלל הזקן שעלה לגג בית המדרש ונשתטח שם כדי לשמוע דברי תורה</span>
>
> *A story of Hillel the Elder who climbed onto the roof of the study house and lay flat there in order to hear words of Torah.* (Avot de-Rabbi Natan A, ch. 6)

The complete narrative elaborates: it was the eve of Sabbath, in the depth of winter, and snow fell upon him as he lay listening through the skylight. At dawn, the masters within noticed that the room was dark and, looking up, found the form of a man covered in snow on the skylight. They brought him down, washed him, anointed him, and seated him before the fire. "This one," they said, "is worthy of having the Sabbath profaned on his behalf." Hillel's dramatic physical action -- climbing the roof and enduring the cold -- is an active action-chreia that demonstrates the value of Torah study more powerfully than any verbal declaration could. The narrative has become one of the foundational stories of the Judaean tradition, and its power derives entirely from the action: a young man too poor to pay the admission fee found another way in.

###### C.2. Passive Action-Chreiai

> *Definition:* "The passive are those pointing out something experienced."
>
> *Theon's example:* Didymon the flute-player, convicted of adultery, was hanged by his namesake.

> <span dir="rtl">שמשון הלך אחר עיניו, לפיכך ניקרו פלשתים את עיניו. אבשלום נתגאה בשערו, לפיכך נתלה בשערו</span>
>
> *Samson followed his eyes, therefore the Philistines gouged out his eyes. Absalom took pride in his hair, therefore he was hanged by his hair.* (Mishnah Sotah 1:7-9)[^61]

What was experienced by the character -- the specific form of punishment -- conveys the moral lesson of divine justice operating measure for measure (*middah ke-neged middah*). The characters are passive recipients of consequences that perfectly mirror their transgressions.

> <span dir="rtl">בשעה שהוציאו את רבי עקיבא להריגה, זמן קריאת שמע היה, והיו סורקין את בשרו במסרקות של ברזל, והיה מקבל עליו עול מלכות שמים</span>
>
> *At the time when they took Rabbi Akiva out to be executed, it was the time for reciting the Shema, and they were combing his flesh with iron combs, and he was accepting upon himself the yoke of the kingdom of Heaven.* (Babylonian Talmud, Berakhot 61b)[^62]

What Rabbi Akiva experienced -- torture and execution -- becomes the supreme demonstration of the Shema's meaning. The passive chreia here achieves an intensity that surpasses anything in the Greek and Roman chreia collections: the sage's body becomes the text on which the theological principle is inscribed.

A further passive action-chreia of great interest:

> <span dir="rtl">אמר רבי טרפון: אני הייתי בא בדרך, והטתי לקרות כדברי בית שמאי, וסיכנתי בעצמי מפני הליסטים. אמרו לו: כדאי היית לחוב בעצמך, שעברת על דברי בית הלל</span>
>
> *Rabbi Tarfon said: I was once traveling on the road and I reclined to recite [the Shema] as Beit Shammai say, and I endangered myself on account of robbers. They said to him: You deserved to bear guilt for violating the words of Beit Hillel.* (Mishnah Berakhot 1:3)

Rabbi Tarfon's personal experience of danger while following the minority ruling becomes itself the teaching: the passive experience (nearly being attacked by robbers) is interpreted by his colleagues not as bad luck but as a consequence of his wrong halakhic choice. The physical experience functions as a legal argument -- the world itself conspires to enforce the majority opinion. This is the passive action-chreia deployed as forensic evidence.

The passive action-chreia appears in the Judaean tradition with a frequency that may reflect the historical circumstances of the sages. Living under Roman occupation, subject to persecution, exile, and martyrdom, the Judaean sages experienced suffering as a constant condition. It is therefore not surprising that their chreia tradition includes a substantial body of passive chreiai -- narratives in which what the sage *undergoes* is as instructive as what he *says* or *does*. Suffering itself becomes a form of rhetorical speech.

##### D. Mixed Chreiai

> "Mixed chreiai are those which share characteristics of both the sayings-species and the action-species but make their point with the action." (Theon)

The mixed chreia is the most complex and rhetorically powerful form in the chreia taxonomy. It combines the verbal clarity of the sayings-chreia with the dramatic immediacy of the action-chreia, creating a composite form in which word and deed reinforce each other. The critical distinction, as Theon notes, is that in the mixed chreia the *action* makes the primary point, while the words serve a supporting or explanatory function. The action is not merely described; it is performed, and the performance carries a meaning that the words alone could not convey.

The Judaean tradition contains some of the most powerful mixed chreiai in all of ancient literature. The combination of dramatic action with theological speech -- a sage performing an act before God while simultaneously declaring his intention -- creates a form of unmatched rhetorical intensity.

> <span dir="rtl">עג עוגה ועמד בתוכה ואמר: ריבונו של עולם, בניך שמו פניהם עלי\.\.\. נשבע אני בשמך הגדול שאיני זז מכאן עד שתרחם על בניך</span>
>
> *He [Honi] drew a circle, stood in it, and said: Master of the Universe, Your children have turned to me\.\.\. I swear by Your great name that I will not move from here until You have mercy on Your children.* (Mishnah Ta'anit 3:8)[^63]

This is a classic mixed chreia: both action (drawing the circle, standing in it) and speech (the prayer), but the point is made primarily through the dramatic action of confronting the divine. The circle functions as a symbolic boundary -- a sacred space of rhetorical confrontation. The narrative continues: the rain first dripped too lightly, then fell too violently, and only upon Honi's third demand fell in the proper measure. Each exchange between Honi and God is a negotiation -- a deliberative speech act in which the speaker adjusts his request and the hearer adjusts his response. The mixed chreia thus contains within it a complete deliberative exchange, enacted not between human parties but between a human and the divine.

The response of Shimon ben Shetach to Honi's behavior is itself a remarkable rhetorical commentary:

> <span dir="rtl">אלמלא חוני אתה, גוזרני עליך נידוי. אבל מה אעשה לך, שאתה מתחטא לפני המקום ועושה לך רצונך, כבן שמתחטא על אביו ועושה לו רצונו</span>
>
> *Were you not Honi, I would decree excommunication upon you. But what can I do with you? You act petulantly before God and He does your will, like a child who acts petulantly before his father and the father does his will.*

Shimon ben Shetach's response acknowledges the tension between Honi's rhetorical boldness and institutional propriety. The simile of the petulant child is itself a chreia within a chreia -- a figurative remark that characterizes Honi's relationship with God while simultaneously expressing the communal ambivalence about a rhetoric so powerful that it crosses the boundary between petition and demand.

The ultimate mixed chreia in the Judaean corpus:

> <span dir="rtl">אמרו לו תלמידיו: רבנו, עד כאן? אמר להם: כל ימי הייתי מצטער על פסוק זה: בכל נפשך — אפילו הוא נוטל את נשמתך\.\.\. היה מאריך באחד עד שיצתה נשמתו באחד</span>
>
> *His students said: Our master, even to this extent? He said to them: All my life I was troubled by this verse: "with all your soul" -- even if He takes your soul\.\.\. He prolonged the word "One" until his soul departed on "One."* (Babylonian Talmud, Berakhot 61b)

The ultimate mixed chreia: the dying words and the act of dying while reciting the Shema combine to make the theological point. Word and deed become indistinguishable at the moment of death -- the highest possible fusion of the sayings-species and the action-species.

##### E. Double Chreiai

> *Definition:* A chreia with statements of two characters, either one of which creates a chreia of one character. The double chreia is formed when two characters make statements or perform actions in interaction, and either statement could stand alone as an independent chreia. The form captures the moment of intellectual encounter between two minds, preserving the friction of disagreement or the complementarity of parallel insight.

In the Greek tradition, double chreiai typically involve a witty exchange between two philosophers -- Alexander and Diogenes, for example. In the Judaean tradition, the double chreia is far more than an occasion for wit: it is the fundamental form of legal discourse.

The double chreia corresponds precisely to the Judaean *machloqet* (dispute), in which two sages take opposing positions on a question, and each position independently constitutes a chreia.

> <span dir="rtl">בית שמאי אומרים: בערב כל אדם יטו ויקראו, ובבוקר יעמדו\.\.\. ובית הלל אומרים: כל אדם קורין כדרכן</span>
>
> *Beit Shammai say: In the evening everyone should recline and recite [the Shema], and in the morning they should stand\.\.\. Beit Hillel say: Everyone recites in their own way.* (Mishnah Berakhot 1:3)[^64]
