Two schools, opposing positions, each with scriptural proof. Each position is independently a chreia; together they form a double chreia that the Judaean tradition values as the paradigmatic form of productive intellectual disagreement.

> <span dir="rtl">רבי ישמעאל אומר: דברה תורה כלשון בני אדם. רבי עקיבא אומר: ריבויין ומיעוטין</span>
>
> *Rabbi Ishmael says: The Torah speaks in human language. Rabbi Akiva says: [Every word teaches something through] inclusions and exclusions.* (Sifrei Numbers 115)[^65]

A fundamental double chreia on hermeneutical method: Rabbi Ishmael holds that the Torah's language follows ordinary conventions and should not be pressed for significance in every verbal detail; Rabbi Akiva holds that every word, indeed every letter, carries interpretive weight. The entire subsequent history of rabbinic textual interpretation may be understood as an extended commentary on this double chreia.

The double chreia is structurally central to the Judaean rhetorical tradition in a way that it is not in the Greek tradition. While Theon treats the double chreia as one type among several, the Judaean sages elevated the paired disagreement -- the *machloqet* -- into the fundamental unit of legal discourse. The vast majority of the Mishnah's legal content is presented in the form of paired opinions: "Rabbi X says... Rabbi Y says..." or "Beit Shammai say... Beit Hillel say..." The double chreia is not an exception but the norm -- and this normalization of intellectual disagreement as a productive mode of discourse is one of the Judaean tradition's most distinctive contributions to rhetorical theory.

The Judaean tradition's own reflection on the double chreia form deserves notice:

> <span dir="rtl">כל מחלוקת שהיא לשם שמים, סופה להתקיים. ושאינה לשם שמים, אין סופה להתקיים. איזו היא מחלוקת שהיא לשם שמים? זו מחלוקת הלל ושמאי. ושאינה לשם שמים? זו מחלוקת קרח וכל עדתו</span>
>
> *Every dispute that is for the sake of Heaven will in the end endure. And one that is not for the sake of Heaven will not in the end endure. Which is a dispute for the sake of Heaven? That of Hillel and Shammai. And which is not? That of Korah and his community.* (Avot 5:17)

This passage is a meta-reflection on the double-chreia form itself. It distinguishes productive from destructive disputation and identifies the Hillel-Shammai disagreements as the paradigm of legitimate intellectual contest. The double chreia "endures" -- both positions are preserved, both are studied, both contribute to the ongoing dialectic of the tradition -- precisely because both participants aim at truth rather than at victory. This is a Judaean theory of what Aristotle calls *eristic* (contentious) versus *dialectical* (truth-seeking) argumentation, and it elevates the double chreia from a literary form to a communal institution: the school that preserves opposing views is wiser than the school that suppresses them.

A further double chreia of exceptional importance:

> <span dir="rtl">אמר רבי אליעזר בן עזריה: מעבירות שבין אדם למקום, יום הכיפורים מכפר. מעבירות שבין אדם לחברו, אין יום הכיפורים מכפר עד שירצה את חברו. אמר רבי עקיבא: אשריכם ישראל, לפני מי אתם מיטהרין ומי מטהר אתכם — אביכם שבשמים</span>
>
> *Rabbi Eliezer ben Azariah said: For transgressions between a person and God, Yom Kippur atones. For transgressions between a person and his fellow, Yom Kippur does not atone until he appeases his fellow. Rabbi Akiva said: Happy are you, Israel! Before whom are you purified, and who purifies you? Your Father in Heaven!* (Mishnah Yoma 8:9)

Two complementary theological statements, each independently a chreia, each addressing the same theme (atonement) from a different angle. Rabbi Eliezer ben Azariah emphasizes the horizontal dimension -- human obligations to one another; Rabbi Akiva emphasizes the vertical dimension -- the direct relationship between Israel and God. Together, the double chreia articulates a complete theology of repentance.

#### Forms of Expression

Theon identifies twelve ways in which chreiai may be expressed. These forms of expression describe not the *type* of chreia (sayings, action, mixed) but the *manner* in which the chreia delivers its content -- the rhetorical strategy by which the point is made. A single chreia may employ one form of expression, or it may combine multiple forms (see form 12, "combined form," below).

We find all twelve forms of expression represented in the Judaean corpus. In several cases, the Judaean examples are more striking and more sophisticated than the Greek and Roman examples that Theon provides.

**1. In the manner of a maxim:** The chreia takes the form of a general moral statement -- a proposition of universal scope attributed to a specific sage. The maxim-chreia is distinguished from the pure maxim (*gnome*) by its attribution; it is distinguished from the explanatory chreia (form 2) by the absence of a stated rationale. The maxim stands alone, self-evident, requiring no justification beyond its own compressed authority.

> <span dir="rtl">במקום שאין אנשים, השתדל להיות איש</span>
>
> *In a place where there are no men, strive to be a man.* (Hillel, Avot 2:6)

**2. In the manner of an explanation:** The chreia includes a statement together with its rationale. The explanatory form adds a *because* clause (*she-*, *ki-*, *shema-*) that provides the grounds for the imperative or assertion. This form is more persuasive than the bare maxim because it invites the listener to evaluate the reasoning, not merely to accept the conclusion.

> <span dir="rtl">הוי זהיר במצוה קלה כבחמורה, שאין אתה יודע מתן שכרן של מצוות</span>
>
> *Be as careful with a minor commandment as with a major one, for you do not know the reward of commandments.* (Rabbi Yehudah ha-Nasi, Avot 2:1)

The rationale -- "for you do not know the reward" -- transforms the imperative from an arbitrary command into a reasoned policy: since the relative value of commandments is unknown, the rational strategy is to treat all of them with equal care. The explanatory chreia thus appeals to prudential reasoning rather than to authority alone.

**3. With wit:** The chreia makes its point through a clever or witty remark. Wit in the chreia tradition is not mere humor but intellectual brilliance -- the capacity to see a connection, exploit an ambiguity, or reverse an expectation in a way that simultaneously entertains and instructs. The witty chreia is memorable precisely because the wit is inseparable from the point: the cleverness of the formulation *is* the argument.

The Judaean tradition, sometimes perceived by outsiders as austere, contains numerous examples of dazzling rhetorical wit:

> <span dir="rtl">לא בשמים היא!</span>
>
> *"It is not in heaven!"* (Rabbi Yehoshua, Babylonian Talmud, Bava Metzia 59b)[^66]

Rabbi Yehoshua's deployment of Deuteronomy 30:12 to refute a heavenly voice that had intervened in a legal dispute is one of the most celebrated witticisms in all of rabbinic literature -- a scriptural quotation weaponized with devastating rhetorical precision.

**4. With a syllogism:** The chreia employs a logical syllogism.

> <span dir="rtl">כל שיראת חטאו קודמת לחכמתו, חכמתו מתקיימת. וכל שחכמתו קודמת ליראת חטאו, אין חכמתו מתקיימת</span>
>
> *One whose fear of sin precedes his wisdom -- his wisdom endures. One whose wisdom precedes his fear of sin -- his wisdom does not endure.* (Rabbi Hanina ben Dosa, Avot 3:9)

If A precedes B, then C. If B precedes A, then not-C. A perfect conditional syllogism. The same structure recurs with a different pair of variables:

> <span dir="rtl">כל שמעשיו מרובין מחכמתו, חכמתו מתקיימת. וכל שחכמתו מרובה ממעשיו, אין חכמתו מתקיימת</span>
>
> *One whose deeds exceed his wisdom -- his wisdom endures. One whose wisdom exceeds his deeds -- his wisdom does not endure.* (Avot 3:10)

The systematically varied syllogism -- the same logical structure applied to a different pair of terms (deeds/wisdom instead of fear of sin/wisdom) -- constitutes what we might call a *syllogistic series*: a chain of formally identical arguments whose cumulative force exceeds that of any single instance.

The fourfold permutation schemes of Avot chapter 5 bring the syllogistic form to its logical extreme:

> <span dir="rtl">ארבע מדות בנותני צדקה: הרוצה שיתן ולא יתנו אחרים — עינו רעה בשל אחרים. יתנו אחרים והוא לא יתן — עינו רעה בשלו. יתן ויתנו אחרים — חסיד. לא יתן ולא יתנו אחרים — רשע</span>
>
> *Four types of charity givers: One who wants to give but not have others give -- grudging toward others. One who wants others to give but not himself -- grudging toward himself. Give and have others give -- pious. Neither give nor have others give -- wicked.* (Avot 5:13)

This is a complete two-by-two logical matrix -- a *divisio* that exhausts all possible combinations of two binary variables (self-giving/not, others-giving/not) and assigns a moral evaluation to each outcome. The rhetorician trained in the Aristotelian tradition will recognize this as an application of the method of division (*diairesis*), but the Judaean sages deploy it with a concision and moral clarity that gives it extraordinary pedagogical force.

**5. With an enthymeme:** The chreia employs an incomplete syllogism where the listener must supply the omitted premise. Aristotle identifies the enthymeme as "the body of persuasion" (*Rhetoric* 1.1.1354a) -- the most powerful form of rhetorical reasoning precisely because it enlists the audience as collaborators in the argument. The listener who supplies the missing premise has, in effect, persuaded himself; the argument becomes his own rather than the speaker's.

> <span dir="rtl">אם אין אני לי מי לי?</span>
>
> *If I am not for myself, who is for me?* (Hillel, Avot 1:14)

The listener must supply the unspoken conclusion: "No one else will be." But the enthymeme does not stop there: the second clause -- "And when I am only for myself, what am I?" -- introduces a counter-enthymeme whose unspoken conclusion is "Nothing" or "Worthless." The two enthymemes stand in dialectical tension: self-regard is necessary (first clause) but insufficient (second clause). The third clause -- "And if not now, when?" -- adds temporal urgency to the moral dialectic. The triple enthymeme thus creates three intellectual gaps that the listener fills in rapid succession, generating a complete ethical argument through participatory reasoning.

> <span dir="rtl">לא בשמים היא!</span>
>
> *"It is not in heaven!"* (Rabbi Yehoshua, Bava Metzia 59b)

The enthymeme is compressed into four Hebrew words. The unstated premise: if the Torah is not in heaven, then heavenly voices cannot overrule the human interpretive community. The unstated conclusion: therefore Rabbi Eliezer's position, though supported by a heavenly voice, is not binding. The audience must construct the entire syllogism from a four-word scriptural quotation -- a feat of compression that makes Hillel's triple enthymeme seem verbose by comparison.

The enthymeme creates an intellectual gap that the audience fills through active engagement -- a technique of participatory persuasion that is especially effective with sophisticated audiences. The Judaean sages, addressing audiences trained in textual reasoning, could rely on their listeners to supply the missing premises; the result is a rhetorical form of extraordinary density, in which more is said through silence than through speech.

**6. With an example:** The chreia illustrates its point by citing an example or precedent. The example-chreia is the dominant form in rabbinic literature, reflecting the tradition's fundamental commitment to grounding every proposition in authoritative evidence. The formula *she-ne'emar* ("as it is said") or *she-na'amar* ("as it was said") introduces the scriptural proof-text that serves as the example confirming the sage's assertion.

> <span dir="rtl">איזהו חכם? הלומד מכל אדם, שנאמר: מכל מלמדי השכלתי</span>
>
> *Who is wise? One who learns from every person, as it is said: "From all my teachers I have gained understanding."* (Ben Zoma, Avot 4:1)

Each of Ben Zoma's four definitions cites a scriptural example as confirmation. The rhetorical function of the scriptural citation is not merely decorative but evidential: it transforms the sage's personal opinion into a proposition grounded in canonical authority. The sage does not ask the listener to accept his definition on trust; he directs the listener to an independent source -- Scripture -- that corroborates his claim. The example thus functions as an external witness, lending the chreia a persuasive force that the sage's authority alone could not provide.

A further notable example-chreia:

> <span dir="rtl">כל מחלוקת שהיא לשם שמים, סופה להתקיים\.\.\. איזו היא מחלוקת שהיא לשם שמים? זו מחלוקת הלל ושמאי</span>
>
> *Every dispute for the sake of Heaven will in the end endure\.\.\. Which is a dispute for the sake of Heaven? That of Hillel and Shammai.* (Avot 5:17)

Here the historical example (Hillel and Shammai) serves as the definitive illustration of the abstract principle (dispute for the sake of Heaven). The counter-example (Korah's rebellion) serves the same function for the negative case. The double exemplification -- one positive, one negative -- creates a complete evidentiary structure that covers both possibilities.

**7. With a wish:** The chreia takes the form of a wish or imprecation. The wish-chreia is a distinctive form that combines the conciseness of the maxim with the emotional force of prayer. The speaker does not merely state a principle but invests it with the urgency of personal desire.

> <span dir="rtl">אמר להם: יהי רצון שתהא מורא שמים עליכם כמורא בשר ודם</span>
>
> *He said to them: May it be God's will that your fear of Heaven be like your fear of flesh and blood.* (Rabban Yohanan ben Zakkai, Avot 2:12)

His students, surprised, asked: "Only that much?" He replied:

> <span dir="rtl">ולוואי! תדעו: כשאדם עובר עבירה אומר: שלא יראני אדם</span>
>
> *Would that it were so! Know: when a person commits a sin, he says, "Let no one see me."*

The wish-chreia is here followed by an explanatory supplement that deepens the wish into a psychological insight: human beings are more deterred by the judgment of their fellow humans (who can be seen) than by the judgment of God (who cannot be seen). The wish -- "May your fear of Heaven equal your fear of people" -- is therefore not a modest aspiration but a radical one: it asks for a transformation of human psychology, a reorientation of the entire motivational structure of moral life. The wish-form allows the sage to express this aspiration without the assertive force of a command, preserving the humility appropriate to a deathbed utterance.

**8. In a symbolic manner:** The chreia makes its point through a symbolic gesture or speech-act. The symbolic chreia differs from the figurative chreia (form 9) in that the symbol is not merely a literary device but an enacted or performed representation -- a gesture, an object, or a spatial arrangement that carries meaning through its physical form.

> <span dir="rtl">העולם הזה דומה לפרוזדור בפני העולם הבא. התקן עצמך בפרוזדור כדי שתיכנס לטרקלין</span>
>
> *This world is like a vestibule before the world to come. Prepare yourself in the vestibule so that you may enter the banquet hall.* (Rabbi Jacob, Avot 3:18)

The spatial symbolism -- vestibule and banquet hall -- maps the temporal distinction between this world and the next onto a physical architecture that the listener can visualize. The imperative "prepare yourself in the vestibule" gains its force from the spatial metaphor: one does not enter a banquet hall unprepared; one dresses, washes, composes oneself in the antechamber. The symbolic space creates an experiential frame for the ethical teaching.

**9. In a figurative manner:** The chreia uses figurative or metaphorical language.

> <span dir="rtl">אל תסתכל בקנקן אלא במה שיש בו: יש קנקן חדש מלא ישן, וישן שאפילו חדש אין בו</span>
>
> *Do not look at the vessel but at what is in it: there may be a new vessel full of old [wine], and an old one that does not even have new [wine] in it.* (Rabbi Yehudah ha-Nasi, Avot 4:20)

The vessel is the person's exterior; the wine is the wisdom within. An exquisite compressed metaphor, developed with a further twist: the "new vessel full of old wine" represents the young scholar possessing ancient wisdom, while the "old vessel without even new wine" represents the aged person who has acquired nothing of value. The double reversal of expectations -- youth may contain age, age may contain emptiness -- makes the metaphor not merely illustrative but argumentative: it overturns the conventional assumption that appearance correlates with substance.

A further figurative chreia of remarkable beauty:

> <span dir="rtl">כל שחכמתו מרובה ממעשיו, למה הוא דומה? לאילן שענפיו מרובין ושרשיו מועטין, והרוח באה ועוקרתו. וכל שמעשיו מרובין מחכמתו, למה הוא דומה? לאילן שענפיו מועטין ושרשיו מרובין, שאפילו כל הרוחות שבעולם באות ונושבות בו אין מזיזין אותו ממקומו</span>
>
> *One whose wisdom exceeds his deeds, to what may he be compared? To a tree whose branches are many but whose roots are few -- the wind comes and uproots it. One whose deeds exceed his wisdom, to what may he be compared? To a tree whose branches are few but whose roots are many -- even if all the winds in the world come and blow against it, they cannot move it from its place.* (Rabbi Elazar ben Azariah, Avot 3:18)

The tree metaphor maps a moral principle (the relationship between knowledge and practice) onto a physical structure (the relationship between branches and roots) with precision. The metaphor is not decorative but explanatory: it makes visible the otherwise invisible principle that deeds (roots) provide stability while wisdom without action (branches without roots) creates vulnerability. The figure achieves what abstract argument cannot: it gives the listener an image to carry in his mind.

**10. With double entendre:** The chreia carries two possible meanings. The double entendre is a form of deliberate ambiguity in which the speaker intends both meanings simultaneously. Unlike obscurity (which is a fault), the double entendre enriches the chreia by allowing it to operate on two levels at once.

> <span dir="rtl">אם אני כאן, הכל כאן; ואם איני כאן, מי כאן?</span>
>
> *If I am here, everyone is here; and if I am not here, who is here?* (Hillel, Mishnah Sukkah 5:4)

"I" can refer to Hillel himself or to the divine Presence (*Shekhinah*). The Talmud itself notes the ambiguity. If Hillel is speaking of himself, the statement is an expression of confident self-assertion: my presence is the presence that matters. If Hillel is speaking in the voice of the *Shekhinah*, the statement is a theological declaration: God's presence is the condition of all meaningful presence. The double entendre allows both readings to coexist, and the tension between them -- between the personal and the divine, between human confidence and theological humility -- is itself the point. The sage who can speak in two voices at once demonstrates the highest form of rhetorical art: the ability to say more than one thing with the same words.

A further example of the double entendre:

> <span dir="rtl">ודאשתמש בתגא חלף</span>
>
> *One who makes use of the crown shall perish.* (Hillel, Avot 1:13)

*Taga* ("crown") can refer to the crown of Torah (using Torah for personal advantage) or to the crown of political authority (exploiting power for personal gain). The double entendre allows the maxim to apply with equal force to both the scholarly and political spheres -- a remarkable economy of expression that doubles the chreia's range of application without adding a single word.

**11. With a change of subject:** The speaker changes the subject and redirects the question to a different, often more fundamental, topic. The change of subject is a subtle rhetorical technique that operates by reframing: the speaker does not answer the question that was asked but instead reveals that the question itself was wrongly formulated. By redirecting the conversation to a different topic, the speaker implicitly argues that the original question was based on a false premise or an inadequate understanding of the issue at hand.

> <span dir="rtl">כשביקש רבי מאיר להתפלל על שכניו הרעים שימותו, אמרה לו ברוריא אשתו: \.\.\. "חטאים" כתיב! — התפלל עליהם שיעשו תשובה</span>
>
> *When Rabbi Meir sought to pray for his wicked neighbors to die, his wife Beruriah said to him: \.\.\. "Sins" is what is written! -- Pray for them that they should repent.* (Babylonian Talmud, Berakhot 10a)[^67]

Beruriah changes the subject from the wicked people to the sins themselves, redirecting the entire discussion from destruction to rehabilitation through a brilliant act of textual reinterpretation. The change of subject operates through a linguistic observation: the Hebrew text of Psalm 104:35 says *hata'im* ("sins"), not *hot'im* ("sinners"). By attending to the precise voweling of the text, Beruriah shifts the object of Rabbi Meir's prayer from the destruction of persons to the elimination of wrongdoing -- a move that is simultaneously philological (based on exact reading), ethical (redirecting violence toward redemption), and rhetorical (answering an argument by reframing the terms of the debate).

This chreia is notable also for its attribution: Beruriah is one of the very few women to whom the Talmud attributes independent intellectual contributions. That the tradition preserves her chreia -- and preserves it as a correction of her husband's position -- testifies to the Judaean recognition that rhetorical excellence is not determined by gender, rank, or social position. The chreia is judged by its aptness, not by the identity of its author.

**12. Combined form:** A chreia combining two or more forms of expression.

The most sophisticated chreiai in the Judaean tradition combine multiple forms of expression within a single unit, creating a layered rhetorical structure in which each form reinforces the others.

Ben Zoma's Four Questions (Avot 4:1) combine inquiry-response, maxim, scriptural example, and redefinition. Each of the four units operates simultaneously as (a) an inquiry chreia ("Who is X?"), (b) a response chreia ("One who does Y"), (c) a maxim (a general moral principle), (d) a citation of example ("as it is said..."), and (e) a redefinition (the conventional meaning of "wise," "mighty," "rich," and "honored" is overturned). Five forms of expression in four compressed sentences -- a density of rhetorical layering that has few parallels in any tradition.

Rabbi Tarfon's saying (Avot 2:15-16) combines figurative language (the day is short -- the day represents human life; the work is great -- Torah study and ethical action; the workers are lazy -- humanity's natural inclination toward ease; the reward is great -- divine recompense; the Master of the house is pressing -- God's urgency), explanation (the metaphor is followed by the rationale), and maxim ("it is not your duty to complete the work, but neither are you free to desist from it"). The final maxim resolves the tension created by the metaphor: the work is vast and the time is short, but the resolution is not despair but persistent effort.

Hillel and the Convert (Shabbat 31a) combines wit (accepting the absurd challenge of teaching Torah on one foot), maxim (the golden rule as the essence of Torah), and enthymeme (the unstated implication that oral tradition requires trust in a teacher -- the same trust the convert extended when he accepted the alphabet from Hillel). The three forms work together to create a chreia of extraordinary rhetorical power: the wit engages, the maxim instructs, and the enthymeme deepens.

#### Exercises with Chreiai

Theon describes eight exercises to be performed with chreiai, each training a different rhetorical competency. These exercises are not merely academic assignments; they are systematic methods for developing the student's ability to work with attributed sayings and actions -- to reproduce them accurately, to adapt them to new contexts, to support and challenge them, and to expand or condense them as the rhetorical situation demands.

All eight exercises are reflected in the practices of the Judaean schools.

**1. Recitation** (*apangelia*): Reporting the chreia clearly, in the same words or in other words.

The phenomenon of variant recitation is pervasive in Judaean literature, where the same teaching appears in multiple sources with verbal variations. Hillel's Golden Rule, for example, appears in several forms:

- Aramaic negative formulation: *"That which is hateful to you, do not do to your neighbor"* (Shabbat 31a)
- Hebrew positive formulation: *"Love your neighbor as yourself"* (Rabbi Akiva, Sifra Qedoshim 4:12)
- The "three pillars" saying appears twice with different verbs and different content: *"The world stands"* (Avot 1:2) vs. *"The world endures"* (Avot 1:18)

Each variant represents a recitation exercise -- the same teaching restated in different words, as Theon prescribes.

The phenomenon of variant recitation is not accidental but pedagogically essential. The student who can only repeat a teaching in its original words has memorized but not understood it. The student who can restate it in his own words -- preserving the substance while varying the expression -- has demonstrated comprehension. The Judaean tradition's tolerance for, and even cultivation of, textual variation in the transmission of oral teachings reflects a sophisticated understanding of the relationship between *verba* (words) and *res* (substance): it is the substance that must be preserved intact; the words may vary according to the reciter's idiom, audience, and pedagogical purpose.

This principle is demonstrated by the existence of two complete recensions of Avot de-Rabbi Natan (Version A and Version B), each transmitting essentially the same body of teachings in significantly different verbal forms. The two versions stand in the same relationship as two students' recitations of the same chreia: the substance is shared, but the expression varies. Their coexistence in the tradition -- neither version was suppressed in favor of the other -- testifies to the Judaean acceptance of variant recitation as a legitimate and indeed valuable rhetorical practice.

**2. Inflexion** (*klisis*): Changing the characters in the chreia into different numbers and cases.

> Rabban Yohanan ben Zakkai poses the same inquiry twice, first positive, then negative: "What is the right path?" (Avot 2:9) / "What is the evil path?" (Avot 2:10). The students' answers are systematically inflected: "good eye" becomes "evil eye," "good companion" becomes "evil companion."

The fourfold permutation schemes of Avot chapter 5 -- four types of students, four types of charity-givers, four types of temperament -- represent systematic inflexion of a chreia through all logical permutations of two variables. The technique is worth examining in detail. Consider the four types of temperament:

> <span dir="rtl">ארבע מידות בדעות: נוח לכעוס ונוח לרצות — יצא שכרו בהפסדו. קשה לכעוס וקשה לרצות — יצא הפסדו בשכרו. קשה לכעוס ונוח לרצות — חסיד. נוח לכעוס וקשה לרצות — רשע</span>
>
> *Four types of temperament: Easy to anger and easy to appease -- the gain is offset by the loss. Hard to anger and hard to appease -- the loss is offset by the gain. Hard to anger and easy to appease -- pious. Easy to anger and hard to appease -- wicked.* (Avot 5:11)

The inflexion here is mathematical: two binary variables (easy/hard to anger, easy/hard to appease) are crossed to produce four logical possibilities, and each possibility receives a moral evaluation. The exercise trains the student in systematic thinking -- the ability to enumerate all possible cases, assign a value to each, and present the results in a symmetrical structure. This is inflexion as a tool of analysis, not merely a stylistic variation.

The Judaean tradition's love of fourfold and sevenfold classification schemes (four types of students, four types of visitors to the bet midrash, seven qualities of the wise, etc.) may be understood as a pedagogical application of the inflexion exercise: each scheme takes a single topic and inflects it through multiple cases, training the student to see the full range of possibilities within a given category.

**3. Comment** (*epiphonemasis*): Commenting that a chreia is true, noble, advantageous.

> <span dir="rtl">רואה אני את דברי אלעזר בן ערך מדבריכם, שבכלל דבריו דבריכם</span>
>
> *I prefer the words of Elazar ben Arakh over yours, because his words include all of yours.* (Rabban Yohanan ben Zakkai, Avot 2:9)

This is a formal comment on the superiority of one chreia over others -- a metacritical evaluation of the kind Theon prescribes. The master does not simply declare Rabbi Elazar's answer correct; he provides a *reason* for his preference -- the argument of inclusion ("his words include all of yours"). This reasoned evaluation is more sophisticated than a simple expression of approval; it teaches the students *how to evaluate* competing chreiai, not merely *which one to prefer*. The comment exercise, as practiced here, is simultaneously an exercise in evaluation and a lesson in evaluative method.

A further example of the comment exercise:

> <span dir="rtl">אמר רבי יהושע: מי יגלה עפר מעיניך, רבן יוחנן בן זכאי</span>
>
> *Rabbi Yehoshua said: Who will remove the dust from your eyes, Rabban Yohanan ben Zakkai!* (Mishnah Kelim 3:8)

This is a comment on a deceased sage's teaching that has been vindicated by subsequent developments. The exclamation expresses the wish that the departed master could witness the confirmation of his position -- a poignant combination of intellectual vindication and personal grief. The comment exercise, in this case, becomes an act of piety toward the teacher and a testimony to the enduring truth of his chreia.

**4. Objection** (*antirrhesis*): Objecting to chreiai from the opposite point of view.

> <span dir="rtl">רבי עקיבא ורבי טרפון אומרים: אילו היינו בסנהדרין לא נהרג אדם מעולם. רבן שמעון בן גמליאל אומר: אף הם מרבין שופכי דמים בישראל</span>
>
> *Rabbi Akiva and Rabbi Tarfon say: Had we been in the Sanhedrin, no one would ever have been executed. Rabban Shimon ben Gamaliel says: They too would thereby increase bloodshed in Israel.* (Mishnah Makkot 1:10)

Rabban Shimon ben Gamaliel objects to the chreia of Rabbi Akiva and Rabbi Tarfon by pointing to its practical consequences -- a textbook objection exercise. The exchange is worth analyzing in detail. Rabbi Akiva and Rabbi Tarfon's chreia -- "Had we been in the Sanhedrin, no one would ever have been executed" -- is a statement of principle: capital punishment should be avoided through procedural stringency (asking unanswerable questions of witnesses to create reasonable doubt). It is a noble chreia, admirable in its moral ambition.

Rabban Shimon ben Gamaliel's objection does not challenge the nobility of the principle but its *consequences*: "They too would thereby increase bloodshed in Israel." The objection operates from the *topos* of result (*apobaion*): regardless of the intention behind the principle, its practical effect would be to remove the deterrent of capital punishment and thereby embolden potential murderers. The objection thus forces a distinction between the *moral quality* of a teaching (noble) and its *practical suitability* (dangerous) -- a distinction of central importance in both rhetorical and legal theory.

The Mishnah preserves this exchange without resolution: it does not tell us who "won" the argument. This refusal to adjudicate is itself significant: the tradition regards the objection as valuable precisely because it introduces a counterweight to the original chreia, creating a productive tension that the student must hold in mind. The Judaean approach to the objection exercise is not to destroy the original chreia but to complicate it -- to surround it with opposing considerations that enrich rather than annul it.

Every dispute between Beit Shammai and Beit Hillel throughout the Mishnah represents a systematic practice of the objection exercise, and the tradition's remarkable decision to preserve both positions -- even after ruling in favor of Beit Hillel -- reflects its commitment to the pedagogical value of sustained intellectual contestation.

**5. Expansion** (*exergasia*): Enlarging upon the chreia.

The entire work Avot de-Rabbi Natan is, in effect, a systematic expansion exercise performed on the chreiai of Pirkei Avot. Each concise maxim of Avot chapters 1-2 is expanded in ARN into elaborate narratives, multiple illustrations, and extended commentary. Avot 1:2 (three pillars in three words each) becomes three full chapters; Avot 1:3 (Antigonos's maxim about serving without expectation of reward) is expanded into a narrative about the origin of the Sadducees and Boethusians -- students who misunderstood the teaching and drew heretical conclusions from it; Avot 1:12 (Hillel's "Be of the disciples of Aaron") becomes an extended narrative cycle demonstrating how Aaron would make peace between quarreling parties. The relationship of ARN to Avot is precisely the relationship of expansion to chreia as Theon describes it.

The expansion exercise also operates between the Mishnah and the Tosefta more generally. The Tosefta (*supplement*) systematically enlarges upon Mishnaic rulings by adding narrative illustrations, exceptional cases, alternative opinions, and extended reasoning. A concise Mishnaic chreia of two lines may generate a Toseftan expansion of twenty lines -- the same teaching restated, illustrated, qualified, and defended. This institutional practice of expanding concise teachings into elaborated commentary is the Judaean equivalent of the progymnasmata expansion exercise, performed not by individual students as a school assignment but by the scholarly community as a mode of intellectual production.

**6. Condensation** (*systole*): Reducing a chreia to its most concise form.

> <span dir="rtl">לפום צערא אגרא</span>
>
> *According to the effort is the reward.* (Ben He He, Avot 5:23)

An entire theology of effort and reward in three Aramaic words -- maximum condensation.

> <span dir="rtl">במקום שאין אנשים, השתדל להיות איש</span>
>
> *In a place where there are no men, strive to be a man.* (Hillel, Avot 2:6)

This chreia condenses a longer ethical teaching -- the obligation of moral leadership, the responsibility of the individual in a community of passivity, the imperative to act when others will not -- into a single memorable sentence. The condensation is so effective that the chreia has become proverbial: it functions independently of its attribution, as a freestanding maxim of ethical conduct. This is the mark of supreme condensation -- when the compressed form achieves such self-sufficient clarity that it detaches from its source and enters the language as a common possession.

The progressive condensation described by Rabbi Simlai (Makkot 23b-24a) -- from 613 commandments to 11 (Psalm 15) to 6 (Isaiah 33:15-16) to 3 (Micah 6:8: "Do justice, love mercy, and walk humbly with your God") to 1 (Habakkuk 2:4: "The righteous shall live by his faith") -- is a chreia *about* condensation: it narrates and demonstrates the exercise simultaneously. The passage itself performs the exercise it describes, moving from the maximally expanded form (613 individual commandments) to the maximally condensed form (a single principle of faith). Rabbi Simlai's chreia is, in this sense, the Judaean tradition's most self-conscious reflection on the rhetorical exercise of condensation as a mode of intellectual inquiry: to condense is not merely to shorten but to penetrate to the essence.

**7. Refutation** (*anaskeuē*): Refuting chreiai on nine grounds.

Theon identifies nine categories of refutation. All nine appear in the Judaean rhetorical tradition:

- **(a) For obscurity:** Teachings are challenged as requiring clarification. In Mishnah Eduyot 1:3, earlier teachings are recorded not because they are accepted but because their very obscurity demands preservation -- a later sage may need to clarify what an earlier sage meant. The challenge "What does this mean?" (*mah hu omer*) is a form of refutation for obscurity that runs throughout the midrashic literature.

- **(b) For pleonasm:** Interpreters regularly ask "Why does the verse need to say this?" (*lamah ne'emar*), identifying apparent redundancies that must be justified. The assumption underlying this challenge is that sacred text contains no unnecessary words; therefore, any apparent redundancy must carry independent meaning. This is a form of refutation directed at the text itself -- or rather, at an interpretation of the text that fails to account for every element.

- **(c) For ellipsis:** The midrashic technique of noting what Scripture "should have said but didn't" (*ha mah talmud lomar*) identifies elliptical formulations requiring completion. When a passage seems to omit a necessary element, the interpreter must supply what is missing -- and the identification of the ellipsis constitutes a refutation of any reading that takes the passage as complete.

- **(d) For impossibility:** The assertion that certain groups have no share in the world to come (Mishnah Sanhedrin 10:3) is challenged on the grounds that repentance is always possible. If God's mercy is infinite, how can any soul be permanently excluded? The challenge targets the *possibility* of the claim.

- **(e) For implausibility:** Legal presumptions (*hazaqot*) are challenged when they conflict with plausible counter-scenarios (Mishnah Bava Batra 3:1-3). "Is it plausible that X would behave in this way?" is a standard Talmudic challenge (*mi-ka-mashma lan?*) that interrogates the verisimilitude of a proposed ruling.

- **(f) For falsity:** The genre of challenging earlier teachings as factually incorrect is pervasive in Talmudic dialectic. The objection "But we have learned otherwise!" (*ve-ha tanya*) introduces counter-evidence from an authoritative source, effectively refuting the chreia as false -- not morally false but factually inconsistent with other established teachings.

- **(g) For unsuitability:** Rabban Shimon ben Gamaliel's objection that abolishing capital punishment would increase bloodshed (Makkot 1:10) is a refutation on grounds of unsuitability -- the teaching, while noble in principle, would produce harmful results in practice. The distinction between principled correctness and practical suitability is a sophisticated rhetorical move that separates the truth of a proposition from its appropriateness as a basis for action.

- **(h) For uselessness:** The Sadducees challenge certain Pharisaic rulings as serving no purpose (Mishnah Yadayim 4:6). "What use is this ruling?" is a challenge that targets not the truth of the teaching but its practical value -- a refutation from the *topos* of utility.

- **(i) For shamefulness:** Akavya ben Mahalalel refuses to retract his opinions for political gain, suggesting that such retraction would be shameful (Mishnah Eduyot 5:6-7). The refutation here is directed not at someone else's chreia but at the *implied chreia* of his opponents: "Retract and we will promote you" -- to which Akavya responds that the very proposal is shameful.

**8. Confirmation** (*kataskeuē*): Providing arguments for each part of the chreia.

Theon describes the confirmation exercise in detail: the student is to take a chreia and provide arguments supporting it from as many *topoi* (topics) as possible -- from the credibility of the speaker, from the truth of the statement, from analogy, from example, from authority, from consequence, and from judgment of others. This exercise corresponds precisely to the methodology of the tannaitic midrashim.

The entire structure of halakhic midrash -- Mekhilta, Sifra, Sifrei -- is a confirmation exercise: a legal principle is stated, then supported through close reading of Scripture, logical inference (*qal va-homer*, *gezerah shavah*), historical precedent, and appeal to tradition. Consider a characteristic passage from the Mekhilta (Bahodesh 8):

A teaching is stated. It is then confirmed through: (1) scriptural citation (*she-ne'emar*, "as it is said"); (2) logical inference (*ha lo din hu*, "is it not a matter of logical reasoning?"); (3) analogical argument (*mah X af Y*, "just as X, so too Y"); (4) appeal to the authority of a named sage (*amar Rabbi X*, "Rabbi X said"); and (5) refutation of potential objections (*yakhol...talmud lomar*, "one might think...but Scripture teaches"). This five-part structure of confirmation corresponds almost exactly to Theon's prescription for the full confirmation exercise.

Avot de-Rabbi Natan performs the same function for the ethical teachings of Pirkei Avot: each concise chreia is "confirmed" through scriptural proofs, logical arguments, narrative illustrations, and appeals to authority -- precisely the confirmation exercise that Theon describes.

The most elaborate example of confirmation in the Judaean corpus is the treatment of Rabbi Akiva's teaching "Beloved are Israel, for they are called children of God" (Avot 3:14):

> <span dir="rtl">חביבין ישראל שנקראו בנים למקום. חיבה יתירה נודעת להם שנקראו בנים למקום, שנאמר: בנים אתם לה' אלהיכם</span>
>
> *Beloved are Israel, for they are called children of God. An extra love was made known to them, that they are called children of God, as it is said: "You are children of the Lord your God" (Deuteronomy 14:1).*

The chreia is confirmed twice: first by restatement with intensification ("extra love" -- a technique of amplification), then by scriptural proof-text. The confirmation exercise, as practiced by the Judaean sages, was not a school assignment performed once and discarded but a permanent mode of intellectual engagement -- a way of treating every authoritative teaching as a proposition that deserves, and rewards, systematic argumentative support.[^68]

---

#### The Chreia in Judaean Rhetorical Education: A Summary Assessment

The rhetorician who surveys the Judaean chreia material cannot fail to be struck by three features that distinguish it from the Greek and Roman tradition.

First, **abundance**. The sheer volume of chreiai preserved in the Judaean corpus -- hundreds in Pirkei Avot alone, thousands more in the Tosefta, the tannaitic midrashim, Avot de-Rabbi Natan, and the baraitot embedded in the Talmuds -- exceeds anything surviving from the Greek or Roman tradition. Diogenes Laertius preserves perhaps a few hundred chreiai scattered across his ten books; the gnomological collections add a few hundred more. The Judaean tradition preserves thousands, and preserves them not as literary curiosities but as authoritative teachings that continue to be studied, recited, and applied.

Second, **systematization**. The Greek chreia tradition is largely anthological: chreiai are collected and preserved but rarely organized into systematic pedagogical sequences. The Judaean tradition, by contrast, organizes its chreiai into chains of transmission (Avot chapters 1-2), thematic clusters (Avot chapters 3-4), and numerical taxonomies (Avot chapter 5). The result is a chreia collection that is also a curriculum -- a body of material organized for progressive pedagogical use.

Third, **integration with practice**. In the Greek and Roman traditions, the chreia is primarily a *school* exercise -- a tool for training students who will eventually move on to "real" rhetorical tasks (deliberative speeches, forensic arguments). In the Judaean tradition, the chreia is not merely a training exercise but a primary form of intellectual and legal discourse. The attributed saying of a sage carries halakhic weight; it can determine the outcome of a legal dispute, establish a precedent, or define a communal norm. The chreia is therefore not a preliminary to the real work but an integral part of it -- a form that the sage practices not in the schoolroom alone but in the courtroom, the academy, and the public assembly throughout his career.

These three features -- abundance, systematization, and practical integration -- suggest that the Judaean tradition developed the chreia exercise to a level of sophistication that the Greek and Roman traditions, for all their theoretical elaboration, never matched in practice.

We conclude this preliminary survey of the first three progymnasmata exercises with an observation that bears upon the remainder of our treatise. In the Greek and Roman traditions, the fable, the narrative, and the chreia are genuinely *preliminary* -- they are exercises that the student performs in preparation for the "real" work of composing complete speeches. The student who has mastered these exercises sets them aside and moves on to the more demanding tasks of thesis, impersonation, and deliberative argumentation.

In the Judaean tradition, the situation is fundamentally different. The fable (*mashal*), the narrative (*ma'aseh*), and the chreia (*ma'amar hakham*) are not preliminary exercises that the sage outgrows but permanent forms of intellectual discourse that he practices throughout his career. The senior sage continues to compose meshalim; the master of the academy continues to narrate ma'asiyot; the greatest scholars are remembered precisely for their chreiai -- their concise, attributed, apt sayings that crystalize a lifetime of learning into memorable formulations. The exercises are not a ladder to be climbed and abandoned but a repertoire to be deepened and refined over a lifetime.

This suggests that the Judaean rhetorical tradition, while structurally parallel to the Greek and Roman tradition, operates with a different relationship between exercise and performance, between training and practice. In the classical tradition, the *progymnasmata* are means to an end (the complete speech); in the Judaean tradition, the exercises *are* the end -- or rather, they are themselves the vehicles of the tradition's intellectual life. The mashal is not preparation for something else; it is itself a primary mode of Torah. The chreia is not a stepping-stone to forensic oratory; it is itself the form in which the sage's wisdom is preserved and transmitted. This integration of exercise and practice, of training and performance, is perhaps the most distinctive feature of the Judaean rhetorical tradition and the feature that most clearly distinguishes it from its Greco-Roman counterpart.

*For the complete catalog of chreia parallels -- including detailed examples of every type, subtype, and form of expression -- the reader is referred to the companion volume, Tannaitic Parallels to Theon's Chreia Taxonomy.*

[^4]: *Rhetorica ad Herennium* 1.2.2. The authorship of this work, once attributed to Cicero, is uncertain; it dates to approximately 86-82 BCE.

[^5]: Cicero, *De Inventione* 1.5.6.

[^6]: On the *bet midrash* as a rhetorical institution, see Hidary, *Rabbis and Classical Rhetoric*, 15-52; Lieberman, *Hellenism in Jewish Palestine*, 83-99; Catherine Hezser, *The Social Structure of the Rabbinic Movement in Roman Palestine* (Tubingen: Mohr Siebeck, 1997), 190-224.

[^7]: Babylonian Talmud, Eruvin 13b. On Rabbi Meir's dialectical abilities, see Hidary, *Rabbis and Classical Rhetoric*, 156-162.

[^8]: On the *meturgeman* system, see Lieberman, *Hellenism in Jewish Palestine*, 47-82; Hidary, *Rabbis and Classical Rhetoric*, 42-46.

[^9]: Avot 5:7 lists seven qualities of the wise man and seven of the fool. Among the qualities of the wise: "He does not interrupt his fellow's words" (*eino nikhnas le-tokh divrei havero*).

[^10]: Mishnah Sanhedrin 4:2: "In capital cases, they begin from the side, from the youngest"; cf. Quintilian, *Inst. Or.* 5.7.26-27 on the order of testimony.

[^11]: Aristotle, *Rhetoric* 1.3.1358a-b. The threefold division is restated by virtually every subsequent rhetorician; see Cicero, *De Inv.* 1.5.7; Quintilian, *Inst. Or.* 3.4.

On the confirmation exercise generally, see Theon, *Progymnasmata* 3 (in Hock and O'Neil, *The Chreia in Ancient Rhetoric*, I:99-108).
### II.D. Maxim (*Gnome* / *Klal*)

#### Definition and Classification

The student of rhetoric must first distinguish, with precision, between the chreia and the maxim, for these two forms are so closely allied that even accomplished speakers confuse them -- to the detriment of their compositions and, more gravely, to the confusion of their audiences. We have treated the chreia at length; we turn now to its near relative, the maxim.

A maxim -- *gnome* in the terminology of the Greek schools, *klal* or *divrei musar* in the idiom of the Judaean academies -- is a general statement regarding some practical matter of human conduct. Theon defines it thus: "A maxim is a summary statement, in a universal form, dissuading from something or persuading toward something, or indicating what is the nature of each thing."[^69] Quintilian, in his treatment of the *sententia*, offers a complementary definition: the maxim is "a universal statement about life, applicable not to particular persons but to the general run of men" (*Institutio Oratoria* 8.5.3).[^70] Aristotle, who provides the philosophical foundation for both treatments, defines the *gnome* as "a statement, not about a particular thing -- as, for instance, what sort of person Iphicrates is -- but of a general kind; and not about all things -- as, for instance, that the straight is the opposite of the curved -- but about all such things as are the objects of [practical] action" (*Rhetoric* 2.21, 1394a21-25).[^71]

The essential point of distinction between chreia and maxim is this: the chreia is always attributed to a named person, whereas the maxim circulates without personal attribution. When we say, "Ben Zoma says: Who is wise? One who learns from every person," we have a chreia -- a concise and pointed declaration anchored to an identifiable authority. When we say simply, "Who is wise? One who learns from every person," we have a maxim -- the same truth, but freed from its personal mooring and set adrift upon the ocean of collective wisdom. The chreia borrows its authority from the reputation of the named speaker; the maxim claims authority from the self-evidence of its content. One notes with interest that this same distinction operates in Judaean legal discourse, where the unattributed ruling (*stam mishnah*) carries the weight of consensus, while the attributed ruling invites dispute and counter-citation.[^72]

Hermogenes classifies maxims into four types, and Quintilian offers a corresponding taxonomy. The student should master all four, for each serves a different rhetorical purpose and requires different compositional technique.[^73]

**First: The Declarative Maxim (*apophantike*).** This type states a fact about the nature of things -- not what should be done, but what is. It declares a truth about the human condition with the calm authority of observed reality. The listener is not commanded but informed, and the force of the declaration lies in its apparent self-evidence.

**Second: The Hortatory Maxim (*protreptike*).** This type exhorts or dissuades. It tells the listener what to do or what to avoid. Its grammatical form is typically imperative or subjunctive, and its rhetorical force lies in the urgency of its command.

**Third: The Compound Maxim (*synthetike*).** This type combines a declarative statement with its supporting reason. It presents both the maxim and its justification in a single construction: "X is the case, *for* Y is the reason."

**Fourth: The Double Maxim (*diple*).** This type presents a thesis and its antithesis in a balanced structure, so that each half illuminates the other through contrast. The double maxim is the most architecturally complex of the four types and the most characteristic of Judaean rhetorical practice.

We observe in the Judaean academies a rich tradition of maxim-composition that illustrates all four types with remarkable clarity. We shall examine the principal examples in turn.

#### Judaean Examples

**Example 1: The Declarative Maxim -- "All Is Foreseen, Yet Freedom of Choice Is Given" (Avot 3:15)**

> <span dir="rtl">הכל צפוי, והרשות נתונה</span>
>
> *All is foreseen, yet freedom of choice is given.*

This teaching is attributed in the Mishnah to Rabbi Akiva, and therefore it partakes of the nature of both chreia and maxim -- a circumstance that Theon himself anticipates when he observes that certain sayings sit upon the boundary between the two forms.[^74] Considered as a chreia, the statement draws authority from Akiva's towering reputation as the systematizer of Judaean oral law. Considered as a maxim -- and it is in this capacity that it most frequently circulates in later Judaean discourse -- it states a declarative truth about the fundamental relationship between divine omniscience and human agency. The statement neither commands action nor offers counsel; it declares what is.

One notes with interest the extraordinary compression of this maxim. Two clauses, each of three Hebrew words, encompass a philosophical problem that the Stoics and Epicureans debated across centuries of elaborate treatises. The Judaean gnome achieves in six words what Chrysippus required volumes to articulate.[^75] This compression is itself a rhetorical virtue: the maxim that can be held in the memory in a single breath exerts an authority that no lengthy argument can match.

**Example 2: The Hortatory Maxim -- "Do Not Look at the Jar" (Avot 4:20)**

> <span dir="rtl">אל תסתכל בקנקן אלא במה שיש בו</span>
>
> *Do not look at the jar but at what is in it.*

This maxim -- attributed in some textual witnesses to Rabbi Meir, in others simply to "Rabbi" -- is hortatory in form: it commands the listener to adopt a specific mode of judgment. The imperative *al tistakel* ("do not look") establishes the tone of direct exhortation. The student should observe the embedded metaphor: the jar represents external appearance (youth, poverty, humble bearing); its contents represent internal worth (wisdom, learning, virtue). The metaphorical vehicle is drawn from the wine trade, where an unassuming vessel may contain aged vintage while a splendid amphora may hold vinegar.[^76]

The Mishnah expands the maxim with an illustrative amplification:

> <span dir="rtl">יש קנקן חדש מלא ישן, וישן שאפילו חדש אין בו</span>
>
> *There is a new jar full of old wine, and an old jar that does not even have new wine in it.*

This expansion transforms a simple hortatory maxim into a compound maxim: the command is now followed by its illustrative reason. The student of rhetoric should note how the expansion rescues the maxim from the reproach of banality. "Do not judge by appearances" is a commonplace of every culture; "a new jar full of old wine" is vivid, particular, and memorable. The force of the Judaean gnome lies not in the originality of its moral content -- which is, as Aristotle observes, typically drawn from "what has been proved or is generally accepted" (*Rhetoric* 2.21, 1394a7) -- but in the distinctiveness of its figurative expression.[^77]

**Example 3: The Compound Maxim -- "Train a Child According to His Way" (Proverbs 22:6)**

> <span dir="rtl">חנוך לנער על פי דרכו, גם כי יזקין לא יסור ממנה</span>
>
> *Train a child according to his way; even when he grows old he will not depart from it.*

This maxim from the Solomonic collection -- cited with great frequency in Judaean educational discourse -- exemplifies the compound type with textbook clarity. The first clause states the precept: educate the child according to his individual nature. The second clause provides the supporting reason: habits formed in youth persist into old age. The conjunction *gam ki* ("even when") marks the transition from precept to justification, producing the two-part structure that Quintilian identifies as the maxim *cum ratione* -- the maxim supported by a reason (*Inst. Or.* 8.5.3-4).[^78]

We observe in the Judaean academies that this maxim is deployed not merely as educational counsel but as a principle of hermeneutical and legal reasoning. When Rabbi Yohanan states that this is "a small verse upon which all the fundamentals of Torah depend" (BT Berakhot 63a), he elevates the Solomonic compound maxim to the status of an axiom from which other principles may be derived. The compound maxim thus acquires a generative function: it does not merely state a truth but serves as a premise from which further truths may be deduced.[^79]

**Example 4: The Double Maxim -- "If There Is No Flour, There Is No Torah" (Avot 3:17)**

> <span dir="rtl">אם אין קמח אין תורה, אם אין תורה אין קמח</span>
>
> *If there is no flour, there is no Torah; if there is no Torah, there is no flour.*

This double maxim is, in the judgment of the present writer, among the most perfectly constructed gnomae in all of ancient literature -- Judaean or otherwise. Two conditional clauses, each the mirror image of the other, together express a relationship of mutual dependence that neither clause alone could capture. Material sustenance requires spiritual purpose; spiritual pursuit requires material sustenance. The symmetrical structure -- thesis and antithesis held in perfect equipoise -- is itself the meaning. Were either clause stated alone, the result would be a partisan argument (either for the supremacy of the practical life or for the supremacy of the contemplative life); stated together, they constitute a comprehensive philosophical position: that body and spirit, matter and meaning, are mutually sustaining.

The student should compare this with the double maxim in Avot 5:17 on the subject of dispute:

> <span dir="rtl">כל מחלוקת שהיא לשם שמים, סופה להתקיים. ושאינה לשם שמים, אין סופה להתקיים</span>
>
> *Every dispute that is for the sake of Heaven will in the end endure. And one that is not for the sake of Heaven will not in the end endure.*

Here the double structure serves a classificatory purpose: it divides the genus "dispute" into two species (for the sake of Heaven; not for the sake of Heaven) and assigns a different outcome to each. The technique is identical to what Aristotle describes when he notes that gnomae are especially effective when they "distinguish and make clear at the same time" (*Rhetoric* 2.21, 1394b8).[^80]

**Example 5: The Fourfold Redefinition -- Ben Zoma's Maxim Cluster (Avot 4:1)**

> <span dir="rtl">איזהו חכם? הלומד מכל אדם, שנאמר: מכל מלמדי השכלתי כי עדותיך שיחה לי. איזהו גיבור? הכובש את יצרו, שנאמר: טוב ארך אפיים מגיבור ומושל ברוחו מלוכד עיר. איזהו עשיר? השמח בחלקו, שנאמר: יגיע כפיך כי תאכל אשריך וטוב לך. איזהו מכובד? המכבד את הבריות, שנאמר: כי מכבדי אכבד ובוזי יקלו</span>
>
> *Who is wise? One who learns from every person, as it says: "From all my teachers I grew wise, for Your testimonies are my meditation" (Psalm 119:99). Who is mighty? One who conquers his inclination, as it says: "Better is one slow to anger than a mighty man, and one who rules his spirit than one who captures a city" (Proverbs 16:32). Who is rich? One who is happy with his portion, as it says: "When you eat the labor of your hands, happy are you and it is well with you" (Psalm 128:2). Who is honored? One who honors other people, as it says: "For those who honor Me I will honor, and those who despise Me will be lightly esteemed" (1 Samuel 2:30).*

This teaching is formally a chreia -- attributed to Ben Zoma in the Mishnah. But the student of rhetoric should observe that each of its four components functions as an independent compound maxim: each poses a question ("Who is wise?"), provides a redefinition ("One who learns from every person"), and supports the redefinition with a scriptural proof ("as it says..."). The structure is fourfold, with each unit exhibiting the same tripartite architecture of question, answer, and proof.

The rhetorical power of this cluster lies in its systematic inversion of conventional categories. Wisdom is redefined as teachability rather than erudition. Might is redefined as self-mastery rather than martial prowess. Wealth is redefined as contentment rather than abundance. Honor is redefined as the giving rather than the receiving of respect. In each case, the popular understanding is overturned and replaced with a moral understanding. This is the technique of the *paradoxical maxim* -- the gnome that challenges received opinion -- and it is among the most powerful instruments in the rhetorical repertoire. Aristotle observes that gnomae are especially effective when they contradict popular sayings, "for they attract attention because of the novelty of the statement" (*Rhetoric* 2.21, 1395a11-15).

The student should further observe that the scriptural proofs function as confirmations of the redefinitions: each verse anchors the paradoxical claim in sacred authority, preventing the listener from dismissing it as mere cleverness. The maxim without the proof might seem willfully contrarian; the maxim with the proof is anchored in revelation.

#### The Transformation Between Chreia and Maxim

One observes with particular interest the fluidity of the boundary between these two forms in Judaean rhetorical practice. A chreia becomes a maxim when the attribution is dropped -- a process that occurs naturally over time as a sage's teaching becomes so thoroughly absorbed into common wisdom that the community ceases to remember its origin. Conversely, a maxim becomes a chreia when the tradition reattaches it to a named sage, lending it the additional authority of personal reputation.

The pedagogical significance of this transformation cannot be overstated. The student who understands it possesses a tool of considerable rhetorical power: by adding or removing attribution, he can alter the character of a teaching without changing its content. The attributed form (chreia) invites challenge through counter-citation ("But Rabbi X holds otherwise!"); the unattributed form (maxim) resists such challenge, for there is no individual authority to set against another individual authority. The skilled speaker deploys the chreia form when the prestige of the named sage serves his argument, and the maxim form when he wishes to speak with the anonymous authority of the tradition entire.

The Mishnah itself provides evidence of this transformation in process. The teaching "It is not your obligation to complete the work, but neither are you free to desist from it" is formally attributed to Rabbi Tarfon (Avot 2:16) -- and yet it circulates in later Judaean culture as an anonymous maxim, quoted without attribution in contexts far removed from its Mishnaic home. The attribution has been shed; the chreia has become a gnome. The student should be alert to further instances of this transformation throughout the corpus.[^81]

#### Exercises

**First exercise: Transform chreia to maxim.** Take the following chreiai and strip them to their maxim forms. For each, compose a brief commentary explaining what is gained and what is lost through the removal of attribution.

(a) "Ben Zoma says: Who is wise? One who learns from every person" (Avot 4:1).

(b) "Rabbi Tarfon says: The day is short, the work is great, the workers are lazy" (Avot 2:15).

(c) "Hillel says: If I am not for myself, who is for me?" (Avot 1:14).

**Second exercise: Transform maxim to chreia.** Take the following maxim and compose a chreia around it. The student should provide a named sage, a narrative circumstance that prompts the saying, and a brief indication of the audience. Compose at least two different versions, observing how the narrative frame alters the maxim's application and force.

> <span dir="rtl">אין אדם חוטא אלא אם כן נכנס בו רוח שטות</span>
>
> *A person does not sin unless a spirit of folly enters him.*

**Third exercise: Compose original maxims.** Compose one maxim of each of the four types (declarative, hortatory, compound, double) on the theme of silence and speech. Each maxim should be concise enough to be committed to memory in a single hearing.

**Fourth exercise: Classify and analyze.** Read through the fifth chapter of Pirkei Avot and classify each teaching as chreia (attributed) or maxim (anonymous). For each maxim, identify its type (declarative, hortatory, compound, or double). For each chreia, determine whether it could stand as a maxim if the attribution were removed -- and explain why or why not.[^82]

---

### II.E. Refutation (*Anaskeue* / *Teshuvah*)

#### Definition

The fifth exercise in the sequence of preliminary training is refutation -- the systematic demolition of a proposition, narrative, or teaching by marshaling arguments against its credibility. Aphthonius defines it with characteristic precision: "Refutation is the overturning of a matter at hand."[^83] Theon provides a more detailed account, instructing that refutation may be directed against fables, narratives, chreiai, and maxims -- against any teaching or story that can be subjected to critical examination.[^84] Hermogenes concurs, treating refutation and its counterpart, confirmation, as complementary exercises that together constitute the dialectical core of rhetorical training.[^85]

The student of rhetoric must understand at the outset that refutation is not mere contradiction. Any person may say "I disagree" or "That is wrong." The art of refutation consists in demonstrating *why* a proposition fails -- identifying the specific ground of its failure, articulating the objection with precision, and supporting the objection with evidence. The untrained mind objects vaguely; the trained rhetorician refutes systematically.

We observe in the Judaean academies the most extensively developed tradition of systematic refutation in all of ancient literature. This claim is not made lightly. The Babylonian Talmud -- that vast record of Judaean legal and philosophical dialectic -- is structured, from first folio to last, around cycles of proposition, objection, and response. The *kushya* (difficulty), the *teyuvta* (refutation), the *pirka* (dismantling) -- these are not occasional features of Judaean discourse but its governing architecture. Where the Greek rhetorical schools teach refutation as one exercise among fourteen, the Judaean academy makes it the fundamental mode of intellectual life. Every proposition is tested; every ruling is challenged; every proof is probed for weakness. The sage who cannot refute has not yet learned to think.[^86]

#### The Six Categories of Refutation

Aphthonius and Theon enumerate the specific grounds upon which a proposition may be refuted. We adapt their categories here, mapping them onto the corresponding structures of Judaean dialectic.[^87]

**First: The Unclear (*asaphes* / *safeq*).** A proposition may be refuted on the ground that its meaning is indeterminate -- that it admits of multiple interpretations, and that upon each interpretation a different difficulty arises. This is refutation not from falsehood but from ambiguity: the proposition fails because it does not say clearly enough what it means.

The Babylonian Talmud possesses a standard formula for this mode of refutation that is deployed with extraordinary frequency:

> <span dir="rtl">היכי דמי? אילימא... אלא...</span>
>
> *What is the case? If you say [it means X]... then [difficulty follows]... Rather [it must mean Y]...*

This formula -- *heikhi dami?* -- is the refutation from unclarity reduced to its essential components: an interrogation of meaning, a hypothetical interpretation, a demonstration of difficulty, and a forced reinterpretation. The student should observe that this single formula appears hundreds of times in the Babylonian Talmud, constituting perhaps the most frequently deployed rhetorical structure in any ancient text.[^88]

**Second: The Incredible (*apithanon* / *eino ra'ui le-he'amen*).** A proposition may be refuted on the ground that it strains credibility -- that it asserts something so improbable that the reasonable listener withholds assent, even if the proposition is not logically impossible.

The corresponding Talmudic formula is devastating in its simplicity:

> <span dir="rtl">אפשר...?</span>
>
> *Is it possible that...?*

This single word -- *efshar?* -- is a complete refutation. It does not argue; it raises an eyebrow. It does not demonstrate impossibility; it expresses the incredulity of the reasonable mind confronted with an implausible claim. One notes with interest that the Talmud applies this formula even to its own sacred narratives. In BT Gittin 56b-57a, where the text recounts extraordinary stories about the destruction of the Temple, the narrative pauses at several points to ask *efshar?* -- subjecting its own traditions to the test of credibility. This reflexive quality -- the tradition interrogating itself -- is, so far as the present writer can determine, without parallel in Greek rhetorical practice, where the student refutes the compositions of others but is not systematically trained to refute his own.[^89]

**Third: The Impossible (*adunaton* / *i efshar*).** A proposition may be refuted on the ground that it contains an internal contradiction -- that its constituent elements are mutually exclusive and cannot coexist within a single coherent statement. This is the strongest form of refutation, for it does not merely claim that the proposition is improbable or unclear but that it is logically incoherent.

The Talmudic formula:

> <span dir="rtl">תרתי דסתרי אהדדי</span>
>
> *These two [statements] contradict each other.*

And the decisive conclusion:

> <span dir="rtl">תיובתא דרב... תיובתא</span>
>
> *It is a refutation of Rav... It is a [conclusive] refutation.*

The doubled word *teyuvta* signals finality. The position has been tested and found to contain an irreconcilable contradiction with an authoritative source. No further defense is possible; the proposition is demolished. The student of rhetoric should observe the rhetorical force of the repetition: the word is said once to announce the refutation, and again to confirm its decisiveness. This is a device of closure -- a verbal seal that marks the end of debate.[^90]

**Fourth: The Inconsistent (*akatallelos* / *stirah*).** A proposition may be refuted on the ground that it contradicts another established and accepted proposition. This differs from the impossible in that the contradiction is not internal to the proposition itself but arises from its relationship to external authorities.

The Talmud deploys two principal formulae for this category:

> <span dir="rtl">ורמינהו</span>
>
> *And we raise a contradiction [from another source]!*

> <span dir="rtl">מיתיבי</span>
>
> *We raise an objection [from a baraita]!*

These two Aramaic words -- *u-reminhu* and *mitivi* -- are among the most common in the entire Babylonian Talmud. Each introduces a passage from another authoritative source that appears to contradict the ruling under discussion. The challenged party must then harmonize the sources (through distinguishing their circumstances, limiting the scope of one, or proposing a textual emendation) or concede the contradiction and abandon his position.[^91]

One notes with interest the structural difference between these two formulae. *U-reminhu* typically introduces a contradiction between two Mishnaic passages or between two baraitot of equal authority; *mitivi* typically introduces a baraita that contradicts an amoraic (post-Mishnaic) ruling. The choice of formula thus signals the relative authority of the conflicting sources -- a subtlety that the student should learn to recognize and exploit.

**Fifth: The Unfitting (*aprepes* / *eino na'eh*).** A proposition may be refuted on the ground that it is morally or aesthetically inappropriate -- that it attributes unworthy conduct to a revered figure, or that its implications violate the established values of the community. This is refutation not from logic but from decorum: the proposition may be internally consistent, but it offends the moral sensibilities of the tradition.

The Talmudic formula:

> <span dir="rtl">חס ושלום! לא עשה כן</span>
>
> *God forbid! He did not do so.*

This formula appears when the Talmud refuses to accept a literal interpretation that would impute moral failure to a biblical hero. The classic instance occurs in BT Shabbat 56a, where the text refuses to accept that King David sinned with Bathsheba: "Whoever says that David sinned is in error." The refutation operates not on logical grounds -- the text of Scripture plainly narrates the incident -- but on grounds of fittingness: it is inappropriate to ascribe such conduct to a figure of David's stature, and therefore the text must be read otherwise.[^92]

The student should observe the philosophical significance of this category. It implies that truth and fittingness are not independent criteria -- that a proposition which is logically possible but morally inappropriate is, for that very reason, presumptively false. This is a principle with deep roots in both Greek and Judaean thought: the *to prepon* ("the fitting") of the Greek rhetoricians corresponds, in Judaean discourse, to the principle that Torah must be interpreted in a manner consistent with the dignity of its subjects.

**Sixth: The Inexpedient (*asympheron* / *eino mo'il*).** A proposition may be refuted on the ground that its practical consequences are intolerable -- that following it would produce results so harmful that no reasonable community would accept them.

The Talmudic formula:

> <span dir="rtl">אם כן, לא הנחת חיים לכל בריה</span>
>
> *If so, you have not left life for any creature!*

And the corresponding legal principle:

> <span dir="rtl">אין גוזרין גזירה על הצבור אלא אם כן רוב צבור יכולין לעמוד בה</span>
>
> *We do not impose a decree upon the community unless the majority of the community can bear it.*

These formulae establish that a ruling -- however logically sound -- is refuted by its own impracticability. If a ruling would make life impossible for the community, that impossibility is itself sufficient grounds for rejection. The student should observe that this is a distinctively practical form of refutation: it does not challenge the truth of the proposition but its *livability*. The Judaean tradition, rooted as it is in the governance of a living community, gives this category a weight that the more theoretical Greek tradition does not always accord it.[^93]

#### The Hierarchy of Refutation in Talmudic Discourse

The student should understand that these six categories are not equal in force. The Talmud implicitly ranks them in a hierarchy of severity:

The *kushya* (difficulty) is the weakest form of refutation: it raises a problem but does not necessarily demolish the proposition. A kushya may be answered; a proposition that survives a kushya is strengthened, not destroyed.

The *tiyuvta* (objection from a baraita) is stronger: it brings the authority of a tannaitic source against an amoraic ruling, and the amoraic authority must either harmonize or yield.

The *teyuvta* (conclusive refutation) is the strongest: it signals that the proposition has been definitively demolished and cannot be revived. When the Talmud declares *teyuvta de-Rav... teyuvta*, the doubled formula marks the finality of the defeat.

Between these poles lie numerous intermediate forms -- the *ibaya* (unresolved question), the *kashya* (acknowledged difficulty left standing), the *teiku* (unresolved query whose resolution is deferred to the messianic age). The student should learn to recognize each form and its position in the hierarchy, for the force of a refutation depends not only on its content but on its formal category. A *kashya* left standing does not carry the same weight as a *teyuvta* pronounced.

One observes with interest that this hierarchy has no precise parallel in the Greek rhetorical tradition. Theon and Aphthonius treat refutation as a binary: a proposition is either refuted or it is not. The Judaean tradition recognizes a graduated spectrum of refutational force -- from the slight difficulty that merely scratches the surface to the conclusive demolition that buries the proposition permanently. This graduated approach reflects the practical orientation of the Judaean academy, where rulings must be decided and applied, and where the degree of damage inflicted by an objection has direct consequences for the ruling's viability.

#### Extended Example: The Mekhilta's Systematic Challenges

The Mekhilta de-Rabbi Ishmael -- the tannaitic commentary on the Book of Exodus -- provides an extended demonstration of systematic refutation that the student of rhetoric should study with particular care. Its characteristic formula is:

> <span dir="rtl">מניין אתה אומר?</span>
>
> *From where do you derive this?*

This formula is not a simple request for information. It is a formal challenge -- a demand that the proponent of a teaching produce his evidence or withdraw his claim. The burden of proof is placed squarely on the speaker, and if the evidence is found insufficient, the teaching falls. The student should examine the Mekhilta's treatment of the Passover laws (Pischa, chapters 1-18), where virtually every halakhic proposition is subjected to this challenge, producing a sustained dialectic of assertion, challenge, and response that extends across dozens of pages.[^94]

Consider also the Talmud's formula of dismissive refutation:

> <span dir="rtl">איני יודע מה מלמדנו</span>
>
> *I do not know what this teaches us.*

This phrase -- attributed to various tannaitic authorities -- refuses not the proof-text itself but the inference drawn from it. The refuter accepts the existence of the verse; he denies its relevance to the proposition it is meant to support. This is a refutation of the *connection* between evidence and conclusion -- an attack on the logic of the proof rather than on the truth of the premise. The student should recognize this as a particularly sophisticated mode of refutation, for it grants the opponent his evidence while destroying his argument.[^95]

#### Extended Example: The Self-Interrogation of Tradition (BT Gittin 56b-57a)

The student of comparative rhetoric will find no more instructive passage than the Talmud's account of the destruction of the Temple in BT Gittin 56b-57a. This extended narrative recounts extraordinary events -- the fall of Jerusalem, the encounter between Rabban Yohanan ben Zakkai and Vespasian, the burning of the Temple, and the subsequent sufferings of the nation. At several junctures, the text pauses to interrogate its own claims:

> <span dir="rtl">ומי יימר?</span>
>
> *And who says [this is true]?*

This formula -- *u-mi yeimar?* -- is the refutation from the incredible applied *reflexively*. The Talmud does not wait for an external challenger to raise doubts about its narrative; it raises them itself, preemptively. The student should reflect on the extraordinary intellectual discipline this represents: a tradition that subjects its own sacred stories to the test of credibility, not in order to destroy them but in order to establish which elements are to be received as historical report and which as edifying narrative.

One observes nothing comparable in the Greek rhetorical handbooks, where the student is trained to refute the compositions of *others* but is not systematically taught to refute his own. The Judaean tradition, by contrast, institutionalizes self-criticism as a mode of intellectual hygiene. The sage who cannot challenge his own arguments is, in the judgment of the Talmud, like the scholar who studies alone and "grows foolish" (BT Berakhot 63b) -- his positions are untested and therefore unreliable.

#### Exercises
