**First exercise: Refute from six grounds.** Take the following maxim and compose a refutation on each of the six grounds (the unclear, the incredible, the impossible, the inconsistent, the unfitting, the inexpedient):

> <span dir="rtl">יפה שעה אחת בתשובה ומעשים טובים בעולם הזה מכל חיי העולם הבא</span>
>
> *One hour of repentance and good deeds in this world is better than all the life of the world to come.* (Avot 4:17)

From the unclear: What does "better" signify here? Better in what respect -- in intensity of experience, in moral value, in divine estimation? From the incredible: Is it truly credible that a single mortal hour surpasses the entirety of eternal existence? From the impossible: Does this maxim not contradict the second half of the same Mishnah, which declares that "one hour of spiritual bliss in the world to come is better than all the life of this world"? From the inconsistent: Does it not stand in tension with numerous other teachings that present the world to come as the supreme reward? From the unfitting: Would it not diminish the divine gift of eternal life to subordinate it to a fleeting moment? From the inexpedient: If this world is truly superior, what incentive remains for the righteous to endure suffering in anticipation of future reward?

After composing the refutation on each ground, the student should then compose an *answer* to each refutation -- defending the maxim against his own objections. The sage who can both destroy and rebuild the same position has achieved the dialectical mastery that is the crown of rhetorical training.

**Second exercise: Identify categories in a talmudic passage.** Select any sustained *sugya* (dialectical discussion) from the Babylonian Talmud and classify each objection that appears within it according to the six categories described above. The student will discover that the Talmud's objections, though not explicitly labeled by category, fall naturally into these types with remarkable consistency.

**Third exercise: Compose a formal refutation of a maxim.** Choose any maxim from Pirkei Avot and compose a systematic refutation using at least four of the six grounds. Then compose a response to each objection. The student who can refute his own position and then restore it has demonstrated command of both sides of the dialectical art.

**Fourth exercise: Self-refutation.** Compose a brief essay defending a proposition of your own choosing. Then compose a refutation of your own essay, attacking it from the most damaging grounds you can identify. Then compose a revised defense that anticipates and answers the refutation. This three-stage exercise -- defense, attack, revised defense -- trains the student to think dialectically, to anticipate objections before they are raised, and to construct arguments that have already survived the fire of challenge.[^96]

---

### II.F. Confirmation (*Kataskeue* / *Re'ayah*)

#### Definition

Confirmation stands as the necessary complement of refutation. If refutation is the art of demolition, confirmation is the art of construction. Aphthonius defines it with characteristic economy: "Confirmation is the establishment of a matter at hand" -- *kataskeue estin pragmatos parastasis*.[^97] Theon treats confirmation and refutation as a matched pair, observing that they employ the same categories with opposite valences: where refutation attacks from the unclear, confirmation defends through the clear; where refutation attacks from the incredible, confirmation supports through the probable.[^98] Hermogenes concurs, placing the two exercises in immediate sequence and prescribing that students practice both on the same material.[^99]

The student of rhetoric must understand that confirmation is not merely the absence of refutation. A proposition is not confirmed simply because no one has yet refuted it; it is confirmed when positive evidence has been assembled in its support -- evidence from authoritative sources, from logical inference, from established precedent, and from the acknowledged fitness of the proposition within the framework of accepted teaching.

We observe in the Judaean academies a confirmation apparatus of extraordinary sophistication. The formula that opens the exercise of confirmation in the Babylonian Talmud is among the most frequently encountered phrases in that vast corpus:

> <span dir="rtl">מנא הני מילי? דכתיב...</span>
>
> *From where do we know this? As it is written...*

This formula -- *mina hani milli? dikhtiv...* -- encapsulates the entire structure of the confirmation exercise in miniature: a proposition has been stated; the question of its ground is raised; and the ground is provided through scriptural citation. The student should observe that this formula presupposes a specific hierarchy of evidence: Scripture is the supreme authority, and a proposition that can be grounded in Scripture stands on the firmest possible foundation.[^100]

#### The Six Methods of Confirmation

Following the structural correspondence between refutation and confirmation articulated by Theon, we enumerate six methods of confirmation, each the inverse of a corresponding ground of refutation.[^101]

**First: From the Clear (*saphes* / *barur*).** A proposition is confirmed when its meaning is transparent and its application self-evident. The clearest form of confirmation is the explicit scriptural proof-text that directly and unambiguously supports the proposition without requiring any interpretive mediation.

The Talmudic formula:

> <span dir="rtl">שנאמר</span>
>
> *As it says...*

When the Talmud introduces a proof with *she-ne'emar* and quotes a verse whose plain sense directly supports the proposition, this is confirmation from the clear. Consider:

> <span dir="rtl">מניין שאין עומדין להתפלל אלא מתוך כובד ראש? שנאמר: עבדו את ה' ביראה</span>
>
> *From where do we know that one should not stand to pray except with a solemn disposition? As it says: "Serve the Lord with reverence" (Psalm 2:11).*

The verse directly states the principle: worship requires reverence. The correspondence between proposition and proof is transparent; no interpretive labor is required. This is confirmation at its most direct.[^102]

**Second: From the Probable (*pithanon* / *karov*).** A proposition is confirmed when it follows plausibly from what is already established, through legitimate inference rather than explicit statement. This is the domain of logical deduction -- and the Judaean tradition has developed the art of logical confirmation to a degree of refinement unmatched in any other ancient rhetorical culture.

The primary instrument of probable confirmation in Judaean discourse is the *kal va-chomer* -- the argument a fortiori, which reasons from the lesser case to the greater:

> <span dir="rtl">קל וחומר</span>
>
> *A fortiori*

This is the first of Rabbi Ishmael's thirteen hermeneutical rules, and it corresponds precisely to the rhetorical argument from probability. If a property holds in the lesser case, it certainly holds in the greater case. The classic example:

> <span dir="rtl">ומה צפרדעים שאין מצוות עליהם קידשו שם שמים, ישראל שמצוות עליהם על אחת כמה וכמה</span>
>
> *If frogs, which are not commanded, sanctified God's name [by entering the ovens of Egypt], Israel, who are commanded -- how much more so!*

The argument proceeds from the lesser case (frogs, who have no obligation to sanctify the divine name) to the greater case (Israel, who are obligated). The conclusion is not stated in any verse but follows from the logical relationship between the two cases. This is confirmation through probability -- the same category that Aristotle treats under the heading of *eikos* ("the likely") in *Rhetoric* 1.2.[^103]

The student should observe the formal structure of the *kal va-chomer*: it consists of four elements: (1) the lesser case, (2) the property attributed to the lesser case, (3) the greater case, and (4) the conclusion that the property applies a fortiori to the greater case. This four-part structure makes the *kal va-chomer* a perfect rhetorical syllogism -- compact, logical, and persuasive. The Mekhilta de-Rabbi Ishmael (Pischa 5) preserves a tradition that there are ten *kal va-chomer* arguments in the Torah itself, demonstrating that Scripture not only records truths but models the methods by which truths may be confirmed.

The student should also be aware of the *kal va-chomer*'s characteristic limitation, known as *dayyo* -- "it is sufficient." This principle holds that the conclusion of a *kal va-chomer* cannot exceed the premise from which it is drawn. If the lesser case carries penalty X, the greater case also carries penalty X -- but not penalty X-plus-Y. The *dayyo* principle restrains the a fortiori argument from producing conclusions more extreme than its evidence warrants. The student of rhetoric should recognize this as an internalized check on confirmation from probability: the Judaean tradition builds restraint into the very structure of its most powerful confirmatory tool.

**Third: From the Possible (*dunaton* / *efshar*).** A proposition is confirmed when it can be shown that no barrier exists to its truth -- that it is consistent with the natural order, with logical coherence, and with the framework of the tradition. This is the weakest form of confirmation; it does not prove that the proposition is true, only that it *could be* true. But it is a necessary first step, for a proposition shown to be possible cannot be dismissed outright.

The Talmudic formula:

> <span dir="rtl">מי לא עסקינן דאיכא...?</span>
>
> *Are we not dealing with a case where there is...?*

This response to a challenge does not prove the proposition; it demonstrates a plausible scenario under which the proposition holds. The burden then shifts back to the challenger: you have raised an objection, but I have shown a possible case. Now demonstrate impossibility, not merely implausibility.

**Fourth: From the Consistent (*akatallelos* / *mathim*).** A proposition is confirmed when it can be shown to harmonize with other established teachings -- when corroborating evidence is adduced from independent sources within the tradition.

The Talmudic formulae:

> <span dir="rtl">תניא נמי הכי</span>
>
> *A baraita also teaches thus.*

> <span dir="rtl">לימא מסייע ליה</span>
>
> *Let us say this supports him.*

Each of these introduces a corroborating source. The principle is cumulative: each additional supporting text strengthens the proposition, and a proposition confirmed by multiple independent sources acquires a weight that no single proof could provide. The student should observe the arrangement of these proofs in talmudic discourse: they are typically marshaled in ascending order of strength, so that the argument builds toward its most compelling evidence.[^104]

**Fifth: From the Fitting (*prepon* / *na'eh*).** A proposition is confirmed when it accords with the moral and aesthetic sensibilities of the community -- when it is consistent with the character of the tradition and the dignity of its subjects.

The paradigmatic example in Judaean literature is Rabbi Akiva's confirmation of the centrality of "Love your neighbor as yourself":

> <span dir="rtl">ואהבת לרעך כמוך -- אמר רבי עקיבא: זה כלל גדול בתורה</span>
>
> *"Love your neighbor as yourself" (Leviticus 19:18) -- Rabbi Akiva said: This is a great principle in the Torah.*

The confirmation operates on moral grounds: the proposition -- that love of one's fellow is the central principle of the Torah -- is confirmed because it *accords* with what the Torah is understood to be. It is fitting that a system devoted to justice and compassion should have at its center a commandment of love. The student should observe that this is not confirmation from logic or from textual proof; it is confirmation from the internal coherence of the tradition's values.

In the Sifra (Sifra Qedoshim 4:12), this confirmation is set against the alternative proposed by Ben Azzai:

> <span dir="rtl">זה ספר תולדות אדם -- בן עזאי אומר: זה כלל גדול מזה</span>
>
> *"This is the book of the generations of Adam" (Genesis 5:1) -- Ben Azzai says: This is an even greater principle than that.*

Ben Azzai grounds human dignity not in the commandment to love but in the fact of common creation. Both sages are engaged in confirmation from fittingness -- each arguing that his principle is the most consonant with the deepest commitments of the tradition. The student should note that confirmation from fittingness often involves a competition among possible truths: the question is not which proposition is true (both may be) but which is most *fitting* as the organizing principle of the whole.[^105]

**Sixth: From the Expedient (*sympheron* / *to'elet*).** A proposition is confirmed when its practical consequences are shown to be beneficial -- when following it leads to outcomes that are good for the individual and the community.

> <span dir="rtl">מפני מה נקבעה הלכה כבית הלל? מפני שנוחין ועלובין היו</span>
>
> *Why was the halakhah established according to Beit Hillel? Because they were gentle and humble.*

The halakhah follows Beit Hillel not because their arguments were logically superior -- the Talmud acknowledges that Beit Shammai's arguments were often sharper -- but because the consequences of following Beit Hillel's approach produce a tradition of gentleness, humility, and communal harmony. This is confirmation from expediency: the teaching is established because its fruits are good.[^106]

#### Extended Example: The Competition of Principles in Sifra Qedoshim

The student should study with particular care the passage in Sifra Qedoshim (parashah 4, on Leviticus 19:18) in which Rabbi Akiva and Ben Azzai debate which verse constitutes the "great principle" of the Torah. This passage is a masterclass in confirmation from fittingness, for both sages are engaged in the same rhetorical exercise -- confirming the centrality of a teaching -- but each selects a different proposition and a different scriptural ground.

Rabbi Akiva confirms "Love your neighbor as yourself" (Leviticus 19:18) as the great principle. His confirmation operates on the ground of fittingness: a system devoted to justice and compassion *ought* to have love as its organizing principle, and indeed it does. The verse is not merely a commandment among commandments; it is the commanding commandment, the principle from which all other interpersonal obligations flow.

Ben Azzai responds with an alternative:

> <span dir="rtl">זה ספר תולדות אדם ביום ברוא אלהים אדם בדמות אלהים עשה אותו</span>
>
> *"This is the book of the generations of Adam. On the day that God created man, in the likeness of God He made him." (Genesis 5:1)*

Ben Azzai confirms the dignity of every human being by grounding it not in the commandment to love (which might be limited to "your neighbor" -- your fellow Israelite) but in the creation narrative, which establishes the divine image in *all* human beings without distinction. His confirmation is likewise from fittingness, but it appeals to a different aspect of the tradition's character: its universalism rather than its particularism.

The student should observe that this passage demonstrates a sophisticated mode of confirmation in which two propositions, both true, compete for the status of most *fitting*. The debate is not about whether either proposition is false; it is about which one is the most adequate organizing principle for the whole. This is confirmation at its most philosophically mature: the question is not truth versus falsehood but adequacy versus greater adequacy.

#### The Asmakhta: A Distinctive Judaean Mode of Confirmation

The student should pay particular attention to a mode of confirmation that is, so far as the present writer is aware, distinctive to Judaean rhetorical practice: the *asmakhta*, or scriptural support for a ruling known to be of rabbinic rather than biblical origin.

> <span dir="rtl">אסמכתא בעלמא</span>
>
> *It is merely a support [not a genuine derivation].*

The asmakhta does not claim that the teaching originates in Scripture; it claims only that Scripture lends it consonance -- that the sacred text and the rabbinic ruling point in the same direction. This is a form of confirmation from consistency, but with a crucial qualification: the relationship between text and ruling is acknowledged to be supportive rather than generative. The verse does not produce the ruling; it harmonizes with it.

This distinction -- between derivation (*derasha*) and support (*asmakhta*) -- is one of the most subtle in Judaean hermeneutics, and the student who fails to grasp it will misunderstand a great deal of talmudic argumentation. The confusion of source and support is an error with practical consequences: it would attribute biblical authority to teachings that possess only rabbinic authority, thereby misrepresenting the halakhic hierarchy.[^107]

#### Exercises

**First exercise: Four-fold confirmation.** Take the following maxim and confirm it from four distinct grounds: Scripture, logical inference, narrative precedent, and appeal to authority.

> <span dir="rtl">דרך ארץ קדמה לתורה</span>
>
> *Proper conduct preceded the Torah.* (Leviticus Rabbah 9:3)

From Scripture: find a verse that supports the priority of ethical behavior over ritual obligation. From reason: construct a *kal va-chomer* or analogical argument for why ethical conduct must precede religious law. From precedent: cite a narrative in which a sage demonstrated this principle through his actions. From authority: cite an authoritative ruling that embodies this priority.

**Second exercise: Progressive confirmation.** Take any maxim from Pirkei Avot and confirm it through a sequence of proofs arranged from weakest to strongest, building toward a climax. Practice the technique of cumulative confirmation described above, observing how each successive proof augments the persuasive force of the whole.

**Third exercise: Confirmation against refutation.** Take the refutations you composed in the previous exercise (II.E) and answer each one with a specific confirmation. For every ground of attack, provide a corresponding ground of defense. The student who can both refute and confirm the same proposition has mastered the dialectical completeness that the Judaean academies call *shakla ve-tarya* -- the "give and take" of mature argumentation.

**Fourth exercise: Distinguish derivation from support.** Examine five instances in which the Talmud cites a scriptural verse in support of a ruling. For each, determine whether the verse functions as a genuine derivation (*derasha*) or as a mere support (*asmakhta*). Explain the criteria by which you make the distinction, and discuss the practical consequences of classifying the relationship incorrectly.[^108]

---

### II.G. Commonplace (*Koinos Topos* / *Mussar Kelali*)

#### Definition

The commonplace -- *koinos topos* in the terminology of the Greek schools, *mussar kelali* in the idiom of the Judaean tradition -- is a speech of amplification directed against a vice or in praise of a virtue, composed in general terms and without reference to any named individual. Theon defines it as "an amplification of admitted evils or goods."[^109] Aphthonius, with greater specificity, describes it as a discourse that "amplifies something that is acknowledged by everyone" -- the wickedness of the traitor, the glory of the benefactor -- without naming any particular traitor or benefactor.[^110] Quintilian places the commonplace among the fundamental exercises, noting that it develops the student's capacity for *amplificatio* -- the art of making what is already acknowledged to be significant appear even more significant than the listener had supposed (*Inst. Or.* 2.4.22).[^111]

The student must understand how this exercise is distinguished from the exercises that precede and follow it. The chreia attributes a teaching to a named person. The maxim states a general truth without attribution. The encomium praises a *specific* person. The vituperation blames a *specific* person. The commonplace occupies a distinctive position: it addresses a quality -- a virtue or a vice -- in universal terms, naming no individual but speaking of the quality itself as it manifests in human life generally.

We observe in the Judaean academies a rich tradition of commonplace composition -- perhaps the richest in all of ancient literature. The aggadic (non-legal) portions of the Talmud abound in sustained discourses on moral themes: extended amplifications of the evil of slander, the glory of charity, the horror of baseless hatred, the corruption of flattery. These passages follow a recognizable structure: statement of the quality, definition of its scope, amplification through consequence, scriptural proof, and concluding exhortation. The student who studies these passages with attention to their rhetorical structure will find in them a complete training in the art of moral amplification.

#### Types and Structure

The commonplace takes two fundamental forms: the speech against a vice and the speech in praise of a virtue. These are mirror images: the condemnation of miserliness is the obverse of the praise of generosity; the denunciation of falsehood is the complement of the exaltation of truth. The skilled speaker can compose both for any given pair of moral opposites.

The structure of a commonplace discourse, as practiced in the Judaean academies, typically follows this arrangement:

**First: Naming and definition.** State the quality to be discussed and define its boundaries with precision. What exactly constitutes this vice or virtue? How is it distinguished from related but distinct qualities?

**Second: Amplification through comparison.** Compare the quality to other known goods or evils, establishing its relative position in the moral hierarchy. Is this vice worse than, equal to, or less than other recognized transgressions? Is this virtue greater than, equal to, or less than other recognized excellences?

**Third: Amplification through consequence.** Show what happens -- to the individual, to the community, to the cosmic order -- when this quality is practiced. The most powerful amplifications ground their consequences in historical events: the vice *caused* a specific catastrophe; the virtue *produced* a specific redemption.

**Fourth: Scriptural proof.** Demonstrate that the assessment of this quality is grounded in the sacred text -- that God Himself condemns this vice or commends this virtue.

**Fifth: Concluding exhortation.** Urge the listener to flee from the vice or embrace the virtue. The conclusion should be direct, urgent, and memorable.[^112]

#### Judaean Examples

**Example 1: Against Evil Speech (*Lashon Hara*) -- BT Arakhin 15b**

The Talmud's treatment of evil speech in Arakhin 15b-16a is one of the most elaborate commonplace compositions in all of ancient literature. It proceeds through the prescribed stages with remarkable fidelity:

*Amplification through comparison:*

> <span dir="rtl">אמר רבי יוחנן משום רבי יוסי בן זמרא: כל המספר לשון הרע כאילו כפר בעיקר, שנאמר: אשר אמרו ללשוננו נגביר שפתינו אתנו מי אדון לנו</span>
>
> *Rabbi Yohanan said in the name of Rabbi Yosei ben Zimra: Whoever speaks evil speech is as if he denied the fundamental [existence of God], as it says: "Who said: With our tongue we will prevail; our lips are with us; who is lord over us?" (Psalm 12:5)*

The amplification begins at the highest possible level: evil speech is equated with atheism. The speaker of slander implicitly denies divine sovereignty -- "Who is lord over us?" -- and thereby commits the gravest of all transgressions. The student should observe the rhetorical strategy: by comparing the vice to the most extreme category available, the sage ensures that the listener cannot dismiss it as minor.

The amplification continues with even greater intensity:

> <span dir="rtl">אמר ריש לקיש: כל המספר לשון הרע מגדיל עוונות כנגד שלוש עבירות: עבודה זרה, וגילוי עריות, ושפיכות דמים</span>
>
> *Resh Lakish said: Whoever speaks evil speech magnifies transgressions equivalent to the three [cardinal] sins: idolatry, sexual immorality, and murder.*

Now the vice is compared not to one cardinal sin but to all three simultaneously -- the three transgressions for which, in Judaean law, a person must accept death rather than transgress. The student should note the ascending structure: first atheism, then all three cardinal sins together. Each successive comparison raises the stakes, producing a crescendo of moral horror.

*Amplification through consequence:*

> <span dir="rtl">אמר רב חסדא אמר מר עוקבא: כל המספר לשון הרע ראוי לסוקלו באבן</span>
>
> *Rav Hisda said in the name of Mar Ukva: Whoever speaks evil speech deserves to be stoned.*

The consequence is stated in the most severe terms available: death by stoning, the most public and horrifying form of capital punishment in Judaean law. The amplification is now complete: the vice has been compared to the worst sins, and its deserved punishment has been declared to be the most extreme. The student should study the entire passage (Arakhin 15b-16a), which continues at length with additional comparisons, scriptural proofs, and illustrative narratives, constituting a complete commonplace discourse of the highest order.[^113]

**Example 2: Praise of Charity (*Tzedakah*) -- BT Bava Batra 9a-10a**

The Talmud's discourse on charity is the positive counterpart of the discourse on evil speech -- a sustained amplification of a virtue that proceeds through the same rhetorical stages:

*Amplification through comparison:*

> <span dir="rtl">אמר רבי אלעזר: גדולה צדקה מכל הקרבנות, שנאמר: עשה צדקה ומשפט נבחר לה' מזבח</span>
>
> *Rabbi Elazar said: Charity is greater than all the sacrifices, as it says: "To do charity and justice is preferred by the Lord to sacrifice" (Proverbs 21:3).*

The virtue is amplified by placing it above a known good -- the sacrificial service, which is itself of immense value in the Judaean religious system. Charity surpasses even the Temple sacrifices, the most sacred rites of the cult.

*Amplification through cosmic consequence:*

> <span dir="rtl">ואמר רבי אלעזר: גדולה צדקה שמקרבת את הגאולה, שנאמר: שמרו משפט ועשו צדקה כי קרובה ישועתי לבוא</span>
>
> *And Rabbi Elazar said: Charity is great because it brings the redemption near, as it says: "Guard justice and do charity, for My salvation is close to coming" (Isaiah 56:1).*

The consequence is elevated to the cosmic plane: charity does not merely relieve individual suffering; it hastens the messianic redemption of the entire world. The individual act of giving becomes an event of eschatological significance.

> <span dir="rtl">ואמר רבי אלעזר: כל אדם שעושה צדקה ומשפט כאילו מילא כל העולם כולו חסד, שנאמר: אוהב צדקה ומשפט חסד ה' מלאה הארץ</span>
>
> *And Rabbi Elazar said: Every person who performs charity and justice is as if he filled the entire world with lovingkindness, as it says: "He loves charity and justice; the lovingkindness of the Lord fills the earth" (Psalm 33:5).*

Now the individual act of charity is compared to filling the entire world with divine lovingkindness. The student should observe the ascending structure: from greater than sacrifices, to hastening redemption, to filling the world with grace. Each successive amplification raises the virtue to a higher plane, until the listener can scarcely conceive of a higher praise.[^114]

**Example 3: Against Baseless Hatred (*Sinat Chinam*) -- BT Yoma 9b**

> <span dir="rtl">מקדש שני, שהיו עוסקים בתורה ובמצוות וגמילות חסדים, מפני מה חרב? מפני שהיתה בו שנאת חנם. ללמדך ששקולה שנאת חנם כנגד שלוש עבירות: עבודה זרה, גילוי עריות, ושפיכות דמים</span>
>
> *The Second Temple, where they were engaged in Torah, commandments, and deeds of lovingkindness -- why was it destroyed? Because there was baseless hatred among them. This teaches you that baseless hatred is equivalent to the three sins: idolatry, sexual immorality, and murder.*

This passage exemplifies the most powerful technique of commonplace amplification: the grounding of moral condemnation in historical catastrophe. Baseless hatred is not condemned in the abstract; it is identified as the cause of the most devastating event in Judaean history -- the destruction of the Second Temple and the exile that followed. The student should observe the extraordinary force this produces: the vice is not merely bad in theory; it *destroyed the Temple*. Abstract moral argument cannot compete with this kind of historical concreteness.[^115]

One notes with interest the structure of the amplification: the passage first establishes that the generation of the Second Temple was not deficient in positive qualities -- they studied Torah, observed the commandments, performed deeds of lovingkindness -- and *then* identifies the single vice that overwhelmed all these virtues. The rhetorical strategy is one of paradox: a generation rich in merit was destroyed by a single failing. This paradox amplifies the horror of the vice: if baseless hatred could overwhelm Torah, commandments, and lovingkindness combined, its destructive power must be of the most extreme order.

**Example 4: Against Flattery (*Chanufah*) -- BT Sotah 41b**

> <span dir="rtl">אמר רבי שמעון בן לקיש: כל המחניף לחבירו סוף נופל בידו</span>
>
> *Resh Lakish said: Whoever flatters his fellow will eventually fall into his hands.*

The discourse on flattery in Sotah 41b provides a commonplace of particular interest because it grounds its amplification in a specific historical incident. The passage recounts how King Agrippa, reading the Torah publicly, wept when he reached the verse "You shall not place over yourself a foreigner" (Deuteronomy 17:15) -- for he was of Herodian descent and knew himself to be of foreign origin. The assembled people called out to him: "You are our brother! You are our brother!" The Talmud records:

> <span dir="rtl">באותה שעה נתחייבו שונאיהם של ישראל כליה, שהחניפו לאגריפס</span>
>
> *At that moment, the enemies of Israel [a euphemism for Israel itself] were condemned to destruction, because they flattered Agrippa.*

The amplification is devastating: a single act of public flattery -- telling the king what he wished to hear rather than what the Torah required -- brought upon the nation a sentence of destruction. The student should observe how this passage combines the abstract commonplace (flattery is destructive) with the specific historical exemplum (the flattery of Agrippa destroyed the nation), producing a discourse that is simultaneously universal in its moral reach and particular in its evidential force.[^116]

#### The Distinctive Force of Judaean Commonplace Discourse

One notes with interest a structural feature that distinguishes Judaean commonplace discourse from its Greek counterpart. In the Greek handbooks -- Aphthonius's model commonplace against a tyrant, for instance -- the amplification proceeds through abstract categories: the tyrant is condemned for violating justice, for corrupting the state, for betraying the public trust. The Judaean commonplace, by contrast, almost invariably grounds its amplification in *historical consequence*. Evil speech is not merely bad in principle; it is "equivalent to the three cardinal sins." Baseless hatred is not merely deplorable in theory; it "destroyed the Second Temple." Flattery is not merely a character flaw; it "condemned Israel to destruction."

This habitual recourse to historical catastrophe as the ground of moral amplification gives Judaean commonplace discourse a weight and concreteness that abstract moralizing cannot match. The listener who is unmoved by the theoretical condemnation of a vice may be shaken by the reminder that this same vice destroyed the Temple. The abstract becomes concrete; the timeless becomes historical; the general becomes particular -- and the commonplace speech, paradoxically, acquires the specificity and vividness more commonly associated with the narrative or the encomium.

The student should also observe the characteristic Judaean technique of *ascending amplification*: each successive comparison or consequence raises the stakes beyond what the previous one established. Evil speech is compared first to atheism, then to all three cardinal sins, then to deserving stoning. Charity is compared first to the sacrifices, then to bringing redemption, then to filling the world with divine lovingkindness. This ascending structure is not accidental; it is a deliberate rhetorical strategy that exploits the listener's capacity for escalating astonishment. The first comparison surprises; the second astounds; the third overwhelms. By the time the discourse reaches its climax, the listener's resistance has been overcome through sheer accumulation of force.

#### Exercises

**First exercise: Amplification of a virtue.** Compose a commonplace speech praising the virtue of hospitality (*hakhnasat orchim*). Follow the five-part structure: (1) define the virtue; (2) amplify through comparison -- establish that hospitality is greater than some other recognized virtue (the tradition states that hospitality to wayfarers is greater even than receiving the Divine Presence; see BT Shabbat 127a); (3) amplify through consequence -- show the rewards that attend the hospitable; (4) provide scriptural proof from the narrative of Abraham greeting the three visitors (Genesis 18); (5) conclude with exhortation.

**Second exercise: Amplification of a vice.** Compose a commonplace speech condemning miserliness (*tzarut ayin* -- "narrowness of the eye"). Follow the same five-part structure, drawing on the contrast between the generous eye and the evil eye in Avot, the consequences of refusing to give charity, and the scriptural condemnation of the miser.

**Third exercise: Paired compositions.** Compose both a speech praising generosity and a speech condemning miserliness. Observe how the two compositions mirror each other and how the arguments employed for one can be adapted, with inverted valence, into arguments against the other. The student should aim for structural symmetry between the two speeches, so that each point of praise in the first has a corresponding point of blame in the second.

**Fourth exercise: Historical amplification.** Choose a vice condemned in the Talmud and compose a commonplace speech that grounds its condemnation in a specific historical catastrophe -- following the model of the discourse on baseless hatred and the destruction of the Temple. Explain, in a brief analytical appendix, why the historical ground makes the speech more powerful than an abstract moral argument alone.[^117]

---

### II.H. Encomium (*Enkomion* / *Shevach*)

#### Definition

The encomium -- *enkomion* in the terminology of the Greek schools, *shevach* or *hesped* in the idiom of the Judaean tradition -- is a formal speech of praise composed according to prescribed categories and directed toward a specific subject: a person, a place, a time, or an institution. Theon defines it as "speech revealing the greatness of virtuous actions and other good qualities belonging to a particular person."[^118] Aphthonius provides a detailed structural template, prescribing the order in which the topoi of praise should be arranged.[^119] Menander Rhetor, in his treatises on epideictic oratory, offers the most comprehensive treatment, extending the encomium to encompass the praise of cities, lands, harbors, seasons, and festivals.[^120]

The student must understand how the encomium is distinguished from the commonplace. The commonplace praises patience in general; the encomium praises the patience of *Hillel* in particular. The commonplace condemns treachery in general; the encomium (or, more precisely, its counterpart the vituperation) condemns the treachery of *Korach* in particular. The encomium is always specific: it names its subject, assembles evidence particular to that subject, and arrives at a judgment of praise through the accumulation of concrete detail. Where the commonplace speaks in universals, the encomium speaks in particulars.

#### The Topoi of Praise

The classical handbooks prescribe a standard sequence of topoi -- categories of praise -- that the encomiast should follow in composing his speech. Theon, Aphthonius, and Menander Rhetor are in substantial agreement on the main divisions, though they differ on points of detail. The sequence, as practiced in both the Greek schools and (with remarkable fidelity) in the Judaean academies, is as follows:[^121]

**First: Origin (*toledot*).** The subject's ancestry, family, nation, and native place. Noble ancestry is a ground of praise; the encomiast establishes that the subject comes from worthy stock and inherits a tradition of excellence.

**Second: Upbringing and Education (*giddulo ve-chinukho*).** The subject's formation -- under whose guidance he was trained, what studies he pursued, what virtues he acquired in youth. The encomiast shows that the subject was shaped by worthy influences and responded to them with diligence.

**Third: Deeds (*ma'asav*).** The subject's accomplishments -- his rulings, his teachings, his acts of piety, his service to the community. This is typically the longest section of the encomium and the one that bears the greatest rhetorical weight.

**Fourth: Fortune and Divine Favor (*hatzlachato*).** The evidences of providence in the subject's life -- the obstacles he overcame, the successes he achieved against the odds, the signs of divine approval that attended his career.

**Fifth: Comparison (*dimyon*).** The subject is compared to acknowledged exemplars of virtue -- to prior sages, to biblical heroes, to paradigms of excellence. The comparison elevates the subject by placing him in distinguished company.

**Sixth: Death and Legacy (*mitato u-yerushat zikhro*).** The manner of the subject's death, the grief of the community, and the enduring influence of his life. The encomiast shows that the subject's legacy outlives him and that his memory remains a source of blessing.

We observe in the Judaean academies that the *hesped* -- the formal eulogy delivered at the death of a sage -- follows these topoi with a fidelity that is, from the perspective of the comparativist, genuinely remarkable. Whether this fidelity reflects direct knowledge of the Greek rhetorical handbooks, independent development of the same formal categories, or (most probably) a combination of both, is a question that the present work does not undertake to resolve. The structural parallel is sufficient for our purposes; the historical explanation may be left to others.[^122]

#### Types of Encomium

The encomium is not limited to the praise of persons. The Judaean tradition recognizes (and practices with great frequency) at least four types:

**Praise of Sages.** This is the most common type: the eulogy delivered at the death of a master, the biographical portrait embedded in the chain of tradition, the formulaic praise that preserves a sage's distinctive quality for posterity.

**Praise of Places.** Jerusalem, the Land of Israel, the Temple, the bet midrash -- all receive encomiastic treatment in the rabbinic corpus.

**Praise of Times.** The Shabbat, the festivals, the world to come -- each is praised in language that follows the encomiastic pattern.

**Praise of Virtues and Institutions.** The Torah itself, the institution of marriage, the covenant of circumcision -- these too are subjects of formal praise.

#### Judaean Examples

**Example 1: Eulogies of the Sages -- BT Mo'ed Qatan 25a-b**

The Talmud in tractate Mo'ed Qatan preserves a collection of eulogistic formulae composed for the deaths of major sages -- a collection that constitutes, in effect, a small anthology of miniature encomia. Each formula captures the sage's distinctive quality in a concentrated, poetic utterance:

> <span dir="rtl">כי נח נפשיה דרבי אבהו, איטפטפו עמודי דקיסרי מיא</span>
>
> *When Rabbi Abbahu died, the pillars of Caesarea dripped water [wept].*

> <span dir="rtl">כי נח נפשיה דרב הונא, תליסר נהרוותא דמשחא ודובשא איעכור</span>
>
> *When Rav Huna died, thirteen rivers of oil and honey were obstructed.*

These formulae employ the technique of pathetic fallacy on a cosmic scale: the pillars of a city weep; rivers of oil and honey are dammed. The natural order itself is disrupted by the sage's death, indicating that the loss extends beyond the human community to the fabric of creation. The student should observe that these formulae are not meant as literal reports; they are exercises in epideictic hyperbole, designed to express the magnitude of communal grief through the language of cosmic sympathy.

And the classic formula that compresses the entire logic of the rabbinic encomium into a single metaphor:

> <span dir="rtl">אם בארזים נפלה שלהבת, מה יעשו אזובי הקיר?</span>
>
> *If a flame has fallen among the cedars, what shall the hyssop of the wall do?*

The comparison (cedars to hyssop, the great to the humble) accomplishes the work of an extended encomium in a single sentence: the deceased was a cedar, we are hyssop; if even he fell, what hope have we? The formula simultaneously praises the dead (as a cedar) and exhorts the living (to take instruction from his mortality).[^123]

**Example 2: Praise of Jerusalem -- BT Kiddushin 49b**

> <span dir="rtl">עשרה קבין יופי ירדו לעולם, תשעה נטלה ירושלים ואחד כל העולם כולו</span>
>
> *Ten measures of beauty descended to the world; nine were taken by Jerusalem and one by the rest of the world.*

This is the encomium of a place accomplished through the technique of numerical proportion. The student should observe the extraordinary rhetorical economy of this formula: it does not describe Jerusalem's beauty in detail (though other texts do this at length); it establishes its *proportion* -- nine-tenths of all the beauty in the world is concentrated in this single city. The hearer's imagination is more powerfully engaged by this numerical claim than it would be by any catalogue of lovely features, for the number forces the mind to contemplate an almost inconceivable concentration of beauty.

The same formula is extended to other qualities:

> <span dir="rtl">עשרה קבין חכמה ירדו לעולם, תשעה נטלה ארץ ישראל ואחד כל העולם כולו</span>
>
> *Ten measures of wisdom descended to the world; nine were taken by the Land of Israel and one by the rest of the world.*

The repetition of the formula with different qualities (beauty, wisdom, suffering, and others in the full text) builds a cumulative portrait of superlative character. Each iteration adds another dimension to the encomium, and the aggregate effect is one of overwhelming distinction. The student should observe the correspondence with Menander Rhetor's instructions for the praise of cities: Menander prescribes that the encomiast should treat the city's beauty, its wisdom, its climate, its situation, its institutions -- precisely the categories that the Judaean text addresses through its numerical formula.[^124]

**Example 3: Praise of Shabbat -- BT Shabbat 119a**

> <span dir="rtl">אמר רבי חנינא: בואו ונצא לקראת כלה, לקראת שבת מלכתא. רבי ינאי לביש מאניה מעלי שבתא ואמר: בואי כלה, בואי כלה</span>
>
> *Rabbi Hanina said: Come, let us go out to greet the bride, to greet the Sabbath queen. Rabbi Yannai would don his garments on Sabbath eve and say: Come, O bride; come, O bride.*

This is the encomium of a time -- the Shabbat -- accomplished through the rhetorical technique of personification. The Shabbat, an abstract division of time, is transformed into a person: a bride, a queen, greeted with ceremony and desire. The personification converts the listener's relationship to the Shabbat from obligation to love, from duty to delight. The student should observe that this transformation is not merely ornamental; it is constitutive of a distinct theological posture -- one in which the sacred time is not endured but *welcomed*, not observed but *embraced*.

The Talmud continues with a narrative that functions as an encomiastic illustration:

> <span dir="rtl">שני מלאכי השרת מלוין לו לאדם בערב שבת מבית הכנסת לביתו, אחד טוב ואחד רע. כשבא לביתו ומצא נר דלוק ושולחן ערוך ומיטה מוצעת, מלאך טוב אומר: יהי רצון שתהא כן לשבת אחרת, ומלאך רע עונה אמן בעל כורחו</span>
>
> *Two ministering angels accompany a person on Sabbath eve from the synagogue to his home, one good and one evil. When he arrives home and finds the lamp lit, the table set, and the bed made, the good angel says: "May it be God's will that it be so next Shabbat," and the evil angel answers "amen" against his will.*

This narrative encomium combines praise with instruction: by praising the well-prepared Shabbat table, the narrative implicitly teaches the listener how to prepare. The encomiastic form becomes a vehicle for paraenesis -- the praise of the ideal simultaneously prescribes the practice. The student should note the detail of the evil angel compelled to say "amen against his will": even the adversary cannot withhold assent from the beauty of proper Shabbat observance. This is amplification through the testimony of the reluctant witness -- a particularly effective technique, for praise from an unwilling source carries greater weight than praise from a sympathetic one. Quintilian makes a related observation when he notes that concessions from one's opponents are among the most powerful forms of proof, precisely because they come from a hostile source (*Inst. Or.* 5.7.1-3).[^125]

One notes with further interest the Talmud's extension of the Shabbat encomium through the motif of the special Shabbat food (BT Shabbat 119a): when a Roman emperor inquires about the superior flavor of Sabbath food, the sage replies that "we have one spice, and Shabbat is its name." This anecdote transforms the encomium of a time into a narrative demonstration. The abstract quality being praised (the sanctity and delight of Shabbat) is given sensory embodiment: it can be *tasted*. The concreteness of the gustatory image (flavor, spice) accomplishes what abstract theological language could not: it makes the holiness of Shabbat available to the bodily senses, and thereby makes the encomium accessible even to the uninitiated listener.

**Example 4: The Topoi of Praise Applied to a Single Figure -- Hillel the Elder**

The student should observe how the biographical traditions about Hillel the Elder, scattered across multiple talmudic passages, collectively form a complete encomium following the classical topoi:

*Origins:* Hillel came from Babylonia, from a family tracing its lineage to the house of David (BT Ketubot 62b; BT Sanhedrin 98b). His origins establish both geographic distance (he traveled far for Torah) and noble ancestry.

*Upbringing and Education:* The famous narrative of his poverty -- "Hillel used to work and earn a half-dinar each day, half of which he gave to the gatekeeper of the house of study and half he used for the sustenance of his household" (BT Yoma 35b) -- establishes his extraordinary dedication to learning despite material deprivation. The narrative of his near-death on the roof of the bet midrash, buried in snow because he could not afford the entrance fee, confirms his devotion beyond all reasonable limits.

*Deeds:* His teachings fill tractate Avot (1:12-14, 2:4-7), and his legal rulings restructured Judaean civil law (the *prozbul* of BT Gittin 36a). His paradigmatic patience -- tested three times by the man who sought to make him angry on the eve of Shabbat and failed (BT Shabbat 31a) -- establishes him as the exemplar of the virtue he preached.

*Comparison:* "Hillel and Shammai received from Shemayah and Avtalion" (Avot 1:12) -- the comparison with Shammai is built into the structure of the tradition itself, and Hillel is consistently presented as the gentler, more accessible, more ultimately authoritative of the two.

*Legacy:* "When Hillel died, they said of him: Alas, the pious one! Alas, the humble one! Disciple of Ezra!" (BT Sotah 48b; Tosefta Sotah 13:3). The eulogistic formula connects him backward (to Ezra) and forward (to all subsequent generations who follow Beit Hillel).

The student should note how these dispersed traditions, when assembled, produce a complete encomium following the classical topoi -- a fact that suggests either conscious imitation of the encomiastic pattern or the independent discovery of the same analytical categories.

**Example 5: The Structure of the Hesped -- Eulogistic Practice**

The halakhah governing the *hesped* reveals the seriousness with which the Judaean tradition takes the art of the encomium:

> <span dir="rtl">מוסיפין על השבח אבל לא על הגנאי</span>
>
> *One may add to the praise but not to the blame.*

And:

> <span dir="rtl">אין מגדילין יותר מדאי, ואם היה אדם גדול מגדילין</span>
>
> *One should not exaggerate excessively, but if the person was great, one may magnify [his qualities].*

These prescriptions establish a fundamental principle of epideictic rhetoric: the encomiast may amplify what is genuinely present but may not fabricate what is absent. This principle corresponds precisely to Quintilian's rule: "It is permissible to amplify what is good, but not to invent what does not exist" (*Inst. Or.* 3.7.21-23). The distinction between amplification and fabrication is the ethical boundary of all epideictic speech, and the student must internalize it before attempting any composition of praise.[^126]

#### Exercises

**First exercise: Full encomium of a sage.** Compose an encomium of your teacher following the prescribed topoi: origins, upbringing and education, deeds, fortune, comparison, and death/legacy. Include at least one scriptural proof, one illustrative narrative, and one comparison to a prior sage. The composition should be extended (no fewer than thirty lines) and should demonstrate mastery of the ascending amplification characteristic of the Judaean hesped.

**Second exercise: Encomium of a place.** Compose a praise of the bet midrash -- the house of study -- using the techniques of numerical proportion, personification, and comparison. Show why this place is uniquely worthy of honor, employing the formula "ten measures of X descended to the world; nine were taken by the house of study."

**Third exercise: Encomium of a time.** Compose a praise of the Shabbat or of a festival, using the technique of personification. Transform the abstract (a division of time) into the concrete (a beloved figure), and construct your praise around the relationship between the speaker and the personified time. The student should aim for the warmth and intimacy of the Shabbat-bride imagery in BT Shabbat 119a.

**Fourth exercise: Eulogy with restraint.** Compose a eulogy for a sage whose qualities were mixed -- who had great learning but also notable failings. Practice the art of amplifying the good without fabricating, and of acknowledging the real without destroying the honor of the dead. This exercise, the most difficult of the four, requires the union of technical skill with moral judgment that is the hallmark of the mature rhetorician.[^127]

---

### II.I. Vituperation (*Psogos* / *Genai*)

#### Definition

The ninth exercise in the sequence of preliminary training is vituperation -- the formal speech of blame, composed according to prescribed categories and directed against a specific subject. Theon defines it as the exact mirror of the encomium: "Vituperation is the opposite of encomium; for it reveals the baseness of actions and other bad qualities."[^128] Aphthonius follows the same principle, providing a model vituperation of Philip of Macedon that inverts every category of his preceding model encomium: where the encomium traced noble ancestry, the vituperation exposes base origins; where the encomium praised virtuous education, the vituperation reveals corrupt training; where the encomium celebrated great deeds, the vituperation catalogs wicked ones.[^129]

The student of rhetoric must understand that vituperation is not mere invective. Invective is the outpouring of uncontrolled anger; vituperation is the disciplined construction of blame according to analytical categories. The distinction is as fundamental as that between wild gesticulation and choreographed dance: both involve movement, but only the latter is art.

One observes that in the Greek rhetorical tradition, the symmetry between encomium and vituperation is treated as complete and unproblematic. Aphthonius devotes essentially equal space to both, and his model vituperation of Philip of Macedon is composed with the same technical polish as his model encomium of Thucydides. The assumption is that praise and blame are two sides of a single coin, and the student should be equally proficient in both. The Judaean tradition, as we shall see, accepts the formal symmetry but introduces a radical asymmetry in the *ethics* of deployment. The Judaean student learns to compose blame with the same technical proficiency as praise -- but he is taught that deploying blame is far more dangerous, far more morally consequential, and far more strictly regulated than deploying praise. This ethical asymmetry between formally symmetrical exercises is one of the most distinctive features of Judaean rhetorical culture.

#### The Judaean Approach: Extraordinary Caution

One notes with particular interest -- and it is a point of the first importance for understanding Judaean rhetorical culture -- that the Judaean tradition surrounds the exercise of vituperation with ethical restraints that have no precise parallel in the Greek handbooks. The Greek teachers present praise and blame as symmetrical exercises, equal in pedagogical weight and rhetorical dignity. The Judaean tradition, while preserving and practicing the art of blame, restricts its deployment with a severity that amounts to a distinct ethical philosophy of speech.

The foundational restriction is this:

> <span dir="rtl">כל המלבין פני חבירו ברבים כאילו שופך דמים</span>
>
> *Whoever shames his fellow in public is as if he shed blood.*

This teaching equates public shaming with homicide -- not metaphorically but in terms of its moral gravity. The visible blanching of the shamed person's face (the blood drains from the skin, leaving it white) provides the physical analogy: the one who shames another drains his lifeblood. The student should reflect on the implications of this equation for the exercise of vituperation. If public blame is equivalent to bloodshed, then the rhetorician who composes a vituperation is, in a sense, handling a lethal weapon. The weapon must be handled with the care and discipline appropriate to its destructive power.[^130]

The restriction extends further:

> <span dir="rtl">כל היורד לגיהנם עולה, חוץ משלושה שיורדים ואינם עולים: הבא על אשת איש, והמלבין פני חבירו ברבים, והמכנה שם רע לחבירו</span>
>
> *All who descend to Gehinnom ascend [eventually], except for three who descend and do not ascend: one who commits adultery, one who shames his fellow in public, and one who gives his fellow a derogatory nickname.*

Public shaming is here placed among the three sins that merit eternal damnation -- a category from which even murder is absent. The student should ponder the significance of this ranking: in the Judaean moral taxonomy, the destruction of a person's reputation is worse than the destruction of his body. The body can be buried and mourned; the reputation, once destroyed in public, cannot be restored. This is why the Judaean tradition treats vituperation with a caution that the Greek tradition does not typically share.[^131]

And there is a further principle that governs the speech of blame directed at the deceased:

> <span dir="rtl">אין מספרין בגנותו של מת</span>
>
> *One does not speak of the disgrace of the dead.*

The dead cannot repent; therefore blame directed at them serves no corrective purpose. Vituperation is justified only when it serves a constructive end -- the correction of the living, the prevention of future harm, the instruction of the community. Blame for its own sake, or for the pleasure of the speaker, is always and absolutely forbidden.

The student of rhetoric must therefore understand that in the Judaean tradition, vituperation is not merely a rhetorical exercise; it is a moral test. The speaker who composes blame must ask himself: Am I speaking for the sake of instruction, or for the sake of gratification? Am I wielding this weapon to protect the community, or to indulge my own anger? The failure to ask these questions -- or the failure to answer them honestly -- transforms the rhetorician from a craftsman into a criminal.[^132]

#### The Permissible Domain: Biblical Villains

Despite these stringent restrictions, the Judaean tradition *does* preserve sustained and vigorous vituperations -- directed not at living persons or at the recently deceased, but at paradigmatic villains of sacred history whose wickedness is established by Scripture and whose condemnation serves the permanent instruction of the community. The vituperation of biblical villains is permissible precisely because it is *didactic*: the purpose is not to wound (the subjects are long dead) but to teach (the listeners are living and in need of warning).

The structure of the permissible vituperation mirrors that of the encomium, inverted:

**Base Origins.** The vituperated figure is shown to come from an unworthy or compromised background.

**Corrupt Education.** His training or upbringing is shown to have been misdirected, producing skills that were deployed for evil purposes.

**Wicked Deeds.** His actions are cataloged in ascending order of gravity, building toward a climax of moral outrage.

**Deserved Misfortune.** His downfall is presented as the just consequence of his wickedness, following the principle of measure-for-measure punishment.

**Warning.** The discourse concludes not with mourning but with admonition: let all who hear take instruction from this villain's fate.

#### Judaean Examples

**Example 1: Vituperation of Korach -- Numbers Rabbah 18**

The midrashic treatment of Korach in Numbers Rabbah is a sustained vituperation that follows the classical structure with precision that the student of comparative rhetoric will find illuminating.

*Base Origins (inverted genealogy):*

> <span dir="rtl">ויקח קרח בן יצהר בן קהת בן לוי -- ולמה לא נאמר בן יעקב? שהיה יעקב מבקש רחמים על עצמו שלא יזכר שמו על מחלוקתם, שנאמר: בקהלם אל תחד כבודי</span>
>
> *"Korach son of Izhar son of Kohath son of Levi" -- and why does it not say "son of Jacob"? Because Jacob had prayed for mercy upon himself that his name not be mentioned in connection with their dispute, as it says: "In their assembly, let my honor not be united" (Genesis 49:6).*

The genealogy, which in an encomium would be a source of distinction, is here turned against the subject. The lineage is truncated -- it stops short of Jacob -- because the ancestor himself recoiled from his descendant's wickedness. The encomiastic topos of noble ancestry is inverted: the ancestry is noble, but the ancestor repudiates the descendant. This is a technique of exceptional subtlety: the villainy is amplified precisely by the nobility of the family from which the villain has fallen.[^133]

*Corrupt education (misused intelligence):*

> <span dir="rtl">קרח שפיקח היה, מה ראה לשטות הזה? עינו הטעתו. ראה שלשלת גדולה עומדת ממנו -- שמואל ששקול כמשה ואהרן</span>
>
> *Korach, who was clever -- what did he see that led him to this folly? His eye deceived him. He saw a great chain [of descendants] arising from him -- Samuel, who was equal to Moses and Aaron.*

The midrash concedes Korach's intelligence -- and then demonstrates how that intelligence was corrupted by ambition. Korach foresaw that his descendant Samuel would be a great leader, and he concluded that he himself deserved leadership now. The vituperation gains force from the initial concession: the subject was not a fool but a brilliant man whose brilliance was perverted by envy. A wicked fool is pitiable; a wicked genius is terrifying.

*Wicked deeds (sophistic rhetoric):*

> <span dir="rtl">מה עשה קרח? עמד וכנס מאתיים וחמישים ראשי סנהדראות... והלבישן טליתות שכולן תכלת. באו ועמדו לפני משה. אמרו לו: טלית שכולה תכלת מהו שתהא חייבת בציצית?</span>
>
> *What did Korach do? He stood and gathered two hundred and fifty heads of courts... and dressed them in cloaks entirely of blue wool. They came and stood before Moses. They said to him: A cloak that is entirely blue -- is it still required to have a fringe?*

The question is a rhetorical trap -- a piece of sophistry designed to make the law of the fringes appear absurd. If a cloak is entirely blue, why does it need a blue thread? The challenge uses the forms of legal argument against the legal system itself. The midrash presents this as the paradigm of misused rhetorical skill: the art of speech deployed in the service of rebellion rather than truth. The student should observe that Korach is condemned not for lacking rhetorical ability but for *possessing* it and using it destructively. The vituperation of Korach is, among other things, a warning about the moral responsibilities of the trained rhetorician.[^134]

**Example 2: The Generation of the Flood -- Genesis Rabbah 26-28**

The midrashic treatment of the antediluvian generation provides a vituperation of a *collective* subject -- a type, rather than an individual. The structure nevertheless follows the standard pattern:

*Wicked deeds (institutionalized vice):*

> <span dir="rtl">וירא ה' כי רבה רעת האדם בארץ -- רבי ברכיה אמר: שהיו כותבים גמומסיות לזכר ולנקבה</span>
>
> *"And the Lord saw that the wickedness of man was great upon the earth" (Genesis 6:5) -- Rabbi Berekhiah said: They used to write marriage contracts for [unions between] male and male, and [between] female and female.*

The amplification proceeds by showing that the generation's vice was not merely practiced but *institutionalized*: they formalized their transgressions in legal documents. The vice was not a matter of private weakness but of public policy. The student should observe how the detail of the marriage contracts transforms the vituperation: a generation that sins privately may repent privately; a generation that codifies its sins has elevated them to the status of social norm, making repentance nearly impossible.

*Deserved misfortune (measure for measure):*

> <span dir="rtl">הם חטאו ברותחין, ונידונו ברותחין</span>
>
> *They sinned with boiling [passion], and they were punished with boiling [waters].*

The principle of measure-for-measure punishment (*middah keneged middah*) is here deployed as a confirmation of the justice of the vituperation. The punishment mirrors the sin in its essential quality (boiling), demonstrating that the blame is proportionate and the downfall deserved. The student should observe the rhetorical force of this correspondence: the punishment is not arbitrary but mirrors the crime, and the mirroring itself serves as a silent argument for divine justice.[^135]

**Example 3: Doeg and Ahitophel -- BT Sanhedrin 106b**

> <span dir="rtl">דואג ואחיתופל שנו ארבע מאות שכלא דטומאה</span>
>
> *Doeg and Ahitophel studied four hundred types of impure reasoning.*

The Talmud's vituperation of Doeg and Ahitophel is of particular interest to the student of rhetoric because it is, at its core, a vituperation of *misused rhetorical and intellectual ability*. Both figures possessed extraordinary gifts: Doeg was a formidable legal scholar, Ahitophel a counselor whose advice was "as if one had inquired of the oracle of God" (2 Samuel 16:23). But their gifts were deployed in the service of evil: Doeg informed against the priests of Nob, leading to their massacre (1 Samuel 22); Ahitophel counseled Absalom's rebellion against David (2 Samuel 15-17).

The student should observe the structural logic of the vituperation: first, the subjects' extraordinary abilities are established (they mastered four hundred modes of argument); then their perversion of those abilities is exposed (they turned their learning to "impure" purposes). The contrast between ability and application is the engine of the amplification: the greater the gift, the greater the blame for its misuse. A mediocre mind that turns to evil is merely unfortunate; a brilliant mind that turns to evil is monstrous.

The Talmud concludes with a practical observation that extracts a lesson from the vituperation:

> <span dir="rtl">ואמר רב יהודה אמר רב: אל ימנה אדם בביתו פרנס בנחת, שאלמלא נתמנה אחיתופל כראוי, לא בא לידי כך</span>
>
> *Rav Yehudah said in the name of Rav: A person should not appoint a leader in his household casually, for had Ahitophel been appointed properly, he would not have come to this.*

The vituperation concludes not with mere condemnation but with a practical lesson: misplaced talent leads to corruption. The blame is partially redirected from the individual (Ahitophel) to the conditions that enabled his corruption (improper appointment). The student should observe how this conclusion transforms the vituperation from a purely retrospective exercise (condemning the past) into a prospective one (warning the future). This is the mark of didactic vituperation as practiced in the Judaean tradition: blame that instructs.[^136]

**Example 4: The Paradigm of Perverted Speech -- Balaam (Sanhedrin 105a-106b)**

The Talmud's treatment of Balaam provides a vituperation that is of particular interest because the subject's primary sin is *rhetorical* -- the misuse of the power of speech. Balaam is presented as one who possessed the divine gift of prophecy and deployed it for mercenary purposes:

> <span dir="rtl">בלעם הוא שנאמר בו נופל וגלוי עינים</span>
>
> *Balaam, of whom it is said: "falling down, but having his eyes open" (Numbers 24:4).*

The Talmud constructs the vituperation around the paradox of Balaam's gifts: he was a prophet -- "falling down" in prophetic trance, "having his eyes open" to divine vision -- and yet he used this prophetic capacity to counsel the seduction of Israel (Numbers 31:16). The vituperation gains its force from the magnitude of the gift that was perverted. As with Doeg and Ahitophel, the condemnation is sharpened by the initial concession of extraordinary ability.

The student should observe how this vituperation reinforces the ethical framework of the entire rhetorical tradition: the gift of speech -- whether prophetic, forensic, or deliberative -- is a trust, and its misuse is among the gravest of transgressions. Balaam, Korach, Doeg, Ahitophel: each is condemned not for lacking eloquence but for possessing it and turning it to destruction. The Judaean tradition of vituperation is, at its deepest level, a warning about the moral responsibilities of the trained speaker.

#### The Ethical Limit: "Do Not Rejoice When Your Enemy Falls"

The entire Judaean tradition of vituperation operates under the governance of a single ethical principle, drawn from the Book of Proverbs and cited in Pirkei Avot:

> <span dir="rtl">בנפול אויבך אל תשמח ובכשלו אל יגל לבך</span>
>
> *Do not rejoice when your enemy falls, and let your heart not be glad when he stumbles.* (Proverbs 24:17; cited Avot 4:19)

This verse establishes the fundamental boundary of all vituperative speech: even when blame is warranted, even when punishment is deserved, the speaker must not take pleasure in it. The exercise of blame is performed, when it must be performed, with sorrow rather than satisfaction, with reluctance rather than relish. The sage who composes a vituperation with visible enjoyment has already failed the exercise -- not on technical grounds, but on moral ones. The technical mastery of blame must be accompanied by the moral reluctance to deploy it.

The student should also note the practical consequence: a vituperation delivered with evident pleasure forfeits the audience's sympathy. The listener who perceives that the speaker is enjoying the destruction of another's reputation will instinctively recoil, suspecting malice rather than instruction. The moral restraint is therefore also a rhetorical advantage: the speaker who blames reluctantly is more credible than the speaker who blames eagerly. Quintilian makes a similar observation when he notes that the orator who appears to take pleasure in his opponent's misfortunes alienates the judges (*Inst. Or.* 6.1.28).[^137]

#### Exercises

**First exercise: Controlled vituperation of a biblical villain.** Compose a formal vituperation of Pharaoh following the five-part structure: base origins (his claim to divinity; Ezekiel 29:3, "I made myself"), corrupt education (the sorcerers and their deceptions), wicked deeds (enslavement, the decree to drown the infants), deserved misfortune (the ten plagues, the drowning at the Sea of Reeds), and warning (let all tyrants learn from his fate). The composition should be vigorous in its condemnation but measured in its tone. At no point should the speaker appear to take pleasure in Pharaoh's downfall.

**Second exercise: Identify the limits.** After composing your vituperation of Pharaoh, review the composition and mark every point where the ethical restraints of the Judaean tradition apply. Where does condemnation risk becoming personal hatred? Where does amplification risk becoming fabrication? Where does eloquent blame risk becoming a source of enjoyment for the speaker? Annotate your composition with these self-critical observations.

**Third exercise: Vituperation with counterbalance.** Compose a vituperation of Esau as he appears in rabbinic tradition -- but include, within the same composition, the tradition's acknowledgment of Esau's virtues. The Talmud credits Esau with extraordinary honor toward his father Isaac; certain midrashic sources acknowledge his physical courage and his tears when the blessing was taken. The student should compose a discourse that condemns the predominant wickedness while conceding the occasional virtue, holding blame and acknowledgment in productive tension. This is the most demanding form of the exercise, for it requires the rhetorician to resist the natural momentum of his own argument -- to pause, mid-condemnation, and grant his subject a measure of human complexity.

**Fourth exercise: Vituperation into commonplace.** Take your vituperation of a specific biblical figure and extract from it a commonplace -- a general discourse on the vice that the figure exemplifies. Strip away the specific person and speak of the vice in universal terms. The student should compose both versions (the specific vituperation and the general commonplace) and then write a brief analytical comparison explaining what is gained and what is lost in the transition from the particular to the universal.[^138]

[^69]: Theon, *Progymnasmata* 3 (Hock and O'Neil, *The Chreia in Ancient Rhetoric*, vol. 1, 62-66). The definition quoted here is drawn from Theon's extended treatment of the *gnome*, which he carefully distinguishes from the chreia. Theon observes that "the chreia differs from the gnome in that the chreia is always attributed to a character, while the gnome is stated without attribution."

[^70]: Quintilian, *Institutio Oratoria* 8.5.3-8. Quintilian treats the *sententia* (his Latin equivalent of the Greek *gnome*) as "a general statement about life" (*vox universalis*) and provides an extensive typology of its forms, noting that maxims may be simple or supported by a reason, and may state what is or urge what should be.

[^71]: Aristotle, *Rhetoric* 2.21, 1394a19-1395b20. Aristotle's treatment of the *gnome* is the philosophical foundation for all subsequent discussions in the rhetorical handbooks. He distinguishes maxims from enthymemes, noting that the maxim is the premise or conclusion of an enthymeme stated without the accompanying syllogistic structure.

[^72]: On the halakhic significance of attributed versus unattributed traditions, see Halivni, *Mekorot u-Mesorot: Bava Metzia* (Jerusalem: Magnes, 2003), introduction; and Hidary, *Rabbis and Classical Rhetoric: Rhetorical Models of Biblical Interpretation in the Talmud and Midrash* (Cambridge: Cambridge University Press, 2018), 155-162, on the rhetorical distinction between personal authority (chreia) and collective authority (maxim/*stam mishnah*).

[^73]: Hermogenes, *Progymnasmata* 4 (Kennedy, *Progymnasmata: Greek Textbooks of Prose Composition and Rhetoric*, 78-79), distinguishes declarative (*apophantike*), hortatory (*protreptike*), compound (*synthetike*), and double (*diple*) types. Quintilian's corresponding discussion (*Inst. Or.* 8.5.3-8) offers a broadly similar taxonomy.

[^74]: Mishnah Avot 3:15. On the philosophical significance of this teaching and its resonance with Stoic and Epicurean debates about fate and free will, see Urbach, *The Sages: Their Concepts and Beliefs*, trans. Abrahams (Cambridge, MA: Harvard University Press, 1975), 255-265.

[^75]: On Chrysippus and the Stoic position on fate and free will, see Long and Sedley, *The Hellenistic Philosophers* (Cambridge: Cambridge University Press, 1987), vol. 1, 386-394. The comparison with Rabbi Akiva's compressed formulation is suggestive but should not be taken as evidence of direct influence; the two traditions may have arrived at similar formulations independently.

[^76]: Mishnah Avot 4:20. The attribution varies in manuscripts; see the apparatus in the Kaufmann MS. On the wine-jar metaphor as a commonplace of Mediterranean wisdom literature, see Fischel, *Rabbinic Literature and Greco-Roman Philosophy* (Leiden: Brill, 1973), 82-84.

[^77]: Aristotle, *Rhetoric* 2.21, 1394a7. On the relationship between maxim and metaphor in rabbinic gnomology, see Stern, *Parables in Midrash: Narrative and Exegesis in Rabbinic Literature* (Cambridge, MA: Harvard University Press, 1991), 15-28.

[^78]: Quintilian, *Inst. Or.* 8.5.3-4, on the maxim *cum ratione* -- the maxim supported by a reason. Quintilian observes that "sententia" is more persuasive when accompanied by its justification.

[^79]: BT Berakhot 63a. On the concept of "small verses" that contain the whole Torah, cf. BT Makkot 23b-24a (Rabbi Simlai's derivation of the 613 commandments and their progressive condensation to a single principle).

[^80]: Aristotle, *Rhetoric* 2.21, 1394b8. On the double maxim as a characteristic form of Judaean gnomology, see the extended analysis of Avot 3:17 in Fraenkel, *Darkhe ha-Aggadah ve-ha-Midrash* (Givatayim: Yad la-Talmud, 1991), 198-205.

[^81]: On the fluidity of attribution in rabbinic tradition and the transformation of chreiai into maxims, see Alexander, "The Oral Law," in *The Cambridge History of Judaism*, vol. 3 (1999), 85-97; and Jaffee, *Torah in the Mouth: Writing and Oral Tradition in Palestinian Judaism 200 BCE -- 400 CE* (Oxford: Oxford University Press, 2001), 100-125.

[^82]: The exercise of classification and analysis parallels Theon's instruction that students should practice composing and analyzing gnomae in each of the recognized types (*Progymnasmata* 3; Hock and O'Neil, 66-68). Hermogenes (*Progymnasmata* 4) similarly prescribes exercises in which students elaborate, condense, and classify maxims.

[^83]: Aphthonius, *Progymnasmata* 5 (Kennedy, *Progymnasmata*, 103-105). The definition is terse by design: *anaskeue estin pragmatos prokeimenou anaskeuasis* -- "refutation is the overturning of a matter at hand."

[^84]: Theon, *Progymnasmata* 3-4 (Hock and O'Neil, 56-58), treats refutation as applicable to chreiai, fables, and narratives. He enumerates nine grounds for refutation: obscurity (*asapheia*), pleonasm (*pleonasmos*), ellipsis (*elleipsis*), impossibility (*adunaton*), implausibility (*apithanon*), falsity (*pseudos*), unsuitability (*aprepes*), uselessness (*anopheles*), and shamefulness (*aischron*).

[^85]: Hermogenes, *Progymnasmata* 5-6 (Kennedy, *Progymnasmata*, 81-83), treats refutation and confirmation as paired exercises, instructing that students should practice both on the same material.

[^86]: On the relationship between the *progymnasmata* exercise of refutation and talmudic dialectics, see Hidary, *Rabbis and Classical Rhetoric*, 155-186. The present writer's claim that the Talmud represents "the most extensively developed tradition of systematic refutation in all of ancient literature" is defended in Hidary, 186-192, and supported by the structural analysis in Jacobs, *The Talmudic Argument: A Study in Talmudic Reasoning and Methodology* (Cambridge: Cambridge University Press, 1984), passim.

[^87]: The six categories presented here consolidate and adapt the nine categories of Theon (see note 121 above) into a framework that corresponds more naturally to the modes of objection attested in the Babylonian Talmud. On this mapping, see Hidary, *Rabbis and Classical Rhetoric*, 155-170; and cf. Aphthonius (*Progymnasmata* 5; Kennedy, 103-105), who enumerates the unclear, the incredible, the impossible, the inconsistent, the unfitting, and the inexpedient -- a sixfold scheme closer to the present one.

[^88]: On the talmudic formula *heikhi dami? ilima... ela...* and its variants, see Jacobs, *The Talmudic Argument*, 35-52. The frequency of this formula in the Babylonian Talmud is discussed in Halivni, *Mekorot u-Mesorot*, introduction, 18-24.

[^89]: BT Gittin 56b-57a. On the Talmud's self-reflexive interrogation of its own aggadic narratives, see Rubenstein, *Talmudic Stories: Narrative Art, Composition, and Culture* (Baltimore: Johns Hopkins University Press, 1999), 238-256; and Boyarin, *Intertextuality and the Reading of Midrash* (Bloomington: Indiana University Press, 1990), 80-92.

[^90]: On the technical meaning of *teyuvta* (conclusive refutation) as distinct from *kushya* (difficulty), see Jacobs, *The Talmudic Argument*, 57-62. The doubled *teyuvta* is a formulaic marker of finality; the defeated position is thereafter abandoned by the tradition.

[^91]: On *u-reminhu* and *mitivi* as contradiction-raising formulae in the Talmud, see Jacobs, *The Talmudic Argument*, 63-78; and Halivni, *Mekorot u-Mesorot*, introduction, 24-38.

[^92]: BT Shabbat 56a. On the defense of biblical heroes through the refutation from unfittingness, see Kugel, *The Bible As It Was* (Cambridge, MA: Harvard University Press, 1997), 131-155.

[^93]: BT Bava Batra 60b and BT Avodah Zarah 36a. This principle functions as a standing refutation from inexpediency. Cf. Aphthonius (*Progymnasmata* 5; Kennedy, 104), who lists "the inexpedient" (*asympheron*) as a ground for refutation.

[^94]: Mekhilta de-Rabbi Ishmael, Pischa 1-18 (Horovitz-Rabin ed.). On the dialectical structure of the Mekhilta, see Epstein, *Mevo'ot le-Sifrut ha-Tanna'im* (Jerusalem: Magnes, 1957), 521-545.

[^95]: The formula *eini yode'a mah melamdenu* ("I do not know what this teaches us") appears in various tannaitic contexts. See Epstein, *Mevo'ot*, 521-525, on this formula and its variants.

[^96]: The three-stage exercise (defense, attack, revised defense) corresponds to the pedagogical principle articulated by Theon (*Progymnasmata* 3; Hock and O'Neil, 56-60) that students should practice both refutation and confirmation on the same material. The talmudic ideal of *shakla ve-tarya* ("give and take") embodies this dialectical principle.

[^97]: Aphthonius, *Progymnasmata* 6 (Kennedy, *Progymnasmata*, 105-107). The definition is the positive counterpart of the definition of refutation in chapter 5: where refutation "overturns," confirmation "establishes."

[^98]: Theon, *Progymnasmata* 3-4 (Hock and O'Neil, 58-60), treats confirmation and refutation as a matched pair employing the same categories with opposite valences.

[^99]: Hermogenes, *Progymnasmata* 5-6 (Kennedy, *Progymnasmata*, 81-83).
