[^100]: On the formula *mina hani milli? dikhtiv...* as the standard confirmation structure of the Babylonian Talmud, see Jacobs, *The Talmudic Argument*, 79-96.

[^101]: The six methods of confirmation here correspond inversely to the six grounds of refutation in II.E, following the structural principle articulated by Theon (*Progymnasmata* 3; Hock and O'Neil, 58-60).

[^102]: BT Berakhot 30b. This is a paradigmatic example of the *mina lan? dikhtiv...* formula in its simplest and most transparent form.

[^103]: On *kal va-chomer* as the first of Rabbi Ishmael's thirteen hermeneutical rules and its correspondence to the rhetorical argument from probability (*eikos*), see Daube, "Rabbinic Methods of Interpretation and Hellenistic Rhetoric," *Hebrew Union College Annual* 22 (1949): 239-264; and Schwarz, *Der hermeneutische Syllogismus in der talmudischen Litteratur* (Vienna: Israel-Theol. Lehranstalt, 1901), 3-28.

[^104]: On the talmudic formulae *tanya nami hakhi* and *leima mesaye'a leih* as modes of cumulative confirmation, see Jacobs, *The Talmudic Argument*, 79-96.

[^105]: Sifra Qedoshim, parashah 4 (on Leviticus 19:18). On the debate between Rabbi Akiva and Ben Azzai, see Fischel, *Rabbinic Literature and Greco-Roman Philosophy*, 115-120; and Fraade, *From Tradition to Commentary: Torah and Its Interpretation in the Midrash Sifre to Deuteronomy* (Albany: SUNY Press, 1991), 24-38.

[^106]: BT Eruvin 13b. The preference for Beit Hillel on grounds of character rather than logical superiority is a remarkable instance of confirmation from moral expediency.

[^107]: On the distinction between *derasha* (genuine derivation) and *asmakhta* (scriptural support), see Halivni, *Peshat and Derash: Plain and Applied Meaning in Rabbinic Exegesis* (New York: Oxford University Press, 1991), 18-37; and Chernick, *Hermeneutical Studies in Talmudic and Midrashic Literatures* (Lod: Habermann Institute, 1984), 45-72.

[^108]: The exercise of distinguishing derivation from support parallels the pedagogical principle that students must learn to evaluate the *quality* of a confirmation, not merely its presence. Theon (*Progymnasmata* 3; Hock and O'Neil, 58-60) instructs that some proofs are stronger than others, and the student must learn to rank them.

[^109]: Theon, *Progymnasmata* 6 (Hock and O'Neil, 68-72). The definition emphasizes that the commonplace addresses "admitted" (*homologoumena*) goods and evils -- qualities whose moral status is not in dispute.

[^110]: Aphthonius, *Progymnasmata* 7 (Kennedy, *Progymnasmata*, 108-110). Aphthonius provides a model commonplace against a tyrant, demonstrating the technique of amplification through successive comparisons and consequences.

[^111]: Quintilian, *Inst. Or.* 2.4.22. Quintilian places the commonplace among the fundamental exercises for developing *amplificatio* and notes its particular value for training in epideictic oratory.

[^112]: The five-part structure corresponds broadly to the arrangement prescribed by Aphthonius (*Progymnasmata* 7; Kennedy, 108-110), who recommends opening with a contrary, then proceeding through exposition, comparison, intention, digression, rejection of pity, and concluding exhortation.

[^113]: BT Arakhin 15b-16a. The entire passage constitutes one of the most sustained commonplace discourses in the Talmud. On its structure and rhetorical technique, cf. the model commonplace speeches in Aphthonius (*Progymnasmata* 7).

[^114]: BT Bava Batra 9a-10a. The discourse on charity constitutes a sustained positive commonplace. On the Talmud's technique of ascending amplification in moral discourse, see Fraenkel, *Darkhe ha-Aggadah ve-ha-Midrash*, 245-260.

[^115]: BT Yoma 9b. The attribution of the Temple's destruction to baseless hatred is the paradigmatic instance of historical amplification in rabbinic commonplace discourse. On the rhetorical and theological significance of this teaching, see Rubenstein, *Talmudic Stories*, 116-138.

[^116]: BT Sotah 41b. On the Agrippa incident as a historical exemplum in moral discourse, see Goodman, *The Ruling Class of Judaea: The Origins of the Jewish Revolt Against Rome, A.D. 66-70* (Cambridge: Cambridge University Press, 1987), 145-150.

[^117]: The exercise of historical amplification corresponds to Quintilian's recommendation (*Inst. Or.* 2.4.22-24) that students connect vices and virtues to their historical consequences, grounding moral argument in concrete experience.

[^118]: Theon, *Progymnasmata* 8 (Hock and O'Neil, 72-80). Theon's treatment of the encomium is among the most detailed in the *progymnasmata* tradition.

[^119]: Aphthonius, *Progymnasmata* 8 (Kennedy, *Progymnasmata*, 110-115). Aphthonius provides a model encomium of Thucydides that follows the prescribed sequence of topoi with systematic fidelity.

[^120]: Menander Rhetor, *Peri Epideiktikon* I-II (Russell and Wilson eds., Oxford, 1981). Menander's treatises constitute the most comprehensive ancient treatment of epideictic genres, encompassing encomia of persons, cities, lands, harbors, occasions, and abstract subjects.

[^121]: On the structural correspondence between the rabbinic *hesped* and the Greco-Roman encomium, see Hidary, *Rabbis and Classical Rhetoric*, 97-115. Menander Rhetor's prescriptions for the funeral oration (*epitaphios logos*) in *Peri Epideiktikon* II.9 (Russell and Wilson, 170-178) provide the closest Greek parallel.

[^122]: On the question of whether the structural parallels between rabbinic eulogy and Greco-Roman encomium reflect direct influence, independent development, or convergent evolution, see Lieberman, *Hellenism in Jewish Palestine* (New York: Jewish Theological Seminary, 1950), 100-114; and Hasan-Rokem, *The Web of Life: Folklore and Midrash in Rabbinic Literature* (Stanford: Stanford University Press, 2000), 55-78.

[^123]: BT Mo'ed Qatan 25a-b. On the eulogistic formulae preserved in this passage and their relationship to the Greco-Roman *epitaphios logos*, see Hidary, *Rabbis and Classical Rhetoric*, 103-115.

[^124]: BT Kiddushin 49b. The "ten measures" formula is applied to beauty, wisdom, suffering, and other qualities. On the correspondence with Menander Rhetor's topoi for the praise of cities (*Peri Epideiktikon* I.2-3; Russell and Wilson, 28-46), see the discussion in Hidary, *Rabbis and Classical Rhetoric*, 108-110.

[^125]: BT Shabbat 119a. The personification of Shabbat as bride and queen is the foundation of the liturgical poem *Lekhah Dodi* by Solomon Alkabetz (16th century). On personification as a technique of encomiastic amplification, see Menander Rhetor, *Peri Epideiktikon* II.7 (Russell and Wilson, 160-168).

[^126]: Quintilian, *Inst. Or.* 3.7.21-23. On the principle that epideictic speech must work with the materials reality provides, see also BT Berakhot 62a and BT Mo'ed Qatan 25a.

[^127]: The exercise of the eulogy with restraint corresponds to Quintilian's discussion of the difficult epideictic cases -- where the subject's qualities are mixed and the orator must exercise judgment (*Inst. Or.* 3.7.15-18).

[^128]: Theon, *Progymnasmata* 8 (Hock and O'Neil, 80-82): "Vituperation is the opposite of encomium; for it reveals the baseness of actions and other bad qualities."

[^129]: Aphthonius, *Progymnasmata* 9 (Kennedy, *Progymnasmata*, 115-118). Aphthonius's model vituperation of Philip of Macedon systematically inverts every category of his preceding model encomium of Thucydides.

[^130]: BT Berakhot 43b; cf. BT Bava Metzia 58b-59a. The equation of public shaming with bloodshed is based on the visible blanching of the shamed person's face.

[^131]: BT Bava Metzia 58b. On the gravity of public shaming in rabbinic ethics, see the discussion in Urbach, *The Sages*, 523-530.

[^132]: On the prohibition against speaking ill of the dead (*lashon hara al ha-metim*), see BT Berakhot 19a and Maimonides, *Mishneh Torah*, Hilkhot Evel 14:1.

[^133]: Numbers Rabbah 18:2 (on Numbers 16:1). On the vituperative inversion of genealogy in rabbinic discourse, compare Menander Rhetor's instructions for the *psogos* (*Peri Epideiktikon* II.12; Russell and Wilson, 182-184).

[^134]: Numbers Rabbah 18:3. On Korach as a paradigm of the sophist who corrupts rhetorical skill, cf. Plato, *Gorgias* 456a-461b; and Hidary, *Rabbis and Classical Rhetoric*, 38-42.

[^135]: Genesis Rabbah 26-28 (Theodor-Albeck ed.). On the principle of *middah keneged middah* (measure for measure) as a rhetorical device in midrashic vituperation, see Fraenkel, *Darkhe ha-Aggadah ve-ha-Midrash*, 320-335.

[^136]: BT Sanhedrin 106b. On Doeg and Ahitophel as paradigms of misused intellectual ability, see Fraenkel, *Darkhe ha-Aggadah ve-ha-Midrash*, 387-395. The concluding practical observation -- that misplaced talent leads to corruption -- transforms the vituperation into a didactic exemplum.

[^137]: Proverbs 24:17, cited in Mishnah Avot 4:19. Quintilian's parallel observation at *Inst. Or.* 6.1.28 concerns the orator's obligation to appear reluctant when attacking his opponent's character, lest the appearance of malice alienate the judges.

[^138]: The exercise of converting vituperation (of a specific figure) into commonplace (on the abstract vice) corresponds to the pedagogical sequence prescribed in the *progymnasmata*: the student moves from the particular to the general, developing the ability to work in both registers. See Theon (*Progymnasmata* 8; Hock and O'Neil, 80-82) and Aphthonius (*Progymnasmata* 7-9; Kennedy, 108-118).
### II.J. Comparison (Synkrisis / Hekesh)

#### Definition and Classification

The comparison is that exercise which sets two subjects side by side for the purpose of mutual illumination, drawing from their resemblances and differences a judgment that could not be reached by examining either subject alone. We define it thus: *Synkrisis is the rhetorical setting of two subjects in parallel, whether persons, things, actions, or times, so that the qualities of each may be discerned more clearly through juxtaposition with the other.* The student who has mastered the encomium and the invective -- who can praise one subject and censure another -- is now prepared to undertake the more demanding task of treating two subjects simultaneously, showing where each excels and where each falls short, and arriving at a conclusion that honors the complexity of both.[^139]

Theon, in his treatment of this exercise, instructs us that comparison proceeds by the same categories as the encomium -- origin, education, deeds, manner of death, posthumous honor -- but applied doubly, so that each category yields a point of correspondence or divergence between the two subjects.[^140] Aphthonius, in the tenth chapter of his handbook, provides a model comparison of Achilles and Hector, demonstrating how the virtues of each warrior are heightened by contrast with the other.[^141] The principle is the same whether one compares warriors or philosophers, cities or constitutions, virtues or vices: the juxtaposition itself is the instrument of understanding.

We observe that the literary culmination of this exercise among the Greeks and Romans is found in the *Parallel Lives* of Plutarch, who devoted a vast biographical project to the systematic comparison of Greek and Roman statesmen -- Theseus with Romulus, Demosthenes with Cicero, Alexander with Caesar -- composing for each pair a formal *synkrisis* that draws the threads of the two biographies together into a single evaluative judgment.[^142] Plutarch's method is instructive: he does not simply declare one subject superior to the other. Rather, he identifies the specific axes along which comparison is meaningful, concedes the superiority of each subject along different axes, and arrives at a balanced assessment that preserves the dignity of both. The student will find that this method -- comparison as mutual illumination rather than as annihilation of one party -- is not merely Plutarchan courtesy but a deep principle of the exercise itself. A comparison that is merely a concealed encomium of one subject and a concealed invective against the other has failed as a comparison; it has collapsed back into the simpler exercises that precede it.

We observe among the sages of Judaea a practice of comparison that is no less rich than the Greco-Roman tradition, though it follows its own distinctive forms and is governed by its own characteristic principles. Indeed, we may say without exaggeration that the Judaean tradition of synkrisis is, in certain respects, more sophisticated than the Greek, for it has developed forms of structured comparison -- the systematic numerical taxonomy, the hierarchical ranking that preserves both terms, the comparison that culminates not in a verdict of superiority but in a declaration of complementarity -- that have no precise parallel in the handbooks of Theon or Aphthonius. The student who attends to both traditions will find his understanding of the exercise immeasurably enriched.

#### Types of Comparison

The student should master the following types, each of which finds abundant illustration in the Judaean sources:

**Comparison of Persons.** This is the most natural and most common form. Two sages, two kings, two prophets, two leaders are set beside each other, and their qualities examined point by point. The comparison of persons requires above all the quality of fairness: the orator must present each subject with equal fullness and equal respect, resisting the temptation to construct one as a mere foil for the other. Plutarch understood this; the Judaean sages understood it no less.

**Comparison of Things.** Abstract goods and values are weighed against each other: Torah study against charitable action, prayer against sacrifice, the written law against the oral law, this world against the world to come. These comparisons are among the most philosophically demanding, for they require the orator to define each term with precision before he can compare them.

**Comparison of Actions.** Specific modes of conduct are set beside each other: teaching publicly against teaching privately, giving charity openly against giving charity in secret, judging favorably against judging strictly. The comparison of actions is closely allied to the thesis exercise (to be discussed in section II.M), for both concern the question of what one ought to do.

**Comparison of Times.** Historical periods are set beside each other: an earlier age against a later age, an age of prosperity against an age of catastrophe, an age governed by one principle against an age governed by another. The comparison of times is a powerful instrument of historical and moral analysis, for it enables the orator to identify what has changed, what has been lost, and what has been gained.

**Comparison by Numerical Taxonomy.** Here we must note a form that is peculiarly developed in the Judaean tradition and has no close equivalent in the Greek handbooks. The Judaean sages constructed elaborate typologies -- "there are four types of students," "there are four types of charity-givers," "there are seven qualities of a sage" -- in which multiple instances of the same category are set beside each other in a systematic grid. These numerical lists are, in effect, multi-term comparisons: instead of comparing two subjects, they compare three, four, seven, or ten, generating a complete taxonomy through systematic juxtaposition. The student will find these lists in the fifth chapter of the Judaean treatise known as *Pirqe Avot* (Chapters of the Fathers), and they repay careful study as models of structured comparison.

#### Examples from the Judaean Tradition

**Example 1: The Comparison of Hillel and Shammai.**

The most celebrated comparison of persons in the entire Judaean tradition is the sustained, multi-source comparison of the two great sages Hillel and Shammai, who flourished in the generation before the destruction of the Temple, and of the schools (*batei midrash*) they founded. The Babylonian Talmud records the following:

> <span dir="rtl">שלוש שנים נחלקו בית שמאי ובית הלל. הללו אומרים: הלכה כמותנו, והללו אומרים: הלכה כמותנו. יצאה בת קול ואמרה: אלו ואלו דברי אלהים חיים הן, והלכה כבית הלל</span>
>
> *For three years the school of Shammai and the school of Hillel disputed. These said: The law is according to us. And these said: The law is according to us. A heavenly voice went forth and declared: Both these and these are the words of the living God -- but the law is according to the school of Hillel.* (BT Eruvin 13b)[^143]

The student will observe the extraordinary structure of this comparison. The divine voice does not declare one school right and the other wrong. It declares both to be "words of the living God" -- that is, both positions possess genuine religious and intellectual validity. Yet the practical law must follow one school, and it follows Hillel. The reason given is itself a comparison of character:

> <span dir="rtl">ומפני מה זכו בית הלל לקבוע הלכה כמותן? מפני שנוחין ועלובין היו, ושונין דבריהן ודברי בית שמאי. ולא עוד אלא שמקדימין דברי בית שמאי לדבריהם</span>
>
> *And why did the school of Hillel merit that the law be fixed according to them? Because they were pleasant and forbearing, and they would study their own teachings and the teachings of the school of Shammai. And not only that, but they would present the teachings of the school of Shammai before their own.*

Here the comparison reveals its deepest lesson. The school of Hillel prevailed not through superior logical force but through superior moral character -- humility, intellectual generosity, the willingness to present the opposing view before one's own. The comparison thus generates a principle that transcends both subjects: that in the pursuit of truth, character matters as much as intellect. Shammai's rigor is not dismissed; it is honored as "words of the living God." But Hillel's gentleness takes precedence, because the gentle scholar can contain and transmit the rigorous scholar's teachings, whereas the rigorous scholar who lacks gentleness cannot.

The comparison extends beyond the schools to the founders themselves. In the celebrated narrative of the three converts (BT Shabbat 31a), three prospective converts approach first Shammai, then Hillel, with provocative or demanding requests. Shammai, in each case, drives the petitioner away with the measuring-rod he holds in his hand. Hillel, in each case, receives the petitioner with patience and converts him. The tradition summarizes:

> <span dir="rtl">לעולם יהא אדם ענוותן כהלל, ואל יהא קפדן כשמאי</span>
>
> *A person should always be gentle like Hillel and not exacting like Shammai.* (BT Shabbat 30b)

Yet the student of rhetoric must not take this summary as the whole of the comparison. Shammai's exactitude has its own virtue: it preserves the integrity of standards, it resists the dilution of demanding ideals. The tradition preserves both models precisely because both are necessary -- gentleness for the teacher, rigor for the guardian of the law. The comparison illuminates both figures without destroying either.

**Example 2: Torah Study and Action Compared.**

Among the most important comparisons in the Judaean tradition is the comparison of abstract goods -- specifically, the comparison of Torah study (*talmud*) and practical action (*ma'aseh*). The Babylonian Talmud records a formal deliberation on this thesis:

> <span dir="rtl">וכבר היה רבי טרפון וזקנים מסובין בעלייה בבית נתזה בלוד. נשאלה שאלה זו בפניהם: תלמוד גדול או מעשה גדול? נענה רבי טרפון ואמר: מעשה גדול. נענה רבי עקיבא ואמר: תלמוד גדול. נענו כולם ואמרו: תלמוד גדול, שהתלמוד מביא לידי מעשה</span>
>
> *It once happened that Rabbi Tarfon and the elders were reclining in the upper chamber of the house of Nit'zah in Lydda. This question was raised before them: Is study greater or is action greater? Rabbi Tarfon answered and said: Action is greater. Rabbi Akiva answered and said: Study is greater. They all answered and said: Study is greater, for study leads to action.* (BT Kiddushin 40b)[^144]

The student will find that this passage exemplifies the highest form of comparison -- comparison that culminates not in the annihilation of one term but in a hierarchical synthesis that preserves both. Study is declared greater, but the reason given -- "for study leads to action" -- establishes action as the ultimate purpose of study. Study without action is a root without fruit; action without study is fruit without a root. The comparison has not destroyed one term in favor of the other; it has established their proper relationship. This is synkrisis at its most philosophically mature, and the student should study it as a model of how comparison can generate insight that neither term, examined in isolation, could yield.

We may observe that this pattern -- comparison leading to a hierarchy that preserves both terms rather than eliminating one -- is deeply characteristic of Judaean rhetoric. Where the Greek synkrisis sometimes culminates in a decisive judgment of superiority (as when Plutarch, for all his balance, typically indicates which of his paired figures he considers the greater), the Judaean comparison more often culminates in a declaration of complementarity or a nuanced ordering that maintains the validity of both positions.

**Example 3: The First Temple and the Second Temple Compared.**

The comparison of historical periods finds its most profound Judaean expression in the comparison of the two Temples:

> <span dir="rtl">מקדש ראשון מפני מה חרב? מפני שלוש דברים שהיו בו: עבודה זרה, וגלוי עריות, ושפיכות דמים... אבל מקדש שני, שהיו עוסקין בתורה ובמצוות וגמילות חסדים, מפני מה חרב? מפני שהיתה בו שנאת חנם. ללמדך ששקולה שנאת חנם כנגד שלוש עבירות: עבודה זרה, גלוי עריות, ושפיכות דמים</span>
>
> *The First Temple -- why was it destroyed? Because of three things that were in it: idolatry, sexual immorality, and bloodshed... But the Second Temple, where they were engaged in Torah and commandments and acts of lovingkindness -- why was it destroyed? Because of gratuitous hatred. This teaches you that gratuitous hatred is equivalent to the three sins of idolatry, sexual immorality, and bloodshed.* (BT Yoma 9b)[^145]

The rhetorical structure here is of exceptional interest. Two periods are set beside each other, each characterized by a distinctive set of qualities. The First Temple generation sinned overtly and egregiously -- theirs were the three cardinal transgressions. The Second Temple generation appeared righteous by all external measures -- they studied, they performed the commandments, they practiced charity. Yet the generation that appeared better was, by the divine calculus, worse: their hidden sin of gratuitous hatred outweighed all three cardinal transgressions combined.

The comparison thus serves as a vehicle for a theological proposition of the utmost gravity -- that interpersonal malice, even when concealed beneath a surface of piety, is more destructive than open sin. This proposition could not have been generated without the synkrisis; it is the juxtaposition itself that produces the insight. The student should note how the comparison functions as an instrument of moral discovery: by setting two eras side by side and asking why each was destroyed, the sage uncovers a truth about the nature of sin that would remain invisible if either era were examined alone.

**Example 4: The Numerical Lists of Pirqe Avot.**

The fifth chapter of the Judaean treatise *Pirqe Avot* contains a remarkable collection of structured comparisons in the form of numerical taxonomies. The student should examine the following with care:

> <span dir="rtl">ארבע מדות בתלמידים: מהר לשמוע ומהר לאבד -- יצא שכרו בהפסדו. קשה לשמוע וקשה לאבד -- יצא הפסדו בשכרו. מהר לשמוע וקשה לאבד -- חכם. קשה לשמוע ומהר לאבד -- זה חלק רע</span>
>
> *Four types of students: Quick to learn and quick to forget -- his gain is offset by his loss. Slow to learn and slow to forget -- his loss is offset by his gain. Quick to learn and slow to forget -- this is a sage. Slow to learn and quick to forget -- this is an evil portion.* (Avot 5:12)[^146]

The student will observe the elegance of the construction. Two variables -- speed of acquisition and speed of retention -- are combined in all four logically possible permutations, generating a complete typology. Each type is illuminated by comparison with the others: the sage (quick to learn, slow to forget) is understood most fully when set beside his opposite (slow to learn, quick to forget), and the two intermediate types are understood as balanced compensations. This is comparison raised to the level of systematic classification -- a form that the Greek handbooks do not explicitly teach but that the Judaean tradition has developed with extraordinary sophistication.

The same chapter provides a parallel taxonomy of charity-givers:

> <span dir="rtl">ארבע מדות בנותני צדקה: הרוצה שיתן ולא יתנו אחרים -- עינו רעה בשל אחרים. יתנו אחרים והוא לא יתן -- עינו רעה בשלו. יתן ויתנו אחרים -- חסיד. לא יתן ולא יתנו אחרים -- רשע</span>
>
> *Four types of charity-givers: One who wants to give but does not want others to give -- his eye is evil toward others. One who wants others to give but does not want to give himself -- his eye is evil toward his own. One who wants to give and wants others to give -- this is a pious person. One who does not want to give and does not want others to give -- this is a wicked person.* (Avot 5:13)

Here the comparison does more than classify: it exposes hidden motivations. The person who gives but wishes others would not give is revealed, through the comparison, as one motivated not by generosity but by the desire for exclusive distinction. Without the fourfold comparison, this hidden motive would remain concealed. The synkrisis is thus an instrument not merely of evaluation but of psychological analysis -- it penetrates beneath the surface of action to reveal the intention that animates it.

These numerical taxonomies represent a distinctive contribution of Judaean pedagogy to the art of comparison. Where the Plutarchan synkrisis compares two subjects, and Aphthonius's model likewise treats only two, the Judaean numerical list compares three, four, seven, or ten subjects simultaneously, generating a complete taxonomy through systematic juxtaposition.[^147]

**Example 5: "Who Is Greater -- Moses or Aaron?"**

The question of comparing Moses and Aaron -- the two brothers who together led Israel out of Egypt -- is itself a subject of explicit Judaean reflection. The sages observed that Scripture sometimes names Moses before Aaron and sometimes Aaron before Moses:

> <span dir="rtl">אלה אהרן ומשה... אלה משה ואהרן. לומר לך: משה ואהרן שקולין, אהרן ומשה שקולין</span>
>
> *"These are Aaron and Moses"... "These are Moses and Aaron" -- to tell you: Moses and Aaron are equal; Aaron and Moses are equal.* (Mekhilta, Pisha 1; cf. Numbers Rabbah 2:1)

The student should observe the hermeneutical principle at work: the alternation of names in Scripture is itself treated as a textual signal that comparison is intended, and that the comparison yields a verdict of equality rather than hierarchy. Moses excelled in prophecy and law; Aaron excelled in priesthood and in the pursuit of peace. The tradition that Aaron was *ohev shalom ve-rodef shalom* -- "a lover of peace and a pursuer of peace" (Avot 1:12) -- places him alongside Moses not as a subordinate but as a complement. The prophet needs the priest; the lawgiver needs the peacemaker.

This pattern -- comparison that discovers complementarity rather than hierarchy -- represents what we may call the ethical dimension of the Judaean synkrisis. The sage who compares does not ask merely "who is superior?" but rather "what does each contribute that the other does not?" The world requires many forms of excellence, and the comparison that reduces all excellence to a single measure has failed to apprehend this truth. The student who internalizes this principle will produce comparisons of greater depth and greater fairness than the student who treats every synkrisis as a contest with a winner and a loser.

#### The Exercise

The student will now practice comparison by the following method, which we prescribe as a graduated sequence of increasing difficulty.

At the first level, the student should select two persons from the Judaean tradition who invite comparison -- two sages, two kings, two prophets -- and compose a synkrisis following the categories of the encomium: origins, education, characteristic actions, manner of teaching or leading, legacy. The student should present each figure with equal fullness, identify the specific points of correspondence and divergence, and conclude with an assessment that illuminates both. The teacher may assign such exercises as: "Compare the leadership of Moses with the leadership of Aaron, showing how each excelled in a distinct domain"; or "Compare the sage Hillel with the sage Shammai, demonstrating how their contrasting temperaments each served the needs of the tradition."

At the second level, the student should attempt the comparison of abstract goods: "Compare Torah study and practical action, arguing first for the primacy of each, then seeking a resolution that preserves both." This form of comparison requires the student to define his terms precisely, to marshal arguments on both sides, and to discover a synthesis -- precisely the skills that will be required in the thesis exercise that follows.

At the third level, the student should undertake the comparison of times: "Compare the generation of the First Temple with the generation of the Second Temple, identifying the virtues and vices of each and drawing a moral lesson for the present day." This is the most demanding form of comparison, for it requires not only rhetorical skill but historical knowledge, moral imagination, and the willingness to apply the lessons of the past to one's own circumstances.

In all cases, the student should remember the fundamental principle of the exercise: compare in order to illuminate, not in order to destroy. The comparison that merely praises one subject at the expense of the other has failed. The comparison that reveals something new about both subjects -- something that could not be seen without the juxtaposition -- has succeeded.[^148]

---

### II.K. Speech-in-Character (Ethopoiia / Dibbur be-Shem)

#### Definition and Classification

Speech-in-character is that exercise in which the student composes a speech that a specific person might deliver in a given set of circumstances. We define it thus: *Ethopoiia is the composition of a speech fitted to the character of the speaker, the nature of the occasion, and the emotional demands of the situation, so that the hearer believes he is hearing the very words that the person in question would have spoken.* The exercise is called *ethopoiia* because it is, in its essence, the *making of character through speech* -- the demonstration that character reveals itself most fully in what a person says, and how he says it, at a moment of crisis or decision.[^149]

Theon instructs us that this exercise requires the student to consider three things: the character of the speaker, the identity of the audience, and the nature of the occasion. The speech must be appropriate to all three -- appropriate to the speaker's age, station, temperament, and known history; appropriate to the audience before whom the speech is delivered; and appropriate to the gravity, the urgency, or the pathos of the situation. A speech that is eloquent but inappropriate to the speaker's character has failed; a speech that suits the character but ignores the occasion has likewise failed. The exercise demands the simultaneous mastery of multiple constraints -- and it is this that makes it one of the most demanding of the preliminary exercises.[^150]

Hermogenes, in the ninth chapter of his handbook, distinguishes three temporal orientations for the exercise: the speaker may reflect upon the past (what has brought him to this moment), address the present (what the situation now demands), and anticipate the future (what consequences will follow). The student who attends to all three temporal dimensions will produce a speech of greater depth and verisimilitude than the student who addresses only the present crisis.[^151]

Quintilian, in his discussion of the exercise at *Institutio Oratoria* 3.8.49-54, emphasizes above all the principle of *decorum* -- the speech must be fitting. "We must consider," he writes, "who it is that speaks, to whom, about what, and on what occasion." A speech for a king must sound kingly; a speech for a young soldier must sound youthful and martial; a speech for a grieving mother must sound like grief. The student who gives Achilles the voice of Nestor, or Nestor the voice of Achilles, has demonstrated not his skill but his failure to understand the exercise.[^152]

#### The Three Types

The teachers of rhetoric distinguish three types of speech-in-character, and all three find extraordinarily rich parallels in the Judaean tradition:

**Speeches for Known Persons (Eidolopoeia).** This is the composition of a speech for a specific, named individual whose character and history are known to the audience from prior tradition. The Greek student might compose "the words Achilles would speak upon learning of the death of Patroclus"; the Judaean student composes the words Abraham would speak before the furnace of Nimrod, or Moses would address to God after the sin of the Golden Calf. The constraint is that the audience knows the character intimately -- every word of the invented speech will be tested against the audience's prior knowledge, and any false note will be immediately detected.

**Speeches for Types of Persons (Prosopopoeia).** This is the composition of a speech for a representative type rather than a named individual -- what a mother would say, what a soldier would say, what a convert would say. The Judaean tradition includes formally prescribed speeches for types: the speech of the priest anointed for war, who addresses the troops before battle (Deuteronomy 20:1-4; Mishnah Sotah 8:1); the confession recited by every Israelite farmer who brings his tithe (Deuteronomy 26:13-15); the formula of confession spoken by every penitent on the Day of Atonement. These prescribed speeches are, in effect, prosopopoeiae institutionalized in law -- the tradition has determined what a person of a given type should say in a given circumstance.

**Speeches for Non-Human Entities (the most daring form).** This is the composition of a speech for God, for angels, for personified abstractions such as the Torah, or for attributes such as Justice and Mercy. In no other ancient tradition has this form of the exercise been practiced with such audacity and such theological depth as in the midrashic literature of the Judaean sages. The Greek student may compose a speech for a god of myth -- for Zeus deliberating, for Athena counseling. But the Judaean sage composes speeches for the one God of Israel -- speeches that purport to reveal the inner life of the divine, the anguish of God over His children's suffering, the debate between the divine attributes of Justice and Mercy. This is prosopopoeia raised to the level of theology, and it represents one of the most remarkable achievements of Judaean rhetorical art.

We must state the matter plainly: in no other ancient tradition has ethopoeia been practiced so extensively, so daringly, and with such profound theological consequences as in the midrashic literature of the Judaean sages. The Greek rhetoricians confined the exercise largely to human speakers in plausible historical situations. The Judaean sages extended it to the divine court itself, composing speeches for God, for angels, for the Torah personified, and for the matriarchs and patriarchs in situations entirely unrecorded in Scripture. The scale and the ambition of the Judaean achievement in this exercise are, we must acknowledge, without parallel.

#### Examples from the Judaean Tradition

**Example 1: The Angels at the Sea.**

When the Israelites crossed the Red Sea and the Egyptian army was drowning, the ministering angels wished to sing a hymn of praise. The Babylonian Talmud records the following:

> <span dir="rtl">בקשו מלאכי השרת לומר שירה לפני הקדוש ברוך הוא. אמר להם הקדוש ברוך הוא: מעשה ידי טובעים בים ואתם אומרים שירה?</span>
>
> *The ministering angels wished to sing a song before the Holy One, blessed be He. The Holy One, blessed be He, said to them: My creatures are drowning in the sea, and you would sing a song?* (BT Megillah 10b; cf. BT Sanhedrin 39b)[^153]

The student should observe the ethopoiia operating on two levels simultaneously. First, the angels are given a characteristically angelic impulse -- they wish to sing, because praise is the angels' essential activity (cf. Isaiah 6:3). Their desire is not malicious; it arises naturally from their nature. Second, and far more daringly, God is given a speech that reveals the divine character at a moment of tension between justice and mercy. God has just executed judgment upon the Egyptians -- yet He grieves for them, calling them "My creatures" (*ma'aseh yadai* -- the work of My hands). The single word *yadai* ("My hands") carries the full weight of the theological claim: the Egyptians are God's creatures no less than the Israelites, and their destruction, though just, occasions divine sorrow.

The parallel passage in Sanhedrin 39b states the principle even more starkly:

> <span dir="rtl">אין הקדוש ברוך הוא שמח במפלתן של רשעים</span>
>
> *The Holy One, blessed be He, does not rejoice in the downfall of the wicked.*

Here the ethopoeia is compressed into a single theological proposition about the emotional state of God. The sage claims to know not merely what God said but what God *feels*. This is prosopopoeia at its most audacious -- and the student should note that it achieves its theological profundity precisely through the rhetorical technique of speech-in-character. The proposition "God grieves even for His enemies" could be stated abstractly; but it is immeasurably more powerful when dramatized as a scene in which God rebukes the angels who would celebrate.

**Example 2: Abraham before Nimrod.**

The midrashic collection known as *Genesis Rabbah* (chapter 38) contains an elaborate dialogue between the young Abraham and King Nimrod that has no basis whatsoever in the biblical text. The scene is this: Abraham, having smashed his father Terah's idols, is brought before Nimrod for judgment. The sage constructs the following exchange:

> <span dir="rtl">אמר לו נמרוד: נעבוד לאש? אמר לו אברהם: נעבוד למים שמכבין את האש. אמר לו: נעבוד למים. אמר לו: נעבוד לענן שנושא את המים. אמר לו: נעבוד לענן. אמר לו: נעבוד לרוח שמפזר את הענן. אמר לו: נעבוד לרוח. אמר לו: נעבוד לאדם שסובל את הרוח. אמר לו: מילין את משתעי -- אני איני משתחוה אלא לאור. הריני משליכך בתוכו, ויבוא אלוה שאתה משתחוה לו ויצילך הימנו</span>
>
> *Nimrod said to him: Let us worship fire. Abraham said: Let us rather worship water, which extinguishes fire. He said: Let us worship water. Abraham said: Let us rather worship the cloud, which carries the water. He said: Let us worship the cloud. Abraham said: Let us rather worship the wind, which disperses the cloud. He said: Let us worship the wind. Abraham said: Let us rather worship the human being, who withstands the wind. Nimrod said: You are speaking mere words -- I bow only to fire. I will throw you into it, and let the God you worship come and save you from it.* (Genesis Rabbah 38:13)[^154]

This is ethopoiia of the highest order -- a philosophical dialogue placed in the mouths of characters whose actual words Scripture does not record. The student should observe how each speech reveals its speaker's character. Abraham's chain of reasoning reveals his logical mind, his fearless wit, and his willingness to follow an argument to its ultimate consequence, even at the cost of his life. Each element he proposes as worthy of worship is immediately surpassed by a greater natural force, demonstrating through a *sorites* -- a chain argument -- the absurdity of worshipping any element of the created order. Nimrod's character is equally revealed: his intellectual impatience ("You are speaking mere words"), his resort to brute force when reason fails him, and his devotion to the most destructive rather than the most creative element of nature.

The student who has read the Socratic dialogues of Plato will recognize in Abraham's chain argument a technique strikingly similar to the Socratic *elenchus* -- the method of systematic refutation through questioning. Whether the Judaean sage was aware of this parallel is a question we need not resolve here; what matters is that the rhetorical structure is the same. The character's speech reveals his philosophical method, and the method reveals his character.

**Example 3: Moses after the Golden Calf.**

The Babylonian Talmud, in tractate Berakhot 32a, constructs an extraordinary forensic speech for Moses in the aftermath of Israel's worship of the Golden Calf. God has declared His intention to destroy the people. Moses must plead their case -- and the sage composes for him a speech of devastating rhetorical power:

> <span dir="rtl">אמר משה לפני הקדוש ברוך הוא: רבונו של עולם, כסא של שלוש רגלים אינו יכול לעמוד לפניך בשעת כעסך, כסא של רגל אחת על אחת כמה וכמה</span>
>
> *Moses said before the Holy One, blessed be He: Master of the Universe, a throne of three legs [i.e., the merit of the three patriarchs] cannot stand before You in Your hour of anger -- a throne of one leg [Moses alone], how much less!*

And further:

> <span dir="rtl">אמר לפניו: רבונו של עולם, אתה אמרת לי: לך רד -- רד מגדולתך. כלום נתתי לך גדולה אלא בשביל ישראל? ועכשיו ישראל חטאו, אני למה לי?</span>
>
> *He said before Him: Master of the Universe, You said to me "Go, descend" -- descend from your greatness. I was given greatness only for the sake of Israel. Now that Israel has sinned, what use am I?*

And still further:

> <span dir="rtl">אמר לפניו: רבונו של עולם, זכור לאברהם ליצחק ולישראל עבדיך -- אם באש אתה שורפן, זכור לאברהם שנתן עצמו לישרף על קדושת שמך. אם בחרב אתה הורגן, זכור ליצחק שפשט צוארו על גבי המזבח. אם בגלות אתה מגלן, זכור ליעקב שגלה לחרן</span>
>
> *He said before Him: Master of the Universe, remember Abraham, Isaac, and Israel Your servants -- if You would burn them with fire, remember Abraham who offered himself to be burned for the sanctification of Your name. If You would slay them with the sword, remember Isaac who stretched out his neck upon the altar. If You would exile them, remember Jacob who went into exile to Haran.* (BT Berakhot 32a)[^155]

The student of rhetoric will recognize here a forensic speech of classical structure, composed for Moses and delivered before the divine tribunal. Three distinct argumentative strategies are deployed in succession. First, a *qal va-homer* (argument *a fortiori*): if even the three patriarchs together cannot withstand God's anger, how much less can Moses alone? This argument establishes Moses's humility and simultaneously reminds God of the patriarchal merit. Second, an argument from logical consequence: Moses's own greatness was bestowed for Israel's sake; if Israel is destroyed, Moses's commission is rendered void, and God's own purpose is frustrated. This argument is remarkably bold -- it implies that destroying Israel would be an act of divine self-contradiction. Third, an argument from precedent matched to punishment: each potential form of divine retribution is paired with a patriarch who voluntarily endured the same suffering, so that punishing the descendants would dishonor the merit of the ancestors.

The speech is a masterpiece of ethopoiia because it is perfectly fitted to Moses's known character: his humility (he places himself below the patriarchs), his fierce loyalty to Israel (he ties his fate to theirs), and his intimate knowledge of the sacred tradition (he matches each patriarch precisely to the form of suffering that patriarch endured). The student who composes a speech for a character must study that character with this same thoroughness -- every word must ring true to what we know of the person.

**Example 4: Rachel before God.**

We conclude with what is perhaps the most powerful example of ethopoiia in all of Judaean literature. In the midrashic commentary on the Book of Lamentations (*Lamentations Rabbah*, Petihta 24), the destruction of the Temple has sent Israel into exile. One by one, the great figures of Israelite history rise to plead before God: Abraham invokes the binding of Isaac; Jacob invokes his years of suffering; Moses invokes his faithful leadership of the people through the wilderness. But none succeeds in moving God to compassion. Then Rachel the matriarch speaks:

> <span dir="rtl">רבונו של עולם, גלוי לפניך שיעקב עבדך אהבני אהבה יתירה, ועבד בשבילי לאבא שבע שנים, וכשהשלימו אותם שבע שנים... יעץ אבי להחליפני לאחותי... ולא עוד אלא שמסרתי לה כל הסימנים שמסר לי יעקב... וכבשתי את רחמי וחסתי על אחותי שלא תצא לחרפה. ומה אני שאני בשר ודם עפר ואפר לא קינאתי לצרה שלי ולא הוצאתיה לידי בושה... ואתה מלך חי וקיים רחמן, מפני מה קינאת לעבודה זרה שאין בה ממש והגלית בני?</span>
>
> *Master of the Universe, it is revealed before You that Jacob Your servant loved me with an extraordinary love, and he labored for my father for seven years on my behalf, and when those seven years were completed... my father schemed to substitute my sister for me... And more than that: I gave her all the secret signs that Jacob had given me... And I suppressed my compassion and had pity on my sister so that she would not be put to shame. And if I, who am flesh and blood, dust and ashes, was not jealous of my rival and did not expose her to shame... then You, who are the living and eternal King, the Merciful One -- why were You jealous of idolatry, which has no substance, and exiled my children?* (Lamentations Rabbah, Petihta 24)[^156]

The student will observe that Rachel's speech succeeds where Abraham's, Jacob's, and Moses's failed. Her argument is a *qal va-homer* of devastating power: from the lesser (a mortal woman) to the greater (the immortal God), from the more forgivable jealousy (a woman's jealousy of a romantic rival) to the less forgivable (God's jealousy of empty idols). If a woman of flesh and blood could suppress her jealousy and protect her sister from shame, then surely the living and eternal God can suppress His jealousy and restore His children.

The response is immediate:

> <span dir="rtl">מיד נתגלגלו רחמיו של הקדוש ברוך הוא ואמר: בשבילך רחל אני מחזיר את ישראל למקומן</span>
>
> *Immediately the compassion of the Holy One, blessed be He, was stirred, and He said: For your sake, Rachel, I will return Israel to their place.*

The ethopoiia is extraordinary in every dimension. The sage has given voice to a woman who is largely silent in Scripture and has made her the most effective advocate in the heavenly court -- more effective than the patriarchs, more effective than Moses himself. Her speech is perfectly fitted to her character as Scripture reveals it: patient, selfless, willing to sacrifice her own happiness for her sister's dignity. And the argument she makes -- an argument from lived moral experience rather than from abstract merit or theological precedent -- succeeds precisely because it is personal, concrete, and grounded in a sacrifice that she herself actually made. The student who composes speech-in-character should take Rachel's speech as the supreme model of the exercise: a speech that is at once rhetorically powerful, psychologically true, and theologically profound.

#### The Ethical Dimension of the Exercise

The student should understand that ethopoiia carries a particular ethical responsibility. When one composes a speech in the character of a revered figure -- whether a patriarch, a sage, or (most audaciously) God Himself -- one must exercise the utmost fidelity to the character as tradition reveals it. The orator must not project his own views onto the character, nor use the character as a mere mouthpiece for opinions the orator happens to hold. He must study the character so thoroughly that he can set aside his own perspective and inhabit another's.

This is an exercise in *empathy* as much as in rhetoric. The sage who can imagine what Abraham felt before the furnace, what Rachel felt on her wedding night, what Moses felt when God commanded him to ascend the mountain and die there -- that sage has achieved a depth of moral understanding that purely intellectual analysis cannot reach. The boundary between self and other has become, for the moment of composition, permeable. And this capacity to enter the experience of another -- to feel what another feels, to see the world through another's eyes -- is not merely a rhetorical skill; it is a moral achievement that enlarges the orator's humanity.

Yet the student must also know the limits. The speeches composed through ethopoiia are *interpretive* -- they represent the orator's best understanding of what a character would say, given what tradition tells us. They are not prophecy. They are not revelation. They remain subject to the text that generated them. If a proposed speech contradicts what Scripture or tradition explicitly states about a character, the speech must be revised or abandoned. The text retains its authority; interpretation serves the text, not the reverse.

#### The Exercise

The student should practice speech-in-character by the following method, which we prescribe as a sequence of graduated difficulty.

First, the student should select a scene from the Judaean Scriptures in which a character is present but silent, or speaks briefly where the situation clearly calls for more extended speech. Suitable subjects include: Ruth addressing Naomi on the road from Moab (the Book of Ruth, chapter 1, preserves Ruth's famous declaration of loyalty but not the inner deliberation that preceded it); Esther preparing to approach the Persian king uninvited (the Book of Esther, chapters 4-5, records the decision but not the private reasoning); Joseph recognizing his brothers in Egypt (Genesis 42, where the actions are described but the private thoughts are not).

Second, the student should study every passage in which the chosen character appears, noting the character's distinctive traits, habitual speech patterns, characteristic virtues and weaknesses, and significant relationships. The speech must be consistent with all of this prior material. If the character is Ruth, the student must note her loyalty, her courage, her willingness to leave her homeland, her attachment to Naomi, and her quiet determination. If the character is Moses, the student must note his humility, his anger, his insistence on justice, and his love for Israel.

Third, the student should compose a speech that arises naturally from the situation and is consistent with the character's established traits. The speech should address all three temporal dimensions: what has led to this moment (the past), what the situation now demands (the present), and what the speaker hopes or fears for the future. The speech should reveal something about the character that the scriptural text implies but does not state -- something that the hearer will recognize as true to the character, even though no source records the words.

Fourth, the student should test the speech against the text. Does anything in the speech contradict what Scripture explicitly says about the character? Does the speech sound like this particular person, or does it sound merely like a generic speaker? Would a learned hearer, intimately familiar with the scriptural text, accept this speech as plausible?

The teacher should assign: "Compose the speech that Ruth might have delivered to Naomi on the road from Moab to Bethlehem. Consider what arguments she would have made, what fears she would have addressed, how she would have spoken of her dead husband, of her native land, of the God of Israel whom she was choosing to follow. Let the speech reveal the inner deliberation that preceded the famous declaration: 'Where you go, I will go; where you lodge, I will lodge; your people shall be my people, and your God my God.'" The student should present his composition to the class, and the class should evaluate it for fidelity to the character, rhetorical effectiveness, and interpretive insight.[^157]

---

### II.L. Description (Ekphrasis / Tziyyur)

#### Definition and the Quality of Enargeia

"Description is an expressive speech which vividly brings what is shown before the eyes." So Theon defines the exercise, and we accept his definition as the foundation of our treatment.[^158] The key word in Theon's definition is *enargeia* -- vividness, the quality of making the absent present, the capacity of language to create in the hearer's mind an experience so vivid that he feels himself a witness to the scene described rather than merely a recipient of information about it. Aphthonius, in the twelfth chapter of his handbook, demonstrates the quality through his model descriptions of the Alexandrian acropolis and a battle scene, showing how the accumulation of sensory detail -- sight, sound, touch, smell -- transforms a verbal account into a lived experience.[^159] Quintilian, in his extended discussion of *evidentia* (his Latin rendering of *enargeia*) at *Institutio Oratoria* 8.3.61-71, declares that the supreme power of the orator lies in his ability to place the thing described "before the eyes" of the hearer, so that the hearer "seems not so much to be told about an event as to be present at it."[^160]

We define the exercise thus: *Ekphrasis is a speech that leads the hearer, through the vivid representation of sensory particulars, into the living presence of the thing described -- whether a person, a place, a time, an event, or an object -- so that he sees, hears, and feels what is described as if he stood there himself.* The student who has mastered narrative can tell the hearer *what happened*; the student who masters description can make the hearer *experience* what happened. The difference is the difference between report and presence, between knowledge and witness.

The quality of enargeia is achieved not through general statements but through the accumulation of specific, sensory, concrete details. The orator who says "the Temple was magnificent" has stated a proposition; the orator who describes the golden vine that hung over the entrance to the sanctuary, the smoke of the incense rising in a straight column through the still air, the sound of the High Priest's bare feet on the stone floor of the Holy of Holies -- that orator has placed the hearer inside the Temple. The principle may be stated as a rule: *describe what the eye sees, the ear hears, the hand touches, the nose smells; let the hearer's own imagination complete the picture; and never tell the hearer what to feel, for the description itself, if it is vivid enough, will produce the feeling without instruction.*

#### Types of Description

The student should master the following types, all of which are enumerated by Theon and Aphthonius, and all of which find abundant illustration in the Judaean sources:

**Description of Persons.** The physical appearance, bearing, dress, and distinctive features of an individual, composed so as to make the hearer see the person as if present. The Judaean sages occasionally produce descriptions of remarkable vividness -- descriptions of physical beauty, of physical prowess, of the bearing and appearance of the great sages -- and these descriptions almost always serve a purpose beyond mere portraiture: they reveal something about the inner character or the symbolic significance of the person described.

**Description of Places.** The Temple, the city of Jerusalem, the study-house, a garden, a wilderness, a city under siege. The Judaean tradition contains some of the most architecturally precise descriptions in all of ancient literature, particularly in the Mishnah's treatment of the Temple and its precincts, where the dimensions, materials, and spatial relationships of every chamber and court are recorded with the exactness of a builder's plan.

**Description of Times.** The dawn of the Day of Atonement, the evening of Passover, the moment before the Sabbath descends, the hour of the destruction. Descriptions of times evoke the emotional and spiritual quality of particular moments -- the anticipation, the solemnity, the terror, the ecstasy that attaches to sacred or catastrophic time.

**Description of Events.** The crossing of the sea, the giving of the law at Sinai, the destruction of the Temple, the martyrdom of a sage. Event-descriptions are the most complex form, for they combine descriptions of persons, places, and times into a unified narrative scene.

**Description of Objects and Works of Art.** The vessels of the Temple, the priestly vestments, the ark of the covenant, the menorah, the breastplate of the High Priest. The Judaean tradition preserves remarkably detailed descriptions of sacred objects, serving both a practical function (preserving the specifications of objects that can no longer be seen) and a liturgical function (evoking the splendor of the sacred service in the absence of the service itself).

#### Examples from the Judaean Tradition

**Example 1: The Service of the Day of Atonement.**

The Mishnah's description of the Day of Atonement service in the tractate *Yoma* is, we assert without hesitation, the most sustained and elaborate example of ekphrasis in all of Judaean literature, and one of the most remarkable in all of ancient literature. The description proceeds through the entire day, from the High Priest's preparations before dawn to his emergence from the Temple at nightfall, with a degree of detail that makes the hearer a witness to every stage of the service.

Consider the description of the High Priest's preparations:

> <span dir="rtl">פרשו סדין של בוץ בינו לבין העם. פשט ירד וטבל, עלה ונסתפג. הביאו לו בגדי זהב, ולבש וקידש ידיו ורגליו</span>
>
> *They spread a linen sheet between him and the people. He undressed, went down and immersed, came up and dried himself. They brought him the golden garments, and he dressed and sanctified his hands and feet.* (Mishnah Yoma 3:6)[^161]

And the climactic moment when the High Priest pronounces the divine Name:

> <span dir="rtl">וכשהיה מזכיר את השם, הכהנים והעם העומדים בעזרה, כשהיו שומעים שם המפורש שהוא יוצא מפי כהן גדול, היו כורעים ומשתחוים ונופלים על פניהם ואומרים: ברוך שם כבוד מלכותו לעולם ועד</span>
>
> *And when he pronounced the Name, the priests and the people standing in the Temple court, when they heard the Explicit Name issuing from the mouth of the High Priest, would kneel and prostrate themselves and fall on their faces and say: Blessed be the name of His glorious kingdom forever and ever.* (Mishnah Yoma 6:2)

The student should observe the descriptive technique. The Mishnah moves systematically through the sensory registers: from the visual (the linen sheet, the golden garments, the movements of dressing and immersing) to the auditory (the pronunciation of the divine Name) to the physical (kneeling, prostrating, falling on the face) to the verbal (the congregational response). The effect is cumulative: each sensory detail adds another layer to the scene, until the hearer feels himself standing in the Temple court, watching the High Priest, hearing the Name, falling to the ground with the crowd.

This description serves a function that goes beyond rhetoric: it is an act of sacred memory. The Temple has been destroyed; the Day of Atonement service can no longer be performed. But the Mishnah's description is so precise, so vivid, so richly detailed that the one who studies it can, in some measure, be present at the service. Description becomes, in the absence of the thing described, an act of preservation -- the spoken word doing the work of the stone and gold that have been lost.

**Example 2: The Joy of the Water Drawing.**

The Mishnah in tractate *Sukkah* (5:1-4) contains a celebrated declaration followed by an elaborate description:

> <span dir="rtl">מי שלא ראה שמחת בית השואבה, לא ראה שמחה מימיו</span>
>
> *He who has not seen the joy of the Water Drawing celebration has never seen joy in his life.* (Mishnah Sukkah 5:1)[^162]

This superlative claim functions as a rhetorical frame -- having set the emotional register at the highest possible level, the Mishnah must now produce a description vivid enough to justify it:

> <span dir="rtl">חסידים ואנשי מעשה היו מרקדים לפניהם באבוקות של אור שבידיהם, ואומרים לפניהם דברי שירות ותושבחות. והלויים בכנורות ובנבלים ובמצלתים ובחצוצרות ובכלי שיר בלא מספר, על חמש עשרה מעלות היורדות מעזרת ישראל לעזרת נשים</span>
>
> *Pious men and men of good deeds would dance before them with burning torches in their hands, and they would recite before them words of song and praise. And the Levites with lyres and harps and cymbals and trumpets and countless musical instruments, upon the fifteen steps that descend from the Court of the Israelites to the Women's Court.* (Mishnah Sukkah 5:2-4)

The description then narrows from the panoramic to the individual, describing the specific feats of named sages:

> <span dir="rtl">אמרו עליו על רבן שמעון בן גמליאל: כשהיה שמח שמחת בית השואבה, היה נוטל שמונה אבוקות של אור וזורק אחת ונוטל אחת, ואין נוגעות זו בזו</span>
>
> *They said of Rabban Shimon ben Gamaliel: When he would rejoice at the Water Drawing celebration, he would take eight burning torches and throw one up and catch another, and none of them would touch each other.*

And further:

> <span dir="rtl">אמרו: כשהיה רבן שמעון בן גמליאל משתחוה, היה נועץ שני גודליו בארץ ושוחה ונושק את הרצפה וזוקף</span>
>
> *They said: When Rabban Shimon ben Gamaliel would prostrate himself, he would plant both thumbs in the ground, bend down and kiss the floor, and straighten up again.*

The student should observe the movement from general to particular -- the classic descriptive technique of establishing the broad scene before populating it with individual figures. First the crowd, the torches, the music, the architectural setting; then the named sage juggling eight torches in the torchlight; then another sage performing a physical feat of extraordinary devotion. The description engages sight (the burning torches in the darkness), sound (the musical instruments), and movement (the dancing, the juggling, the prostration) simultaneously, creating an experience of overwhelming sensory richness.

**Example 3: The Beauty of Rabbi Yohanan.**

The Babylonian Talmud in Bava Metzia 84a contains a description of remarkable vividness:

> <span dir="rtl">שופריה דרבי יוחנן מעין שופריה דיעקב אבינו. שופריה דיעקב אבינו מעין שופריה דאדם הראשון</span>
>
> *The beauty of Rabbi Yohanan was a reflection of the beauty of Jacob our father. The beauty of Jacob our father was a reflection of the beauty of the first man, Adam.* (BT Bava Metzia 84a)[^163]

The student should observe that this is not merely a description of physical beauty but a theological statement about the nature of beauty itself -- that human beauty is a descending chain from the primordial perfection of Adam, diminishing with each generation. The description of a person becomes simultaneously a description of the human condition: the progressive loss of an original splendor. Moreover, the passage introduces the figure of Resh Lakish -- a man of extraordinary physical strength -- who encounters Rabbi Yohanan at the Jordan River, and the contrast between the two men (beauty and strength) generates a narrative of conversion, intellectual partnership, and tragic loss that is one of the great stories in Judaean literature. Description here functions not in isolation but as the engine of narrative -- the vivid depiction of a person sets in motion an entire dramatic sequence.

**Example 4: The Destruction of the Temple.**

The Babylonian Talmud in Gittin 55b-58a contains a sustained narrative of the destruction of Jerusalem that achieves its power almost entirely through the art of description. Consider the description of the famine during the Roman siege:

> <span dir="rtl">מרתא בת בייתוס, עשירה שבירושלים היתה. שיגרה לשלוחה: לך הבא לי סלת. עד דאזל, איזדבן. אתא אמר לה: סלת ליכא, חיוורתא איכא. אמרה ליה: זיל אייתי. עד דאזל, איזדבן. אתא ואמר לה: חיוורתא ליכא, גושקרא איכא... נפקה לשוקא ואיתלי פרתא בכרעא ומתה</span>
>
> *Martha bat Boethus was the wealthiest woman in Jerusalem. She sent her servant: Go and bring me fine flour. By the time he went, it was sold. He came and said: There is no fine flour, but there is white flour. She said: Go and bring it. By the time he went, it was sold. He came and said: There is no white flour, but there is coarse flour... She went out to the marketplace and dung stuck to her foot and she died.* (BT Gittin 56a)[^164]

The student should observe the technique with particular care. The sage does not state the abstraction: "there was a terrible famine." He shows the famine through the experience of a single woman -- a woman of great wealth, accustomed to the finest provisions -- who is reduced, step by step, to coarser and coarser sustenance until even the coarsest is unavailable. The descending sequence -- fine flour, white flour, coarse flour, barley flour -- each sold out before the servant can return -- creates a rhythm of accumulating desperation. And the final image -- the great lady who goes out into the street and dies from stepping in refuse -- is so specific, so physical, so utterly undignified that it conveys the horror of the siege more powerfully than any general statement could.

This is *enargeia* at its most devastating. The sage has made the hearer see not the siege as a whole (which is too vast to visualize) but one woman's descent from luxury to death (which is vivid and intimate). The particular stands for the universal; the single case illuminates the catastrophe. And the description achieves its emotional effect without any explicit statement of pathos -- the sage does not say "it was terrible"; he describes what happened, and the terribleness arises from the description itself.

This last point deserves emphasis as a principle of the exercise: the most powerful descriptions are those that produce emotion without naming it. The orator who says "it was sad" has told the hearer what to feel; the orator who describes the details and allows the sadness to arise naturally from the description has made the hearer feel it for himself -- and what one feels for oneself is felt more deeply than what one is told to feel.

#### The Principle of Enargeia Restated

Let us dwell upon the quality of *enargeia* a moment longer, for it is the soul of the exercise. Quintilian distinguishes between *narratio* (narration), which tells the hearer what happened, and *descriptio* (description), which shows him. The distinction is not one of subject matter but of technique. The same event -- the destruction of Jerusalem, the Sinai theophany -- can be narrated (in which case the hearer receives information) or described (in which case the hearer receives an experience). The difference lies entirely in the selection and deployment of sensory particulars.

The student should observe how the Judaean sages achieve enargeia through what we might call the *technique of the telling detail*. They do not attempt to describe everything; they select one or two details so vivid, so specific, so unexpected that the detail stands for the whole. Martha bat Boethus dying from stepping in dung -- that single image conveys the horror of the famine more powerfully than a census of the dead could. Rabban Shimon ben Gamaliel juggling eight torches, none touching the other -- that single image conveys the ecstasy of the Water Drawing celebration more powerfully than any general account of merrymaking. The student should learn this economy: one right detail is worth a thousand generalities.

Moreover, the student should understand that description in the Judaean tradition serves a function beyond aesthetic pleasure. It serves the function of *memory*. The Temple has been destroyed. The sacred service has ceased. The pilgrimage festivals are no longer celebrated as they were in the days when the Temple stood. But the descriptions preserved in the Mishnah and the Talmud keep alive what would otherwise be lost. When the sage describes the Day of Atonement service with such precision that the hearer can visualize every gesture, every garment, every moment of silence and speech -- the sage is performing an act not merely of rhetoric but of sacred remembrance. Description is the instrument by which the past is made present, and by which the community maintains its connection to what it has lost. In this sense, ekphrasis in the Judaean tradition is not merely an exercise; it is a liturgical and theological act.

#### The Exercise

The student should practice description by the following method.

First, select a scene from the Judaean tradition that calls for vivid depiction. Suitable subjects include: the giving of the law at Sinai (Exodus 19-20), the binding of Isaac (Genesis 22), the crossing of the Red Sea (Exodus 14-15), the dedication of Solomon's Temple (1 Kings 8), or any scene from the Mishnah's descriptions of the Temple service.

Second, identify the sensory details that the text provides or implies. What would one see at this scene? What would one hear? What would one feel on one's skin -- the heat, the wind, the trembling of the ground? What would one smell -- the incense, the smoke, the dust? The student must think with his senses, not merely with his intellect.

Third, compose a description that moves from the general to the particular -- first establishing the broad setting (the mountain, the sky, the weather, the time of day), then narrowing to specific details (a particular figure, a particular gesture, a particular sound). Let the description engage multiple senses simultaneously, as the Mishnah does in its account of the Water Drawing celebration.

Fourth, test the description for enargeia. Read it aloud. Does the hearer see the scene? Does he feel present? If the description remains abstract or propositional -- if it tells rather than shows -- it has not yet achieved its purpose. Add more sensory detail. Make the hearer see the lightning, hear the shofar, feel the ground shake.

The teacher should assign: "Describe the scene at Sinai so that your hearer trembles as if he stood at the foot of the mountain. Use the details provided by Scripture -- the thunder, the lightning, the thick cloud, the sound of the shofar growing steadily louder, the smoking mountain, the trembling people -- and compose a description that makes these details vivid and immediate. Remember: your hearer was not at Sinai, but through your description, he must feel as if he was." The student should present his composition to the class, and the class should evaluate it for vividness, faithfulness to the text, and the presence or absence of that quality which is the supreme aim of the exercise -- the quality of *enargeia*.[^165]

---

### II.M. Thesis (Thesis / She'elah Kelalit)

#### Definition and the Distinction from Hypothesis

The thesis is that exercise in which the student argues a general proposition -- not a particular case involving named persons and specific circumstances, but a universal question that applies to all persons and all circumstances. We define it thus: *Thesis is the investigation of a general question, without reference to particular individuals or specific situations, asking whether a thing is true, or good, or expedient, or just.* The thesis asks not "Should Reuven study or work?" but "Is study greater than action?" The thesis asks not "Should Shimon accept his sufferings?" but "Is suffering a good thing?" The generality is the essence: the thesis strips away the particular circumstances that make a specific case unique and examines the question in its most universal form.[^166]

The student must understand clearly the distinction between the thesis (which the Greeks call *thesis* and the Judaean sages term *she'elah kelalit*, the general question) and the hypothesis (which the Greeks call *hypothesis* and which corresponds to the specific case that comes before the Judaean court). Cicero, in *De Inventione* 1.8, provides the classic formulation: the thesis is a question without persons and circumstances (*quaestio infinita*); the hypothesis is a question involving particular persons and circumstances (*quaestio finita*).[^167] Quintilian, at *Institutio Oratoria* 2.4.24-25 and again at 3.5, elaborates the distinction and argues that the thesis is the more philosophically demanding of the two, because it requires the student to reason about universal principles rather than contingent facts. The thesis prepares the student for the hypothesis, because the student who has already argued the general proposition can more readily apply it to a specific case.

The Judaean sages did not always distinguish between thesis and hypothesis with explicit terminology, but the distinction is operative throughout their literature. When the sages debated whether Torah study or practical action is greater, they were arguing a thesis. When a specific sage faced the question of whether to continue his studies or go tend to his aged father, he confronted a hypothesis -- a particular application of the general question. The thesis provides the principles; the hypothesis applies them to the case.

#### Types of Thesis

Theon and Aphthonius distinguish two principal types, both abundantly represented in the Judaean tradition:[^168]

**The Practical Thesis.** This concerns what one should *do*: Is it better to study or to act? Should one pray at fixed times or spontaneously? Should a sage teach Torah for payment? Should a person marry? Should one live in the homeland or in the diaspora? The practical thesis is closely allied to deliberative discourse, for it asks the same kind of question that a legislative assembly would ask: what course of action is best for the community?

**The Theoretical Thesis.** This concerns what is *true*: Is the world eternal or created? Is suffering a punishment or a purification? Is divine providence general or particular? Does the soul survive the death of the body? The theoretical thesis is more philosophically demanding than the practical, because it asks questions that may not admit of definitive resolution. Yet the Judaean sages debated theoretical theses with remarkable vigor and with a characteristic willingness to leave competing positions standing side by side, each acknowledged as possessing partial truth.

The student should also observe that many theses in the Judaean tradition combine the practical and the theoretical. The question "Is suffering a good thing?" is simultaneously theoretical (what is the nature of suffering in the divine economy?) and practical (how should one respond when suffering comes?). The most powerful theses operate on both levels at once, and the student should learn to exploit this double register.

#### Examples from the Judaean Tradition

**Example 1: "Is Study Greater or Is Action Greater?"**

We have already encountered this debate in our treatment of comparison (section II.J), but here we consider it specifically as a thesis -- a general question formally argued before an assembly. The Babylonian Talmud records:

> <span dir="rtl">וכבר היה רבי טרפון וזקנים מסובין בעלייה בבית נתזה בלוד. נשאלה שאלה זו בפניהם: תלמוד גדול או מעשה גדול? נענה רבי טרפון ואמר: מעשה גדול. נענה רבי עקיבא ואמר: תלמוד גדול. נענו כולם ואמרו: תלמוד גדול, שהתלמוד מביא לידי מעשה</span>
>
> *It once happened that Rabbi Tarfon and the elders were reclining in the upper chamber of the house of Nit'zah in Lydda. This question was raised before them: Is study greater or is action greater? Rabbi Tarfon answered and said: Action is greater. Rabbi Akiva answered and said: Study is greater. They all answered and said: Study is greater, for study leads to action.* (BT Kiddushin 40b)[^169]

The student should analyze this passage as a model of the thesis exercise in its most developed form. We observe, first, the formal setting: a convocation of named sages at a specific location, lending gravity to the deliberation. Second, the question is stated in its purely general form -- not "should this student study or act?" but "is study or action greater?" -- that is, a *thesis*, not a *hypothesis*. Third, the debate proceeds through named speakers who represent opposing positions: Rabbi Tarfon for action (because action produces tangible results in the world), Rabbi Akiva for study (because study generates the knowledge that makes right action possible). Fourth, and most significantly, the resolution transcends both positions through a formula that preserves both: "Study is greater, *for study leads to action.*" The causal clause transforms the verdict from a simple ranking into a reasoned synthesis: study is the greater because it is the *cause* of right action. Without study, action is blind; but study that does not lead to action is barren. The resolution honors both positions by establishing their proper causal relationship.

The student should internalize this pattern: the well-argued thesis does not seek the annihilation of one side but the discovery of a resolution that accounts for the strongest arguments on both sides. This is the distinctive characteristic of Judaean thesis-argumentation, and it represents a mode of resolution that the Greek handbooks, with their more agonistic orientation, do not always emphasize.

**Example 2: "Should a Sage Teach Torah for Payment?"**

This practical thesis is argued across multiple sources in the Judaean tradition, and the student should trace the argument through its full development.

The initial position is stated with aphoristic force in the treatise Avot:

> <span dir="rtl">ודאשתמש בתגא -- חלף</span>
>
> *One who makes use of the crown [of Torah for personal gain] -- shall perish.* (Avot 1:13)

And again:

> <span dir="rtl">אל תעשם עטרה להתגדל בהן, ולא קרדום לחפור בהן</span>
>
> *Do not make them [words of Torah] a crown with which to aggrandize yourself, nor a spade with which to dig.* (Avot 4:5)

The counterargument emerges from the Babylonian Talmud:

> <span dir="rtl">כדרך שלמדני חנם כך אני מלמד חנם... אמר רב: שכר שימור, שכר פיסוק טעמים</span>
>
> *"Just as I learned freely, so I teach freely"... Rav said: [What is permitted is] payment for supervision, payment for the teaching of cantillation marks.* (BT Nedarim 37a)[^170]

The thesis develops through a dialectic of principle and practicality. The principle is clear: Torah, being divine wisdom, cannot be treated as a commodity. The practical objection is equally clear: if teachers cannot earn a livelihood, they will abandon teaching, and the transmission of Torah will cease. The resolution -- that payment is permitted for ancillary services (supervision, vocalization) but not for the teaching of Torah content itself -- is a characteristic Judaean compromise: the principle is upheld in its full force, but a practical mechanism is found that allows the system to function without violating the principle.

The student should observe that this resolution is not a weak splitting of the difference but a conceptually precise distinction: there is a real difference between paying someone to teach you divine wisdom (which debases the wisdom) and paying someone for the time and effort of providing structured instruction (which merely compensates a service).
