The thesis exercise, when practiced at this level, trains the student not merely to argue but to *think* -- to discover conceptual distinctions that resolve apparently intractable tensions.

**Example 3: "Is Suffering a Good Thing?"**

The Babylonian Talmud, in tractate Berakhot 5a-b, contains one of the most searching theological discussions in all of Judaean literature, structured as a thesis on the nature of suffering:

> <span dir="rtl">אמר רבא, אמר רב סחורה, אמר רב הונא: כל שהקדוש ברוך הוא חפץ בו מדכאו ביסורין, שנאמר: "וה' חפץ דכאו החלי." יכול אפילו לא קבלם מאהבה? תלמוד לומר: "אם תשים אשם נפשו" -- מה אשם לדעת, אף יסורין לדעת</span>
>
> *Rava said, Rav Sehora said, Rav Huna said: Whoever the Holy One, blessed be He, desires, He crushes with suffering, as it is said: "The Lord desired to crush him with sickness" (Isaiah 53:10). One might think: even if he does not accept them with love? The verse teaches: "If his soul offers itself as a guilt offering" -- just as a guilt offering requires conscious intent, so suffering requires conscious acceptance.* (BT Berakhot 5a)[^171]

The passage develops the thesis through the concept of "sufferings of love" (*yissurin shel ahavah*) -- the doctrine that God sends suffering not as punishment but as an expression of love, to purify and elevate the righteous:

> <span dir="rtl">אם רואה אדם שיסורין באין עליו, יפשפש במעשיו. פשפש ולא מצא, יתלה בביטול תורה. ואם תלה ולא מצא, בידוע שיסורין של אהבה הם</span>
>
> *If a person sees that sufferings come upon him, let him examine his deeds. If he examines and finds nothing wrong, let him attribute it to neglect of Torah study. And if he attributes it and finds nothing, it is certain that these are sufferings of love.*

But the thesis is not so easily resolved. The passage presents a devastating counterpoint:

> <span dir="rtl">רבי יוחנן חלש, על לגביה רבי חנינא. אמר ליה: חביבין עליך יסורין? אמר ליה: לא הן ולא שכרן. אמר ליה: הב לי ידך. יהב ליה ידיה ואוקמיה</span>
>
> *Rabbi Yohanan fell ill. Rabbi Hanina came to visit him. He said to him: Are your sufferings dear to you? He said to him: Neither they nor their reward. He said to him: Give me your hand. He gave him his hand and he raised him up.* (BT Berakhot 5b)

The student should observe the extraordinary intellectual honesty of this passage. The theological thesis -- that suffering is an expression of divine love, that its acceptance yields spiritual reward -- is stated with full force. But then it is tested against the experience of a sage who has actually suffered (Rabbi Yohanan, who lost ten sons to illness), and the sage rejects the thesis outright: "Neither they nor their reward." He wants neither the suffering nor whatever spiritual benefit it might confer. And his colleague does not rebuke him; he simply takes his hand and heals him.

The thesis remains unresolved. The passage presents both the theology of redemptive suffering and its rejection by one who has lived through suffering -- and it lets both positions stand. This is one of the most important lessons the thesis exercise can teach the student: some questions do not admit of definitive resolution. The honest orator argues both sides as forcefully as he can and then acknowledges that the question exceeds the capacity of human reason to settle. The thesis exercise trains the student not only in the art of argument but in the recognition of the limits of argument.

**Example 4: "Should One Pray at Fixed Times or Spontaneously?"**

The Mishnah in tractate Berakhot 4:4 records a debate that is, in its essence, a practical thesis on the nature of prayer:

> <span dir="rtl">רבי אליעזר אומר: העושה תפלתו קבע -- אין תפלתו תחנונים</span>
>
> *Rabbi Eliezer says: One who makes his prayer fixed -- his prayer is not supplication.* (Mishnah Berakhot 4:4)[^172]

The Talmud elaborates by offering three definitions of what constitutes "fixed" prayer:

> <span dir="rtl">מאי קבע? רבי יעקב בר אידי אמר רב: כל שתפלתו דומה עליו כמשאוי. ורבנן אמרי: כל שאינו אומרה בלשון תחנונים. רבה ורב יוסף דאמרי תרוייהו: כל שאינו יכול לחדש בה דבר</span>
>
> *What is "fixed"? Rabbi Jacob bar Idi said in the name of Rav: Anyone whose prayer seems to him like a burden. And the rabbis say: Anyone who does not pray in the language of supplication. Rabbah and Rav Yosef both say: Anyone who cannot innovate something in his prayer.* (BT Berakhot 29b)

The thesis concerns the fundamental nature of prayer: Is prayer primarily a legal obligation to be performed with set words at set times, or is it primarily a spontaneous expression of the heart that cannot be reduced to formula? The Judaean tradition resolves the thesis not by choosing one side but by integrating both: the set form of prayer (*matbe'a shel tefillah*) provides the structure, while the intention (*kavvanah*) of the one who prays provides the spontaneous, personal element. One prays with fixed words but makes them new each time through the depth of one's concentration.

The student should observe that this is a resolution of the type that the Judaean tradition characteristically favors: not the victory of one position over the other, but the integration of both into a practice richer and more complex than either position alone could produce. The three definitions of "fixed" prayer are themselves instructive for the student of thesis-argumentation: each defines the problem differently (prayer experienced as a burden, prayer without the language of supplication, prayer without the capacity for innovation), and each definition implies a different remedy. The orator who argues a thesis must be aware that the way a problem is *defined* already determines, in large measure, the range of available solutions. The student who can redefine the terms of a thesis has gained a decisive advantage over the student who merely accepts the terms as given.

We may also observe that the prayer debate illuminates a structural feature of many Judaean theses: they concern not two mutually exclusive alternatives but two goods that stand in tension with each other. Fixed prayer and spontaneous prayer are both genuine goods -- structure provides reliability, spontaneity provides authenticity -- and the thesis asks not which good should be discarded but how both can be preserved simultaneously. This is a fundamentally different kind of thesis from the agonistic type, in which one side must be eliminated for the other to prevail. The student should learn to distinguish between theses that pose a genuine either/or choice and theses that pose an apparent either/or but in fact admit of synthesis. Many of the most interesting theses in the Judaean tradition are of the second type.

#### The Exercise

The student should practice the thesis exercise by the following method.

First, formulate the thesis as a clear, general question. The question must be genuinely debatable -- it must admit of strong arguments on both sides. A thesis that can be resolved by a simple appeal to an authoritative text is not a thesis but a question of fact. The thesis must concern a genuine tension between two goods, two values, or two principles that are each defensible in their own right.

Second, argue one side with full force. Marshal every available argument: authoritative texts, logical reasoning, precedent, analogy, appeal to consequences. Present the strongest possible case. Do not weaken your arguments in anticipation of the opposing side.

Third, argue the other side with equal force. This is the more difficult step, for the student's natural inclination is to favor one position. But the thesis exercise requires that both sides be argued with equal vigor and equal intellectual honesty. The student who presents a weak version of the opposing position has not mastered the exercise.

Fourth, seek a resolution. The resolution may take one of several forms: it may declare one side the winner and provide the reason; it may discover a synthesis that integrates both positions; or it may honestly acknowledge that the question remains open. All three forms are legitimate. What is not legitimate is to pretend that one side has no merit, or to resolve the question by ignoring the difficulty.

The teacher should assign: "Argue both sides of this thesis: Is it better to give charity publicly, so as to inspire others, or privately, so as to preserve the dignity of the recipient? Marshal the strongest arguments on each side, and seek a resolution." The student should present his argumentation to the class, and the class should evaluate both the force of each side and the wisdom of the resolution.[^173]

---

### II.N. Introduction of a Law (Nomou Eisphora / Takkanah)

#### Definition and Status as Capstone Exercise

The introduction of a law is the most advanced of the preliminary exercises -- the capstone and culmination of the entire sequence. Aphthonius, in the fourteenth and final chapter of his handbook, places it last among the exercises for good reason: it requires all the skills practiced in the preceding thirteen exercises, combined and deployed in the service of a single, complex act of persuasion.[^174]

We define the exercise thus: *Nomou eisphora is the arguing for or against a proposed law or enactment, deploying every art of persuasion at the orator's command -- narrative, praise, blame, comparison, description, thesis, speech-in-character -- in order to convince an assembly that a specific measure should be adopted, modified, or rejected.* The student who undertakes this exercise stands, for the first time, in the position of the legislator -- not merely analyzing existing materials (as in the chreia and the maxim), not merely composing isolated set-pieces (as in the encomium and the description), but constructing a complete, multi-part argument directed at a specific practical outcome: the creation or modification of law.

Theon discusses the exercise as the natural terminus of the preliminary curriculum, the point at which the student's training intersects with the actual work of civic life.[^175] Quintilian, at *Institutio Oratoria* 2.4.33, affirms that the proposal of a law is the exercise that most directly prepares the student for actual forensic and deliberative oratory, because it requires the student to address a complex practical question using every tool he has learned. And so it is in the Judaean tradition: the sage who rises before the assembly to argue for or against a proposed enactment -- a *takkanah* (positive regulation) or a *gezerah* (preventive decree) -- is performing the highest and most consequential form of rhetorical speech in the Judaean legal system. He is not merely analyzing texts or composing exercises; he is shaping the law that will govern the life of the community.

Consider what the student must do when he argues for or against a proposed enactment. He must *narrate* the circumstances that have given rise to the need for the law -- and this requires the skills of narrative. He must *praise* the values the enactment protects and *censure* the harms it prevents -- and this requires the skills of encomium and invective. He must *compare* the proposed enactment with existing laws -- and this requires the skills of comparison. He must *describe* the consequences that will follow if the law is adopted, and those that will follow if it is not -- and this requires the skills of description. He must argue the *thesis* that the underlying principle is sound -- and this requires the skills of thesis-argumentation. He must *compose speeches* for those who will be affected by the enactment, giving voice to the poor who will benefit, the powerful who will resist, the community that will bear the cost -- and this requires the skills of ethopoeia. Every exercise the student has practiced reaches its practical application in this final exercise.

#### Methods of Argument

The student who argues for or against a proposed enactment should employ the following heads of argument, which correspond to those enumerated by Aphthonius for the proposal of a law and which are also operative in the legislative deliberations of the Judaean sages:[^176]

**Argument from Justice (to dikaion / tzedek).** The enactment should be *just*. It should protect the rights of the vulnerable, prevent the exploitation of the weak, and ensure that the burdens it imposes are distributed fairly. The orator argues: "This enactment is necessary because without it, a class of persons will suffer injustice." Or conversely: "This enactment should be rejected because it will create an injustice greater than the harm it seeks to remedy."

**Argument from Legality (to nomimon / din).** The enactment should be *consistent* with existing law. It should not contradict established legal principles without compelling justification. The orator argues: "This enactment is supported by the following existing principles and precedents." Or: "This enactment contradicts established law and should be rejected unless the proponents can demonstrate that circumstances have changed sufficiently to warrant departure from precedent."

**Argument from Possibility (to dunaton / efshar).** The enactment should be *practicable*. It should be something the community can actually observe. The Judaean sages formulated this principle explicitly:

> <span dir="rtl">אין גוזרין גזירה על הציבור אלא אם כן רוב הציבור יכולין לעמוד בה</span>
>
> *One does not impose a decree upon the public unless the majority of the public can endure it.* (BT Bava Kamma 79b; Avodah Zarah 36a)[^177]

This principle -- that legislation must be practicable by the majority -- is the Judaean counterpart to the Greco-Roman rhetorical topic of *dunaton* (possibility), and it represents a fundamental constraint on the legislator's authority. The most just and legally sound enactment is worthless if the community cannot bear it.

**Argument from Consistency (to akolouthon / hashva'ah).** The enactment should be *consistent* with the general spirit and principles of the legal system, even if no specific text directly addresses the matter. The orator argues: "This enactment is consonant with the Torah's overarching concern for human dignity" -- or for the sanctity of life, or the protection of the poor, or the integrity of the family.

**Argument from Expedience (to sympheron / to'elet).** The enactment should produce *benefit*. It should serve a recognizable good and prevent a recognizable harm. The orator argues: "The practical benefits of this enactment outweigh its costs." Or: "The harms produced by this enactment exceed its benefits."

**Argument from Propriety (to prepon / kavod).** The enactment should be *proper* -- it should conform to standards of decency, dignity, and the honor due to God, to the law, and to human beings. The Judaean sages invoked the principle of *kavod ha-beriyot* (human dignity) as a legislative argument of extraordinary power -- so powerful, indeed, that it could override certain negative commandments:

> <span dir="rtl">גדול כבוד הבריות שדוחה את לא תעשה שבתורה</span>
>
> *Great is human dignity, for it overrides a negative commandment of the Torah.* (BT Berakhot 19b)

The student should master all six heads of argument and should learn to deploy them in combination, for the strongest legislative arguments are those that succeed on multiple grounds simultaneously -- demonstrating that the proposed enactment is just *and* legal *and* practicable *and* consistent *and* beneficial *and* proper.

#### Examples from the Judaean Tradition

**Example 1: Hillel's Prozbul.**

The most celebrated example of legislative innovation in all of Judaean law is the institution by the sage Hillel of the legal instrument known as the *prozbul*. The Mishnah records:

> <span dir="rtl">פרוזבול אינו משמט. זה אחד מן הדברים שהתקין הלל הזקן. כשראה שנמנעו העם מלהלוות זה את זה, ועוברין על מה שכתוב בתורה: "השמר לך פן יהיה דבר עם לבבך בליעל" -- התקין הלל פרוזבול</span>
>
> *A prozbul is not cancelled [by the sabbatical year]. This is one of the things that Hillel the Elder enacted. When he saw that the people were refraining from lending to one another, and were transgressing what is written in the Torah: "Beware lest there be a base thought in your heart" [Deuteronomy 15:9] -- Hillel enacted the prozbul.* (Mishnah Sheviit 10:3-4)[^178]

The student should analyze the arguments implicit in this enactment, for they illustrate the deployment of multiple heads of argument simultaneously.

The biblical law commands the cancellation of all debts in the sabbatical year (Deuteronomy 15:1-2). The *purpose* of this law was to protect the poor from perpetual indebtedness. But the *effect* of the law was paradoxically the opposite: as the sabbatical year approached, potential lenders refused to extend credit, knowing that their loans would be cancelled. The poor -- the very class the law was designed to protect -- found themselves unable to borrow.

Hillel's argument runs as follows. From *justice*: the poor are being harmed by the law intended to help them; the enactment corrects this injustice. From *expedience*: the prozbul restores the flow of credit and thereby serves the economic welfare of the entire community. From *consistency*: the prozbul does not abolish the sabbatical cancellation; it creates a legal mechanism (transferring the debt to the court, which is not subject to the sabbatical release) that respects the formal structure of the existing law while adjusting its practical operation. From *legality*: the authority to make such an adjustment rests on the principle that the sabbatical cancellation, in the post-Temple era, is of rabbinic rather than biblical force, and the sages possess the authority to modify their own enactments when circumstances require. From *propriety*: it is undignified and destructive for the poor to be denied the credit they need to sustain themselves and their families.

The student should observe the rhetorical brilliance of this legislative argument. Hillel does not argue *against* the Torah's law; he argues *for* the Torah's deepest purpose, demonstrating that his enactment serves the law's original intention (protecting the poor) even as it modifies the law's practical operation (cancellation of debts). This is the highest form of legislative advocacy: to show that the proposed change is not a departure from tradition but a more faithful expression of it.

**Example 2: Rabban Gamaliel's Divorce Enactments.**

The Mishnah in Gittin 4:2 records several enactments by Rabban Gamaliel the Elder designed to protect women in the process of divorce:

> <span dir="rtl">בראשונה היה עושה בית דין במקום אחר ומבטלו. התקין רבן גמליאל הזקן שלא יהו עושין כן, מפני תיקון העולם</span>
>
> *Originally, [a husband] could convene a court in another place and cancel [the bill of divorce after it had been dispatched]. Rabban Gamaliel the Elder enacted that they should not do so, for the betterment of the world.* (Mishnah Gittin 4:2)[^179]

The problem was this: a husband could send a bill of divorce (*get*) to his wife through a messenger, and then -- before the wife received it -- convene a court in a different location and cancel it. The wife, not knowing of the cancellation, would believe herself divorced. She might remarry. Her second marriage would then be invalid, and any children from it would be of impaired legal status.

The justification given is the comprehensive formula *mipnei tikkun ha-olam* -- "for the betterment of the world." This formula, which appears repeatedly throughout the fourth chapter of Gittin as the justification for various enactments, is itself a rhetorical signal of the broadest possible argument from justice and expedience: the measure is adopted not for narrow technical reasons but because the social order itself requires it. The student should observe that this formula functions in Judaean legislative rhetoric much as the appeal to *the public good* or *the common welfare* functions in Roman deliberative oratory -- it signals that the argument transcends the interests of any particular party and addresses the welfare of the entire community.

**Example 3: The Canonicity of Ecclesiastes and the Song of Songs.**

The Mishnah in Yadayim 3:5 records a legislative debate of a different kind -- not the enactment of a new regulation, but the determination of whether two books should be included in the sacred canon. The question is whether the books "defile the hands" -- a technical term indicating sacred status:

> <span dir="rtl">כל כתבי הקודש מטמאין את הידיים. שיר השירים וקוהלת מטמאין את הידיים. רבי יהודה אומר: שיר השירים מטמא את הידיים, וקוהלת מחלוקת. רבי יוסי אומר: קוהלת אינו מטמא את הידיים, ושיר השירים מחלוקת</span>
>
> *All the Holy Scriptures defile the hands. The Song of Songs and Ecclesiastes defile the hands. Rabbi Judah says: The Song of Songs defiles the hands, but Ecclesiastes is disputed. Rabbi Yose says: Ecclesiastes does not defile the hands, and the Song of Songs is disputed.* (Mishnah Yadayim 3:5)[^180]

The passage preserves Rabbi Akiva's passionate defense of the Song of Songs:

> <span dir="rtl">אמר רבי עקיבא: חס ושלום! לא נחלק אדם מישראל על שיר השירים שלא תטמא את הידיים, שאין כל העולם כולו כדאי כיום שניתן בו שיר השירים לישראל. שכל הכתובים קודש ושיר השירים קודש קודשים</span>
>
> *Rabbi Akiva said: God forbid! No one in Israel ever disputed that the Song of Songs defiles the hands, for all the world is not worth the day on which the Song of Songs was given to Israel. For all the Scriptures are holy, but the Song of Songs is the holy of holies.*

The student will recognize in Rabbi Akiva's argument several of the rhetorical techniques proper to the introduction of a law. The exclamation *chas ve-shalom* ("God forbid!") functions as a *praemunitio* -- a preemptive defense that characterizes the opposing position as unthinkable. The superlative claim that "all the world is not worth the day" functions as an *amplificatio* -- a magnification of the subject's importance that overwhelms objections by the sheer weight of the claim. And the final classification -- "all the Scriptures are holy, but the Song of Songs is the holy of holies" -- establishes a hierarchical category that places the text beyond dispute by assigning it the highest possible rank.

**Example 4: The Eighteen Decrees.**

The Mishnah in Shabbat 1:4 records one of the most controversial legislative events in Judaean history -- the passage of eighteen restrictive decrees (*gezerot*) by the school of Shammai over the objections of the school of Hillel:

> <span dir="rtl">אלו מן ההלכות שאמרו בעליית חנניה בן חזקיה בן גוריון, שעלו לבקרו. נמנו ורבו בית שמאי על בית הלל, ושמונה עשרה דברים גזרו בו ביום</span>
>
> *These are among the laws that they stated in the upper chamber of Hananiah ben Hizkiyah ben Garon, when they went up to visit him. They counted [the votes] and the school of Shammai outnumbered the school of Hillel, and they decreed eighteen things on that day.* (Mishnah Shabbat 1:4)[^181]

The Jerusalem Talmud records that this day was regarded as grievous:

> <span dir="rtl">אותו היום היה קשה לישראל כיום שנעשה בו העגל</span>
>
> *That day was as grievous for Israel as the day on which the Golden Calf was made.* (JT Shabbat 1:4, 3c)

The student should observe that this comparison -- equating a legislative session with the sin of the Golden Calf -- is itself a devastating rhetorical attack on the legislative process. The criticism is directed not at the content of the decrees but at the manner of their adoption: that they were passed by the school of Shammai using a numerical majority, apparently over the fierce objections of the school of Hillel, and that the circumstances of the vote may have involved coercion. This is argument from propriety: even if the decrees were substantively defensible, the process by which they were adopted was improper, and an improper process taints the legitimacy of its product.

The student should study this episode as an illustration of how the methods of argument for the introduction of a law (justice, legality, possibility, consistency, expedience, propriety) can also be deployed *against* an enactment. The opponent of a proposed law can challenge it on any of these grounds: it is unjust, it contradicts existing law, it is impracticable, it is inconsistent with established principles, it produces more harm than benefit, or it was adopted through an improper process.

**Example 5: The Enactments of Ezra.**

The Babylonian Talmud in Bava Kamma 82a preserves a tradition of ten enactments attributed to Ezra the Scribe -- the figure who, in Judaean tradition, stands at the transition between the biblical and the rabbinic eras:

> <span dir="rtl">עשרה תקנות תיקן עזרא: שקורין במנחה בשבת, ושקורין בשני ובחמישי, ודנין בשני ובחמישי, ומכבסין בחמישי בשבת, ואוכלין שום בערב שבת... ושיהו רוכלין מחזירין בעיירות... ושתיקן עזרא טבילה לבעלי קריין</span>
>
> *Ten enactments were made by Ezra: that the Torah be read at the Sabbath afternoon service; that the Torah be read on Mondays and Thursdays; that courts convene on Mondays and Thursdays; that laundry be done on Thursdays; that garlic be eaten on Sabbath eve... and that peddlers travel through the towns... and that Ezra instituted immersion for those who have had a seminal emission.* (BT Bava Kamma 82a)[^182]

The student should analyze these enactments as a program of social legislation, observing how each addresses a practical need of the community: the reading of Torah on market days (Mondays and Thursdays) ensures that the people who come to town for commerce also encounter Torah; the scheduling of courts on those same days ensures access to justice for the rural population; the timing of laundry before the Sabbath ensures that the Sabbath is honored with clean garments. Each enactment is defensible on the grounds of justice, expedience, and propriety; taken together, they constitute a coherent legislative program that structures the weekly rhythm of communal life.

The attribution of these enactments to Ezra is itself rhetorically significant: by associating the legislation with a figure of quasi-scriptural authority, the tradition lends the enactments a weight and permanence that purely rabbinic legislation might not possess. The student should be aware that the attribution of laws to ancient authorities is itself a legislative argument -- an argument from the prestige and trustworthiness of the source. This technique -- grounding innovation in the authority of the past -- is a pervasive feature of Judaean legislative rhetoric and has no exact counterpart in the Greco-Roman tradition, where laws are typically argued on their merits without appeal to ancestral attribution. The Judaean legislator argues not only "this law is just, expedient, and practicable" but also "this law has the sanction of a revered ancestor" -- adding the argument from *ethos* (the character of the proposer) to the arguments from substance.

The student should also observe the cumulative structure of Ezra's enactments: taken individually, each addresses a modest practical need; taken together, they constitute a comprehensive program for the ordering of communal life -- a rhythm of Torah reading, judicial activity, domestic preparation, and ritual observance that shapes the weekly experience of the entire community. The legislator who can design such a program demonstrates not merely the ability to argue for individual laws but the capacity for *systemic vision* -- the ability to see how multiple enactments interact and reinforce each other to create a coherent pattern of life. This systemic dimension of the legislative exercise exceeds what the Greek handbooks describe, but it is implicit in the exercise's position as the capstone of the curriculum: the student who has mastered all thirteen preceding exercises should now be able to see not only the parts but the whole.

#### The Exercise

The student should practice the introduction of a law by the following method.

First, formulate a proposed enactment -- either a positive regulation or a preventive restriction -- that addresses a genuine need in the community. The proposed enactment should be specific enough to admit of concrete analysis and general enough to raise questions of principle.

Second, argue *for* the enactment, deploying all six heads of argument: justice (who will benefit and how?), legality (what existing principles support it?), possibility (can the community bear it?), consistency (is it consonant with the spirit of the law?), expedience (what practical benefits will it produce?), and propriety (does it honor human dignity?).

Third, argue *against* the enactment, deploying the same six heads: injustice (who will be harmed?), illegality (what existing principles does it contradict?), impossibility (can the community not bear it?), inconsistency (does it violate the spirit of the law?), inexpedience (do the costs exceed the benefits?), and impropriety (does it affront human dignity or communal norms?).

Fourth, compose a conclusion that weighs both sides and arrives at a reasoned judgment -- recommending adoption, rejection, or modification of the proposed enactment.

The teacher should assign: "Argue for and against the following proposed enactment: that a sage who has been offered a position of communal leadership must accept it, even against his personal preference, if no equally qualified alternative is available. Deploy all six heads of argument on each side, and arrive at a reasoned conclusion." The student should present both sides of the argument and his conclusion to the class, and the class should evaluate the fullness of his argumentation, the fairness of his treatment of both sides, and the wisdom of his final judgment.[^183]

---

#### Transition: From the Preliminary Exercises to Complete Discourse

Having completed the fourteen preliminary exercises -- from the fable, which is the simplest, to the introduction of a law, which is the most complex -- the student is now prepared to undertake the composition of complete orations. The exercises he has practiced are not ends in themselves; they are the elements from which finished discourse is constructed, the bricks and mortar from which the edifice of a complete speech is built. The sage who has mastered the fable can illustrate; the sage who has mastered the narrative can set the scene; the sage who has mastered the chreia and the maxim can anchor his speech in the authority of tradition; the sage who has mastered refutation and confirmation can test and defend propositions; the sage who has mastered the commonplace can amplify and denounce; the sage who has mastered the encomium and the invective can praise and blame; the sage who has mastered comparison can set two subjects beside each other for mutual illumination; the sage who has mastered speech-in-character can give voice to those who are absent or silent; the sage who has mastered description can make the absent present through the vividness of his language; the sage who has mastered the thesis can argue general propositions on both sides; and the sage who has mastered the introduction of a law can shape the legislation that governs the life of the community.

All of these skills, practiced separately, must now be combined. A complete halakhic discourse may require narrative (to establish the facts of the case), comparison (to juxtapose the case with relevant precedents), refutation and confirmation (to test the proposed ruling against objections), thesis-argumentation (to establish the underlying principle), and description (to make vivid the practical consequences of the decision). A complete midrashic sermon may require the fable and the mashal, the chreia and the maxim, the encomium and the speech-in-character, woven together into a single sustained performance that instructs, delights, and moves the audience. A complete legislative address may require, as we have seen, every exercise in the curriculum without exception. The student who proceeds to the next stage of training will find that the preliminary exercises have furnished him with every tool he needs. What remains is to learn how to use them together -- to discover the art of arrangement and combination that transforms isolated exercises into unified, powerful discourse.

We may note, in closing this section of our treatise, that the Judaean tradition and the Greco-Roman tradition have arrived, by different paths and in different idioms, at a remarkably similar understanding of the relationship between preliminary exercises and mature discourse. The Greek rhetoricians designed the progymnasmata as a graduated sequence that builds from simple to complex, from imitation to invention, from the handling of inherited materials to the creation of original argument. The Judaean sages, whether or not they were consciously following the Greek model, developed a pedagogical tradition that follows the same logic: the student begins by learning to recite the teachings of others, progresses to the analysis and testing of those teachings, and finally arrives at the capacity for independent halakhic reasoning and midrashic composition. In both traditions, the goal is the same: to produce an orator -- or a sage -- who can speak with authority, argue with precision, move with eloquence, and persuade with justice. The student who has completed the preliminary exercises and now undertakes the composition of full discourse stands at the threshold of that achievement.

---

[^139]: On *synkrisis* (comparison) as a progymnasmata exercise, see Theon, *Progymnasmata* 10 (Kennedy, *Progymnasmata: Greek Textbooks of Prose Composition and Rhetoric* [Atlanta: Society of Biblical Literature, 2003], 62-65); Aphthonius, *Progymnasmata* 10 (Kennedy, 112-115); and Hermogenes, *Progymnasmata* 8 (Kennedy, 82-84). Theon defines synkrisis as "a speech which sets things together and compares them by means of their common similarities and differences," emphasizing that comparison may conclude either with a judgment of superiority or with a declaration of equality.

[^140]: Theon, *Progymnasmata* 10 (Kennedy, 62-63), instructs that comparison should proceed through the same categories (*topoi*) as the encomium -- origin, upbringing, education, deeds, fortune, manner of death, posthumous reputation -- but applied to both subjects simultaneously, so that each category yields a point of comparison.

[^141]: Aphthonius, *Progymnasmata* 10 (Kennedy, 112-115), provides a model comparison of Achilles and Hector in which each warrior's qualities are examined point by point. The model demonstrates the principle that comparison should illuminate both subjects, not merely praise one at the expense of the other. On Aphthonius's pedagogical method, see Webb, *Ekphrasis, Imagination and Persuasion in Ancient Rhetorical Theory and Practice* (Farnham: Ashgate, 2009), 42-48.

[^142]: On Plutarch's *Parallel Lives* as the literary culmination of the synkrisis exercise, see Duff, *Plutarch's Lives: Exploring Virtue and Vice* (Oxford: Clarendon Press, 1999), 243-286; and Pelling, "Synkrisis in Plutarch's Lives," in *Miscellanea Plutarchea*, ed. Brenk and Gallo (Ferrara, 1986), 83-96. Plutarch's formal *synkriseis* -- the comparative chapters appended to each pair of biographies -- represent the most systematic application of the progymnasmata exercise to large-scale literary composition in the ancient world.

[^143]: BT Eruvin 13b. The declaration "both these and these are the words of the living God" is one of the most remarkable theological and epistemological statements in rabbinic literature. On its implications for the theory of legal pluralism, see Halbertal, *People of the Book: Canon, Meaning, and Authority* (Cambridge, MA: Harvard University Press, 1997), 52-72. On its significance as a mode of comparison that preserves both terms, compare the more agonistic orientation of the Plutarchan synkrisis, in which a verdict of superiority is typically (if tactfully) rendered.

[^144]: BT Kiddushin 40b. On this passage as a model of the thesis exercise, see below, section II.M. The resolution -- "study is greater, for study leads to action" -- represents a characteristic Judaean form of comparison: a hierarchical synthesis that preserves both terms by establishing their causal relationship. Compare Quintilian's discussion of the thesis at *Inst. Or.* 3.5.5-18.

[^145]: BT Yoma 9b. On the comparison of the two Temples as a vehicle for moral and theological analysis, see Urbach, *The Sages: Their Concepts and Beliefs*, trans. Abrahams (Jerusalem: Magnes Press, 1975), 620-635; and Stern, *Parables in Midrash: Narrative and Exegesis in Rabbinic Literature* (Cambridge, MA: Harvard University Press, 1991), 155-162.

[^146]: Mishnah Avot 5:12 (in some editions 5:15). The fourfold typology of students illustrates what we might call "comparison by combinatorial analysis" -- a form of structured comparison in which all logically possible combinations of two variables are enumerated and evaluated. This form has no direct parallel in the Greek progymnasmata handbooks.

[^147]: On the numerical lists of Pirqe Avot as a distinctive form of Judaean comparison, see Tropper, *Wisdom, Politics, and Historiography: Tractate Avot in the Context of the Graeco-Roman Near East* (Oxford: Oxford University Press, 2004), 185-215. The multi-term comparison generated by these lists represents a form of systematic classification that extends the two-term synkrisis of the Greek tradition into a more comprehensive analytical framework.

[^148]: Compare Theon's instructions for the comparison exercise (*Progymnasmata* 10; Kennedy, 62-65), which emphasize that comparison may conclude with a judgment of superiority, a judgment of equality, or a judgment of complementarity. The Judaean tradition characteristically favors the third option -- comparison that reveals complementarity rather than hierarchy -- as illustrated by the comparison of Moses and Aaron ("Moses and Aaron are equal," Mekhilta, Pisha 1) and the comparison of the schools of Hillel and Shammai ("Both these and these are the words of the living God").

[^149]: On *ethopoiia* (speech-in-character) as a progymnasmata exercise, see Theon, *Progymnasmata* 8 (Kennedy, *Progymnasmata*, 47-49), who defines it as "the imitation of the character of a person proposed"; Hermogenes, *Progymnasmata* 9 (Kennedy, 84-86), who distinguishes three temporal orientations (past, present, future); and Aphthonius, *Progymnasmata* 11 (Kennedy, 115-117), who provides model speeches for Niobe and Achilles. The Greek term *ethopoiia* (from *ethos*, character, and *poiein*, to make) literally means "character-making" -- the making of character visible through the medium of speech.

[^150]: Theon, *Progymnasmata* 8 (Kennedy, 47-49). Theon's instruction that the student must consider the speaker's character, the audience, and the occasion corresponds precisely to the classical requirement of *decorum* -- the principle that speech must be fitting to its circumstances. On *decorum* as a rhetorical principle, see Quintilian, *Inst. Or.* 11.1.1-93.

[^151]: Hermogenes, *Progymnasmata* 9 (Kennedy, 84-86). The threefold temporal orientation -- past, present, future -- provides a structure for the exercise that ensures the composed speech possesses depth and dimension. A speech that addresses only the present moment lacks the resonance that comes from reflection on the past and anticipation of the future.

[^152]: Quintilian, *Inst. Or.* 3.8.49-54. Quintilian's emphasis on *decorum* in the ethopoeia exercise -- "we must consider who it is that speaks" -- is the Latin counterpart of the Greek requirement that the speech be *prepousa* (fitting). On the rabbinic practice of ethopoeia in midrash, see Kugel, "Two Introductions to Midrash," in *Midrash and Literature*, ed. Hartman and Budick (New Haven: Yale University Press, 1986), 77-103; and Boyarin, *Intertextuality and the Reading of Midrash* (Bloomington: Indiana University Press, 1990), 93-115.

[^153]: BT Megillah 10b; cf. BT Sanhedrin 39b. On the theological audacity of this passage, which attributes to God an emotion (sorrow for enemies) that complicates the triumphalist reading of the Exodus narrative, see Stern, *Parables in Midrash*, 188-204. The passage functions as a dual ethopoeia -- both the angels and God are given speech (or, in God's case, speech that reveals an emotional disposition) that illuminates their respective characters.

[^154]: Genesis Rabbah 38:13 (Theodor-Albeck edition). On the Abraham-Nimrod dialogue as a philosophical debate in the Socratic mode, see Fischel, *Rabbinic Literature and Greco-Roman Philosophy* (Leiden: Brill, 1973), 120-135, who compares Abraham's chain argument to the Socratic *elenchus*. The narrative has no scriptural basis but became one of the most widely known midrashic narratives in the Judaean tradition.

[^155]: BT Berakhot 32a. On Moses's advocacy before God as a form of forensic rhetoric, see Hidary, *Rabbis and Classical Rhetoric: Secular Education and Preaching in the Talmudic Period* (Cambridge: Cambridge University Press, 2018), 108-114. The passage deploys three distinct argumentative strategies in succession: argument *a fortiori* (the throne metaphor), argument from logical consequence (Moses's commission is void without Israel), and argument from precedent matched to punishment (each patriarch paired with a form of suffering). Quintilian (*Inst. Or.* 3.8.49-54) emphasizes that the orator composing speech-in-character must fit the arguments to the speaker's known qualities -- and Moses's arguments are perfectly suited to his established character: humble, loyal to Israel, and learned in the traditions of the patriarchs.

[^156]: Lamentations Rabbah, Petihta 24 (Buber edition). On Rachel's speech as the rhetorical climax of the passage, see Stern, *Parables in Midrash*, 140-145; and Boyarin, *Intertextuality and the Reading of Midrash*, 121-133. Rachel's argument -- a *qal va-homer* from human to divine compassion -- succeeds where the arguments of the patriarchs and Moses fail because it is grounded in lived moral experience rather than abstract merit. On the prominence of female voices in Lamentations Rabbah, see Mandel, "The Loss of Center: Changing Attitudes Towards the Temple in Aggadic Literature," *Harvard Theological Review* 99 (2006): 17-35.

[^157]: Compare Theon's exercise instructions for ethopoeia (*Progymnasmata* 8; Kennedy, 47-49), which include composing speeches for known persons in specific circumstances, with attention to the character's age, status, emotional state, and the nature of the situation. Hermogenes (*Progymnasmata* 9; Kennedy, 84-86) emphasizes the three temporal orientations. Quintilian (*Inst. Or.* 3.8.49-54) stresses the importance of *decorum*. The Judaean exercise of composing Ruth's speech to Naomi combines all these requirements: the student must attend to Ruth's character (loyal, courageous, selfless), her circumstances (bereaved, an outsider, choosing a foreign land), and the three temporal dimensions (her past in Moab, her present decision, her uncertain future in Bethlehem).

[^158]: Theon, *Progymnasmata* 11 (Kennedy, *Progymnasmata*, 65-69). Theon's definition of ekphrasis -- "a descriptive speech that brings the subject vividly (*enargos*) before the eyes" -- establishes *enargeia* as the defining quality of the exercise. Cf. Hermogenes, *Progymnasmata* 10 (Kennedy, 86), who provides a similar definition. On the concept of *enargeia* and its centrality to the theory of description, see Webb, *Ekphrasis, Imagination and Persuasion*, 87-130.

[^159]: Aphthonius, *Progymnasmata* 12 (Kennedy, 117-120). Aphthonius provides model descriptions of the Alexandrian acropolis and a battle scene, demonstrating the accumulation of sensory detail as the primary technique for achieving *enargeia*.

[^160]: Quintilian, *Inst. Or.* 8.3.61-71. Quintilian's term *evidentia* is his Latin rendering of the Greek *enargeia*. He defines it as the quality that makes the hearer "seem not so much to be told about an event as to be present at it." Quintilian's discussion is the most extended treatment of descriptive vividness in the Latin rhetorical tradition. See also Zanker, "Enargeia in the Ancient Criticism of Poetry," *Rheinisches Museum* 124 (1981): 297-311.

[^161]: Mishnah Yoma 3:6. The Mishnah's description of the Day of Atonement service is the most sustained example of ekphrasis in Judaean literature. On its descriptive techniques and its function as sacred memory, see Kulp, "The Origins of the Seder and Haggadah in the Context of the Early History of the Liturgy," *Currents in Biblical Research* 4 (2005): 109-134.

[^162]: Mishnah Sukkah 5:1. The superlative claim functions as a rhetorical frame for the description that follows -- a technique also observed in classical ekphrasis, where the orator establishes the emotional register before proceeding to the description proper.

[^163]: BT Bava Metzia 84a. On the description of Rabbi Yohanan's beauty and its literary functions, see Rubenstein, *Talmudic Stories: Narrative Art, Composition, and Culture* (Baltimore: Johns Hopkins University Press, 1999), 64-92. The chain of beauty -- Adam to Jacob to Rabbi Yohanan -- is itself a comparison through description: each generation represents a diminishment from primordial perfection.

[^164]: BT Gittin 56a. The descending sequence of flour types is a rhetorical device -- a *climax* in reverse (anticlimax or *bathos*) -- that conveys the progressive desperation of the siege through the specificity of its details. Compare Quintilian's discussion of descriptive specificity as a tool of *evidentia* at *Inst. Or.* 8.3.66-70. On the destruction narrative as a literary composition, see Rubenstein, *Talmudic Stories*, 139-175.

[^165]: Compare Theon's exercise instructions for ekphrasis (*Progymnasmata* 11; Kennedy, 65-69), which include composing descriptions of persons, places, events, and times, with attention to sensory detail and the quality of *enargeia*. Aphthonius (*Progymnasmata* 12; Kennedy, 117-120) provides model descriptions. Quintilian (*Inst. Or.* 8.3.61-71) discusses the techniques for achieving *evidentia*. The Judaean exercise of describing Sinai corresponds precisely to the Greek exercise of describing a theophany or a battle: the student must make the hearer feel present at an event of overwhelming sensory and emotional power.

[^166]: On *thesis* as a progymnasmata exercise, see Theon, *Progymnasmata* 12 (Kennedy, *Progymnasmata*, 69-72); Aphthonius, *Progymnasmata* 13 (Kennedy, 120-124); and Hermogenes, *Progymnasmata* 11 (Kennedy, 86-88). Theon defines the thesis as "a logical investigation of a matter under consideration" and distinguishes it from the hypothesis (a question involving specific persons and circumstances).

[^167]: Cicero, *De Inventione* 1.8. Cicero provides the classic distinction: the thesis (or *propositum*) is a general question (*quaestio infinita*), while the hypothesis (or *causa*) is a specific question (*quaestio finita*). Quintilian elaborates the distinction at *Inst. Or.* 2.4.24-25 and 3.5.5-18, arguing that the thesis is more philosophically demanding because it requires reasoning about universal principles. On the relationship between thesis and hypothesis in ancient rhetorical theory, see Kennedy, *The Art of Persuasion in Greece* (Princeton: Princeton University Press, 1963), 10-12.

[^168]: Theon, *Progymnasmata* 12 (Kennedy, 69-72); Aphthonius, *Progymnasmata* 13 (Kennedy, 120-124). Both authors distinguish practical theses (concerning what one should do) from theoretical theses (concerning what is true). Quintilian (*Inst. Or.* 3.5) provides a more detailed taxonomy, distinguishing theses that concern knowledge, action, and the quality of things. The Judaean tradition's characteristic practice of combining practical and theoretical dimensions within a single thesis (as in the debate on suffering, which asks both "what is the nature of suffering?" and "how should one respond to it?") represents a synthesis that the Greek handbooks recognize but do not always emphasize.

[^169]: BT Kiddushin 40b. On this passage as a paradigmatic thesis debate, see the discussion in section II.J above. In the context of the thesis exercise specifically, the passage exemplifies the ideal structure: a general question is formally posed, opposing positions are argued by named authorities, and a collective resolution is reached that preserves the insights of both sides through a causal synthesis.

[^170]: BT Nedarim 37a; Mishnah Avot 1:13, 4:5. The distinction between teaching Torah itself (which must be gratuitous) and providing ancillary services (for which compensation is permitted) is a characteristic Judaean resolution of a thesis: the general principle is upheld in its full force, but a precise conceptual distinction is drawn that allows the system to function without violating the principle. On the economic dimensions of Torah teaching in the tannaitic period, see Hezser, *The Social Structure of the Rabbinic Movement in Roman Palestine* (Tubingen: Mohr Siebeck, 1997), 334-378.

[^171]: BT Berakhot 5a. On the theology of suffering in Judaean literature, see Urbach, *The Sages*, 444-461; and Kraemer, *Responses to Suffering in Classical Rabbinic Literature* (New York: Oxford University Press, 1995), 73-125. The passage in Berakhot 5a-b is the *locus classicus* for the doctrine of *yissurin shel ahavah* (sufferings of love).

[^172]: Mishnah Berakhot 4:4; BT Berakhot 29b. The thesis on the nature of prayer -- fixed or spontaneous -- is resolved through the integration of both positions into a single practice: the set form of prayer (*matbe'a shel tefillah*) provides structure, while *kavvanah* (intentionality) provides the spontaneous element. On the relationship between fixed liturgy and spontaneous prayer in the Judaean tradition, see Heinemann, *Prayer in the Talmud: Forms and Patterns* (Berlin: de Gruyter, 1977), 13-36.

[^173]: Compare Theon's exercise instructions for the thesis (*Progymnasmata* 12; Kennedy, 69-72), which require the student to argue both sides of a general question before reaching a conclusion. Aphthonius (*Progymnasmata* 13; Kennedy, 120-124) provides a model thesis on whether one should marry, demonstrating the deployment of arguments from justice, expedience, and possibility on both sides. Quintilian (*Inst. Or.* 2.4.24-25, 3.5) discusses the thesis as the exercise that most directly trains the student in philosophical argumentation.

[^174]: Aphthonius, *Progymnasmata* 14 (Kennedy, *Progymnasmata*, 124-127). Aphthonius places the introduction of a law (*nomou eisphora*) last among the fourteen exercises, calling it the most demanding because it requires all the skills practiced in the preceding exercises. See also Theon, *Progymnasmata* 13 (Kennedy, 72-76); Hermogenes, *Progymnasmata* 12 (Kennedy, 88); and Quintilian, *Inst. Or.* 2.4.33, who affirms that the proposal of a law is the exercise that most directly prepares the student for actual forensic and deliberative oratory.

[^175]: Theon, *Progymnasmata* 13 (Kennedy, 72-76). Theon discusses the introduction of a law as the natural terminus of the preliminary curriculum, the exercise that bridges the gap between school exercises and the actual work of civic life. The Judaean parallel -- the enactment of *takkanot* (positive regulations) and *gezerot* (preventive decrees) -- represents the most consequential form of discourse in the halakhic tradition, the point at which rhetoric becomes law.

[^176]: The six methods of argument enumerated here -- justice, legality, possibility, consistency, expedience, propriety -- correspond to the *topoi* (commonplaces) for arguing about laws in the Greco-Roman rhetorical tradition. See Aphthonius, *Progymnasmata* 14 (Kennedy, 124-127), who lists legality (*nomimon*), justice (*dikaion*), expedience (*sympheron*), and possibility (*dunaton*) as the principal heads of argument. Hermogenes provides a similar list. Quintilian (*Inst. Or.* 3.8.22-35) discusses the topics for deliberative argumentation, emphasizing the honorable (*honestum*), the useful (*utile*), and the possible (*possibile*).

[^177]: BT Bava Kamma 79b; BT Avodah Zarah 36a; BT Horayot 3b. The principle that legislation must be practicable by the majority of the community is the Judaean counterpart to the Greco-Roman rhetorical topic of *dunaton* (possibility). On this principle and its implications for Judaean legislative theory, see Elon, *Jewish Law: History, Sources, Principles* (Philadelphia: Jewish Publication Society, 1994), vol. 2, 507-534.

[^178]: Mishnah Sheviit 10:3-4. On the prozbul as a paradigmatic exercise in legislative argumentation, see Elon, *Jewish Law*, vol. 2, 421-425; and Urbach, *The Sages*, 570-578. The term *prozbul* is itself a Greek loanword (*prosbol* / *prosbolē* = a presentation to the court), illustrating the interpenetration of Greco-Roman and Judaean legal terminology.

[^179]: Mishnah Gittin 4:2. On Rabban Gamaliel's enactments as social legislation designed to protect women's interests in the divorce process, see Hauptman, *Rereading the Mishnah: A New Approach to Ancient Jewish Texts* (Tubingen: Mohr Siebeck, 2005), 161-185; and Elon, *Jewish Law*, vol. 2, 533-540. The formula *mipnei tikkun ha-olam* ("for the betterment of the world") functions in Judaean legislative rhetoric as a comprehensive appeal to the public good.

[^180]: Mishnah Yadayim 3:5. On the debate over the canonicity of Ecclesiastes and the Song of Songs as a form of legislative deliberation, see Leiman, *The Canonization of Hebrew Scripture: The Talmudic and Midrashic Evidence* (Hamden, CT: Archon Books, 1976), 86-102. Rabbi Akiva's defense of the Song of Songs deploys the rhetorical techniques of *praemunitio* (preemptive defense), *amplificatio* (magnification), and *definitio* (hierarchical classification). See also Lightstone, "The Rabbis' Bible," in *The Canon Debate*, ed. McDonald and Sanders (Peabody, MA: Hendrickson, 2002), 163-184.

[^181]: Mishnah Shabbat 1:4; BT Shabbat 13b, 17a. On the eighteen decrees and the controversy surrounding their enactment, see Alon, *The Jews in Their Land in the Talmudic Age*, vol. 1 (Jerusalem: Magnes Press, 1980), 253-261. The Jerusalem Talmud's comparison of this day to the day of the Golden Calf (JT Shabbat 1:4, 3c) is itself a form of invective directed at the legislative process -- an argument from impropriety that challenges the legitimacy of the enactments by attacking the manner of their adoption.

[^182]: BT Bava Kamma 82a. On the ten enactments of Ezra as foundational Judaean legislation, see Elon, *Jewish Law*, vol. 2, 413-421; and Urbach, *The Sages*, 556-563. The attribution of these enactments to Ezra -- a figure who stands at the transition between the biblical and rabbinic periods -- lends them a quasi-scriptural authority and illustrates the rhetorical practice of anchoring legislative innovation in the prestige of revered predecessors.

[^183]: Compare Aphthonius's model exercise for the introduction of a law (*Progymnasmata* 14; Kennedy, 124-127), which argues for a law rewarding tyrannicides. Aphthonius structures his argument under the heads of legality, justice, expedience, and possibility -- the same categories deployed in the analysis of Judaean legislative argumentation presented above. Quintilian (*Inst. Or.* 2.4.33) affirms that the introduction of a law is the exercise that most directly prepares the student for the work of actual civic oratory. The transition from the preliminary exercises to full discourse mirrors the structure of the Greco-Roman rhetorical curriculum, in which the progymnasmata served as preparatory training for the *meletai* (practice declamations) and ultimately for actual forensic and deliberative speech. In the Judaean context, the transition is from the preliminary exercises to the full practice of halakhic argumentation, midrashic composition, and public preaching. See Bonner, *Education in Ancient Rome: From the Elder Cato to the Younger Pliny* (Berkeley: University of California Press, 1977), 250-276.
# Institutio Oratoria Hebraica

## PART III: STASIS AND INVENTION

### III.A. The Four Questions (Stasis Theory)

The theory of stasis, as Hermagoras of Temnos first systematized it in the second century before the common era, constitutes the foundation of all forensic and deliberative invention. Before the orator can discover what arguments to deploy, he must first determine what *kind* of question is at issue. The failure to identify the stasis correctly is the most fundamental error an advocate can commit --- more damaging than poor style, more fatal than weak delivery --- for it means that the entire discourse will address a question that is not, in fact, the question before the tribunal.[^184]

We possess Hermagoras's system not in his own words, which are largely lost to us, but through the testimony of those who built upon his foundation. Cicero presents the doctrine in the first book of *De Inventione* (1.8--10), where he identifies four constitutiones or states of the case: conjecture (*coniectura*), definition (*definitio*), quality (*qualitas*), and translative or procedural issues (*translatio*).[^185] Hermogenes of Tarsus, writing in the second century of the common era, elaborated the system into a more complex taxonomy in his treatise *On Stases*, distinguishing rational and legal stases and subdividing each with characteristic precision.[^186] Quintilian, in the third book of the *Institutio Oratoria* (3.6), provides his own magisterial synthesis, preferring a tripartite division --- conjecture, quality, and definition --- while acknowledging the translative question as a subordinate concern.[^187]

The student of rhetoric will observe that these four stases are not merely categories for the convenience of the theorist; they represent the four fundamental questions that any inquiry, in any legal system, must ultimately address. They are:

1. **Did it happen?** (*an sit* / *stasis stochastikē*): The question of fact or conjecture.
2. **What is it?** (*quid sit* / *stasis horistikē*): The question of definition.
3. **What is its character?** (*quale sit* / *stasis poiotēs*): The question of quality or degree.
4. **Before whom should it be adjudicated?** (*translatio* / *stasis metalēpsis*): The question of jurisdiction and procedure.

What has not been sufficiently recognized by modern scholarship is that the Judaean legal tradition operates with a stasis framework as sophisticated as anything in the Greco-Roman tradition, and that the procedural texts of the Mishnah --- particularly tractate Sanhedrin and the related forensic tractates --- exhibit a systematic attention to stasis classification that bears close comparison with the theoretical writings of Hermagoras, Cicero, and Hermogenes.

#### The First Stasis: Conjecture (*Stasis Stochastikē* / *Ha-im Hayah?*)

The first and most fundamental stasis is the question of fact: did the alleged act occur? In the Greek rhetorical tradition, this is the *stochastikē stasis*, the stasis of conjecture, so called because the tribunal must aim (*stochazesthai*) at the truth through inference from signs, testimony, and probability, since the fact itself lies in the past and cannot be directly observed.[^188]

We find among the Judaean sages a remarkably elaborate system for the adjudication of this first stasis. The examination of witnesses in capital cases, as prescribed in Mishnah Sanhedrin chapters 4 and 5, constitutes a formal procedure for establishing or refuting the factual basis of the charge. The Mishnah distinguishes two classes of forensic questioning: *haqirot* (examinations) and *bediqot* (investigations).[^189] The seven *haqirot* establish the spatiotemporal coordinates of the alleged act:

> <span dir="rtl">בשבע חקירות היו בודקין את העדים: באיזה שבוע, באיזו שנה, באיזה חודש, בכמה בחודש, באיזה יום, באיזו שעה, באיזה מקום</span>
>
> *They would examine the witnesses with seven examinations: In which septennate? In which year? In which month? On which day of the month? On which day of the week? At which hour? In which place?*[^190]

The student of rhetoric will recognize that these seven questions constitute a systematic application of what the Greek rhetoricians call the *peristasis* --- the circumstances surrounding the act.[^191] Hermagoras himself taught that the circumstances of an act are determined by answering the questions of person (*quis*), act (*quid*), time (*quando*), place (*ubi*), manner (*quomodo*), and cause (*cur*). The Judaean *haqirot* address precisely the temporal and spatial dimensions of this circumstantial inquiry, and with a specificity that exceeds anything prescribed in the extant Greek rhetorical handbooks.

The *bediqot*, by contrast, probe the sensory and material details of the event: What was the color of the garment? What type of fig tree was involved? Was the soil dark or light?[^192] These correspond to what Quintilian calls the *signa* or *semeia* --- the material signs from which the fact may be inferred (Inst. Or. 5.9). A witness who contradicts himself on a *haqirah* (fundamental examination) invalidates the entire testimony; a witness who contradicts himself on a *bediqah* (detailed investigation) merely weakens it. This hierarchical distinction between framework facts and evidentiary details parallels the rhetorical distinction between necessary and contingent signs (*tekmēria* and *sēmeia*) that Aristotle draws in the *Rhetoric* (1.2, 1357b).[^193]

The procedural goal of this elaborate system of conjecture-testing is made explicit in the Mishnah's own language:

> <span dir="rtl">כל המרבה בבדיקות הרי זה משובח</span>
>
> *Whoever increases the number of investigations --- behold, this is praiseworthy.*[^194]

The more extensively the factual basis is probed, the more reliable the judgment. This principle --- that thoroughness in the conjectural stasis is a judicial virtue --- is precisely the counsel that Quintilian gives to the advocate: exhaust the available evidence before venturing an inference (Inst. Or. 5.9.1--9).

Furthermore, the Judaean system imposes a remarkable constraint upon conjecture that has no exact parallel in Greco-Roman procedure: the requirement of prior warning (*hatra'ah*). In capital cases, the witnesses must testify not merely that the accused committed the act, but that the accused was warned immediately before committing it, acknowledged the warning, and proceeded nonetheless:

> <span dir="rtl">עד שיתירו עצמן למיתה</span>
>
> *Until they have surrendered themselves to death.*[^195]

This requirement effectively collapses the gap between conjecture and intention: by demanding proof that the accused acted with full and immediate knowledge of the consequences, the Judaean system eliminates the most difficult aspect of the conjectural stasis --- the inference of mental state from external action. The student of rhetoric will appreciate the elegance of this procedural device: rather than requiring the tribunal to *infer* intent (as Greco-Roman rhetoric requires through the analysis of motive, capacity, and character), the Judaean system requires that intent be *established as an observed fact* through the testimony of the warning witnesses.

#### The Second Stasis: Definition (*Stasis Horistikē* / *Mah Hu?*)

The second stasis arises when the fact is conceded but its classification is disputed. Did Orestes kill his mother? That is not in question. The question is: what *kind* of act was it? Was it murder, or was it righteous vengeance? The stasis of definition --- *horistikē* in the Greek, *definitio* in the Latin --- is the stasis of naming, of categorization, of determining under which legal heading a conceded act falls.[^196]

In the Judaean legal tradition, definitional disputes pervade the entire halakhic corpus, and the system of classification is extraordinarily refined. The paradigmatic example is the Mishnah's enumeration of the thirty-nine categories of labor (*avot melakhah*) prohibited on the Sabbath:

> <span dir="rtl">אבות מלאכות ארבעים חסר אחת: הזורע, והחורש, והקוצר, והמעמר, הדש, והזורה, הבורר, הטוחן, והמרקד, והלש, והאופה, הגוזז את הצמר, המלבנו, והמנפצו, והצובעו, והטווה, והמסך, והעושה שני בתי נירין, האורג שני חוטין, והפוצע שני חוטין, הקושר, והמתיר, והתופר שתי תפירות, הקורע על מנת לתפור שתי תפירות, הצד צבי, השוחטו, והמפשיטו, המולחו, והמעבדו, והמוחקו, והמחתכו, הכותב שתי אותיות, והמוחק על מנת לכתוב שתי אותיות, הבונה, והסותר, המכבה, והמבעיר, המכה בפטיש, והמוציא מרשות לרשות</span>
>
> *The principal categories of labor are forty less one: sowing, plowing, reaping, binding sheaves, threshing, winnowing, selecting, grinding, sifting, kneading, baking, shearing wool, bleaching it, combing it, dyeing it, spinning, stretching the warp, making two heddle loops, weaving two threads, separating two threads, tying, untying, sewing two stitches, tearing in order to sew two stitches, trapping a deer, slaughtering it, flaying it, salting it, tanning its hide, scraping it, cutting it, writing two letters, erasing in order to write two letters, building, demolishing, extinguishing a fire, kindling a fire, striking the final hammer blow, and carrying from one domain to another.*[^197]

This catalogue is itself a masterwork of definitional rhetoric. Each of the thirty-nine categories functions as what the Greek rhetoricians would call a *horos* --- a boundary term that marks the limits of a class of prohibited actions.[^198] The question that arises with respect to any particular act on the Sabbath is definitional: under which of these thirty-nine headings does the act fall? Or does it fall under none of them, being instead a *toledah* (derivative) of one of the principal categories, or perhaps an act not prohibited at all?

Consider the definitional complexity. A man moves an object from a private domain to a public domain. This falls under the thirty-ninth category: *ha-motzi me-reshut li-reshut* --- carrying from one domain to another. But what constitutes a "domain"? What constitutes "carrying"? If a man stands in a private domain and extends his arm into a public domain, holding an object --- has he "carried"? What if the object is placed in the public domain by another person after the first person extended his arm?[^199] These questions are not conjectural --- the facts are stipulated. They are definitional: the tribunal must determine whether the stipulated facts satisfy the definition of the prohibited category.

The student of rhetoric will observe that this mode of definitional argument employs the same analytical technique that Cicero prescribes in the *Topica* (26--28): the division of a genus into its species, the enumeration of the differentia that distinguish one species from another, and the testing of a particular case against the definition to determine whether all elements of the definition are satisfied. The Judaean sages call this process *haqdamat ha-geder* --- the establishment of the boundary --- and they pursue it with a rigor that would satisfy the most demanding Aristotelian logician.[^200]

A further illustration may be drawn from the definitional disputes concerning the categories of ritual impurity. The Mishnah distinguishes *avot ha-tum'ah* (primary sources of impurity) from *toledot ha-tum'ah* (derivative sources), and each category carries different legal consequences.[^201] When the sages debate whether a particular substance or situation constitutes a primary or derivative source of impurity, they are engaged in precisely the kind of definitional argument that Hermogenes describes: the fact is not in dispute; what is disputed is the name, the category, the legal classification under which the fact falls.

#### The Third Stasis: Quality (*Stasis Poiotēs* / *Keitzad Hu?*)

The third stasis --- quality or qualification --- is the stasis in which both the fact and its definition are conceded, but the character, degree, or justifiability of the act is at issue. Hermogenes subdivides this stasis into the pragmatic (concerning the act itself) and the juridical (concerning the legal evaluation of the act).[^202] Quintilian, characteristically, provides a more expansive treatment: quality encompasses everything from the evaluation of motive to the assessment of mitigating circumstances to the weighing of competing values (Inst. Or. 3.6.36--82).

The Judaean legal tradition has developed its own systematic treatment of qualitative stasis, centered on the fundamental distinction between *shogeg* (inadvertent transgression) and *meizid* (deliberate transgression):

> <span dir="rtl">אחד שוגג ואחד מזיד בחילול השם</span>
>
> *Both the inadvertent and the deliberate transgressor [are liable] in matters of the profanation of God's Name.*[^203]

This distinction permeates the entire system. A person who performs a prohibited act on the Sabbath inadvertently --- because he did not know it was the Sabbath, or because he did not know the act was prohibited --- is classified as a *shogeg* and is liable for a sin-offering. A person who performs the same act deliberately is a *meizid* and is, in principle, liable for the death penalty (in the case of Sabbath violation, stoning).[^204] The same physical act, falling under the same definitional category, receives radically different legal treatment depending on the qualitative assessment of the actor's mental state.

The student of rhetoric will recognize this as a precise parallel to the Greco-Roman treatment of the juridical quality (*iuridicialis constitutio*). Cicero distinguishes between the *absolutum* (where the act is defended as right in itself) and the *adsumptivum* (where the defense is based on external circumstances, such as compulsion, accident, or mitigation). The Judaean categories of *shogeg* and *meizid* correspond broadly to Cicero's distinction, but the Judaean system develops the qualitative spectrum with considerably greater refinement.[^205]

Between *shogeg* and *meizid*, the Judaean sages identify a spectrum of intermediate states. There is the category of *mitasseq* --- one who performs an act without any intention whatsoever, as when a person falls and in falling inadvertently causes damage or transgression.[^206] There is the *omer mutar* --- one who mistakenly believes the act is permitted, as distinguished from the *shogeg* who knows the act is prohibited but does not know that it applies to the present circumstances.[^207] There is the category of *ones* --- compulsion, where the actor performs the prohibited deed under duress:

> <span dir="rtl">אונס רחמנא פטריה</span>
>
> *One who acts under compulsion is exempted by the Torah.*[^208]

And there is the category of *meizid* that itself admits of degrees: the deliberate transgressor who acts from appetite is distinguished from the one who acts from spite (*le-hakh'is*), and the latter is treated with greater severity.[^209]

This graduated scale of culpability --- from complete absence of intention (*mitasseq*) through various degrees of inadvertence (*shogeg*) and ignorance (*omer mutar*) to full deliberation (*meizid*) and beyond to spite (*le-hakh'is*) --- constitutes a taxonomy of the qualitative stasis that, in its precision and comprehensiveness, surpasses anything found in the extant Greek and Roman rhetorical handbooks. Quintilian recognizes that the qualitative stasis admits of degrees (Inst. Or. 7.4), but he does not develop a classification of those degrees with the systematicity that the Judaean sages bring to the task.

Furthermore, the Judaean system recognizes mitigating and aggravating circumstances that function precisely as the Greco-Roman *color* --- the characterization of circumstance that determines the qualitative evaluation of the act. A theft committed by day is treated differently from a theft committed by night (because the nighttime intruder may be presumed to pose a mortal threat to the householder).[^210] A killing committed in self-defense (*rodef*) is not merely excused but affirmatively mandated:

> <span dir="rtl">הבא להורגך השכם להורגו</span>
>
> *If one comes to kill you, arise and kill him first.*[^211]

This principle --- that the act of killing is evaluated not merely by the fact and the definition but by the entire circumstantial context in which it occurs --- is the essence of the qualitative stasis.

#### The Fourth Stasis: Procedure (*Stasis Metalēpsis* / *Lifnei Mi?*)

The fourth stasis is the translative or procedural question: not whether the act occurred, what it was, or what character it bears, but whether the present tribunal has jurisdiction to adjudicate it, and whether the proper procedures have been followed. In the Greco-Roman tradition, this stasis was sometimes treated as subordinate to the others, and Quintilian was inclined to subsume it under the general heading of quality (Inst. Or. 3.6.63). But Hermagoras, as reported by Cicero, gave it independent standing, and rightly so --- for a question of jurisdiction is prior to all questions of substance.[^212]

The Judaean legal system devotes extraordinary attention to the procedural stasis. The first chapter of Mishnah Sanhedrin establishes a comprehensive jurisdictional hierarchy:

> <span dir="rtl">דיני ממונות בשלושה. גזילות וחבלות בשלושה. נזק וחצי נזק, תשלומי כפל ותשלומי ארבעה וחמישה בשלושה. האונס והמפתה והמוציא שם רע בשלושה, דברי רבי מאיר. וחכמים אומרים: מוציא שם רע בעשרים ושלושה, מפני שיש בו דיני נפשות</span>
>
> *Property cases [are adjudicated by a court of] three. Robbery and personal injury [by a court of] three. Damages and half-damages, double payment and fourfold and fivefold payment [by a court of] three. The rapist, the seducer, and the slanderer [by a court of] three --- the words of Rabbi Meir. But the Sages say: the slanderer [is adjudicated by a court of] twenty-three, because a capital charge may be involved.*[^213]

The Mishnah continues with a systematic classification of which matters require a court of three, which require twenty-three, and which require the Great Sanhedrin of seventy-one:

> <span dir="rtl">דיני נפשות בעשרים ושלושה... אין דנין לא את השבט ולא את נביא השקר ולא את כהן גדול אלא על פי בית דין של שבעים ואחד. ואין מוציאין למלחמת הרשות אלא על פי בית דין של שבעים ואחד</span>
>
> *Capital cases [are adjudicated by a court of] twenty-three... They may not judge a tribe, or a false prophet, or a High Priest except before a court of seventy-one. They may not declare a war of choice except before a court of seventy-one.*[^214]

The student of rhetoric will observe that this jurisdictional taxonomy operates on a principle of proportionality: the gravity of the matter determines the size and authority of the tribunal. Property cases, where the stakes are financial, require only three judges. Capital cases, where a life is at stake, require twenty-three. Matters affecting the entire nation --- the judgment of a tribe, the authorization of war --- require the full seventy-one. This principle of proportional jurisdiction is itself a rhetorical argument, for it embodies and communicates a hierarchy of values: human life is more weighty than property, and national welfare is more weighty than individual life.

The procedural stasis also governs questions of standing, competence, and disqualification. The Mishnah specifies that certain persons are disqualified from serving as judges or witnesses: gamblers, usurers, pigeon-racers, and dealers in sabbatical-year produce.[^215] A relative of either party is disqualified, as is a person with a personal interest in the outcome.[^216] These rules of disqualification function as procedural arguments: before the substance of the case is reached, the composition and authority of the tribunal itself must be established as legitimate. The advocate who challenges the composition of the court is arguing in the translative stasis, and the Judaean system provides ample grounds for such challenges.

In sum, the Judaean legal system operates with all four stases fully developed and systematically integrated. The examination of witnesses addresses the conjectural stasis. The classification of acts under legal categories addresses the definitional stasis. The evaluation of intent, circumstances, and degree of culpability addresses the qualitative stasis. And the hierarchical system of courts, the rules of disqualification, and the procedural requirements of warning and testimony address the translative stasis. No ancient legal system --- not the Athenian, not the Roman --- can claim a more comprehensive or systematic application of stasis theory to forensic practice.[^217]

---

### III.B. The Topics of Invention (*Topoi* / *Middot*)

The theory of topics (*topoi* in the Greek, *loci* in the Latin) is the theory of where arguments are found. Aristotle, in his treatise entitled precisely *Topics* (*Topika*), established the foundation: a topos is a "place" --- a category, a heading, a seat of argument --- to which the orator may resort when seeking premises for his reasoning.[^218] The topos is not itself an argument; it is a *source* of arguments, a generative principle from which specific arguments may be derived for specific cases. Cicero, in his own *Topica* --- composed, he tells us, from memory while sailing to Greece --- adapted Aristotle's system for Roman forensic practice, organizing the topics under the headings of definition, partition, etymology, genus, species, similarity, difference, contrary, consequence, antecedent, contradiction, cause, effect, and comparison.[^219] Quintilian devotes the greater part of Book 5 of the *Institutio* to the topics of invention, treating them as the practical instruments by which the orator discovers and marshals his proofs (Inst. Or. 5.10).[^220]

What has been insufficiently appreciated --- though David Daube perceived it with characteristic acuity in his landmark 1949 essay, and Saul Lieberman confirmed it with his customary philological precision in 1950 --- is that the Judaean sages have codified their own system of topics with a degree of formalization that exceeds anything found in the Greco-Roman tradition.[^221] For whereas Aristotle's topics are presented discursively, and Cicero's are enumerated but without rigid formalization, the Judaean middot --- the hermeneutical rules by which Scripture is expounded and legal arguments are constructed --- are presented as a fixed, numbered, canonical list, recited daily as part of the liturgy, and transmitted with the authority of named masters.

The most celebrated of these codifications is the list of thirteen rules (*middot*) attributed to Rabbi Ishmael ben Elisha, a tanna of the early second century of the common era. This list, preserved in the *Baraita de-Rabbi Ishmael* that opens the tannaitic midrash *Sifra*, functions as the canonical enumeration of the topics of halakhic invention:

> <span dir="rtl">רבי ישמעאל אומר: בשלוש עשרה מידות התורה נדרשת</span>
>
> *Rabbi Ishmael says: The Torah is expounded by thirteen rules.*[^222]

Let us examine each of these thirteen topics in turn, with attention to both their logical structure and their parallels in Greco-Roman rhetorical theory.

**1. *Qal va-homer* (a fortiori argument):**

> <span dir="rtl">קל וחומר</span>

The first and most natural of the hermeneutical topics is the argument from the lesser to the greater. If a certain consequence holds in a lesser case, it must hold *a fortiori* in a greater case. This is the *argumentum a minori ad maius* of the Latin rhetoricians, and it corresponds to one of Aristotle's topics of degree (*Topics* 2.10, 114b--115a).[^223]
